HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Former L. L. Bean Official Named to Lead Lands' End

BYLINE: By ANDREA ADELSON

 BODY:
Lands' End Inc., the catalogue merchant, announced yesterday that William T. End would succeed Richard C. Anderson as chief executive on Feb. 1.
In addition, the company said David Dyer, a merchandising and sales vice president, had been named a vice chairman. Mr. Anderson, who is 63, will remain a vice chairman and stay on as a consultant. 
 Analysts said the moves had been anticipated and should not result in major changes at the company, the nation's fourth-largest direct-mail merchant.
"These gentlemen were brought in and given more responsibility in the last nine months or so, so the succession was pretty clear," said Steven Ashley, a retail analyst with Cleary, Gull, Reiland & McDevitt in Milwaukee.
 
Lower Response Rates
 Even so, industry analysts said that Lands' End, based in Dodgeville, Wis., was facing hurdles like lower response rates to its catalogues, which could result in cost-cutting drives to lower inventory or staff. The mail-order retailer reported earnings of $5.5 million in its third quarter ended Oct. 30, down 5 percent from the year earlier.
"They've had tough times recently," Laura Christiana, editor of Catalog Age, a monthly trade publication, said, predicting weak fourth-quarter results. For example, according to Ms. Christiana, Lands' End mailed one of its Christmas holiday catalogues before the November elections and missed the upswing in consumer confidence that followed Bill Clinton's Presidential victory.
Lands' End was begun in 1963 as a sailboat equipment and hardware catalogue. Sailing apparel, like foul-weather gear, was added soon after and eventually outsold sailing equipment, so hardware was dropped in 1976.
 
Executive from L. L. Bean
 Mr. End, who is 45 and spent 15 years with the rival L. L. Bean catalogue, joined the company in 1991 and became president a year later. His name led to a bit of ribbing in the company newsletter, employees said. "Lands' End Lands End" was one headline.
Mr. Dyer, who is 43, was hired in 1989, helping the company introduce "Coming Home With Lands' End," one of its three specialty catalogues. Among bed and bath items, it features handmade quilts.
Mr. Ashley, the analyst, said it had been the goal of the company's founding chairman, Gary Comer, who is 64, to groom heirs to the chief executive's post who will perpetuate the company's culture. Mr. Comer is not expected to retire soon.

HEADLINE: U.S. SAYS ECONOMY GREW AT FAST PACE IN FOURTH QUARTER

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 28

 BODY:
The economy grew at a faster-than-expected annual rate of 3.8 percent in the final quarter of 1992, the strongest performance in four years, the Commerce Department reported today.
The encouraging report was issued as the chairman of the Federal Reserve Board, Alan Greenspan, offered a surprisingly strong endorsement of President Clinton's plans to cut the Federal budget deficit. He met with the President this morning, and afterward told Congress that Mr. Clinton's goal of reducing the annual deficit by $145 billion by the end of his term, from a projected $319 billion, seemed about right. 
Mr. Greenspan also suggested that if the effort weakened the economy, the central bank might cut interest rates to soften the blow. [Page D2.]
 
 
Possible Easing of Conflict
 The Fed chairman's endorsement of the deficit-reduction plan came a day after he gave his blessing to the Administration's plan for about $20 billion in Federal spending to stimulate the economy. His support signals that in contrast to the tug-of-war often waged in recent years between the Federal Reserve and the White House, the Fed will support the new Administration's fiscal policy and is prepared to take monetary steps to keep the economy strong should it falter.
Despite the unexpectedly strong report on fourth-quarter growth, investors pushed long-term interest rates down today, viewing the economic strength not as a harbinger of inflation but as one more reason for the Clinton Administration to keep its fiscal-stimulus proposals modest.
The growth was spurred largely by consumers, but the economy also benefited from a big jump in housing and increased investments by businesses in new plants and equipment, especially computers.
 
 
Help for Governments
 The faster economic growth of the last quarter will help raise tax payments, shrinking the Federal budget deficit and easing financial burdens on state and local governments. A higher gross domestic product also reduces the need for benefit payments associated with hard times.
 Although initial jobless claims rose slightly in the latest week, the number of jobless Americans fell to 9.28 million last month, from 9.98 million in June.
Most private economists were quick to contend that the October-December pace, the fastest of seven straight quarters of expansion, was too rapid to continue and that growth would soon slow.
"That kind of spending is not sustainable," said Thomas J. Moeller, chief economist for Chancellor Capital Management in New York. He referred specifically to a 4.3 percent rate of growth in consumer outlays in the fourth quarter, after a hefty 3.7 percent gain during the summer.
In a survey of leading economists earlier this month by Blue Chip Economic Indicators, an economic newsletter, the consensus was that growth would amount to 2.8 percent for the first quarter of 1993, followed by slight improvements in succeeding quarters that would raise growth for the year to 2.9 percent.
For the fourth quarter of last year, housing increased at a 29.1 percent annual rate and business investment rose 9.7 percent. Those gains more than offset declines in inventory; spending by Federal, state and local governments, and results in the import-export sector.
Consumer spending accounts for just over two-thirds of an economy that rose above the $6 trillion mark, in terms of current dollars, during the latest quarter. The growth in the fourth quarter was the strongest since a 3.9 percent rate was recorded in the fourth quarter of 1988, at the end of Ronald Reagan's Presidency.
But Mr. Moeller and other analysts point to lagging growth in jobs and incomes, as well as to related depressants -- a shrinking military establishment, poor conditions in commercial real estate and floundering economies abroad.
Indeed, the Labor Department today reported a third straight weekly increase in first-time claims for unemployment benefits, to the highest level since mid-November. Although the rise was only 2,000, analysts had been expecting a modest decline.
 
Greater Efficiency Seen
 What is happening, economists said, is that the gross domestic product -- the total output of goods and services within American boundaries -- is being swelled by improvements in corporate efficiency. "Growth is being staffed by more productive workers instead of just more workers," said William C. Dunkelberg, chief economist for the National Federation of Independent Business.
This ultimately raises living standards, but in the short run acts as a drag on the economy as idle workers -- as well as their relatives and neighbors -- cut their spending.
"We've got an awful lot of work to do," President Clinton said today as he met with advisers, mentioning "trouble about lost jobs."
One measure of the previously sluggish nature of the economic rebound was the fact that since the recession officially ended in the first quarter of 1991, G.D.P. has climbed only 1.6 percent above its pre-recession peak.
By contrast, said Carol S. Carson, head of the Commerce Department's Bureau of Economic Analysis, G.D.P. during the first seven quarters of the preceding five expansions rose 5.8 percent above its pre-recession level.
Today's report also shed light on how consumers paid for their holiday shopping sprees. Credit-card issuers had reported a surge in customer charges, but the Government found that incomes rose as well, at least temporarily.
Current-dollar personal income surged $93.7 billion during the quarter, up from $33.1 billion during the July-September period, the report showed. An official said about $9 billion of the latest increase represented year-end bonus payments by Wall Street firms and automobile companies. Many such payments have been accelerated because of Mr. Clinton's expressed intention to raise taxes on the wealthy.
The nation's savings rate -- personal savings as a percentage of disposable income -- scarcely changed in the fourth quarter, edging down to 4.5 percent from 4.6, the report showed.
 
Inventories Decrease
 The drawdown of inventories subtracted $7.8 billion from the gross domestic product during the quarter, after growth in inventories added $7.2 billion last summer. The movement was considered a favorable sign because supplies will probably need to be replenished even if demand tails off. The department assumed a moderate $20 billion drawdown of inventories in December.
Other pluses for the economy are low inflation, low interest rates and competitively low foreign-exchange rates for the dollar, analysts said.

HEADLINE: Israel's Highest Court Upholds The Deportation of Palestinians

BYLINE: By CLYDE HABERMAN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: JERUSALEM, Jan. 28

 BODY:
In a much-awaited decision carrying immediate implications for Israel's diplomatic standing and for the future of the Middle East peace talks, the Israeli High Court of Justice today unanimously validated the deportation of more than 400 Palestinians from the occupied territories to Lebanon.
Although the seven justices expressed reservations about some aspects of the Government action, they declared the mid-December mass expulsions to be legitimate and rejected appeals to bring the Palestinians back. 
 The ruling, welcomed by the Government and denounced by Palestinian leaders and Arab countries, is likely to put Israel squarely on a collision course with the United Nations Security Council, which has condemned the deportations as a violation of international law and is poised to do so again.
 
Question of U.N. and U.S.
 For Israeli leaders, a dominant question now is whether they can avoid United Nations sanctions, especially after Secretary General Boutros Boutros-Ghali recommended this week that the council take "whatever measures are required" to enforce its demand that the banished Palestinians be returned.
The decision upholding the deportation disappointed the Clinton Administration, which was clearly hoping that the court might relieve it of having to choose between voting sanctions against Israel or antagonizing the Arab states. The Bush Administration has already voted to condemn Israel for the deportations. [Page A9.]
Prime Minister Yitzhak Rabin expressed confidence that the Clinton Administration would block any attempt at using sanctions to bolster the condemnation. The Palestine Liberation Organization, for instance, has circulated a draft resolution to ban Israel from human rights meetings and to prevent foreign companies from doing business with the Israelis in the occupied territories.
At the United Nations, the P.L.O. said it had asked the nonaligned members of the Security Council to sponsor its resolution, which has been endorsed by all Arab countries. But Venezuela and Pakistan, two nonaligned members of the Council, said they wanted to avoid a United States veto and would seek changes in the draft Arab resolution. The other nonaligned members are Cape Verde, Djibouti and Morocco. Brazil, a developing country that does not belong to the nonaligned movement, is also a Security Council member.
 
'The Struggle That Awaits Us'
 Mr. Rabin said, "It's hard for me to believe that an Administration and President in the United States wouldn't fight against sanctions." This has been American policy "for 35 years or more," he added.
Nonetheless, Gad Yaacobi, Israel's representative at the United Nations, cautioned that the court ruling "does not change at the moment the struggle that awaits us in the Security Council."
The court ruling also does not change uncertainties about immediate prospects for the American-brokered peace negotiations, which are on hold with no date set for new sessions.
Israeli officials have said repeatedly that they are sure that Syria and Jordan will show up. But Palestinian delegates insist they will boycott the talks until all the exiles are returned, not just the 19 who have been brought back in the last few weeks because of illness and mistakes in their expulsions.
While it is not the first time that the Palestinian representatives have threatened to stay away from the talks, all signs suggest that this time they mean it. If anything, the High Court decision reinforced the delegates' resolve. "We hold Israel responsible for the deadlock in the peace talks and for the total halt to negotiations," said Hanan Ashrawi, spokeswoman for the Palestinian team.
 
Exiles Are Adamant
 At their makeshift camp in southern Lebanon, the nearly 400 remaining Palestinian exiles denounced both the court and Mr. Rabin as "Nazis" and "terrorists." The banished men, most of them said by Israel to be members and sympathizers of the militant Islamic group Hamas, also rejected a High Court call for procedures that would make it possible for them to appeal their expulsions to a special military committee on an individual basis.
The Palestinians have said any appeal would give the deportations legitimacy, and they reiterated that they would remain where they are, between Israeli and Lebanese Army checkpoints, until they are returned to the West Bank and Gaza Strip.
"Do you have any other choice than return?" their leader, Abdul Aziz al-Rantisi, asked men gathered on a mountain slope near the tents.
"No! No! No!" they shouted.
Mr. Rabin rejected the Palestinians' assertions that the deportations, which had followed the killing of five Israeli servicemen by an armed wing of Hamas, were an obstacle to peace talks. The Israeli radio quoted him as saying that Israel "did not make continuing talks contingent on an end to terror" and that therefore "it was inconceivable that the Arabs should make continuation of the peace talks dependent on the cessation of the Government's legal activities."
Given the fallout from the deportations, some Israelis, both in and out of the Government, had hoped that the High Court might issue an order that could make diplomatic life easier for them. Mr. Rabin was decidedly not in this group, saying he did not seek any such assistance.
 
Rabin's Position Hardens
 In the last few days, his position has hardened, especially after Mr. Boutros-Ghali's call for new Security Council measures. Interviewed on television Wednesday night, the Prime Minister called it a "twisted and repulsive report," and complained that international criticism was one-sided because it had focused on the expulsions and not on the Hamas violence that had led to them.
"I am not surprised by the report," he said. "Boutros-Ghali is an Egyptian."
Essentially, the court did what it had done almost unfailingly in past deportation cases, which had never involved anywhere near as many people at one time as in this situation: It did not try to undo a Government action taken in the name of national security, and said Israel had the right to expel people according to emergency regulations left over from the British Mandate in Palestine, before the creation of Israel in 1948.
Among those once deported by the British in the 1940's was Meir Shamgar, the Chief Justice who announced the High Court decision today.
 
Protests Dismissed
 The judges dismissed protests from Israeli civil-rights lawyers that the expulsions were not valid because, among other things, the Palestinians had been summarily removed to Lebanon without first being able to appeal the orders to a military "advisory committee."
Those appeals can take place after the fact, the court said. Without elaborating on how it could be done, the court ordered the Government to enable the men to meet in safety with their lawyers. It is at those military committee hearings, the court said, that the Palestinians can make their argument that the expulsions violate the Fourth Geneva Convention of 1949, which lays out rules of conduct in occupied areas.
Mr. Rabin's office said that to speed the hearings, the Army had begun setting up 14 appeals committees. According to the Israeli radio, the army was clearing ground near a checkpoint in what Israel calls its security zone in southern Lebanon to create an area where lawyers and exiles can meet.
For now, however, this effort would seem beside the point because the Palestinians say they will not bring an appeal.
The one quarrel that the High Court had with the Government was with a special emergency order, written with an eye to eluding normal appeal procedures, that had authorized the expulsion of hundreds of people at one time by declaring it to be a "temporary removal" for no more than two years.
 
Consistent With Past Rulings
 The judges voided the order. But they added that as a practical matter it made no difference because the Palestinians had also been expelled as individuals, and those orders were valid. The finding was consistent with their past rulings that the Geneva Convention had in mind Nazi-style deportations of entire peoples and not the banishment of specific individuals deemed to be security threats.
Some civil-rights lawyers argued that the decision on the emergency order, while technical, amounted to a court warning against large-scale deportations in the future. "The illegality of the Government's actions was made clear, and no Government will be able to do this again," said Joshua Schoffman, who appealed in behalf of the Palestinians for the Association for Civil Rights in Israel.
Uri Dromi, director of the Government Press Office, acknowledged that "we got a strong indication from the court that this kind of mass deportation is not exactly an advisable thing."
But Leah Tsemel, another lawyer for the Palestinians, said the court did not rule out the expulsion of any number of people so long as each one receives an individual order. "What prevents the Army from issuing 4,000 individual deportation orders?" she said, calling the ruling a "catastrophe."

HEADLINE: Chinese Buy American Dream by the Inch

DATELINE: GUANGZHOU, China

 BODY:
Sooner or later someone was going to discover a way to make money on the Chinese craze for America. Now it looks as if someone has -- perhaps at the expense of thousands of naive Chinese.
All across the country, from the ice-glazed terrain of Harbin in the north to this moneyed metropolis in the south, Chinese people are talking about the chance to buy a "piece of America." 
 "I want to be a landowner in America!" said Bi Fei, a young financier who carries a cellular telephone and dashes around from luxury hotel to fancy restaurant. "How many Chinese people can say that they own American land?"
Driven by a belief that possessing land in the United States gives the owner the right to inspect the property -- and therefore obtain that sorely wanted visa -- tens of thousands of Chinese are rushing to buy tiny bits of America.
 
A $1,700 Gimmick
 In the United States, a "Deed of Land to the United States of America" is a deed to a square inch of terrain in each of the 50 states. It sells for $49.95 as a novelty gift, like a pet rock or canned air.
But in China, the deed represents a dream, and prices are going berserk. In Guangzhou, the price this month was $468, in Beijing $700. In Shanghai, the price once hit $1,700.
While the novelty was intended for sale primarily in the United States, 100,000 Chinese have purchased the deeds since they arrived on the market in China last November. That is twice as many as bought the deeds in the United States, where they have been available for more than a year.
The mania reflects the infatuation with most things American. But what really captures the Chinese imagination is the prospect that as American landowners, they will be allowed to visit their property. This is coupled with the fear that, as with real estate in China, there is not much around.
 
An Inch Here, an Inch There
 In fact there is plenty, according to American Acres Inc., which sells the product in the United States. The company, which says it has purchased an acre in each of the states, divided its holdings into 6,272,640 shares, one for each square inch. Three of the properties are in Saint Lawrence County, N.Y.; Danbury, Conn., and Oldmans Township, N.J.
About 250,000 deeds have been sold around the world, with China by far the largest market.
In Guangzhou, where enough people have hidden bank accounts that they can begin to appreciate the concept of buying novelties, a few business executives are thinking about the deeds as a collector's item or a speculative stock.
But for many Chinese the land offers a remote possibility: the chance to clinch that visa to America. Unfortunately, the Chinese are mistaken.
United States Embassy and consular officials deny that a deed will help obtain a visa. The calls and the questions, however, are still coming in.
The fine print is in English, and many buyers ignore it.
In a dilapidated Guangzhou office, a middle-aged engineer tells how he was sadly disappointed when the United States Consulate told him his property would not win him a visa.
 
The Fine Print
 "What we bought is not a piece of land at all," he complained. "What we have is just a piece of paper. We can't even resell the land on our own. The only thing we can resell are the pieces of paper."
The engineer said his company had purchased several hundred shares in the hope of reselling them at a profit. But after discovering that the deeds do not help to get a visa, the company decided to drop its plans and absorb the loss.
In fact, the deed states that owners shall not resell it as a security, but a secondary market has sprouted anyway in several cities. Many of the people who buy the deeds, even distributors who buy in bulk, cannot read English and seem to have little idea what they are buying.
 
The Real Estate 'Market'
 "The sellers tell us to buy now, when the price of land in America is low, and then sell later, when the economy and the real estate market rises," said a Chinese reporter who covers business and the economy.
The main distributor in China is the Haiwang Group, an influential company based in Shenzhen, next to Hong Kong. Haiwang plans to arrange for new property owners to visit the United States to "inspect" their land, a marketing strategy that seems to have contributed to the confusion over visas.
Haiwang representatives refused to comment when reached by phone in Shenzhen. "I'm sorry, I can't answer your questions," said a staff member who answered the phone and would not give his name. "Our officials are on vacation. They cannot be contacted."
Scott Moger, the president of American Acres, which is based in New York, said the marketing in China was handled by Haiwang. He said he was concerned that buyers may be misinformed, and he added that he had given interviews to the BBC and Voice of America, which broadcast to China, explaining that the deeds are novelties rather than investments.
"I know nothing about China," Mr. Moger said in a telephone interview. "We sell it at one price. The subdistributors seem to be trying to mark it up."

GRAPHIC: Photos: In Danbury, Conn., a one-acre site holds 6,272,640 parcels of land. (pg. A1); Scott Moger, the president of American Acres, said the square-inch tracts of land his company is marketing are meant to be novelties. But in China, they are selling wildly in hopes that the investors will be granted visas in order to inspect their "property" in the United States. (Suzanne DeChillo/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Dinkins to Propose Using Unpaid Taxes To Get a Bank Loan

BYLINE: By JAMES C. McKINLEY Jr.

 BODY:
To help close a projected $2.1 billion budget gap in the next fiscal year, the Dinkins administration plans to borrow $215 million from Chemical Bank, using as collateral unpaid property-tax debts, senior officials said last night.
The unusual plan would allow the administration to use the funds on the first day of the fiscal year, July 1, instead of waiting until delinquent taxpayers finally get around to paying up. 
 The bank will lend the city the $215 million at about 5 percent interest, according to city officials, taking $400 million in unpaid property-tax obligations as collateral. The city will repay the money in the following fiscal year, when some of the delinquent taxes are finally collected.
The plan was revealed as Mayor David N. Dinkins prepared to unveil today his budget for the 1994 fiscal year. It was immediately denounced by state fiscal monitors and other budget experts, who said it was simply a scheme for deficit financing and an effort to avoid making hard budget choices now.
The borrowing plan is one in a series of financing proposals that the administration wants to use to close next year's budget gap. Those plans, amounting to more than $415 million, also involve stretching out bond payments, refinancing bonds and revising some debt.
But by far the most creative part of the package is the Mayor's plan to borrow money from Chemical Bank against property taxes that the city does not expect to collect until the next fiscal year.
Because it often takes years to collect back taxes, the city each year issues tax bills, deducts from the total due what historically taxpayers have failed to pay in a given year, then adds the delinquent taxes that it expects to collect from previous years. That is the amount of revenue usually put in the budget plan.
But under the Mayor's proposal, the city would borrow from Chemical Bank against property taxes that will be levied next year but that are not likely to be paid until the fiscal year that begins in July 1994. As collateral, the bank would get $400 million in unpaid tax obligations, on the assumption that at least $215 million of that amount will be collected eventually. Theoretically, if the city was not able to collect that much, the bank would be left to try to collect the rest on its own, though city officials said that was highly unlikely.
In essence, the move would allow the city to spend money in the next fiscal year that it would not collect until the following year, fiscal experts said. "It's like selling bad credit-card accounts," explained a senior mayoral adviser who spoke only on the condition that his name not be used.
 
Plan Criticized
 But fiscal experts -- many of whom gave the Mayor grudging respect last year for his budget effort -- assailed the plan. They said Mr. Dinkins -- who has promised not to raise taxes or lay off workers -- was borrowing against uncertain revenues to avoid making tough decisions in an election year.
Allen J. Proctor, the executive director of the State Financial Control Board, which oversees city finances, called the idea "the howler of the financial plan."
"All it does is make 1995's problem bigger," Mr. Proctor said. "It's nothing but pure putting off until tomorrow what you don't want to do today.
"They are borrowing against future income," he added. "It's literally like a cash advance on your credit card."
Raymond D. Horton, president of the Citizens Budget Commission, said the plan would not be viewed with favor by Wall Street bond raters and other fiscal monitors.
"They are in a sense auctioning off money that could be coming in next year," he said. "That's what makes it not kosher. That's what makes it like deficit financing."
Officials at Chemical Bank could not be reached for comment last night, nor could officials of the two main bond-rating agencies, Moody's Investors Service and Standard & Poor's.
The city's budget director, Philip R. Michael, said that although the bank would own the $400 million in debt under the deal, the city would continue to collect the taxes and would retain the power to foreclose on the property. But the bank would be able to claim the first $215 million in delinquent taxes collected in fiscal year 1995, plus about 5 percent interest.
 
2 to 4 Percent Uncollected
 City officials said that as far as they knew, this was the first time New York had ever borrowed against uncollected property taxes. In Chicago, unpaid property-tax obligations have been sold to private collection agencies, but that arrangement differs from the Dinkins proposal because New York City would remain the collector and would retain the right to foreclose.
Mr. Michael said the city historically fails to collect in a given year between 2 and 4 percent of the property taxes it is owed, with each percentage point being worth about $100 million. The amount going uncollected has been growing in recent years because of the recession in real estate, he said.
Mr. Michael dismissed the notion that the city was borrowing against future revenues, saying that the plan was a way to even out over the long term the peaks and troughs in the real-estate market. When the market improves, he said, tax revenues will rebound and further borrowing will become unnecessary. "This is something we would envision every year," he said.
Fiscal monitors were less charitable. They said it amounted to borrowing from the future in an election year to avoid raising taxes or cutting services. "The process of dealing with problems has come to a grinding halt," Mr. Proctor said. "His administration has lost all of its energy. It's basically going on hold until the election."

 NEW YORK CITY
 
 DINKINS PLANS $215 MILLION LOAN
To help close a projected $2.1 billion budget gap in the next fiscal year, the Dinkins administration plans to borrow $215 million at 5 percent interest from Chemical Bank, using as collateral unpaid property-tax debts, senior officials said. The unusual scheme would allow the administration to use the funds on the first day of the fiscal year instead of waiting until delinquent taxpayers get around to paying up. A1. 

 PROMISES AND BUYOUTS AT 2 DAILIES
The prospective new publisher of The New York Post, Steven Hoffenberg, told about 100 applauding but chary newsroom employees that he would restore half the 20 percent pay cut they agreed to last week as soon as he receives the required permission of Federal Bankruptcy Court. And at The Daily News, seven reporters met a deadline for resigning and accepted a severance package offered by the newspaper's new publisher, Mortimer B. Zuckerman. B3.

Testifying at the trial of a doctor accused of attempting an illegal third-trimester abortion on a woman and severing her fetus's arm, the woman described the doctor's East Village clinic as a terrifying place where dirty instruments were used and screams could be heard. B3.

 PARENTS ADMIT SCHOOL PLOYS

B4.

 CITYWIDE INTEGRATION PLAN URGED
The plaintiffs in a lawsuit seeking to integrate Hartford's schools with those in surrounding towns presented their last witness, an expert who testified that a meaningful integration plan must cover the entire metropolitan area and set mandatory goals and deadlines. B5.

HEADLINE: Court Ruling Leaves Clinton With Uncomfortable Choices

BYLINE: By ELAINE SCIOLINO, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 28

 BODY:
The decision by the Israeli High Court upholding the deportation of more than 400 Palestinians disappointed the Clinton Administration, which was clearly hoping that the court might relieve it of having to choose between supporting United Nations sanctions against Israel or antagonizing the Arab countries that are likely to seek sanctions.
In any event, the continuing impasse over the deportees jeopardizes the Middle East peace talks. No date has been set for their resumption, but at any rate they are now threatened by an Arab-wide boycott. 
 President Clinton has pledged that making progress toward peace is one of his highest foreign policy priorities, but he now finds that unless there is an early break over the deportees, there may not be talks in February.
 
'We Don't Think It's Time'
 Although the Bush Administration voted in favor of a Security Council resolution that unanimously condemned Israel for deporting the Palestinians and demanded their immediate return, the Clinton Administration has indicated it will oppose any initiative to enforce the resolution with even limited sanctions.
"We don't think it's time for a debate in the Security Council on sanctions," the State Department spokesman, Richard A. Boucher, said today. But he declined to say whether the impasse was a setback to the peace talks, and repeated over and over that the United States was consulting with Israel and other governments to resolve the issue.
Secretary of State Warren Christopher will probably plead for more time when he visits Secretary General Boutros Boutros-Ghali at the United Nations on Monday. He will accompany Madeleine Albright on her first day as the chief United States representative to the United Nations.
Mr. Boutros-Ghali recommended on Tuesday that the Council take "whatever measures are necessary" to force Israel to comply with the Council's demand to re-admit the Palestinians.
 
Top Spot on Agenda
 If the issue is not resolved in the next few weeks, Mr. Christopher will find the deportees at the top of his agenda when he travels to the Mideast in the second half of February as part of his first foreign trip. He is also to meet with his NATO counterparts in Europe and with Foreign Minister Andrei V. Kozyrev of Russia, State Department officials said.
The Palestine Liberation Organization today garnered support for sanctions against Israel from at least 22 Islamic nations and is seeking broader backing from "nonaligned" members on the 15-nation Security Council. Some of these members were ready to begin an informal Council debate on Friday, but the United States was seeking to stall it, according to diplomats at the United Nations.
Administration officials had urged key Arab governments to block the P.L.O.-drafted resolution until the Israeli High Court ruled.
Richard N. Haass, the senior official on the Middle East at the National Security Council during the Bush Administration, said, "The Administration could be forced to choose between some sanctions against Israel -- which would clearly be a major departure and sets a dangerous precedent for pressuring Israel -- and seeming to employ a double standard when it comes to taking a tough line against those who do not conform to Security Council resolutions."
 
No Relief From Justice
 Until today, the Administration had gambled that the Israeli High Court of Justice would break with precedent and rule against the deportations, thus providing Prime Minister Yitzhak Rabin with a face-saving way to take back the Palestinians, perhaps in stages, and comply with the Security Council resolution.
Despite the Security Council vote in favor of the immediate return of the deportees, neither Mr. Clinton nor Mr. Christopher explicitly repeated that demand when they spoke with Mr. Rabin in separate telephone conversations last weekend, according to Israeli officials.
When Mr. Clinton telephoned Mr. Rabin Saturday, these officials said, Mr. Clinton merely expressed the hope that the deportee matter would be solved as soon as possible. Mr. Christopher was somewhat more specific during his 30-minute conversation on Sunday, saying that he hoped the matter would be resolved quickly so that the two sides could once again focus on the peace process.
 
The Diplomacy Route
 As part of its diplomatic efforts, Mr. Christopher prepared tonight what State Department officials described as a tough message to Mr. Rabin, but they declined to discuss its content.
The United States Ambassador to Israel, William C. Harrop, met with Mr. Rabin today in Israel, and in Washington Assistant Secretary of State Edward P. Djerejian met with Lebanon's Ambassador, Simon Massoud Karam.
Mr. Karam said in an interview that Lebanon would accede to the United States request that it not push for sanctions immediately, despite the fact that the deportees are trapped in an area of southern Lebanon that Israel in effect occupies and calls its security zone.
"From the beginning we have stressed that unless this is solved, no one can count on resumption of the peace process," Mr. Karam said.

HEADLINE: 1992 Unemployment Rate Hit a 15-Year High

BYLINE: By RICHARD D. LYONS

 BODY:
New York City's average unemployment rate last year was the highest in 15 years, according to Federal statistics released yesterday.
The city's unemployment rate rose to 10.8 percent from 8.6 percent of 1991, according to the Federal Bureau of Labor Statistics, the highest level since the end of the city's last fiscal crisis in 1976. 
 The total number of New Yorkers employed last year fell to 2,952,000 from 3,023,000 a year earlier, the first time in six years that the city's annual employment level fell below 3 million, the figures showed.
Samuel M. Ehrenhalt, the Regional Commissioner of the Bureau of Labor Statistics, said there is some evidence that employment is starting to pick up locally.
"This is not a statistical glitch," Mr. Ehrenhalt said in an interview. "New York City has gone through an economic downturn second in recent decades only to the great economic crisis of the early to mid-70's." But he added, "We see a flicker of light at the end of the tunnel because, while unemployment continue ins the double-digit range, we have seen the downturn bottom out in the last quarter of the year."
The preliminary data released yesterday noted that the grim unemployment statistics were spread across all demographic groups studied by the Department of Labor.
The impact was particularly heavy on blacks and Hispanics. The rate for blacks last year rose from 10.4 percent in 1991 to 14 percent, while that for Hispanics climbed from 10.4 percent to 15.9 percent.
In New York State, the average 1992 unemployment rate of 8.5 percent contrasted with a national figure of 6.7 percent. In 1991, the state figure was 7.2 percent, while the nation rate was 6.7 percent.
Officials of the state-administered Unemployment Trust Fund said that because of the high unemployment rate, the fund is expected to run out of money next month, triggering an automatic increase in the amount employers in New York will have to contribute.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKET;
Bond Prices Close Up in Seesaw Day

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER

 BODY:
Prices in the bond market rode a roller coaster yesterday, rising, falling and then rising again at the end of the day. The ride revealed a market that has become more and more driven by public comments surrounding the development of President Clinton's economic plan.
Comments on Sunday by Treasury Secretary Lloyd Bentsen drove the yield on the 30-year bond down to 7.20 percent on Monday, a six-and-a-half-year low. Yesterday, a prominent Senator's proposal of a minimum price of $25 a barrel on imported oil quashed a morning rally that had pulled the yield on the long bond a notch lower, to 7.19 percent. 
 But later in the day comments by Alan Greenspan, the Federal Reserve Board chairman, revived the market, sending prices higher again and yields lower. These remarks, which included an endorsement of Mr. Clinton's deficit-reduction goals, helped to push the yield on the 30-year bond from 7.24 percent back down to 7.20 percent by the end of the trading day in New York. And the yields on Treasury notes fared even better, ending at their lows for the day.
"This is what we are going to see for a long time to come," said Joel Kazis, managing director for government trading at UBS Securities. "There is a lot of discussion going on inside and outside the Administration that will drive our market around. We will be tossed around here until we get some real word out of the President."
As trading began in New York yesterday, the Commerce Department reported that the nation's economy grew at a 3.8 percent annual rate in the last quarter of 1992, the best performance in four years.
 
Growth in Economy Slow
 But traders and investors seemed to dismiss the data quickly, focusing on other recent reports indicating that the economy has been growing at a slower pace. A report that initial jobless claims rose for the third week in a row added to this argument. The jump was just 2,000, but analysts had been expecting a decline.
In addition, the report on the gross domestic product showed that the rate of inflation had remained low.
This all helped to send bond prices higher. But later, Senator J. Bennett Johnston, Democrat of Louisiana and chairman of the Energy and Natural Resources Committee, proposed a floor price of $25 a barrel for imported oil. The suggestion rocked the petroleum markets; the price of oil for March delivery jumped to close at $20.41 on the New York Mercantile Exchange.
The higher oil prices caused inflationary jitters in the bond market and prices fell sharply. But then Mr. Greenspan came to the rescue.
Prices of Treasury notes and bonds moved higher again after Mr. Greenspan said that Mr. Clinton's goal of cutting the Federal budget deficit by $145 billion by 1996 was not "off base"; that if deficit reduction put a drag on the economy the Fed would consider cutting short-term interest rates, and that credible deficit reduction would quickly bring down long-term interest rates.
In late trading in the secondary market, the price of the 30-year bond was up 17/32 to 105 6/32 to yield 7.20 percent, down from 7.24 percent Wednesday. The price of the 10-year note was up 14/32 , to yield 6.38 percent, down from 6.45 percent.
And, as Mr. Kazis noted, Mr. Greenspan's remarks bolstered shorter maturities because they allowed investors to conclude that the Fed would not raise short-term rates soon and could even cut them again if the economy stumbled. The price of the two-year note jumped 7/32 as the yield dropped to 4.14 percent, from 4.25 percent on Wednesday.
The discount rates on Treasury bills also fell sharply, with the rate on the three-month bill down 5 basis points, or hundredths of a percentage point, to 2.85 percent. This brought it below the Federal funds rate, which is the interest charged on overnight loans between banks.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Catalogue Plant To Close

BYLINE: Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: CHICAGO, Jan. 28

 BODY:
One of the first ripples from Monday's huge restructuring of Sears, Roebuck & Company surfaced today, when the R. R. Donnelley & SonsCompany said it would close the Chicago plant that had printed the Sears catalogue for 80 years.
The Donnelley facility, the oldest of its 140 plants worldwide, employs 660. The company said it would take a one-time charge of $60 million in the first quarter to cover the cost of shutting the facility. Meanwhile, Donnelley, the world's largest commercial printer, today reported record sales and earnings for the fourth quarter and the year. 
 The closing "is the beginning of the secondary reactions that will be felt as a result of the Sears's decision," said John Skorburg, chief economist and director of government affairs at the Chicagoland Chamber of Commerce.
Sears plans to close its ailing $3.3 billion catalogue unit, shut 113 stores and take other steps that will result in the elimination of 50,000 full-time and part-time jobs nationwide. About 4,000 jobs in Illinois will be cut.
Mr. Skorburg estimated that another 6,000 employees would be laid off at Illinois companies that do business with Chicago-based Sears, especially companies that have the Sears catalogue as a customer, like photographers and paper suppliers. Mr. Skorburg, estimating that each job accounts for annual income of between $50,000 to $60,000, said the economic impact on Illinois could reach $600 million.
Jan M. Grayson, director of the Illinois Department of Commerce and Community Affairs, said the planned job losses "are a blow to state, no doubt about it." But, he added, "The state economy is viable and rich enough that we can absorb it."
The plant to be shut is a landmark on the city's near South Side. The 1.1-million-square-foot building was completed in 1928 and is listed in the National Register of Historic Places.
Because of the building's age and unusual design, it can not operate economically without the Sears catalogue work, said Diane M. Dunne, Donnelley's manger of corporate communication. Ms. Dunne said that Donnelley was studying ways to relocate the work when Sears announced its plans to end the catalogue.

HEADLINE: Major Powers Back a Fund For Soviet-Design Reactors

BYLINE: By MARLISE SIMONS

DATELINE: PARIS, Jan. 28

 BODY:
After months of bickering among themselves, the United States and six other major industrial nations have agreed to create a multimillion-dollar fund to improve the safety of nuclear reactors in Eastern Europe and the former Soviet Union.
The results of a secret meeting in London on Wednesday were disclosed today by French and German officials, who said at a joint news conference here that an accord had been reached unexpectedly and that the London-based European Bank for Reconstruction and Development would manage the fund. 
 The ministers also announced today that France and Germany together had pledged to pay $80 million into the fund over the next three years. The United States has not yet committed itself to any specific amount, but officials close to the negotiations said that American as well as Japanese negotiators had dropped past objections and agreed to go along.
 
Disaster Prevention
 The money going into this nuclear safety account will generate a great deal of business for Western manufacturers. It will be spent in the first place on measures to prevent disasters like the one at Chernobyl in 1986 and to make other improvements that will guarantee an energy supply to the faltering economies of Eastern Europe, Russia and Ukraine.
Western experts have long expressed alarm over dilapidated conditions at many of the 60 Soviet-designed reactors operating in the former Communist nations, and since the end of the cold war, Western countries have repeatedly said they would need to help.
At their Munich summit last July, the Group of Seven industrial nations -- which includes the United States, Britain, Canada, France, Germany, Italy and Japan -- proposed a nuclear energy assistance program that would need a minimum of $700 million. But their plan has since failed to get off the ground because the seven could not agree on what aid to provide and who should be in charge of managing it.
"The United States and Japan were very reluctant to go along with a multilateral program, but now they have agreed," said a spokesman for the German government, which has led the drive for the fund. "The consultations have been long and difficult." Officials of the Clinton administration could not be reached for comment
 
An Issue of Control
 A major stumbling block was that the United States and Japan wanted to give direct, bilateral aid in order to keep more control over the money and to retain more leverage in getting business contracts, officials close to the negotiations said.
As part of her confirmation process, Energy Secretary Hazel R. O'Leary told a Senate committee last week that she intended to make assisting the civilian nuclear power industry in Russia and the Baltic states a "priority." But department officials say there is no money in their 1993 budget for dismantling Soviet-design nuclear plants.
European countries, and France and Germany specifically, have pushed hardest to get the nuclear safety program going because the region is so exposed to fallout in case of a major nuclear disaster in the former communist countries, as it was in the case of Chernobyl.
Another issue that has slowed down talks, experts said, is the absence of clear legal liability in those countries.
 
A Need for Clarity
 "If Western manufacturers go in there and people get hurt by something that has a Western piece bolted to it, this can have all sorts of repercussions," said a specialist at the International Atomic Energy Agency in Vienna. "Western companies are very eager to go and get the business, but their legal departments are very nervous."
The first countries to receive funds, experts said, will be Bulgaria and Slovakia, both of which operate some of the least safe reactors, the first generation of pressurized water reactors known as VVER-440 type 230.
Bulgaria's only plant at Kozloduy, which has three types of reactors, is in the worst shape, in the view of Western nuclear specialists. The European Community has already spent $13 mmillion on maintenance work and staff training at Kozloduy.
Slovakia is known for better workmanship, but its reactors of three different generations suffer the design faults criticized by Westerners.
Ukraine and Russia will also receive money from the fund. But it is still not known which of the types of Soviet-built nuclear plants in the former Communist bloc will be fixed up.

HEADLINE: Post Buyer Says He'll Restore Some Pay

BYLINE: By RONALD SULLIVAN

 BODY:
The prospective new publisher of The New York Post told about 100 applauding but chary newsroom employees late yesterday that he would restore half of the 20 percent pay cut they agreed to last week as soon as he receives the required permission of the Federal Bankruptcy Court.
Steven Hoffenberg, the chairman of the Towers Financial Corporation, who has signed a letter of intent to purchase The Post and has put up a $2.5 million line of credit to back it up, said a 10 percent restoration would cost The Post about $75,000 a week at a time when its financial condition is regarded as extremely precarious. 
 Under an agreement with the unions at The Post, Mr. Hoffenberg was not required to restore the salary cuts until Feb. 20 or until he buys the paper from Peter S. Kalikow, its bankrupt owner.
But he said he was moving more quickly than required "because these people cannot pay their bills, they cannot pay their rent and they did not become employees of The Post to become lenders of first resort."
In a telephone interview, Mr. Hoffenberg conceded that his largess to The Post unions would go a long way in countering a series of negative stories that portrayed him as a streetwise wheeler-dealer with a long history of what critics describe as questionable business transactions.
"But streetwise I admit to, and I'm proud of it," he said yesterday. "I'm not Maxwell or Murdock," he said, referring to Robert Maxwell, the former owner of The Daily News and Rupert Murdoch, a former Post owner. "I'm just a guy from the corner who's worked all his life and who's trying to save this newspaper."
 
Applause and Cynicism
 At The Post, reporters who heard Mr. Hoffenberg's offer to restore half of their pay cut reacted with applause and considerable cynicism.
Karen Phillips, a Post court reporter in Brooklyn and an official of the paper's unit of the New York Newspaper Guild, which represents news, advertising and circulation employees, said of Mr. Hoffenberg's promise, "I'll believe it when I see it."
In the interview, Mr. Hoffenberg also said he would restore all employee deductions for employee retirement fund, known as 401(k) account, that were missed during the financial upheaval that preceded his agreement to purchase the newspaper.
At The News, meantime, seven reporters met yesterday's deadline to resign and accepted a severance deal offered by the newspaper's new publisher, Mortimer B. Zuckerman.
The pay cuts at The Post and the resignations at The News reflected the shaky financial structure and lingering animosities at the two newspapers that many experts say will someday result in one killing off the other in an anticipated newspaper circulation and advertising war that would also include New York Newsday, the city's third tabloid.
The reporters who resigned at The News were Donald Singleton, Tom Robbins, Serge Kovaleski, Salvatore Arena and LynNell Hancock and two columnists, Juan Gonzalez and Jerry Capeci, both vocal members of the guild's opposition to Mr. Zuckerman's stormy tenure at The News.
 
Guild Supporters
 When The News dismissed 182 guild-represented employees on Jan. 7 as part of a staff cutback that Mr. Zuckerman said was necessary to save the newspaper, the seven reporters were offered jobs and stayed on.
But Mr. Capeci said his decision to leave yesterday was a response to Mr. Zuckerman's continued actions against the guild. "I'm not happy with the way things progressed under Zuckerman, and I support the guild's boycott of The News," he said.
The News was also hit with what the guild said was its first full day of pamphleteering. Handbills distributed at most of New York City's major transportation centers called for a boycott of The News and of Mr. Zuckerman's weekly magazine, U.S. News & World Report.
As for Mr. Hoffenberg, he said that as well as being a "$1 a year" publisher of a feisty newspaper that he said takes pride in its underdog position, he would back up his plan to trade advertising space at The Post for merchandise at a time in the city's economy when he said everyone is short of cash.
"You won't see me behind some big publisher's desk," he said. "You'll see me in discount-merchandizing outlets selling the very stuff we took in for advertising that day."
"As you see, I don't stand on a lot of privilege," he said. "You want pants, I got pants."

HEADLINE: Fed's Chief Also Likes Clinton Plan on Deficit

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 28

 BODY:
Alan Greenspan, chairman of the Federal Reserve Board, gave another endorsement to President Clinton's economic plans today, calling the Administration's goals for deficit reduction about the right size and suggesting that if the effort weakened the economy, the central bank might cut interest rates to soften the blow.
Hours after he met with Mr. Clinton this morning, Mr. Greenspan appeared before the Senate Budget Committee and signaled a surprisingly cooperative spirit with the Administration, endorsing the President's promise to cut the annual budget deficit $145 billion by the end of his term. 
 "I don't find that the number the President Clinton has indicated to be off base," he told the Senators. For Mr. Greenspan, known for speaking indirectly, the remark was a ringing endorsement.
Further, he said, if a large-scale deficit-reduction plan undercut economic growth in several years, the Federal Reserve would take that weakness into account when setting interest rates.
Mr. Greenspan predicted that long-term rates would fall quickly in response to such a "credible deficit-reduction package." He added that "there is no stronger stimulus to this type of economy," not even a short-term stimulus program involving increased deficit spending.
"Observing the bond markets over the last few weeks," he added, "I think there is very clear indication that the markets are ready to move if there is a really credible reduction in the deficit."
The Fed chairman even held out the possibility that short-term interest rates would soon be cut if long-term rates were to fall quickly, in response to a major plan on the deficit.
"To the extent that in the short run there is a significant decline in long-term interest rates," Mr. Greenspan said, "obviously monetary policy would adjust to that fact, as well."
 
Logic of the Marketplace
 Federal Reserve officials often say the reason they have not cut short-term rates further is that the financial markets, fearing future inflationary pressures, have kept long-term rates high.
Officials at the Clinton White House seemed to signal a new spirit of cooperation with the Federal Reserve, after the tension between Mr. Greenspan and their predecessors in the Bush Administration.
"We've had good discussions with the Fed," George Stephanopoulos, the White House communications director, said today. "We're all committed to the same goals." He listed these as "getting growth in this economy, holding inflation down and really bringing up job growth."
Mr. Greenspan, in an appearance on Wednesday before the Joint Economic Committee of Congress, seemed to give his approval to the Administration's plans for a modest stimulus package of about $20 billion, saying that both he and the President favored "maximum sustainable growth" rather than a "stop-go" economic policy.
By that, Mr. Greenspan indicated he did not think that the Administration's plans to stimulate the economy threatened to speed up growth at such a pace that it would set off new inflationary pressure. Such pressure could cause the Federal Reserve to raise short-term interest rates, a move that would slow growth.
Some Administration officials worry, however, that the bonhomie between Mr. Clinton and the Fed chairman could disappear before long because the Administration favors faster growth than the Fed and because the President is so eager to reduce unemployment.
Senator Jim Sasser, Democrat of Tennessee and chairman of the Budget Committee, began today's hearing by pressing Mr. Greenspan to embrace easier monetary policy.
"An anemic economic recovery is Public Enemy No. 1," he said, not inflation. "No action, more baby steps, or worse, monetary contraction, will without a doubt in my mind lay a killing frost over the economic spring that President Clinton is trying to force."
 
Spending More Than Taxes
 Mr. Greenspan gave the Senators -- and indirectly the President -- some advice about how to cut the deficit. He suggested that they focus on reducing spending, saying that tax increases alone would not be sufficient because spending is increasing considerably faster than the tax base.
He warned that for the financial markets to take any deficit-reduction package seriously, the proposals should not be "just a set of goals, or caps, or various other procedural elements." Instead, he said, Congress should enact specific, line-by-line legislation to cut the deficit.
The Fed chairman said that with inflation as low as it is, long-term interest rates -- now a little above 7 percent for 30-year Treasury bonds -- were about two percentage points higher than they should be because bond buyers worry about a projected series of $300 billion deficits.
He said that the negative effects of deficit-reduction measures on growth -- tax increases or spending cuts -- would "be offset by the major impact coming from" the decline in long-term rates that he foresees in reaction to a deficit package.

HEADLINE: A.T.& T. Reports $1 Billion In Record Quarterly Profits

BYLINE: By ANTHONY RAMIREZ

 BODY:
A.T.& T. yesterday reported record fourth-quarter earnings of $1 billion, or 75 cents a share, up 57.5 percent from a year earlier, when it earned $635 million, or 48 cents a share.
Quarterly revenue of A.T.& T., the world's largest telecommunications company, which has been struggling in an increasingly competitive industry, totaled $17.5 billion, up 6.3 percent from a year earlier and about equal to the gross domestic product of Morocco. 
 The company, whose earnings were within analysts' expectations, was helped mainly by large increases in sales of telecommunications equipment, but also gains in its Universal credit card as well as a reduction in operating expenses brought about by prior reorganizations and work force reductions.
The earnings gain also appeared large because of reduced 1991 earnings, stemming from a sharp drop in the value of A.T.& T.'s holdings in Compagnie Industriali Riunite of Italy, which owns the Olivetti office-equipment company and other concerns. The value has been successively cut from $348 million to $131 million to $63 million. A.T.& T. took a charge of 14 cents a share in 1991 and a charge of 3 cents in the most recent quarter.
Analysts say that in view of the Riunite reduction and restructuring charges, a more realistic comparison in the quarter would be 78 cents a share versus 62 cents, a gain of about 26 percent.
American Telephone and Telegraph shares are the most widely held in the country, with more than 1.3 billion shares outstanding. In trading on the New York Stock Exchange yesterday, A.T.& T. shares fell 12.5 cents each, to $53.125.
A.T.& T.'s quarterly performance was aided substantially by more than $900 million in increased sales of expensive telephone switching and transmission equipment and undersea cable systems.
Such purchases by the world's telephone companies, mostly huge state-owned carriers, have more to do with modernizing and expanding aging telephone networks than any general turnaround in the global economy. Regional Bell companies in the United States were especially large buyers, said Charles W. Schelke, an analyst at Smith Barney.
 
Long -Distance a Disappointment
 But such purchases tend to encourage increased domestic and international calling, which still makes up 60 percent of A.T.& T.'s annual revenue. Long-distance traffic growth has been somewhat disappointing. A.T.& T. told analysts that growth in telephone traffic was about 5 percent, about 1 percent lower than Wall Street had expected. MCI and, to a lesser extent, Sprint, have been taking market share from A.T.& T.
Revenue from telecommunications services, mainly domestic and international long distance, totaled $9.72 billion in the quarter, down less than a percent from the $9.79 billion in the corresponding period a year ago.
Full-year earnings totaled $3.8 billion, or $2.86 a share, compared with $522 million, or 40 cents a share, in 1991. Results for the prior year were hurt by restructuring charges and a recessionary economy. Full-year revenue totaled $64.9 billion, up 2.9 percent from $63.09 billion a year earlier.

HEADLINE: Mandatory Goals Urged for Integration

BYLINE: By GEORGE JUDSON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WEST HARTFORD, Conn., Jan. 28

 BODY:
The plaintiffs in a lawsuit challenging the racial and economic segregation of Hartford's schools presented their last witness today, an expert who testified that a meaningful integration plan must cover the entire metropolitan area and set mandatory goals and deadlines.
Little has been accomplished without mandatory goals for addressing segregation, because "everyone knows it's there and no one ever wants to take responsibility for doing anything about it," said the expert, Gary Orfield, a Harvard professor of education and social policy. 
 A metropolitan plan, he said, would eliminate a major incentive for white flight: the ability to escape integrated schools by moving to the next community.
"The best kind of desegregation remedy involves the entire housing market, so a line is not drawn somewhere that says segregation will exist outside this line, and integration will exist inside this line," he said.
 
Provoking White Flight
 As the plaintiffs' last witness, Professor Orfield, a nationally known researcher on desegregation, surveyed the dos and don'ts of integrating schools, with an eye toward a major point that the state, in its defense, intends to raise: that court-ordered plans provoke white flight.
He discounted the chief evidence on flight that the state will present, research by Prof. Christine Rossell of Boston University, saying that she had not studied metropolitan plans like the one the plaintiffs are seeking.
Nearly all court-ordered desegregation plans have involved either single cities, or cities and suburbs linked by a common county school system. The plaintiffs, in contrast, have proposed linking Hartford with 21 suburbs that have their own school districts.
When Alfred Lindseth, an Atlanta lawyer assisting the state in its defense, challenged him on the flight issue, Professor Orfield explained a chart showing that whites have left virtually all central cities over the last 30 years, whether or not their schools have had court-ordered desegregation plans.
The losses have been smallest, he said, where court-ordered plans existed and covered entire metropolitan areas.
While the plaintiffs in the case, known as Sheff v. O'Neill, have not specified what they want in a court order, Professor Orfield described several elements that he considers essential.
One goal, he said, should be schools that "look like the overall community, and the overall community is the larger economic reach and housing market of the urban community." In 1991, Hartford and the 21 towns specified in the suit reported 93,791 students, 63.4 percent of whom were white, and 33.8 percent black or Hispanic. The rest were Asian or American Indian.
 
A Reasonable Rule
 A reasonable rule, he said, is that schools have racial balances within 10 percentage points of the region's average; magnet schools, which typically offer programs to attract suburban students to city schools, often are permitted a 50-50 balance.
Under those guidelines, suburban schools would be in compliance if their enrollments were 23.8 percent to 43.8 percent black and Hispanic.
But other goals are also important, he said. Similar balance should also be reached among teachers, administrators and staff members. And city children should improve academically, as measured in higher test scores, lower dropout rates and more graduates going to college.
"It's not reasonable to ask schools to solve everything and it's not reasonable to ask that it be done instantly," Professor Orfield said. "But it is reasonable to expect progress."
A plan, ideally, should be designed not by a judge, but by local authorities acting under goals and deadlines set by a court, he said. What must be mandatory, he said, is that the state and the communities participate.
Once goals have been set, he said, they can be achieved flexibly. While almost any plan is likely to require that some children be reassigned to schools away from their neighborhoods, parents can be given some choice. Areas that have achieved stable integration in their housing could be exempted, so that residents regain their neighborhood schools.
"The point of a desegregation plan is not to transport students, but to get them into integrated schools," he said. "If they're in a position to walk to such schools, so much the better."
Early deadlines are crucial, Professor Orfield said, because delay can cause racial division. The great danger, he said, is "political exploitation of racial fears," and that is highest between the time a court orders a plan and the first day of school under the plan.
How soon any desegregation order could be carried out in the Hartford region is, in a sense, moot, because both the Sheff lawyers and the state have vowed to appeal a decision against them.
Some positive steps could be taken while appeals are pending, Professor Orfield said. The quickest would be an immediate expansion in September of Project Concern, a voluntary busing program that sends Hartford children to suburban schools. While more than 500 children applied to join the program next September, he said, it has places for only 85.
The state could also block the continued spread of suburban segregation by banning the construction of new public schools unless they offer interdistrict programs or are built in integrated neighborhoods.

HEADLINE: COMPANY REPORTS;
Dow Chemical Co. (N)

 BODY:
The Dow Chemical Company said yesterday that its fourth-quarter loss resulted from a $433 million pretax charge against earnings and the adoption of two new accounting changes. In the corresponding period of 1991, Dow lost $94 million, because of a $370 million pretax special charge.
Dow said its $496 million loss for the year was a result of the special charge, which reflected plant shutdowns, work force reductions and the accounting changes regarding retiree health-care costs and the method used to calculate income taxes. 
 "Results for the year were disappointing despite progress in restoring margins for our U.S. businesses," said Frank Popoff, the company's chairman and president. "Improvement in the U.S. was more than offset by deteriorating economic and business conditions in Europe."
Mr. Popoff added that "the first quarter of 1993 will include a gain from selling our interest in Dowell Schlumberger, thereby strengthening our cash flow position for the year." He said, "We are counting on our increased productivity to help us achieve earnings to help us achieve earnings improvement, while looking forward to an upturn in prices."

HEADLINE: Blanche Bernstein, 80, Official Who Criticized Welfare System

BYLINE: By BRUCE LAMBERT

 BODY:
Dr. Blanche Bernstein, a welfare expert and former government official known for her criticisms of the welfare system, died on Wednesdayat Mount Sinai Medical Center in Manhattan. She was 80 and lived in Manhattan.
She died of lung cancer, said Barbara Rulnick, a niece. 
 Dr. Bernstein argued that the welfare system's major failing is fostering continued dependency for people receiving aid, instead of guiding them off the welfare rolls to independence.
"Reduction of dependency is the greatest unmet need in social policy today," she once said. "We have not gotten to the heart of the problem."
Her pronouncements often provoked opposition from other experts and advocates. They accused her of being unsympathetic to poor people, which she denied. Supporters said her aim was to help the truly needy while paring others from public assistance. She was a firm proponent of measures to eliminate ineligible recipients from welfare and to reduce erroneous overpayments.
She recounted her battles and explained her policies in "The Politics of Welfare: The New York City Experience" (Abt, 1982), which Fortune magazine called "wonderfully instructive."
 
High-Ranking Posts
 Dr. Bernstein's opinions were not merely academic. She held high-ranking welfare posts in both New York City and State government.
She headed the nation's largest local welfare agency when she served as the city's Human Resources Administrator in 1978-79, with more than a million residents receiving aid. Mayor Edward I. Koch appointed her when he came into office.
For two years before that, she was the State Social Services Department's deputy commissioner for income maintenance, under Gov. Hugh L. Carey. She was in charge of public assistance, food stamps and welfare job programs.
An economist by training, Dr. Bernstein earlier held policy posts in the State Department, worked for charities, was an educator and researched a wide array of social problems.
Among the issues she studied were foster care, the effect of welfare on family stability, obstacles to drug treatment for addicts, impoverished Jews, day care, obstacles to employment for welfare recipients, mental health services, welfare spending, family budgets and deterrents to prenatal care.
 
Director at New School
 Dr. Bernstein focused increasingly on the growing and entwined problems of teen-age mothers, single-parent families, poor education, school dropout and unemployment. Unless society "breaks the cycle of dependency and poverty," she said, "we are in danger of creating a permanent underclass."
On leaving government in 1979, she became the director of the Social Policy Research Institute at the New School for Social Research. The institute studied welfare, housing, health care, child neglect and abuse and services for the aged.
After retiring from the New School, she remained active in education as a trustee of the City University of New York.
Dr. Bernstein was born in New York City. After graduating from Hunter College, she earned a master's degree and a doctorate at Columbia.
Early in her career she was the chief of program and planning for Europe in the Foreign Operations Administration from 1951-53 and the research director for the Community Council of New York from 1953-61.
From 1961-68 she served as the chief of the State Department's office dealing with the United Nations on housing, health, children, welfare, urban affairs, development, education and job training. From 1969-75 she was the New School's research director on urban social problems and also editor of the City Almanac.
Her survivors include a brother, Hilliard, of Manhattan.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Mixed Trading for Dollar Despite U.S. Economic Data

BYLINE: By Reuters

 BODY:
The dollar ended mixed yesterday, as the currency markets paid little attention to a barrage of United States economic reports as well as comments from German officials defending high interest rates.
Gold prices rose 60 cents on the Commodity Exchange in New York, where bullion for current delivery settled at $330.50 an ounce. A late quote from the Republic National Bank said spot gold rose 50 cents, to $330.35. 
Technical factors exerted the most influence on the choppy market. Dealers said many players were more intent on squaring their month-end books than on taking new positions.
"What we're seeing is a distraction from dollar buying," John Lyman, senior customer dealer at Bank of Tokyo, said. "The market is not getting interest rate cuts out of Europe, so that triggered some selling." But he added that the dollar's relative strength yesterday in the face of hawkish comments on German interest rates suggested that the dollar "may be getting to the end of its correction."
 
Dollar: 1.5860 Marks
 In New York, the dollar settled at 1.5860 marks in late afternoon trading, barely down from 1.5869 marks Wednesday. Earlier in London, the dollar rose to 1.5860 marks, from 1.5838 marks Wednesday.
The dollar barely moved against the yen. In Tokyo, the dollar ended at 124.15 yen, slightly above the 124.00- yen level of late Wednesday. In London, it ended at 124.14 yen, up from 124.05 yen Wednesday. In New York, the dollar dipped to 124.15 yen, from 124.20 yen late Wednesday.
In London, the British pound recovered somewhat against the German mark but weakened against the dollar to settle at around $1.5100, down from $1.5130 late Wednesday. In New York, the pound rose to $1.5170, from $1.5119 Wednesday.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
USX Sees Steel Back In Demand

BYLINE: Reuters

DATELINE: PITTSBURGH, Jan. 28

 BODY:
The USX Corporation's chairman and chief executive, Charles A. Corry, said today that the company's steel orders had been rising sharply, up nearly 50 percent from the third quarter of 1992.
Steel orders are running in the mid- to upper-40,000 tons a day, compared with 26,000 to 27,000 tons a day in the third quarter, USX officials said. 
 At a news conference, Mr. Corry said the stronger orders, combined with a general pickup in the economy, an improvement in the diversified energy business and the imposition of duties on imported steel, would lead USX to a better year in 1993.
USX on Tuesday reported a loss of $1.83 billion for 1992. The company said its steel and energy subsidiaries were hit by a weak economy and by $1.66 billion in accounting charges. Overall sales slipped, falling to $17.84 billion from 1991's $18.83 billion.
Mr. Corry said his optimism for the steel sector was strengthened by a round of steel price increases last week, even though most earlier attempts to raise prices ultimately failed.
"I'm considerably more confident that they will stick," he said. "When you have that kind of demand for product and you have rather a fixed capacity to deliver it, that typically leads to stronger market prices for the products."
Steelmakers have repeatedly tried to raise prices for their products in the last two years, but they failed to win any increase until the beginning of this month, analysts said.
Two of the company's three separate stocks closed higher today. The USX-U.S. Steel Group gained 50 cents, to $37.125; USX-Marathon rose 62.5 cents, to $17.375, and USX-Delhi, the natural gas unit, was unchanged, at $16. All are traded on the New York Stock Exchange.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Charles A. Corry, chairman of USX, reporting his company's sharp rise in steel orders at a news conference yesterday in Pittsburgh. (Associated Press)
