HEADLINE: $25 and Under;
Ah, Heaven: A Big Game, A Jug of Beer and Thou

BYLINE: By ERIC ASIMOV

 BODY:
SPORTS fans, you're in luck. Life sometimes is like a beer commercial.
Happy, handsome young people, cheerful and affectionate, engaged in friendly competition over a few brews, winners and losers united by the spirit of the game. No sore losers here. Waiter, bring us another round! 
 Such is the world of sports bars, those temples of spectator sports where life's little miseries can be forgotten in a sea of 19-inch video monitors and 6-foot projection televisions. The old tavern on the corner still exists, with its quirky television set perched up above the bar and tuned at the whim of the bartender, but in this era of specialization the place to go to catch the game (or games) is the sports bar. And with the Dallas Cowboys playing the Buffalo Bills in the Super Bowl on Sunday, this is a busy weekend for sports bars.
Probably any bar with more than one television can call itself a sports bar. But to really qualify, a sports bar must have at least one set visible from every single seat, and preferably more. That's because these are not simply Knicks fans flocking to these bars every night, but Rangers fans, St. John's fans, Michael Jordan fans, football fans of every stripe as well as sports fans who might, shall we say, have more than a rooting interest in a particular game.
But lest anybody be shocked -- shocked! -- that gambling is going on, it should be clear that sports bar patrons are not all sports nerds of the type that populate radio phone-in shows.
In fact, while most patrons seem to fall into that prosperous 25-to-40-year-old-male niche that beer advertisers so covet, many women also turn out, looking just as prosperous and having just as rowdy a good time.
Although television is important, there's more that goes into the true sports bar. There's food, there's drink, there's history and mythology, there's music, and there's religion.
First, the food. We're talking excess: Buffalo chicken wings, fried everything from mozzarella to calamari to potato skins, huge tureen-size salads complete with kitchen sinks, giant club sandwiches. We're talking burgers, burgers, burgers. Ribs, chili, shrimp and, for the faint-of-stomach, an occasional pasta dish. We're talking Mississippi mud pie, apple pie covered in gooey caramel, slabs of cheesecake and hot fudge sundaes with double helpings of whipped cream. We're talking antacids.
Perhaps easing the digestive process is the beverage of choice, beer. Most sports bars have a wide-ranging selection, from Budweiser to top imports to regional American brews. Precious little white wine drinking goes on in sports bars, thank you, although an increasingly gallant nod is made in the direction of those who prefer no alcohol. Prodding the appetite and fueling the competitive instinct at many sports bars are participant side shows: pool tables, basketball hoops and other games. The whoops and yelps of happy players abound.
All this is served up in an atmosphere of reverence for America's sports past. Framed photos of Hall of Fame ballplayers, cute autographed caricatures, witticisms (Yogi Berra and Damon Runyon are favored sources), old pennants. And then there are the shrines, homages to American sentimentality and economic clout. Behind bulletproof glass lies the bat used by Choo Choo Coleman to ground into a triple play in the hapless days of the New York Mets: yours for only $850! A basketball autographed by the assistant trainer of the New Jersey Nets: $120. A baseball jersey worn -- and apparently mended -- by Billy Martin when he played for the New York Yankees: priceless.
Treasured above all else, sacred in the culture of the sports bar, is anything touched by Mickey Mantle. Old Mantle baseball cards, old Mantle magazine covers, any Mantle newspaper picture, they all find there way onto the wall of sports bars. It's no coincidence that the classiest sports bar in New York is Mickey Mantle's.
Take this simple test: If somewhere -- in an old shoebox, in your childhood bedroom, in your den or car, even in your wallet -- you keep an old Mickey Mantle baseball card, any of these Manhattan sports bars are for you.
 
Sports on Broadway
 2182 Broadway, at 77th Street, (212) 874-7208. Open Mondays  through Thursdays, 5 P.M. to 1 A.M.; Fridays, 5 P.M. to 2 A.M.; Saturdays, noon to 2 A.M., and Sundays, noon to midnight.
This huge, black, cavernous bar was made for spectators. From one of the long tables in the middle of the open main room, you can watch two huge projection televisions. Behind the long black wood bar, six sets are lined up, as in a television producer's control booth, each tuned to a different event. Such an arrangement is a balm for fans suffering hand pain through overuse of the remote-control clicker, although the head-swiveling necessary for keeping track of a half-dozen different events may pose a new hazard: sports bar whiplash.
In the back, a stand of individual chairs and tables rises like bleachers up to a second floor, giving single spectators an opportunity to survey the entire scene. This is sports saturation, drawing serious fans from thick-necked former jocks gathering after work to weedy collegiate types.
From the second level, shouts from pool players and basketball shooters echo over the raucous atmosphere of bluff, back-slapping heartiness, interrupted only by those discordant moments when the Rangers fans cheer a goal just as the Knicks fans groan over a missed shot.
As far as the menu goes, the simpler the better. A simple shrimp cocktail ($7.50) was not simple enough. The four big shrimp were cold and tasteless, as if they hadn't been completely defrosted. Far better was the hamburger ($7.50), thick, juicy and cooked to order with crisp bacon, served with thick, fresh cottage fries, the skin sides fried to a tasty crisp. For dessert, pecan pie ($4.50) comes very sweet and very cold.
 
Park Avenue Country Club
 381 Park Avenue South, at 27th Street, (212) 685-3636. Open Sundays through Thursdays, 11:30 A.M. to 1 A.M., Saturdays and Sundays, 11:30 A.M. to 4 A.M.
This new preppy fantasy, which opened just two months ago, caters to the golf and sailing set. Enter through a vestibule past what is to become a pro shop, selling equipment and memorabilia. Walk down a corridor past a private dining room and two simulated 18-hole golf courses, where players tee off into a computer projection of, say, the 18th hole at Pebble Beach. Pass the putting greens and pool tables. Pass the horseshoe-shaped bar, packed with a good-looking young crowd, and enter the vast, open dining room carpeted with artificial turf. Leroy Nieman-style scenes of golf, sailing and hunting are scattered about on the white walls.
The wooden tables, with form-fitting plastic garden chairs, are set wide enough apart to overcome the room's rowdy buzz. Projection televisions hang on the walls, while clusters of smaller sets are scattered around the room. Personable, jaunty waiters in polo shirts and shorts stride between the bar on one end and the open kitchen on the other.
The ubiquitous Buffalo chicken wings ($5.95) made a fine, thirst-inducing starter, meaty and nicely spicy with a mellow blue-cheese sauce. Spinach salad ($7.95), with mushrooms, bacon and hard-boiled egg, was as big as a spinnaker but needed a good dressing to meld the ingredients. A hamburger ($7.95) was decent enough but marred by those old bugaboos, a soggy roll and soggy fries. Sliced London broil bordelaise ($10.95), with delicious, buttery mashed potatoes, suggested the kitchen might be getting fancy, but, while the meat was tender enough, it was encased in a sauce more mushroom soup than bordelaise.
A thin slice of apple pie covered in caramel ($4.95) was surprisinglygood. The Park Avenue Country Club, unusual among sports bars, offers a nice selection of nonalcoholic cocktails.
Perhaps carrying the country club analog too far, this may be the only sports bar with restroom attendants. Yes, they expect tips.
 
Runyon's
 932 Second Avenue, at 50th Street, (212) 759-7800. Open daily, 11:30 A.M. to 4 A.M.
This relatively sedate spot -- a sports restaurant, as it bills itself -- draws an older crowd. In the spirit of its name, Runyon's is a joint. The walls are decorated haphazardly with newspaper clippings of sports favorites -- the Knicks, the Yankees, the Mets, back to the Brooklyn Dodgers -- they're all there.
No projection televisions or ear-splitting soundtracks here. Rather, there are low ceilings, tables with rattan chairs and vinyl banquettes, and small television monitors, with the sound turned off, placed so that no table is without a view.
Runyon's is the kind of place where everybody sitting at the long bar in front seems to know the bartender. Not everybody is here for sports. A group of women in the dimly lighted dining room compare notes on their jobs and love life, ignoring the television. An older man keeps one eye on the television, the other on a newspaper. A young man eats while reading a book. Two beer-drinking hockey fans quietly exult when the Rangers score.
The menu ranges beyond chicken wings, to include pastas, salads, veal dishes and seafood. The friendly, efficient waitresses even carry peppermills. Nonetheless, it's the atmosphere and the company that draw people to Runyon's; the food is incidental. A house salad ($2.75) is constructed of stiff, icy iceberg lettuce and prefab croutons. A hamburger ($7.95) was big but overdone, served with soft, soggy french fries. Linguine with white clam sauce ($9.50) was soupy and nondescript. Key lime pie ($4.50), though, was mellow and rich as cheesecake. Draft beer is served by the pint, and if they run out of beer there is another Runyon's right around the block.
 
The Sporting Club
 99 Hudson Street in TriBeCa, (212) 219-0900. Open daily, 11:30 A.M. to, depending on sporting events, 10:30 P.M. or later.
Immediately on entering this dark, packed bar, you are enveloped in pounding music. The Sporting Club, a sports bar with a disk jockey, is not a place to listen to a game.
It is a place to keep track of point spreads, though. On one wall is a huge electronic scoreboard, listing every game in any conceivable team sport. Dedicated bettors will keep one eye on the board, the other on one of the three projection television screens. Smaller monitors are placed strategically so that there are two games visible from every seat.
The dark paneled walls are covered with the usual sports memorabilia. The 1970's Yankees seem to be special favorites, with numerous portraits, including an oil of Billy Martin discreetly tucked into a back wall. The shouts of pool players, circled around two tables on one side of the bar, are occasionally heard over the music.
The house salad ($3.95) is huge but bland, with piles of lettuce, tomato, onion and mushrooms and good toasted croutons. One starter that sounded promising, shrimp brochette in a spicy Thai sauce ($7.95), was overcooked, with shrimp tough, vegetables soggy and a sauce of sesame oil and soy that was hardly spicy. A burger ($6.95) was big and beefy, as befitting a dish called "the George Steinbrenner." Key lime pie ($4.50) was light and tart, with a crunchy graham crust. Service was congenial despite the ear-splitting conditions.
 
Mickey Mantle's
 42 Central Park South, near the Avenue of the Americas, (212) 688-7777. Open daily, noon to 11 P.M., the bar until 1 A.M.
With its handsome blond wood floors and booths (pinstripe upholstery, naturally), marble tables, bright lighting and exuberant service, Mickey Mantle's is as much restaurant as bar. No video games, no shooting hoops.
Mantle's is the artiest crowd. Naturally, Mantle paraphernalia dominate the walls, with silkscreens, paintings, portraits, old uniforms and bats hanging everywhere. Unusual for sports bars, Mantle's also pays its respects to women's sports, with a large, prominent photo of the track stars Florence Griffith Joyner and Jackie Joyner-Kersee.
Small television sets, discreetly placed, are visible from each seat. Mantle's does a thriving tourist business, with fans of all ages coming to pay their respects, hoping to happen on sports celebrities, who do in fact show up from time to time. How many of them order the matzoh ball soup ($4.95), which seems as out of place in a sports bar as Wade Boggs does in a Yankee uniform? The wan chicken soup was a match for the bland baseball-size matzoh ball, which was still cold on one side.
An aberrant dish, one hopes, because the hamburger ($11.95) was pretty good, juicy and cooked to order with a helping of peppery waffle fries. A fudgy chocolate pecan bourbon pie ($5.25) was dessert overkill.
 
Play-by-Play
 4 Penn Plaza (at Madison Square Garden), (212) 465-5888. Open Mondays through Fridays, 11:30 A.M. to 2:30 P.M., and at various times for ticket-holders after events at the Garden.
The Play-by-Play is a special case, sort of a secret sports bar hidden in the Madison Square Garden complex. It is open to the public for lunch, to Madison Square Garden ticket-holders on event nights, and only to certain ticket-holders on nights when the Knicks or the Rangers are playing.
By day, the Play-by-Play, a handsome two-level bar and restaurant with a circular motif that mimics the garden, is a pleasant lunch spot. Video screens replay recent games or fights, or show tennis matches live from half way around the world. By night, after a Knicks game, however, it becomes a vibrant, splashy bar, pulsing with post-game excitement. Celebrities and athletes show up for a post-game television interview show, "Live From the Play-by-Play," that originates here.
While the requirements of the television show force audience participation at night -- producers mingle, calling for applause and appropriately rambunctious behavior -- it is all high-spirited fun, abetted by good bar food. Babyback ribs ($6.50 a half rack) are tender and meaty, dripping with smoky sauce. The M.S.G. griller ($8.95), chicken, beef and shrimp, grilled in a sweet hoisin sauce and served as kebabs with onions and peppers, was well cooked. A hamburger ($7.75) was juicy and served on a bready hard roll with seasoned waffle fries. For relief, there's the "Thai Cobb" salad ($8.75), cool grilled chicken breast over a tumble of greens with crumbled blue cheese, bacon, tomato and avocado.
Desserts are all appropriately rich and, given the heat of the television lights, melty.

HEADLINE: On Stage, and Off

BYLINE: By Bruce Weber

 BODY:

The Price of 'Angels'
Among the complicating factors in the negotiations over bringing Tony Kushner's "Angels in America" to Broadway was, on the one hand, the playwright's stated intention to make the play affordable (and thus accessible) to a less affluent, less-than-traditional downtown audience and, on the other hand, the economic risk involved in staging a long, challenging and expensive work about AIDS. 
 Budgeted at $2 million, the first part of the play, "Millennium Approaches," is to open on April 29 at the 945-seat Walter Kerr Theater, a relatively small house. Part II, "Perestroika," is to begin rehearsals in July, with the same cast, and is scheduled to open, in repertory with Part I, in October. During part of the rehearsal period, the schedule of "Millennium Approaches" will be restricted to four performances a week.
This week, in what seems sure to arouse the ire of those who lament the cost of Broadway tickets, the producers announced the pricing plan intended to accommodate the conflicting needs. In addition to providing some inexpensive second-balcony seats -- $7.50 for Wednesday matinees; $15 on Monday through Thursday evenings, and $20 on Friday and Saturday nights -- to be sold only at the box office on the day of the performance, the plan includes a top price of $60 for seats on Friday and Saturday nights, the most expensive ticket ever for a non-musical Broadway show.
In addition, the producers said that for every ticket sold, $1 will be donated to Broadway Cares/ Equity Fights AIDS: about $8,000 for a week of sellouts. The show is also bound by agreement to provide discount tickets to subscribers of the New York Shakespeare Festival, which was originally scheduled to put on the play, and the New York Theater Workshop, which helped develop it.
"The economics of this show are onerous, to say the least," said Rocco Landesman, president of Jujamcyn Theaters, which owns the Kerr and is a producer of the show. "The appropriate price for this show would be $100, if the investors are going to have a decent shot at getting their money back."
Mr. Landesman said other measures had been taken to reward investors ("They should be called donors," he said) for their risk, including giving them 50 percent of the royalty pool instead of the usual 40 percent, and allowing them to recoup 120 percent of their investment before sharing profits with the producers. With all the discounting, the show will have a chance to recoup its investment in about 35 weeks, Mr. Landesman said.
Mr. Kushner said he was satisfied with these arrangements, which he hopes will regularly include discounted orchestra and mezzanine seats even after the obligations to the Shakespeare Festival and the Theater Workshop have been fulfilled.
"Basically, I don't think price should be a reason for anyone not to come see the show," he said. "The trick is to get the discount tickets to those who really need them, instead of to scalpers. That's what's still being worked out."
 
Will Success Spoil 'Anna Christie'?
 "Anna Christie," Eugene O'Neill's drama at the Roundabout Theater, starring Natasha Richardson, Liam Neeson and Rip Torn, has sold out its limited run, which was extended through Feb. 28, but anyone so inclined has one more shot to see the show. This week the producers announced a special benefit performance at midnight on Feb. 18. Proceeds for the "midnight matinee," as it's being billed, will go to Broadway Cares/Equity Fights AIDS. Tickets are $75 and $100. Information: (212) 869-8400 or (212) 719-9393.
Meanwhile, the show's success may have its costs. Tickets have been in such demand that many Tony voters have found themselves sitting in the last three rows of the theater, and some are grumbling.
"I've never been treated this way in all my years as a voter," said Mort Mitosky, a longtime theater investor and a voter for 15 years. "There's no way you could involve yourself in the stage." Asked if he thought the seats would affect the awards, Mr. Mitosky, who said he left "Anna Christie" after the first act, answered: "Absolutely. If you can't engage yourself with the play, how can you vote?"
Byron Goldman, an investor who is another longtime voter, was less angry but no less disappointed. "I imagine that Ms. Richardson and Mr. Neeson will be nominated," he said. "It would be nice to be able to see their facial expressions so you could tell what you're voting for."
Of course, there are a lot of opinions out there. Asked if the seat assignment irritated him, the producer Morton Gottlieb replied: "In that theater, the farther up you go, the better it is. I always ask for seats in the last three rows."
 
Life in 'The Boiler Room'
 Reuben Gonzalez, a 40-year-old New Yorker, lives in Los Angeles now, where he's making a living tapping out contracted scripts for the movies. A playwright and a fiction writer, he admits he's seeking a medium for his realistic but hopeful tales of the Hispanic urban underclass, admits he's been frustrated by an unwillingness of publishers and theater and film producers to take on what he calls "my stories." But perhaps the tide is turning. At the moment, he's involved in talks with the producer Martin Brest ("Scent of a Woman") and the young director Matty Rich ("Straight Out of Brooklyn") for a film about a rehabilitative labor camp for young criminals. And his play, "The Boiler Room," is currently up at the Puerto Rican Traveling Theater at 304 West 47th Street. (Performances are in English Wednesday through Friday nights, in Spanish Saturdays and Sundays, matinees and evenings.)
A frankly autobiographical story, the play is about the travails of a Puerto Rican family in New York -- a mother, her borderline-hoodlum teen-age son and an upwardly aspiring daughter in her 20's -- trying to hang on after the death of the father, who had been a building superintendent. They live in the titular digs, where the boiler itself (frighteningly grandiose, in the current production) commands their lives. Indeed, it's the symbolism of the thing (along with a complicated, winning performance by Miriam Colon as the mother) that holds the play together.
"There's a line in the play that truthfully describes my life," said Mr. Gonzalez, who grew up in the Bronx and Spanish Harlem. "When the mother says, 'Twenty years I followed that man from basement to basement,' that's how it was. Once, we got a ground-floor apartment, and we thought we were in heaven. Of course, in retrospect it was a dump."
A seventh-grade dropout -- "I was suspended for two weeks, but after two weeks no one came looking for me, so I never went back" -- Mr. Gonzalez, like the young boy in the play, broke into cars, snatched purses, spent some time in jail. But eventually he saw life on the streets as a dead end.
"You get older, and the judges don't fool around with sentences," he said. "Either that or I was going to die."
So at age 21, he picked up, moved by himself to Weehawken, N.J., took a job as a stock clerk and set about reclaiming his life. Six years later, he walked into Fordham University, told his story to an admissions officer, passed an entrance exam and won a full scholarship.
"I don't know," he said. "I think I'm a pretty good role model."
A handsome, soft-spoken man with gray-streaked hair and a sense of perpetual befuddlement about him, Mr. Gonzalez said that his mother was much like the lost-seeming, vain but forcefully maternal character Ms. Colon brings to life in "The Boiler Room." His mother, who has returned to Puerto Rico and remarried, hasn't seen the play There's a videotape of it in Spanish, he said, but he's been hesitating about sending it.
"What do you think?" he said. "Should I?"
 
Comings and Goings
 Kevin Spacey and Frankie Faison are to appear in "Playland," the Athol Fugard play that will complete Manhattan Theater Club's Stage II season; it opens May 18. From March 22 through May 2, Stage II will present two one-actor plays in repertory: "Jenny Keeps Talking," by Richard Greenberg, starring Leslie Ayvazian as two estranged sisters, and Charlayne Woodard's "Pretty Fire," an autobiographical series of stories, presented by the author.
It looks as though "Jeffrey," Paul Rudnick's play about sex and love and gay men in the age of AIDS, already extended at the WPA Theater until Feb. 14, may have an afterlife. Kenneth Frankel, who, along with Steve Baruch and Tom and Jack Viertel, acquired the rights to the play before rehearsals began, said Wednesday that the four had made a contract offer to move the play to the Minetta Lane Theater in early March.
Meanwhile, David Henry Hwang's "Face Value," which has been homeless since it was determined that "Five Guys Named Moe" would not be vacating the Eugene O'Neill, is headed for the Cort Theater of the Shubert Organization.
The other possibility for "Face Value" had been the Brooks Atkinson, a Nederlander house. However, James M. Nederlander, president of the Nederlander Organization, confirmed his commitment to staging Lanford Wilson's play "Redwood Curtain," at the Atkinson this spring, though producers of the show have yet to fix an opening night.
Finally, David Mamet said this week that Treat Williams would replace William H. Macy as the harassing and/or harassed college professor in the playwright's "Oleanna" when Mr. Macy's contract expires three months from now. A spokesman for the show, Jim Randolph, said that Mr. Williams had expressed interest but no commitments had been made.
"It'll happen," Mr. Mamet said.

The play, the American premiere of Howard Barker's "Scenes From an Execution," runs through May 9.

HEADLINE: Books of The Times;
Building on the Pain of a Past in China

BYLINE: By MICHIKO KAKUTANI

 BODY:

Bone
 By Fae Myenne Ng
 194 pages. Hyperion. $19.95.
"Remembering the past gives power to the present," says Leila Leong, the narrator of Fae Myenne Ng's incantatory first novel. "Memories do add up." Our memories, she continues, can't bring back the dead, "but they count to keep them from becoming strangers." 
 Moving backward in time like Harold Pinter's "Betrayal" or the Stephen Sondheim musical "Merrily We Roll Along," "Bone" is a memory-novel, a novel composed of Leila's memories of her family, and her family's memories of their past lives in China and their new lives in the United States. It is a meditation upon the circumstances surrounding the mysterious suicide of Leila's sister Ona and the endless reverberations that that death has had on the entire Leong clan.
As Leila tells us, she is the oldest of the three Leong daughters: Ona was the middle sister; Nina, the youngest. Leila's own father disappeared before her birth, and she regards Leon -- her mother's second husband, and the father of Ona and Nina -- as her own father.
All of the Leongs blame themselves for Ona's death. Leon thinks he's brought bad luck on the family by failing to honor a promise he made to his adoptive father to return his bones to China. Mah, the mother, worries that her own sins -- having an affair with her boss, Tommy Hon -- have doomed the family to unhappiness. And Leila berates herself for not having paid more attention to Ona's problems, for not figuring out that something was wrong.
All three sisters, we learn, have had a difficult time straddling their parents' circumscribed world in San Francisco's Chinatown and the bustling, contemporary world beyond. They feel at once suffocated by Mah and Leon's provincialism and guilty about the freedoms and luxuries they take for granted as young American women; at once resentful of their parents' enslavement to the past and wistful about the history that eludes them here in the United States.
"We're lucky, not like the bondmaids growing up in service, or the newborn daughters whose mouths were stuffed with ashes," Leila says. "The beardless, soft-shouldered eunuchs, the courtesans with the three-inch feet and the frightened child brides -- they're all stories to us. Nina, Ona and I, we're the lucky generation. Mah and Leon forced themselves to live through the humiliation in this country so that we could have it better. We know so little of the old country. We repeat the names of grandfathers and uncles, but they have always been strangers to us. Family exists only because somebody has a story, and knowing the story connects us to a history."
Each of the Leong daughters has tried to make her own separate peace with her past. Leila has remained at home, working as a counselor at a local school and keeping a watchful eye on her parents. Nina has fled Chinatown altogether: she has moved to New York and taken a job as an airline flight attendant. Ona has found herself stuck midway between the old world and the new: she works as a hostess at Trader Vic's, and dates Osvaldo, the son of her father's former partner and current nemesis. Under fierce pressure from her parents, she breaks off with Osvaldo, an event that will play a pivotal role in her eventual suicide.
The Romeo and Juliet story of Osvaldo and Ona isn't the only melodrama in the Leong family saga. As Leila unpeels the layers of her family's past, we learn that her parents' lives, too, have been fraught with passion, anger and sadness. Summarily abandoned by her first husband, Mah married Leon, knowing his job as a merchant sailor would keep him traveling a good part of the time: such a marriage, she reasoned, would enable her to calibrate her emotional commitment and protect herself from further hurt. Instead, there were loneliness and days and weeks of waiting, and Mah soon fell into an affair with her employer.
When Leon discovered the affair, he moved out of the house on Salmon Alley and took a shabby room at a nearby boarding house. There, he passes the time dreaming up get-rich-quick schemes and silly new inventions: electric sinks, cookie-tin clocks, cash registers with intercoms.
Though a rapprochement of sorts had been arranged, Leon and Mah have recently been estranged again by the very losses that should have made them partners in grief: their life savings in a failed business venture, and Ona, who leaped off the 13th floor of a nearby housing project.
Having grown up in San Francisco's Chinatown herself, Ms. Ng conjures the immigrant world of Mah and Leon with the affectionate knowledge of an insider and the observant unsentimentality of an outsider. She conveys to the reader the incredible hardship of these characters' lives: the long years spent in sweatshops and kitchens and laundry rooms, the hours and hours spent waiting in lines for Social Security and unemployment compensation. We are made to see, through the eyes of Leila, both the remarkable perseverance of her parents in the face of so much difficulty, and the degree to which that perseverance has been built, in equal parts, on naivete and genuine courage.
Blessed with a poet's gift for metaphor and a reporter's eye for detail, Ms. Ng writes with grace, authority and grit. The reader eagerly awaits her next book.

GRAPHIC: Photo of Fae Myenne Ng. (Jerry Bauer)

HEADLINE: HORSE RACING;
N.Y.R.A. Warns of Enormous Deficits

BYLINE: By JOSEPH DURSO

 BODY:
Officials of the New York Racing Association, renewing their proposals to restructure horse racing in the state, predicted yesterday that the sport could produce a deficit of $58 million a year within five years unless changes were made.
Specifically, they called for the merger of the state's six Off-Track Betting corporations under the racing association's control, the reduction of the state's tax on bets and the suspension of racing in New York for six weeks each winter. 
 The prediction was made Wednesday in testimony during a closed meeting of the Governor's Commission on Racing in the 21st Century and was made public yesterday at Aqueduct race track by Gerard J. McKeon, president of the N.Y.R.A.
For the last year, Gov. Mario M. Cuomo has been criticizing the association for its business practices and demanding that it reduce costs and increase profits transferred to the state. Lately, he has been calling for private ownership of the racing business. The Governor appointed a nine-member commission last fall to conduct hearings on the structure of racing, and it is expected to report back to him in the spring.
Vincent Tese, the state's director of economic development and chairman of the Governor's commission, said through a spokesman that the commission had no comment at this time on the N.Y.R.A. proposals.
"We have no reaction yet," said the spokesman, Steve Villano, vice president of the commission. "We're still in the process of reviewing the materials they offered so that we can complete our report to the Governor."
The racing association, created in 1955, operates under a state franchise to conduct racing at Belmont Park, Aqueduct and Saratoga. The franchise expires in the year 2000, but bids will be taken starting in 1997 from groups interested in operating thoroughbred racing in the state.
 
$3 Million Loss in '92
 Officials of the association testified last Nov. 10 in a public hearing, and again on Wednesday in executive session. At his briefing yesterday, McKeon said: "Our presentation in November was philosophical, not tactical. The commission asked us to follow it with a business plan. This is it."
"We agree with Governor Cuomo," he said, "that racing should be run by a private corporation. Government should not be in the racing business."
But he emphasized that the racing association fits the Governor's definition, and warned that racing was already costing the state money and would cost even more unless basic changes were made in the pattern of taking and taxing bets. During 1992, he said, the combined operations of the racing association and the Offtrack Betting corporations resulted in a deficit of $3 million.
"We project that by the year 1997," McKeon said, "if the current structure is held intact, combined N.Y.R.A. and OTB operations will lose $58 million, versus the $46 million earned 10 years earlier. And that doesn't even assume any proliferation in casino gambling or Keno."
McKeon proposed several strategies to achieve a "turnaround of $100 million in five years." They involved the takeover of OTB and the closing of many OTB shops, a new emphasis on home-telephone and teletheater betting and a drop in the takeout on bets from 17 percent to 14 percent to return more money to the bettor and the horsemen.
"Most OTB stores in Brooklyn and Queens are unprofitable because they don't have TV pictures," he said. "Under our plan, we would eliminate many of the stores, but the rest would all carry races on TV. We offer a stage where the main players come. The horsemen and fans have not been faring well."

HEADLINE: CHRONICLE

BYLINE: By KATHLEEN TELTSCH

 BODY:
Thirty-five Hispanic women from government, corporate board rooms, small businesses and universities across the United States will meet in Washington next week to talk about ethnic and sexual barriers to career advancement.
The chairwoman of the conference, the National Hispanic Women's Summit, is MARIA ELENA TORANO, chief executive of META, a Miami environmental and management concern, who participated in President Clinton's economic conference last month in Little Rock, Ark. "All 35 have accepted invitations to the Feb. 6 summit," she said. 
 They include Dr. Elsa Gomez, president of Kean College in Union, N.J.; Miriam Santos, City Treasurer of Chicago, and Leticia Quezada, president of the Los Angeles school board. The conference speaker will be Maria Echaveste, deputy director of personnel for Mr. Clinton's transition team.
"The glass ceiling restraining Hispanic-American women is even thicker and harder to break than that holding back other women," Ms. Torano said. "By the time our summit ends, we'll have a plan to break through that ceiling."

HEADLINE: Stop the Porn Explosion

BYLINE: By Charles Millard; Charles Millard, Republican-Liberal, is a member of the New York City Council from the Upper East Side.

 BODY:
H ow do you explain to a child that the Doll House across the street is not for children but is a topless bar with lap dancing? Or that the movie "Edward Pe-nishands" is not an instructional toilet-training video?
Residential areas across the country have recently been inundated with adult video stores and topless and even bottomless bars. The problem is difficult: freedom of speech must be respected, but no one wants advertisements for "Debbie Does Dallas" plastered on neighborhood walls. 
 Americans have been taught a healthy respect for the First Amendment. We hesitate to express opposition to pornography and are loath to regulate it. But New York City should follow the lead of other municipalities by issuing zoning regulations to protect neighborhoods from the crime and quality-of-life problems that come with it.
Since 1989, sex businesses in midtown Manhattan have increased by nearly 40 percent. In Chelsea, community groups have demonstrated against a growing concentration of adult video stores; on East 53d Street, two such businesses opened within 30 yards of each other in the same month; a few blocks north, around the corner from an "upscale" topless bar, the owners of a soon-to-be vacated restaurant have received only three offers to occupy the space, each from a topless establishment; and in Queens, a topless bar stopped serving alcohol so it could ignore State Liquor Authority regulations and go bottomless.
Several factors are blamed for this explosion of sex businesses. Voyeurism and masturbation are forms of safe sex in the age of AIDS. People have VCR's. In recessions, landlords will rent to anyone who can pay. And in New York, cleanup of 42d Street has pushed these businesses to other areas.
Regulating the content of these businesses violates the First Amendment. To avoid such problems, some officials advocate trying to persuade sex businesses to operate more discreetly. But the voluntary approach rarely works. If getting rid of lurid signs and flashing lights were good business, owners would do it on their own. Prurience is profitable; sex sells.
Nonetheless, in Young v. American Mini-Theatres and in City of Renton v. Playtime Theatres, the U.S. Supreme Court upheld the use of zoning laws to restrict sex businesses. Paradoxically, it is not permissible to regulate these establishments simply because they are offensive -- though it should come as no surprise that businesses that degrade women and encourage misogynistic violence would generate an increase in crime and prostitution.
Rather, zoning codes are intended to address quality-of-life issues. They may impose reasonable time, place and manner restrictions on sex businesses if it can be shown that the businesses generate social problems. While zoning changes cannot prevent the blatant exploitation of women, they can address other negative effects. In Detroit, adult theaters cannot be within 500 feet of a residential area. In Buffalo, sex businesses must be 1,000 feet apart. And in Boston, these establishments can only be located in the infamous "combat zone."
Why does New York City lag behind other cities in efforts to combat the ill effects of sex businesses? The sex industry is powerful. It generates billions of dollars each year, and it benefits from the protection of not only the First Amendment but, occasionally, of organized crime as well. In addition, political correctness seems to demand an unthinking fealty to free expression without regard for its negative effects -- even when the Constitution allows us to regulate them.
A year ago, Councilman Walter McCaffrey of Queens and I introduced legislation to keep certain sex businesses 500 feet away from residentially zoned areas. Yet no progress had been made on the proposal in the City Council, nor has Mayor David Dinkins shown any support.
Instead, the Department of Consumer Affairs wants to get out of the business of regulating topless bars completely because the constitutional issues are so complex. Thus, while the department advocates a law against loud discos within 200 feet of a legal residence, nothing is done to address topless or bottomless dancing establishments, no matter where they are.
We are fortunate to live in a country that protects its citizens' rights, and the First Amendment is the paramount protection. But responsible lawmaking requires a careful, nuanced balancing of competing interests. So Debbie may do Dallas -- but not in New York City's neighborhoods.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: The People Are Heard, at Least Those Who Call Talk Radio

BYLINE: By ELIZABETH KOLBERT

 BODY:
It is becoming a familiar rite of democracy in the talk show age.
An issue, usually one with visceral appeal, becomes the topic of the moment on AM radio. Within days, or even hours, the Capitol switchboard in Washington is flooded with calls . 
 This rite was performed to great effect last week, when Zoe Baird was forced to withdraw her nomination to be Attorney General. Now it may be under way again.
Earlier this week, on talk radio stations across the country, the issue of homosexuals in the military was the No. 1 topic. By Wednesday, a near-record number of callers had phoned the Capitol to register their opinions.
To be sure, there is no way to draw a direct connection between the message that goes out over talk radio and the message that is delivered to Capitol Hill. Indeed, some right wing Christian groups, which have made opposition to gay rights the main focus of their grass-roots organizing, say their efforts have generated the outcry against President Clinton's position. And some issues simply strike such an emotional chord with the public that people act on their own.
 
Gathering Strength
 But among politicians, there is a sense that the talk shows are increasingly potent in American politics.
"Congress believes in the interactive phenomenon," said Michael Deaver, a former top aide to President Reagan. "They feel the people feel this may be their new franchise."
Senator James M. Jeffords, Republican of Vermont, said that during his 18 years in Washington the number of calls and letters he receives had drastically increased. He called talk radio a significant factor.
"Rush Limbaugh is a factor now," he said. "No question he generates calls and letters." Mr. Limbaugh's syndicated program has by far the largest following of any talk show nationally.
There are now more than 800 radio stations in the country that have a talk or talk-news format, practically double the number there were just five years ago and four times as many as there were in the early 1980's.
In part, the rise of talk radio is a product of economics: AM stations are looking for ways to compete with FM stations, which, with better sound quality, have taken over music radio. But the phenomenon also seems to reflect a growing disenchantment with the so-called establishment media.
Since the beginning of the week, the issue of homosexuals in the military has been the hot topic among radio talk show hosts of all political persuasions. Such is the fast-burning nature of debate on talk radio, though, that by yesterday there already was a sense that the topic had started to cool.
"It's still topic A," Michael Harrison, said who has a talk program on WTIC in Hartford. "But it won't be for long." On his Wednesday night program, he said, one out of five calls "were protest calls against the topic being this big."
While the calls to Congress have been overwhelmingly opposed to lifting the military ban on homosexuals, those on the talk shows seem to have been somewhat more mixed.
Barry Young, at KFYI in Phoenix, who opposes lifting the ban, said his calls had been heavily against the President. But Mr. Harrison, who favors lifting the ban, said his calls had been roughly even.
"There's a difference between that which is true, legitimate public opinion and that which is activist zealots overwhelming the air waves," Mr. Harrison said. In the case of opposition to homosexuals in the military, he said, "I don't think it's so clear which it is."
An informal survey of talk radio shows across the country yielded a wide range of opinions among both callers and hosts, couched in language that ran from calmly measured to wildly inflammatory.
Yesterday afternoon, Robert Namer, host of an afternoon talk show on WASO in Covington, La., opened a discussion of homosexuals in the military by saying: "This arrogant Clinton -- this guy is so insensitive. I have figured it out. Either his wife's a lesbian, or he is homosexual." He urged listeners to "get on the phone and call Washington and keep jamming" to protest the President's plan, and twice he read off the White House switchboard number.
In New York yesterday morning, former Mayor Ed Koch, host of a talk show on WABC, tried to steer callers away from the topic, as did Bob Grant, host of an afternoon show on the same station. But often even their own conversation drifted back.
Shortly after 3:30 P.M., a caller named Tom phoned Mr. Grant, wanting to talk about an incident from his own military career.
 
Talk Show Host's Nightmare
 "What incident do you have to share with us?" Mr. Grant interrupted him.
But then Mr. Grant went on: "I woke up this morning in a cold sweat, thinking that all of my listeners are World War II veterans who had to take a shower with a soldier who is double-gated. These old geezers call up and say, 'I don't want to have to fight in the foxhole with these people.' There are no more foxholes."
On Wednesday, Michael Jackson, a morning talk show host on Los Angeles's KABC, pointed out to his listeners that the military's ban on homosexuals had not become official policy until 1982. That prompted some callers to change their opinions.
"I was against the idea, but if the military tolerated them before 1982, they can tolerate them now," said one male caller, who did not give his name.
Kathleen Hall Jamieson, dean of the Annenberg School for Communications at the University of Pennsylvania and an expert on political communications, said that she was concerned that the radio talk shows were more than just communicating public opinion on the issue of homosexuals in the military: they were also helping to formulate it. And she noted that those who oppose homosexuals in the military may feel more strongly about the issue, and hence be more likely to call in about the topic, than those who feel the opposite, creating the illusion over overwhelming opposition.
"There's a phenomenon that moves people toward the consensus position," she said. "The danger is you may be creating a false consensus."

HEADLINE: At the Bar;
Prefering more red ink to more black eyes, the bar association hires an image maker

BYLINE: By David Margolick

 BODY:
During the inaugural festivities for President Clinton, Michael T. Scanlon Jr. walked into one of his favorite Washington restaurants. "Anybody here heard any good lawyer jokes recently?" he asked. There was silence. "See!" Mr. Scanlon declared. "It's working already!"
"It" is Mr. Scanlon's mission to burnish the battered image of the nation's lawyers. To him the problem was neatly reflected in the election, when the Democrats refrained from lawyer-bashing, he says, only because the Republicans had beaten them to it. Though the Republicans lost, a nevertheless chastened American Bar Association hired Mr. Scanlon as its first public relations guru. 
 At first blush, another high-priced bureaucrat is precisely what the bar group does not need. Even association leaders like R. William Ide 3d of Atlanta, the incoming president, admit that the 370,000-member organization is awash in apparatchiks. Moreover, battered by a bad economy and 3,850 resignations over its endorsement of abortion rights, the A.B.A. is facing a $4.3 million deficit in its current budget.
The financial problem has grown so serious that the organization has laid off dozens of staff members. It has also cut a variety of corners, from scaling back its use of Federal Express deliveries to serving hors d'oeuvres instead of a full meal at last year's Christmas party.
Thus, bringing in a media maven, and paying him $170,000 a year, did not sit well with some A.B.A. employees. But the bar group's president, J. Michael McWilliams of Baltimore, insists that the move will ultimately pay for itself, both in increased membership and in enhanced good will among a skeptical public. In recent years, the only time the public seemed to hear about the A.B.A. was when Dan Quayle came before it to attack the legal profession.
"I'd choose to describe it not as P.R. but a communications strategy to insure that members, nonmembers and the public have an accurate picture of what the A.B.A. stands for and the work that it does," Mr. McWilliams said. "It's amazing how, for a bunch of lawyers, what poor communicators we've been."
While Mr. Quayle's broadside did not prompt the A.B.A. campaign, which began last spring, Mr. Scanlon said it served as "a wake-up call" to the profession.
"In the short term it ruffled a lot of feathers," he said, "but in the long term it may serve as the low-water mark, and from here on out a rising tide might take place."
Mr. Scanlon, the son and stepson of lawyers, is a man with a mania for metaphors. After two months spent "climbing up the greasy learning curve," he said by telephone from the group's headquarters in Chicago, he is ready to reach out on behalf of the insular A.B.A.
"We end up talking to the choir all the time," said Mr. Scanlon, formerly the executive vice president of the Petroleum Marketing Education Foundation. "It's time to talk to the congregation, and maybe to the folks on the sidewalk."
If one were to conduct a computer analysis of Mr. Scanlon's vocabulary, no word would pop up more often than "pro-active." Too often, he contends, the A.B.A. has sat on the sidelines, rousing itself only when pummeled. A survey of the association's members by the poll taker Peter Hart corroborated Mr. Scanlon's sense that the nation's lawyers are tired of being punching bags.
"Rather than wait for the issue to sweep over us and drown us out, we're going to be surfing in on the wave," Mr. Scanlon said.
He insists that he has no plans to conduct a public relations campaign ("Whitewashing an old stone fence doesn't make it any stronger," he said), but his description of what is in store sounds suspiciously like one.
Eventually, he said, it could include more television programs, public-service announcements and even paid advertisements. He also wants bar presidents, few of whom ever leave their fingerprints on the public consciousness, to become more vocal.
"We're going to try to show people that there's a face behind all these lawyers, that the profession is not just arguing in a courtroom but dealing with people's problems and solving them on a daily basis," he said.
Two lawyers, neither with any apparent professional self-hatred, are now living at 1600 Pennsylvania Avenue. "We haven't had a lawyer in the White House for some time, and we now have two who are very visible," Mr. Scanlon said. "If they are anything close to successful, it can only help the profession overall."
But Mr. Scanlon acknowledges that the task of making America's lawyers more lovable will not be simple, and he is settling in for the long haul. "I'm 45 years old, so I've got 20 years of work left," he said. "The good Lord willing, and if the creek don't rise, maybe I'll spend all 20 of those years here."

HEADLINE: Two New Law Journals Plan To Focus on Asian Americans

BYLINE: Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SAN FRANCISCO, Jan. 28

 BODY:
University of California students in Los Angeles and Berkeley are working separately to produce the nation's first two journals that will focus on legal issues of concern to Asian Americans.
The undertakings reflect in part the growth in the number of Asian-American law students and lawyers. In 1991, the latest year for which data are available, 5,028 Asian Americans were enrolled in accredited American law schools, up from 1,755 in 1981, according to the American Bar Association. 
 The plans for the journals also reflect the view among many Asian-American lawyers and law students that the issues that matter to them particularly have long been neglected by law publications. Both journals are scheduled to start publishing in the fall.
The Berkeley journal plans to draw on a national group of scholars and tackle more global issues, like international trade, and adopt an international perspective.
"What we're hoping to do is be a catalyst and a forum," said Nicole Wong, a second-year law student at Berkeley and editor in chief of the university's Asian Law Journal. "We want to be a forum to raise issues that have been bottled up inside people and a catalyst for people to think about issues they haven't thought about."
The U.C.L.A. journal will be written mostly by students and focus on issues of particular interest to Southern California.
"At U.C.L.A., there's the Black Law Journal, which discusses African-American issues, there's the Chicano Law Review, which deals with Latino issues, and other journals that deal with trade and the environment," said Theresa Han, a third-year law student at U.C.L.A. and co-editor in chief of the university's Asian American and Pacific Islands Law Journal. "What we're talking about are issues that specifically affect the Asian-American community, like voting rights, how admission policies at universities affect Asian Americans or how civil-rights legislation affects Asian Americans."
Both journals have adopted as their approach to legal analysis a young and controversial area of legal scholarship known as "critical race theory." The theory holds that to properly understand law in American society, one must recognize that it has systematically subordinated non-whites.
"Law is not just neutral, to be used by good and bad people," said Angela Harris, a Berkeley law professor and adviser to the Asian Law Journal. She noted, for example, how economic competition between Chinese and white laborers near the turn of the century led to the Exclusionary Acts, which suspended Chinese immigration. "Subordination of people of color is woven into the law," she said.

HEADLINE: On Stage, and Off

BYLINE: By Bruce Weber

 BODY:

The Price of 'Angels'
Among the complicating factors in the negotiations over bringing Tony Kushner's "Angels in America" to Broadway was, on the one hand, the playwright's stated intention to make the play affordable (and thus accessible) to a less affluent, less-than-traditional downtown audience and, on the other hand, the economic risk involved in staging a long, challenging and expensive work about AIDS. 
 Budgeted at $2 million, the first part of the play, "Millennium Approaches," is to open on April 29 at the 945-seat Walter Kerr Theater, a relatively small house. Part II, "Perestroika," is to begin rehearsals in July, with the same cast, and is scheduled to open, in repertory with Part I, in October. During part of the rehearsal period, the schedule of "Millennium Approaches" will be restricted to four performances a week.
This week, in what seems sure to arouse the ire of those who lament the cost of Broadway tickets, the producers announced the pricing plan intended to accommodate the conflicting needs. In addition to providing some inexpensive second-balcony seats -- $7.50 for Wednesday matinees; $15 on Monday through Thursday evenings, and $20 on Friday and Saturday nights -- to be sold only at the box office on the day of the performance, the plan includes a top price of $60 for seats on Friday and Saturday nights, the most expensive ticket ever for a non-musical Broadway show.
In addition, the producers said that for every ticket sold, $1 will be donated to Broadway Cares/ Equity Fights AIDS: about $8,000 for a week of sellouts. The show is also bound by agreement to provide discount tickets to subscribers of the New York Shakespeare Festival, which was originally scheduled to put on the play, and the New York Theater Workshop, which helped develop it.
"The economics of this show are onerous, to say the least," said Rocco Landesman, president of Jujamcyn Theaters, which owns the Kerr and is a producer of the show. "The appropriate price for this show would be $100, if the investors are going to have a decent shot at getting their money back."
Mr. Landesman said other measures had been taken to reward investors ("They should be called donors," he said) for their risk, including giving them 50 percent of the royalty pool instead of the usual 40 percent, and allowing them to recoup 120 percent of their investment before sharing profits with the producers. With all the discounting, the show will have a chance to recoup its investment in about 35 weeks, Mr. Landesman said.
Mr. Kushner said he was satisfied with these arrangements, which he hopes will regularly include discounted orchestra and mezzanine seats even after the obligations to the Shakespeare Festival and the Theater Workshop have been fulfilled.
"Basically, I don't think price should be a reason for anyone not to come see the show," he said. "The trick is to get the discount tickets to those who really need them, instead of to scalpers. That's what's still being worked out."
 
Will Success Spoil 'Anna Christie'?
 "Anna Christie," Eugene O'Neill's drama at the Roundabout Theater, starring Natasha Richardson, Liam Neeson and Rip Torn, has sold out its limited run, which was extended through Feb. 28, but anyone so inclined has one more shot to see the show. This week the producers announced a special benefit performance at midnight on Feb. 18. Proceeds for the "midnight matinee," as it's being billed, will go to Broadway Cares/Equity Fights AIDS. Tickets are $75 and $100. Information: (212) 869-8400 or (212) 719-9393.
Meanwhile, the show's success may have its costs. Tickets have been in such demand that many Tony voters have found themselves sitting in the last three rows of the theater, and some are grumbling.
"I've never been treated this way in all my years as a voter," said Mort Mitosky, a longtime theater investor and a voter for 15 years. "There's no way you could involve yourself in the stage." Asked if he thought the seats would affect the awards, Mr. Mitosky, who said he left "Anna Christie" after the first act, answered: "Absolutely. If you can't engage yourself with the play, how can you vote?"
Byron Goldman, an investor who is another longtime voter, was less angry but no less disappointed. "I imagine that Ms. Richardson and Mr. Neeson will be nominated," he said. "It would be nice to be able to see their facial expressions so you could tell what you're voting for."
Of course, there are a lot of opinions out there. Asked if the seat assignment irritated him, the producer Morton Gottlieb replied: "In that theater, the farther up you go, the better it is. I always ask for seats in the last three rows."
 
Life in 'The Boiler Room'
 Reuben Gonzalez, a 40-year-old New Yorker, lives in Los Angeles now, where he's making a living tapping out contracted scripts for the movies. A playwright and a fiction writer, he admits he's seeking a medium for his realistic but hopeful tales of the Hispanic urban underclass, admits he's been frustrated by an unwillingness of publishers and theater and film producers to take on what he calls "my stories." But perhaps the tide is turning. At the moment, he's involved in talks with the producer Martin Brest ("Scent of a Woman") and the young director Matty Rich ("Straight Out of Brooklyn") for a film about a rehabilitative labor camp for young criminals. And his play, "The Boiler Room," is currently up at the Puerto Rican Traveling Theater at 304 West 47th Street. (Performances are in English Wednesday through Friday nights, in Spanish Saturdays and Sundays, matinees and evenings.)
A frankly autobiographical story, the play is about the travails of a Puerto Rican family in New York -- a mother, her borderline-hoodlum teen-age son and an upwardly aspiring daughter in her 20's -- trying to hang on after the death of the father, who had been a building superintendent. They live in the titular digs, where the boiler itself (frighteningly grandiose, in the current production) commands their lives. Indeed, it's the symbolism of the thing (along with a complicated, winning performance by Miriam Colon as the mother) that holds the play together.
"There's a line in the play that truthfully describes my life," said Mr. Gonzalez, who grew up in the Bronx and Spanish Harlem. "When the mother says, 'Twenty years I followed that man from basement to basement,' that's how it was. Once, we got a ground-floor apartment, and we thought we were in heaven. Of course, in retrospect it was a dump."
A seventh-grade dropout -- "I was suspended for two weeks, but after two weeks no one came looking for me, so I never went back" -- Mr. Gonzalez, like the young boy in the play, broke into cars, snatched purses, spent some time in jail. But eventually he saw life on the streets as a dead end.
"You get older, and the judges don't fool around with sentences," he said. "Either that or I was going to die."
So at age 21, he picked up, moved by himself to Weehawken, N.J., took a job as a stock clerk and set about reclaiming his life. Six years later, he walked into Fordham University, told his story to an admissions officer, passed an entrance exam and won a full scholarship.
"I don't know," he said. "I think I'm a pretty good role model."
A handsome, soft-spoken man with gray-streaked hair and a sense of perpetual befuddlement about him, Mr. Gonzalez said that his mother was much like the lost-seeming, vain but forcefully maternal character Ms. Colon brings to life in "The Boiler Room." His mother, who has returned to Puerto Rico and remarried, hasn't seen the play There's a videotape of it in Spanish, he said, but he's been hesitating about sending it.
"What do you think?" he said. "Should I?"
 
Comings and Goings
 Kevin Spacey and Frankie Faison are to appear in "Playland," the Athol Fugard play that will complete Manhattan Theater Club's Stage II season; it opens May 18. From March 22 through May 2, Stage II will present two one-actor plays in repertory: "Jenny Keeps Talking," by Richard Greenberg, starring Leslie Ayvazian as two estranged sisters, and Charlayne Woodard's "Pretty Fire," an autobiographical series of stories, presented by the author.
It looks as though "Jeffrey," Paul Rudnick's play about sex and love and gay men in the age of AIDS, already extended at the WPA Theater until Feb. 14, may have an afterlife. Kenneth Frankel, who, along with Steve Baruch and Tom and Jack Viertel, acquired the rights to the play before rehearsals began, said Wednesday that the four had made a contract offer to move the play to the Minetta Lane Theater in early March.
Meanwhile, David Henry Hwang's "Face Value," which has been homeless since it was determined that "Five Guys Named Moe" would not be vacating the Eugene O'Neill, is headed for the Cort Theater of the Shubert Organization.
The other possibility for "Face Value" had been the Brooks Atkinson, a Nederlander house. However, James M. Nederlander, president of the Nederlander Organization, confirmed his commitment to staging Lanford Wilson's play "Redwood Curtain," at the Atkinson this spring, though producers of the show have yet to fix an opening night.
Finally, David Mamet said this week that Treat Williams would replace William H. Macy as the harassing and/or harassed college professor in the playwright's "Oleanna" when Mr. Macy's contract expires three months from now. A spokesman for the show, Jim Randolph, said that Mr. Williams had expressed interest but no commitments had been made.
"It'll happen," Mr. Mamet said.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
GlenFed Will Miss A Payment

BYLINE: By ANDREA ADELSON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, Jan. 28

 BODY:
GlenFed Inc., parent of the nation's fifth-largest savings and loan association, said today that it had run short of cash and would be unable to make a $4.6 million debt payment due in March, which could result in the company being forced into bankruptcy by its bondholders.
In addition, GlenFed announced a $79.4 million loss for its fiscal second quarter, ended Dec. 31, compared with earnings of $27.6 million in the comparable period a year earlier. The results reflect a $77 million loan-loss provision in the most recent quarter, as the company's nonperforming assets climbed 6 percent. 
 GlenFed's capital, which had been depleted by two years of heavy losses, was further eroded by the quarter's loss. That led the Office of Thrift Supervision to impose even harsher operating restrictions on GlenFed's Glendale Federal Bank, the company said today. While before, GlenFed's potential acquisitions needed regulatory approval, now so do the level of the company's interest rates and executive salaries.
The parent of Glendale Federal has been under regulatory pressure since last March to meet new capital requirements by June 30 or risk seizure. It has proposed a restructuring plan based on asset sales or a merger, but analysts say chances of a capital infusion remain slim if losses from bad loans continue unabated.
Stephen J. Trafton, GlenFed's chairman and chief executive, said he was optimistic the looming default could be averted after negotiations with GlenFed's bondholders, who control $59.9 million in securities.
 
 
'Best Course of Action'
 "We've advised them their best course of action is to stand still and wait for the recapitalization," he said.
In its first major step toward meeting new capital requirements, GlenFed also said today that it had begun an exchange with bondholders for preferred stock that would inject $128 million into the company after taxes.
Even so, Mr. Trafton said the company must still raise up to $450 million to meet regulatory requirements. "If GlenFed is unable to raise the capital -- and it will be a very difficult task -- it is possible the institution will face seizure, a liquidation that will cost us as taxpayers $3 billion to $4 billion," he said.
The holding company's cash crunch began last March when dividends paid by its banking subsidiary were suspended when the company failed to meet capital requirements. Its cash was further depleted when regulators forced GlenFed to repay its thrift $2.3 million in holding company expenses in the recent quarter.
Peter Treadway, a savings and loan analyst with Smith Barney, Harris Upham & Company, said the company's best hope for survival lay in winning a settlement in its lawsuit against the Government over an accounting procedure.

HEADLINE: INVESTING;
Holding Down the Costs Of Buying Treasuries

BYLINE: By Carole Gould

 BODY:
WITH yields on Treasury securities scraping bottom -- this week the bellwether 30-year Treasury bond yielded just percent, a six-year low -- the costs of investing in these securities count all the more.
Do you get the best payoff buying Treasuries directly from the Federal Government? From stockbrokers? In mutual funds? Here's a quick review of the options , with some strategies to increase yields. 
 People who buy Treasury bills, bonds and notes are making loans to the Government, which uses the money to finance the budget deficit.
Treasury bills, usually called "T-bills," mature in 3, 6 or 12 months. The minimum investment is $10,000, with additional investments sold in increments of $5,000.
T-bills are sold at a discount, meaning that you pay less than the full face value of the bill. When they mature, the Government buys them back at par, or face value. Your profit equals the difference between what you paid and the par value of the T-bill (or its market value, if you sell before maturity).
By contrast, you can buy Treasury notes and bonds for as little as $1,000 but you tie up your money much longer. Treasury notes mature in 1 to 10 years; bonds, even longer.
Unlike T-bills, however, bonds and notes are sold at face value so investors must write a check for the full amount. Interest is paid every six months.
The bonds pay a stated coupon rate, or interest rate, but the yield depends on the price paid for the bond. Yields are reduced when bonds sell for more than par value, or 100. Last week, a five-year Treasury note with a coupon rate of 8.125 percent sold for $109.75, so its actual yield is 5.87 percent.
Factor in the tax savings, too. Interest paid by Treasuries is subject to Federal tax, but is free of state and local taxes. You can defer the Federal income taxes due by buying T-bills that mature the following year because none of the interest would be taxed until then.
Because they are backed by the Government, Treasuries carry virtually no credit risk. However they are subject to market risk. When interest rates rise, the prices of Treasuries fall, and vice versa. The longer the term of the security, the more prices fall when rates rise. You can eliminate market risk completely by holding the Treasury securities to their maturity.
Still, many advisers recommend that individuals stay on the shorter end of the maturity spectrum.
Very conservative investors should own two-year securities, and those willing to accept slightly more risk for more yield might venture out five to eight years, said Maria Crawford Scott, who edits the American Association of Individual Investors Journal, in Chicago. She advises leaving longer issues to institutional investors because of the greater volatility.
Because interest rates have nowhere to go but up, using a "staggered maturity" approach is particularly valuable, says Lewis J. Altfest, a New York City financial planner.He advises splitting a $75,000 portfolio into three lots of $25,000, which would then be invested in 3- , 7- and 12-year issues. The money is rolled over as each matures, creating a constant stream of money available for reinvestment at the then-prevailing rate.
What's the best way to buy Treasuries? The Treasury Department sells Treasuries at auction. Prices are based on competitive bids for specific issues submitted by institutions and professional traders. Individual investors submit noncompetitive bids, meaning that they simply request securities without specifying prices, and then receive the average of the competitive bids.
You can buy Treasuries without paying a middleman through the "Treasury Direct" program. To participate, request the forms and information needed from the Federal Reserve office nearest you (in New York, 212 720-5000).
One problem is that buyers of small lots sacrifice some yield in the form of "odd lot" markups. Last week a one-year T-bill due February 1994 with a coupon rate of 6.875 sold at 103.375, yielding 3.61 percent for million-dollar investors but only 2.77 percent for a $5,000 lot and 3.14 for $10,000 worth, Mr. Altfest figured.
On the other hand, buying direct lets you avoid paying stockbrokers' fees. Merrill Lynch charges $50; Charles Schwab & Company, a discount broker, charges a flat $39 for all Treasury transactions, a hefty fee for small purchases.
However, because the Federal Reserve maintains no secondary market, investors who want to sell before their Treasuries mature must deal with intermediaries.
Treasuries also come packaged in mutual funds. Because funds hold many issues, they never "mature." Shifts in interest rates are reflected in the fund's share price.
Still, buying fund shares is easier than buying directly and might make sense for people who prefer monthly over semiannual interest payments or who invest only small amounts. Shares can be cashed in at any time for full market value.
You pay a price for the convenience. Roughly half the funds carry sales charges; paying such fees for funds that are simple packages of Treasuries makes no sense. Fund expenses average three-quarters of 1 percent but careful shoppers can pay as little as .30 percent for funds sold by the Vanguard Group in Valley Forge, Pa., the industry's lowest-cost provider.

HEADLINE: LAW;
Lawyers More Willing To Discuss Their Fees

BYLINE: By Laura Mansnerus

 BODY:
LAWYERS are not blase about money these days, which means that they are not blase about clients. While still not very amenable to negotiation -- lawyers have fairly firm rates, like surgeons or baby sitters -- they are increasingly receptive to requests to keep costs down.
Clients can minimize rude surprises by asking questions at the outset about fees. Since the advent of computerized billing systems, they can also monitor statements more easily. And increasingly, experts on billing say, clients are doing both. 
 The subject "is in the public eye more than when lawyers weren't allowed to discuss fees publicly," said George Kuhlman, ethics counsel for the American Bar Association. Mr. Kuhlman said advertising had impressed upon people who need lawyers that they are shoppers of a sort.
For new clients, the general practice, urged but not required by most state ethics codes, is to send an "engagement letter" confirming billing terms. Usually a retainer, or down payment, is required; this will be worked off and is sometimes a nonrefundable minimum.
For tasks that are discrete and predictable, like a simple will or real estate closing, most lawyers charge a flat fee that is not likely to exceed $1,000. In personal injury cases, plaintiffs' lawyers work on a contingency basis. Otherwise, billing by the hour, usually in units of 6 or 15 minutes, is the norm. It is also the source of most trouble and suspense.
The hourly rate is disclosed in the first consultation and on each bill. But a firm might have several people working on one matter, including an associate at $100 an hour and a partner at twice that rate. Most clients do not know who is working or how many hours are being rung up.
"People are concerned that the meter is running when the taxi isn't moving," said Richard C. Reed, a lawyer in Bellevue, Wash., who headed an A.B.A. task force on billing.
Nearly all lawyers say, however, that there is no such thing as an estimate -- at least not the kind an auto mechanic gives.
"I'm not an apologist for the profession, but it's almost impossible to say what the bill is going to be," said Daniel R. White, author of "What Lawyers Do . . . and How to Make Them Work for You" (E. P. Dutton, 1987). He added, however: "They will bill as much as they think they can get away with."
So what should a client ask? First, seek a probable range of costs, which is better than no estimate. The client can also worry aloud and in advance about expenses.
"I would say, 'I want you to understand that I'm not a deep pocket,' " Mr. White said. "I have definitely heard lawyers at firms say, 'This client is concerned about the bill.' " For associates doing research, "that means you yuk it up less in the library."
It is certainly legitimate to set an upper limit, although some say that encourages the lawyer to reach it. And no lawyer would argue with a request to notify the client when the tab hits a certain point.
Even with a fixed fee, it's advisable to ask exactly what it covers: How many consultations are included? How many times can the beneficiaries in the will be changed? What if the adoption is contested?
And sometimes neither an hourly nor a flat fee covers photocopies and overnight mail, which can add costs at astonishing speed.
Finally, the client should know the policy on telephone consultations. Almost all lawyers bill them; as for the two-minute call, some record a minimum charge and others don't.
Clients should be aware that conversation costs. Mr. Kuhlman said, "If you want to spend time talking about things that don't require a lawyer, call your sister-in-law."
