LENGTH: 1328 words

HEADLINE: Dinkins Proposes a Financial Plan To Fill Budget Gap

BYLINE: By JAMES C. McKINLEY Jr.

 BODY:
Mayor David N. Dinkins proposed a financial plan for New York City yesterday that relies on modest spending cuts, an unusual borrowing scheme and increased aid from the state and Federal governments that many fiscal experts consider doubtful.
The proposals are the heart of his plan to close a $2.1 billion budget gap for the fiscal year beginning July 1. Over all, the Mayor proposed spending $31.9 billion, a 4.9 percent increase over spending in the current fiscal year. 
 To close the gap, the Mayor counted on about $614 million in increased state and Federal aid and $296 million in additional city taxes subject to state approval. The city also plans to borrow $215 million from a bank by using unpaid property taxes as collateral. The Mayor defended the borrowing, but some fiscal experts said it was reminiscent of the maneuvering that brought on the fiscal crisis of the 1970's. [Page 24.]
 
Cuts in Services
 The Mayor also called for $623 million in savings in efficiencies and some spending cuts, including not replacing retiring workers in some agencies, contracting out a few city services like the collection of parking tickets and streamlining some operations. The $623 million includes about $96 million in service cuts.
The heaviest blow would fall on the Board of Education, where the Mayor has called on cutting through attrition 2,315 of the current 65,000 teachers positions -- even though enrollment is growing -- and 258 clerical positions to save about $155 million. To make up for the lost teachers, Mr. Dinkins proposed reducing preparation periods and increasing the size of some special tutoring classes. But these efficiencies must be negotiated with the teachers' union.
The remaining $387 million in the budget gap would be filled through a variety of administrative devices, including $200 million by refinancing bonds and stretching out payments on some debt.
State fiscal monitors and budget experts said Mr. Dinkins's plan was unrealistic and would leave the city in an extremely vulnerable position should the state and Federal governments not come through.
"It's one of the riskiest plans we've seen in years," said Allen J. Proctor, the executive director of the state Financial Control Board, which oversees city finances. "He's putting a tremendous amount of stake in the state and Federal governments. When will they know this legislation is dead and how much time will they have to react? The city's going to have $700 to $800 million that it will have to cut if it doesn't get this legislation."
City Council leaders also voiced skepticism about the plan, calling its reliance on increased state aid overly optimistic. Speaker Peter F. Vallone characterized the increased state aid as "place holders" that bought the Mayor some time before he had to outline real service cuts.
The Council Finance Committee chairman, Herbert E. Berman, predicted that Mr. Dinkins would get no additional help from Albany and that the administration would end up slashing services late in the spring, possibly through layoffs.
 
'Don't See Any Escape'
 "We yet will face the very real potential for the reduction of the work force," he said. "I don't see any escape from that as a reality."
Mr. Dinkins said he remained optimistic that the city would get at least some of the aid it is seeking from Albany and Washington, especially with a Democrat as President. He defended his budget as "progressive but prudent" and said, "The sacrifice and belt-tightening which characterized last year's budget must be replicated this year as well."
Specifically, Mr. Dinkins is asking the Legislature for a package of $296 million in additional tax revenue: an extension of a surcharge on personal-income taxes, an increase in the income tax on commuters, new sales taxes on alcohol and tobacco and a fuel tax. He is also asking the state to take over $169 million in Medicaid costs the city now pays and an increase of nearly $85 million in educational aid, an annual battle. Another $110 million would come from increases in other state aid.
State legislative leaders reacted coolly to Mr. Dinkins's tax proposals, pointing out that many of them died in the Legislature last year. They said he would face an uphill battle squeezing more educational aid and Medicaid money from the state in a year when the Legislature is trying to close a $3.6 billion gap.
Should the state fail to help, Mr. Dinkins has put forward in his plan a doomsday list of $168 million in contingency cuts that would crimp high-profile services. They include such measures as eliminating summer school programs, closing public pools, cutting day-care programs, increasing case loads for foster-care social workers, slashing a Staten Island Ferry at rush hour and delaying a police class for six months.
Coming eight months before Mr. Dinkins faces a re-election fight, the preliminary spending plan clearly reflected his desire to sew together the coalition of labor, minorities and liberals that elected him four years ago by avoiding deep service cuts or layoffs. The plan included a group of new initiatives worth about $25 million, ranging from community health clinics to lengthening library hours.
Under the Financial Control Law, the Mayor is required to present this financial plan to show that the city has a plan to keep its budget in balance. After Council hearings, he will submit his actual budget proposal.
Budget experts said it is to the Mayor's advantage to paint a bleak picture at this point in the five-month budget process to put pressure on the state, the Federal Government and the municipal unions to come up with more money for the city.
The Mayor's plan assumes a modest growth in the local economy of about 1.6 percent this year, though it takes into account that wages will probably grow faster than the real-estate market will rebound. As a result, the city is expecting some income and business taxes to rise about $245 million, even as property taxes fall about $388 million. Last year, the city produced a surplus of more than $500 million.
The plan also takes into account a 2 percent raise for most civilian city employees, which will cost an additional $428 million in the next fiscal year, the result of a settlement reached with a union representing 180,000 employees earlier this month.
Although most agencies were asked to shrink their city-financed staffs through attrition and early retirement, a few agencies will grow. Over all, the work force will grow to about 214,000 workers from 212,000 by the end of next year, fueled by the expansion of the Police Department and growing welfare rolls, which under state law automatically require increased numbers of social workers.
The plan takes advantage of a number of so-called one-shot revenues to close part of the budget shortfall, like $55 million from the sale of the Offtrack Betting Corporation and $45 million from the renegotiation of leases with the Port Authority at the city's airports. The administration is planning to dip into its general reserve fund to cover $110 million of operating expenses, leaving $40 million for emergencies, the plan said.
The City Council President, Andrew J. Stein, a Democrat who is planning to challenge the Mayor in the fall, charged that Mr. Dinkins was trying to balance the budget by punishing school children instead of forcing municipal unions to agree to greater concessions. "It's a flim-flam budget," he said. "The Mayor is aiming a knife at the heart of the city's children and teachers."
The Republican candidate, Rudolph W. Giuliani, questioned the wisdom of borrowing money against future tax revenues.
"It seems like a lot of questionable assumptions and one very big mistake that has impact on the fiscal stablility of the city: the borrowing," he said. "Then there is the problem of the administration's double talk. To describe this loan as anything else but a loan is irresponsible."

The proposal includes the borrowing of $215 million from Chemical Securities, an investment bank, using unpaid city property taxes as collateral. (Ruby Washington/The New York Times)(pg. 24)

HEADLINE: THE GAY TROOP ISSUE;
CLINTON ACCEPTS DELAY IN LIFTING MILITARY GAY BAN

BYLINE: By GWEN IFILL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 29

 BODY:
Giving ground to his political opponents, President Clinton authorized the Pentagon today to continue its ban on homosexuals for six months with little practical change while the Defense Department drafts an executive order to lift the prohibition.
After days of negotiation and rancor in the military and Congress, Mr. Clinton temporarily suspended the formal discharge of homosexuals from the military and announced that new recruits would no longer be asked if they are homosexuals. He ordered the Pentagon to produce an order for him to sign by July 15.
 
Victory for Senator Nunn
 In the meantime, the military will continue discharge proceedings against avowed homosexuals or those it believes to be gay. While acknowledged homosexuals will not actually be ousted from the service, Mr. Clinton was forced to agree to a plan that will place them in the unpaid standby reserves -- in effect, putting their military careers in limbo -- and require that they petition for reinstatement if the ban is permanently lifted. 
 Mr. Clinton had wanted to halt all proceedings against homosexuals for the six months. The compromise was a victory for Senator Sam Nunn of Georgia, the chairman of the Senate Armed Services Committee, who wanted to make clear that avowed homosexuals were still not welcome in the military.
But the compromise appeared to have averted an immediate move in Congress to write the ban into law by attaching it to a family and medical leave bill that is scheduled for a vote next week. The President wants that bill and others to be passed as soon as possible to show that the Democrats have broken the gridlock in Congress.
 
'Step in the Right Direction'
 Mr. Clinton called his announcement today a "dramatic step forward"; it represented the first time a President had taken steps to aid gay men and lesbians in the armed services. But he acknowledged that he had yielded on important issues. [Excerpts, page 8.]
"This compromise is not everything I would have hoped for, or everything that I have stood for, but it is plainly a substantial step in the right direction," Mr. Clinton said during the first news conference of his Presidency, which was entirely devoted to the question of homosexuals in the military and not the economic issues that Mr. Clinton has sought to emphasize.
He said he was aware that Congress might still override any executive order he signed that would allow homosexuals in the military to declare their sexual orientation. "The issue is not whether there should be homosexuals in the military," he said. "Everyone concedes that there are. The issue is whether men and women who can and have served with real distinction should be excluded from military service solely on the basis of their status. And I believe they should not."
Saying he had not "given up on my real goals," Mr. Clinton added, "Normally, in the history of civil rights, the advancements of Presidents have not necessarily been in the forefront in the beginning."
Aides to Mr. Clinton said they expected the issues surrounding homosexuals in the armed forces to die down only temporarily as both sides fight the issue out in the Senate hearings that Mr. Nunn will hold in the next few months.
 
No Real Change in Policy
 Under the agreement, openly gay men and lesbians who are now in the process of being discharged will not be thrown out of the military. But aides to Senator Nunn emphasized that there would be no real change in current policy, because standby reserve status is not far different from discharge.
They will still be separated from service and lose privileges and benefits and could hope to serve again only if the executive order to lift the ban is unchallenged in Congress.
Mr. Clinton indicated that he still planned to sign that order in July and would be prepared to use his veto if Congress sent him a bill specifically intended to overturn such an order. He left unclear whether he would veto a larger bill that he might want if a ban on homosexuals was attached.
Military officials said they were satisfied with the agreement, and looked forward to further discussions in the next six months.
"The Chiefs felt that they had a good opportunity to discuss this with the President and we will support him in this effort," Gen. Colin L. Powell, Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff, told reporters at the Pentagon today.
But Senator Bob Dole, the Senate Minority leader, called the move "a big blunder on the White House's part."
"They never talked with anyone on the Republican side," he said on the ABC News program "Nightline."
Representatives of gay groups said they were happy to have gained even the appearance of a victory today. Instead of asking military applicants about their sexual orientation, recruiters would brief them on sexual conduct, explaining what is considered unacceptable in either homosexual or heterosexual activity.
But at military bases around the nation, like Mayport Naval Station in Jacksonville, Fla., initial reaction to the President's announcement was largely negative. Military personnel said relaxing restrictions on homosexuals would lower morale, raise administrative costs and create a host of practical problems.
"What are they going to do, create special barracks for gays, the same way as they do for men and women?" asked Chad Nuss, a sailor aboard the carrier Saratoga. "I'm totally against it."
But others on active duty said they would learn to adjust to the new situation. "We're just standing by," said Johnny Stewart, who serves aboard the destroyer Spruance. "Whatever the President decides, we'll just have to abide by it."
 
Conduct vs. Status
 Senator George J. Mitchell of Maine, the majority leader, said the negotiations leading to the compromise had established the core issue at stake for President Clinton: "the distinction between disciplinary action taken on the basis of conduct as opposed to disciplinary action taken on the basis of status."
He added: "That is, what a person does as opposed to what a person is. From the President's standpoint, this directive establishes that principle, and it is very important that everybody understand that."
But Mr. Nunn and his supporters insisted that they had prevailed in their effort to prevent the possibility that large numbers of men and woman in the armed services would declare their homosexuality during the interim period.
"If there is one thing I've learned on military matters in my 20 years of serving in the United States Senate and working with the military virtually every day, it is that our armed forces function well if we respect and support their basic requirements for cohesion and effectiveness," Mr. Nunn said at a news conference today.
"Resolving this conflict between individual rights and the basic needs of our military is always difficult," he continued. "But our nation has had an effective military because we have achieved an acceptable balance over the years. The balance must be maintained."
 
Judge Helps Clinton
 The President said his hand had been strengthened by a Federal judge in California, who ruled on Thursday that excluding homosexuals from the armed services is unconstitutional.
"It makes the practical point I have been making all along," Mr. Clinton said of the decision. "Which is that there is a not insignificant chance that this matter would ultimately be resolved in the courts in a way that would open admission into the military, without the opportunity to deal with this whole range of practical issues which everyone who's ever thought about it, or talked it through, concedes are there."
No decision has been made about whether the Justice Department would appeal the decision, which applies specifically to the case of a Navy officer who had already been discharged for admitting his homosexuality on national television.
George Stephanopoulos, the White House communications director, said that today's agreement would affect few people but that it would buy time for the Administration to win support in Congress for the eventual lifting of the ban.
"This is going to apply to a very, very small number of people," he said, noting that many homosexuals would probably not risk action, even in the interim period, by declaring their sexual orientation before the ban is lifted. "You're talking about situations which are solely based on status, where it's acknowledged. I would expect that it just wouldn't apply in that many cases."
With today's action, the White House also clearly hoped to allow Mr. Clinton to concentrate on economic issues. "The timing of this whole issue was clearly forced by the people in the Senate who are opposed to any change of the policy no matter what the facts are, and I think that was their right to do," Mr. Clinton said. "But they controlled the timing of this, not me."
During the Presidential campaign, Mr. Clinton said on several occasions that he would sign an executive order to lift the ban if he were elected. Although the issue did not get much attention during the campaign, General Powell objected strenuously to the plan in a post-election meeting with Mr. Clinton and subsequently led the military's opposition to it.
Mr. Clinton's aides said that although they had expected that the policy reversal would stir opposition -- especially among Republican leaders in the Senate -- they did not expect it to come as ferociously and from as many quarters as it did.
 
WHAT WILL CHANGE FOR NOW
Questions about sexual orientation will be removed from the induction form
Homosexuality will no longer be grounds for outright discharge from the military
Court proceedings against those discharged for being homosexual will be delayed
Rules on sexual conduct will be explained to recruits
The Pentagon will draft an executive order banning discrimination against homosexuals
 
WHAT REMAINS UNCHANGED
Serveice members who are gay remain subject to discharge proceedings. Homosexuals can still be removed from active duty
However, those who have reached the point of discharge will be transferred instead to standby reserve, where they will receive no pay and no benefits.

HEADLINE: Durable Goods Orders Rise 9.1% In Sign of Manufacturing Growth

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 29

 BODY:
In what economists took as a clear sign of growing momentum in manufacturing, the Commerce Department reported today that new orders for durable goods jumped 9.1 percent in December, the biggest gain in 17 months.
The surprisingly robust performance, which was broad-based, lifted orders for long-lasting goods to $134.5 billion, the highest level in four years. 
 Such orders for items that are designed to last at least three years -- ranging from motorcycles to jets -- are widely watched as an indicator of future economic activity. Orders are the first step in a process that generally leads to buying materials, hiring and transporting finished products.
The consumer-goods sector has been strong since the summer, but factories have so far experienced only a modest -- and more recent -- rebound.
 
'Falling Into Place'
 "Everything seems to be falling into place," said Michael P. Niemira of the Mitsubishi Bank. The December orders may be somewhat overstated, he added, "but the themes are real. Something's going on."
Separately, the Commerce Department reported that disposable personal income climbed 1 percent last month while personal spending rose five-tenths of 1 percent. But incomes were swollen by year-end bonuses, and without them Americans again spent a bit more than they earned.
The report on durable goods included increases in shipments and in unfilled orders as well as in new orders, a three-sided improvement that has been rare in recent years.
"Every single part of the report was strong," said James F. O'Sullivan, an economist at J. P. Morgan. "The economy certainly looks better lately."
The report on durable goods orders tends to be among the most erratic of the Government's main statistical reports, partly because big-ticket items like planes and weapons are often bunched together. Moreover, today's report was only an estimate and is subject to revision.
As a result, analysts cautioned that the December gains were almost certainly too large to be sustained and that other data showed that employers were reluctant to hire.
Lagging growth in jobs and incomes has been a drag on the economy even as it posted seven straight quarters of expansion. Growth in the last two quarters averaged a solid 3.6 percent at an annual rate.
In December, the Labor Department had reported earlier, American factories added 3,000 jobs while the average work week rose for the third month to a very high 41.3 hours.
 
Investment Tax Credit
 Analysts said the December orders might have benefited from the Government's clarification on the tax treatment that capital goods were likely to get. But this effect was thought to be small.
Some businesses had been withholding commitments for items expected to qualify for an investment tax credit that the Clinton Administration has discussed. But some of this uncertainty was removed last month when the Government said that any such credit would be retroactive to Dec. 3.
Analysts, despite their caution, said they could not help but be impressed with the report released today, which exceeded almost all expectations.
There was a 6.4 percent advance, for example, in new orders for capital goods other than aircraft and military equipment, a tabulation widely followed as a guide to basic factory activity. When transportation items of all kinds were excluded, the December gain was 5.5 percent.
Moreover, increases were posted in all the leading groups except furniture and fixtures. Orders for transportation equipment soared 20.8 percent, to $35.2 billion, after a 10.5 percent decline in November.
Electronic- and electrical-equipment orders climbed 6 percent, to $19.3 billion, mainly because of communications equipment; orders for primary metals jumped 9 percent, to $11.7 billion.
Orders for industrial machinery and equipment rose 3 percent, to $22.2 billion, the report also showed.
In capital-goods industries, military orders advanced 14.3 percent, while nonmilitary goods, including aircraft, moved ahead 17.9 percent.
The report also showed that shipments of durable goods rose 3.7 percent last month, to $132.8 billion, after a 2.3 percent rise in November. Shipments increased for all leading groups except furniture and fixtures.
Unfilled orders rose four-tenths of a percent, to $450.2 billion, a tiny increase but only the second gain in 16 months. Backlogs of transportation equipment slipped to their lowest level since September 1989, however, while industrial machinery and equipment eased to the lowest level since July 1987.
A 1 percent rise in personal incomes last month helped to raise earnings for the year by 4.7 percent. But the Commerce Department reported that the December increase would have been just three-tenths of a percent without accelerated bonus payments in the securities industry, subsidies to farm proprietors, bonuses in the auto industry and retroactive Social Security benefits.
The 1 percent rise in disposable incomes -- incomes after taxes -- followed a drop of two-tenths of a percent a month earlier.
The savings rate in December -- savings as a percentage of disposable income -- was 4.7 percent, up from 4.2 percent in November. Consumer spending rose 5.3 percent for the year, and prices rose 2.9 percent.

(Source: Agriculture Department); "Personal Income" shows total personal income in trillions of dollars from July 1991 to Dec. 1992. "Durable Goods Orders" shows manufacturer's total new orders for durable goods. (Source: Commerce Department)(pg. 44)

HEADLINE: Washington Memo;
Housing Secretary Is Suddenly Assertive on Aiding Homeless

BYLINE: By JASON DePARLE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 29

 BODY:
In his first full week on the job, Housing Secretary Henry G. Cisneros declared homelessness "a highest priority," began drafting an eight-point policy plan and spent an evening touring a shelter and the streets.
"There will be fewer homeless people on the streets when we are finished -- that's a promise," he said today, following Thursday's tour of grief and grates. 
 But it was only two weeks ago when Mr. Cisneros, appearing before a Senate panel, struck a much more cautious tone. During those confirmation hearings, he offered few specific solutions and seemed to be laying a groundwork for dampened expectations. "We cannot promise today that we will meet all of those needs," he told the senators, and talked instead of simply reversing the direction of the disturbing trends.
 
Uncertainty Over Real Policy
 The question is, which Cisneros speaks for the Clinton Administration? Is it the cautious Secretary who, like the President who appointed him, had little to say about the problem? Or the poet of the poor who emerged from the streets this week to say of the homeless:
"Their faces were gentle, their eyes were kind; their language was soft. They had suffered in their lives, it was clear."
Leaders of advocacy groups, and members of the transition itself, admit to being a bit confused.
"I'm more optimistic than I was during the campaign," said Fred Karnas, director of the National Coalition for the Homeless. "This is a great first step, but the jury's still out."
The confusion begins not with Mr. Cisneros, but with Mr. Clinton. In his eagerness to court the middle class, Mr. Clinton spent little time talking about the poor and virtually none discussing homelessness.
Advocacy groups crossed their fingers and hoped Mr. Clinton's silence was more a matter of political expediency than a signal of his intentions. "We understood the political realities," said Mr. Karnas.
From the advocates' perspective, things picked up a bit after the election. The transition team invited representatives from about 30 groups to Little Rock, Ark., for a Dec. 7 meeting, which they viewed as an encouraging sign, even though the session was run by the transition's political outreach section, not its policy planners.
And Mr. Clinton then appointed an advocate of the homeless, Andrew Cuomo, to oversee the housing department's transition. Mr. Cuomo, the son of Gov. Mario M. Cuomo of New York, runs H.E.L.P., a nonprofit housing group in New York City, and served as chairman of a homelessness study group for Mayor David N. Dinkins.
An Administration official said today that Mr. Cisneros had chosen Mr. Cuomo to serve as an assistant housing secretary, in charge of homelessness. The appointment is awaiting White House approval.
Advocacy groups have been releasing their own wish lists of Government action on the homeless. And all of them are expensive.
This month, the National Coalition for the Homeless called on the Clinton Administration to guarantee housing to all those who need it.
Switching from advocate to realist, Mr. Karnas acknowledged this was unlikely to happen. "I'm not sure how much is going to be able to happen, even with our friends there, given the economic situation," he said.
Mr. Cisneros enjoys a reputation for being a quick study, and when he offered his account of Thursday's tour of shelters, one could hear a bit of the star student in his voice. When the tour ended, he said, he jotted down four observations "for myself about what we need to do."
He said the country needed to link housing and social services, to build more single-room occupancy homes, to distinguish between those who need shelter beds and those ready for permanent homes, and to find jobs for the large number of street people who say they want to work.
Like others who have studied the subject, Mr. Cisneros stressed that different parts of the homeless population had different needs. Perhaps a third require mental health treatment; another third need drug or alcoholism programs, and others are simply in need of housing and jobs.
Digressing in the middle of his formal outline, however, Mr. Cisneros seemed a bit astonished by what he had witnessed in the streets.
"An African-American woman, eight months pregnant, sleeping on the lawn of the Justice Department!" he said. "Five veterans, sleeping on a heat grate for warmth. Incredible things -- veterans!" Mr. Cisneros said some of the people he had met seemed "almost too gentle, too kind for the jostling competitive world."
 
Executive Order Planned
 Although he brought a reporter from The Washington Post along on the tour, Mr. Cisneros said, "This was not a showpiece thing; this was a learning thing."
After meeting with his staff today, Mr. Cisneros said he was recommending that an economic stimulus package include $100 million to $150 million for homeless programs that mix housing and social services.
He said he was drafting an executive order for President Clinton that would instruct the military to make surplus bases available for homeless groups. And he said he would seek a joint venture with Washington's Mayor, Sharon Pratt Kelly, to show members of Congress that programs for the homeless could work.
Mr. Cisneros said that anyone inferring lack of interest from his confirmation hearing was misinformed. "It has always been a priority," said Mr. Cisneros, who said he would have spoken more about homelessness had the Senate panel directed questions in that direction.
What does it all mean?
Like many advocates, Maria Foscarinis, director of the National Law Center on Homelessness and Poverty, was swaying between encouragement and skepticism. While she said she was "heartened" by the tour, she warned that oratory had its limits. "He's the H.U.D. Secretary," she said, "and he's got to take some specific action."

HEADLINE: Despite U.S., Yeltsin Backs Rocket Deal With India

BYLINE: By SANJOY HAZARIKA, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: NEW DELHI, Jan. 29

 BODY:
President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia declared today that despite opposition from the United States, Moscow remained committed to the supply of powerful rocket engines for India's space program.
"We do not plan to backtrack," Mr. Yeltsin said at a news conference here, adding that he would discuss the issue with President Clinton. Mr. Yeltsin said he hoped that the United States would respond with "common sense, sensibility" to the announcement. 
 The Russian leader, on his first visit to India, which was a close friend and strong supporter of the Soviet Union, also spoke of what he called Moscow's new "purposeful Eastern policy."
Mr. Yeltsin said he was "moving away from a pro-Western emphasis" and described his country as a "Euro-Asian power" with economic and cultural interests in this continent.
 
U.S. Banned Such Sales
 His strong support for India on the rocket deal signaled his opposition to the United States on the issue. The engines are powered by supercold liquids known as cryogenic fuels, which are difficult to master, rather than less efficient room-temperature ones.
Last year Washington ordered a two-year ban on the sale of sensitive space technology to Glavkosmos, the Russian Space Agency, and the Indian Space Research Organization, the Indian space agency, because of the contract. It was the first such action against either country.
[In Washington, a State Department official said the sanctions on the companies involved in both countries would remain unless the deal was canceled. The official added that what Mr. Yeltsin said on his visit to India was neither new nor unexpected.]
The United States said last year that a $250 million agreement signed in 1990 with Glavkosmos, then a Soviet space agency, violated the 1987 Missile Technology Control Regime.
The Missile Control Regime has been approved by the United States and other Western nations but without the sanction of international law that seeks to bar the proliferation of medium- and long-range missile technology capable of delivering nuclear warheads.
Both Russia and India say that the cyrogenic rocket technology transfer is aimed at developing giant rockets to place satellites into space and that the project is for peaceful purposes. But American officials say the technology could help India, which has an extensive medium-range missile program, develop long-range weapons.
 
Trying to Ease Differences
 During his two-day visit, Mr. Yeltsin signed a series of agreements with Indian leaders that sought to end differences on economic and military cooperation. A central issue over the last years has been the size of India's debt to Russia, which had been in dispute because of the rapidly falling value of the ruble.
Under the terms of the new settlement, India's debt has fallen from about $11 billion to about $8 billion, Shiv Mukherjee, an Indian spokesman, said.
As part of the economic package, India and Russia scrapped a 15-year agreement that permitted trading in rupees and rubles. Instead, they will now conduct trade in hard currency.
The rupee-ruble arrangement had developed as an integral part of Indian-Soviet relations and helped India, as a specially favored trading partner, pay for MIG fighter jets and other Soviet military equipment with its own currency, saving tens of billions of dollars in foreign exchange at the time. Moscow has been the largest sellers of arms to India.
India and Russia have agreed to increase military cooperation and develop joint military production projects. The supplies of spare parts and equipment, which had slowed in the last years, are to be hastened under this accord.
Mr. Yeltsin said the two sides were also negotiating the purchase of advanced jet trainer fighters for the Indian Air Force. India is seeking about 40 of the jets, and their estimated cost is $1 billion, or roughly one-seventh of the annual military budget. The British Prime Minister, John Major, who was in India recently, discussed British offers to sell Hawk trainer aircraft to India with officials here.
 
Won't 'Play India Card'
 In his wide-ranging remarks, Mr. Yeltsin also redefined relations with India, saying Moscow would not longer "play the India card" against China and the United States.
"The earlier relationship with India was seen in Moscow as a counterweight to the United States and China, that cannot be concealed," he said. He said the two nations renewed a Friendship Treaty on Thursday after dropping a vital security clause that said either side would come to the assistance of the other in the case of an armed conflict.
Mr. Yeltsin said the security clause was no longer relevant because Russia was interested only in a direct relationship with India and not with aligning with it to oppose a third country.
But Indian leaders were buoyed by Mr. Yeltsin's unequivocal support for New Delhi's position on Kashmir. The state of Jammu and Kashmir has been divided by wars between India and Pakistan, and both nations assert sovereignty over the area.
"The truth is on the side of India on this question," he said, adding that Russia supported this country's territorial integrity and unity.

HEADLINE: U.S. Backs Council Seats for Bonn and Tokyo

BYLINE: By PAUL LEWIS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: UNITED NATIONS, Jan. 29

 BODY:
The Clinton Administration has indicated that it favors allowing Germany and Japan to have permanent seats in the United Nations Security Council.
The Council has 15 members, but only 5 -- the United States, Russia, China, Britain and France -- have permanent seats, which allows each of them to block any action with a veto. 
 In recent years, Germany, the most powerful economic power in Europe, has said it felt entitled to a seat, as has Japan. So have third-world countries, arguing that the nonindustrialized world should also have a permanent voice. Until now, Washington has gone along with keeping the issue off the agenda, arguing that once the Council seating was up for change, it might open up a Pandora's box of requests.
 
A Sign from Christopher
 The sign that the Clinton Administration would support change came from Secretary of State Warren M. Christopher this week in Washington. In a question-and-answer session with State Department employees, he said that "during the campaign, President Clinton said that he could envisage the addition of Germany and Japan to the permanent members."
The Secretary of State also acknowledged "the complexity of that decision," saying, "I'm sure we've all been in organizations where when you begin to make changes, there are other people who feel that they are entitled to a seat at the table."
On Wednesday, Mr. Christopher's call for changes in the Security Council's composition drew a pained response from Britain's Foreign Secretary, Douglas Hurd, who said, "If it ain't broke, don't fix it."
While avoiding any direct criticism of Mr. Christopher, the British Foreign Secretary told a meeting of the Royal Institute of International Affairs in London that his American counterpart had recognized the practical difficulties of what he was proposing.
Giving permanent membership to Germany and Japan would need a change in the United Nations Charter and lead to pressure for similar status for other countries as well.
 
Privileged Status Resented
 In the last couple of years, resentment at Britain's and France's privileged position at the United Nations has increased sharply as their relative power in the world has diminished and the cold war's end enabled the Council to start functioning properly again.
At the same time, Britain and France have become increasingly sensitive about talk of changing the Council's membership, evidently feeling their own permanent seats are becoming harder to justify. But as permanent members, they can also veto the change in the United Nations Charter necessary to create new permanent members.
The current German and Japanese Governments have both made clear that they want a permanent Security Council seat in recognition of their economic power and their contributions to the United Nations budget, which are now larger than those of Britain, France and China.
But constitutional restrictions have prevented Germany from sending armed forces overseas to take part in United Nations peacekeeping operations. Although Japan faces similar restrictions, its Parliament has relaxed the ban and sent a small contingent to the operation in Cambodia.
Any American move to give new permanent seats to Germany and Japan alone would certainly run into sharp opposition from developing countries and probably from many other countries as well.
 
A Plan for 20 Seats
 A much discussed proposal, first put forward by Brazil, would create 5 new permanent seats but without a veto, raising the Council's membership to 20. Germany and Japan would take 2 of the seats, with the others going to three of the most populous developing countries, probably India, Nigeria and Brazil. But this proposal is unpopular with the developing countries' regional rivals. A refinement might provide for the 3 third-world permanent seats to be rotated annually among the biggest countries of each region.

The Economy
 New orders for durable goods jumped 9.1 percent in December, the biggest gain in 17 months and a clear sign of growing momentum in manufacturing. The surprisingly strong gains lifted orders for long-lasting products to the highest level in four years. [Page 1.]

HEADLINE: Judge Blocks California Cuts In Welfare to New Residents

BYLINE: Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SACRAMENTO, Calif. Jan. 29

 BODY:
A Federal district judge has issued a preliminary injunction barring California from carrying out a law intended to reduce welfare payments to people who have lived in the state less than a year.
The injunction, issued on Thursday by Judge David F. Levi, was hailed by advocates of the poor as sending a message to other states considering similar measures -- that while such actions might be a politically expedient way to deal with a budget crunch, they will not stand up to court challenge. 
 But supporters of the law said the decision would hurt the state's budget.
"This ruling plunges California deeper into debt, requiring more spending cuts on other programs next year, like education and preventative programs for children," said Eloise Anderson, director of the California Department of Social Services.
 
Would Have Saved $22 Million
 Under the law, welfare recipients who had lived in California less than 12 months and had applied for public assistance after Dec. 1, 1992, would have received welfare payments no higher than the payments they would have received in their home state.
For instance, a women with two children who had recently moved to California from Louisiana would have received only $190 a month, compared with $624 a month for a California resident. California has the nation's fourth highest schedule of payment, behind Alaska, Hawaii and Vermont.
About 50,000 welfare recipients in California have been in the state for less than a year. The Department of Social Services estimates that the residency requirement would have saved the state $22 million in the 1993 fiscal year.
New York has adopted a different schedule of benefits for new residents, and Wisconsin and Minnesota have received Federal approval to carry out similar measures. Mark Greenberg, a lawyer with the Center for Law and Social Policy in Washington, said other states had considered similar measures but had been deterred by questions of constitutionality and fears of lawsuits.
"This reinforces what many people believed: that such laws are unconstitutional," Mr. Greenberg said of the ruling.
In his ruling, Judge Levi wrote, "If the purpose of the measure is to deter migration by poor people into the state, and it appears that this may be the purpose, then the measure must be unconstitutional."
Judge Levi said the law restricted the right of citizens to travel and migrate freely. Quoting from a 1966 Supreme Court decision in the case of United States v. Guest, he wrote, "The right to migrate from one state to another occupies a position fundamental to the concept of our Federal union and has long been recognized as a basic right under the Constitution."
He added, "The state's interest in reducing welfare costs is not sufficient to justify the disparate treatment of this class of needy residents."
The residency requirement, which won bipartisan support, was part of an attempt to close a gap of $11 billion in the state's 1992 fiscal budget.
Mark Rosenbaum, a lawyer for the American Civil Liberties Union, which was part of a coalition that brought the suit, said, "This ruling stops dead in its track a crass attempt by Gov. Pete Wilson and the Legislature to shift responsibility for ineffective and short-sighted economic policies in California to poor women and children."
Advocates of the poor say the $22 million savings realized by the law would have represented a small portion of the $6 billion the state pays out each year to welfare recipients.
"Within the context of state welfare budgets, this functions more as a political statement," Mr. Greenberg said.
State officials said they were considering appealing the decision.

HEADLINE: Back to the Big Issue;
After 10 Rocky Days, Clinton Seems Eager To Concentrate on Economic Problems

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 29

 BODY:
After 10 days of hullabaloo about gay personnel in the military and the household help of his nominee for Attorney General, President Clinton now seems hungry to concentrate on the issue that got him elected: the economy.
This week's huge layoffs by large corporations are pushing him to do something to create jobs, while strong economic growth in the fourth quarter is pulling him not to do too much. But there is another factor -- probably the decisive one -- putting pressure on him to get under the hood and do something big about the economy -- and that is politics. 
 The political controversies of his first 10 days in office are pressing him to get the public's mind back on the issue that was the center of his campaign. In announcing an agreement today with Congressional Democrats and the Joint Chiefs of Staff about gay personnel in the military, Mr. Clinton seemed to struggle to shout, above the political din, that he is eager to turn the page and get on to the economy.
At a news conference, Mr. Clinton said the agreement would "allow us to move forward on other terribly important issues affecting far more Americans."
"My Administration came to this city with a mission," he said, "to bring critical issues of reform and renewal and economic revitalization to the public debate, issues that are central to the lives of all Americans,"
During the campaign, Clinton aides gloated in secret when news about layoffs undercut support for President George Bush. Although the recent layoffs by Boeing, Sears, Roebuck and other large companies happened on Mr. Clinton's watch, some of his aides are no doubt smiling in private that these layoffs are shifting the public's focus away from Mr. Clinton's political woes and toward the economic problems he inherited.
In a speech Thursday night, Treasury Secretary Lloyd Bentsen said, "We must reverse the slide that brings us headlines like Sears cutting 50,000 jobs, and I.B.M. taking out nearly 75,000 jobs."
The week was marked by television appearances, speeches and hints of new programs by members of the Administration's economics team. The White House even arranged a meeting between the President and Alan Greenspan, chairman of the Federal Reserve System, and made sure news photographers were there to record the event.
All this created murmurs in political circles that the Clinton team was struggling to do what it had done so well during the campaign -- focus on the economy and divert the public's attention from the negatives.
George Stephanopoulos, the White House communications director, Labor Secretary Robert B. Reich and Deputy Treasury Secretary Roger Altman all went public about the likelihood of a short-term plan to stimulate the economy.
Mr. Reich provided some news when he said the Administration would probably extend unemployment benefits and might develop a new training program for the high number of workers permanently laid off during the recent downturn.
"The continuing massive layoffs are evidence that although technically we may be in an economic recovery, we're not yet out of the woods," Mr. Reich said in an interview. "Until you get jobs back you don't have a genuine recovery. Creating jobs remains the President's No. 1 priority."
The last thing Mr. Clinton wants is an economy stumbling badly. His aides remember what happened in late 1991 when consumer confidence and economic growth tumbled after General Motors and other corporate giants announced huge layoffs.
 
A Need for 'Insurance'
 One top Government official said the recent layoffs underlined the need for a short-term stimulus plan "as insurance" that the economy was not going to take a new slide.
David A. Wyss, an economist with DRI/McGraw Hill, a consulting firm in Lexington, Mass., agreed, saying "the Administration wants to make sure that this recovery doesn't stall out the way it did last time."
Administration officials believe the 3.8 percent fourth-quarter growth rate the Commerce Department reported is not sustainable. They point to forecasts predicting growth of 2.5 percent or less in the first half of 1993, so low that it would not cut the 7.3 percent jobless rate.
While many economists consider those forecasts pessimistic, Administration officials also point to reports that consumer spending has grown considerably faster than personal income. This means consumer spending, which has fed recent growth, may have to stop to catch its breath.
Another concern for the Administration is that headlines about layoffs could cause consumer confidence to tumble, pulling spending and the whole economy with it. Policy makers say that if the President shows he is shoring up the economy, that should help consumer confidence.
Mr. Clinton's aides talked this week of a stimulus package that would push up the deficit by $15 billion to $25 billion this year. This money is supposed to create jobs through tax credits for business investment and spending on public works.
Several economists said the fairly strong fourth-quarter growth might make stimulus unnecessary, but Mr. Clinton's political troubles and the bad news about jobs seemed to argue otherwise.
"I think this week's round of layoffs cemented the $15 to $20 to $25 billion economic stimulus," said Allen Sinai, chief economist with the Boston Company.

HEADLINE: New York Lags on Meeting Pollution Standards

BYLINE: By SARAH LYALL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ALBANY, Jan. 28

 BODY:
After years of struggle, the Federal Clean Air Act was revised in 1990 with a grand purpose: to force all 50 states to meet new air pollution standards so strict that everything from the way people got to work to the way factories were designed would have to change.
But three years later, the struggle is far from over, as industry groups, environmentalists and consumers continue the battle in state legislatures around the country. Many states have been unable to comply with the first requirements of the law, and in New York, partisan squabbling and a recent court ruling have left state officials warning that the state could well fail to complete a plan altogether. 
 Two weeks ago, the Federal Environmental Protection Agency notified New York and 34 other states that they had failed to meet their first big deadline: the submission of a preliminary pollution-control plan that satisfied the E.P.A.'s requirements.
For New York, this means the state must within 18 months fashion a plan showing, among other things, how it intends to reduce pollution in the New York City region by at least 48 percent by the year 2007. If it fails to devise such a plan, it will automatically lose billions of dollars in Federal transportation funds or face a virtual halt to its economic development.
"It's a real threat that would have a very significant impact on the state," said Thomas C. Jorling, Commissioner of the State Department of Environmental Conservation, who is trying to prod the State Legislature into passing Gov. Mario M. Cuomo's clean air plan.
As New York wrestles with its troubles, experts say that the result is likely to reverberate through the Northeast. "New York has traditionally been out front on these air-quality issues," said Rich Kassel, a senior project attorney at the Natural Resources Defense Council, an environmental group. "To the extent that New York hasn't been able to put its package through, it's going to have a ripple effect through the rest of the region."
Amendments adopted in 1990 to the Clean Air Act, a law originally passed in 1970, were supposed to reduce air pollution drastically through changes in consumer and business behavior. The law mandated things like cleaner fuel, tighter controls on industry, more efficient cars and an emphasis on mass transit and car-pooling over driving alone, but set different goals for each state and left it to the states to develop the specifics of their programs.
 
Emission Reductions
 But the law is exceedingly intricate and complicated, and it has infuriated powerful constituencies in New York and elsewhere, making it hard for states to agree on a plan that would satisfy the E.P.A.
"The Clean Air Act will require emission reductions from every sector of the economy, and what we're seeing is various sectors -- be they automotive, utility, or petroleum -- all trying to minimize the pain that's being inflicted on them," said Michael J. Bradley, executive director of the Northeast States for Coordinated Air Use Management, an air quality consultant.
If New York cannot present an acceptable plan within 18 months, the law provides mandatory sanctions: either the loss of all the state's Federal transportation money (in New York's case, $1 billion a year); or a new regulation requiring that any new polluter, like a power plant or factory, be required to find some way to offset twice as much pollution as it generates. The provision, officials say, could effectively end new economic development in the state.
Six months after that deadline, the Federal Government is required to impose its own pollution-control plan on the recalcitrant states. "We take these sanctions very seriously for one simple reason: the administrator has no discretion," said Raymond W. Werner, chief of the state implementation plan section for the E.P.A.'s New York region. "We have to impose them."
 
More Smog?
 In New York City, recent data have suggested that one pollution problem, carbon monoxide, may be abating but that another problem, smog, is rising. Largely because of the city, New York State has one of the country's worst pollution problems. Yet several factors are conspiring against compliance with the law.
The first is legislative. Last year, Governor Cuomo proposed a plan that he said would allow the state to comply with the clean-air law. But the plan depended on the state's imposing new automobile standards -- known as the California low-emission vehicle standards -- that were even more stringent than the Federal ones. The proposal generated a huge uproar from car manufacturers, who warned that it could increase the price of a new car by as much as $1,000.
The debate over car standards so consumed the Legislature last year that they failed to reach an agreement on an overall plan.
To further confuse things, a Federal District judge last week ruled that the Governor's plan to switch to the California cars was illegal because it placed an undue burden on car manufacturers. Unless the state also agreed to switch to California's more stringent fuel requirements, the judge ruled, it could not impose the automobile standards.
The ruling delighted the Republicans and others who objected to the California standards, but left Governor Cuomo and other Democrats cold. The state is appealing the decision.
"The practical consequences of this ruling are beyond anyone's comprehension," said Assemblyman Richard L. Brodsky, a Scarsdale Democrat who is the chairman of the Assembly Environmental Conservation Committee. Mr. Brodsky warned that unless the state could switch to the cleaner cars, it would be forced to impose Draconian pollution controls on other polluters -- factories, power plants, and employers whose workers drive to work.
Lawmakers from the Governor's office and the Legislature all say they hope to work out an acceptable agreement this year, one that would enact required programs like a new inspection and maintenance program for cars, car-pooling programs for employers, and emissions controls on factories and power plants. But they are not even close, and the impasse has generated an unusual amount of finger-pointing and blame-shifting.
Commissioner Jorling of the Environmental Conservation Department charged that the Legislature does not appreciate the problem. "They're not capable of writing their own plan, and they care only about their specific constituent problems," he said. "We need to assure the Legislature how difficult a task New York State faces. This isn't a partisan issue or something we can choose to do or not do."
But the Senate Republicans, who often feud with Mr. Jorling on environmental matters, said there was no reason to worry. "Eighteen months is a lifetime in the Legislature," said Dominic Jacangelo, committee director for the State Senate Environmental Conservation Committee. "This is no big deal. We'll get it done, and we'll get it done quickly."
And the committee's chairman, State Senator Owen H. Johnson, a Republican from Babylon, L.I., said that Mr. Jorling was being arrogant and alarmist. "We can act this year, but not if someone is going to throw spitballs at auto manufacturers," he said. "I'm confident that Bill Clinton will not penalize New York State -- he will give us an extension if we need it."
Even the Assembly Democrats, who on many issues can be expected to stand behind Governor Cuomo, are divided because of upstate lawmakers' opposition to the California standards, and because of downstate lawmakers' concerns that three proposed incinerators in New York City, including one at the Brooklyn Navy Yard, would be exempt from some parts of the law.
Meanwhile, time is running out. And environmental groups who applauded the Clean Air Act amendments say that both Commissioner Jorling, and particularly the State Legislature, must get to work.
"The Clean Air Act is unbelievably complicated and there are not a large number of people who are really focused on this problem in the Legislature," said Larry Shapiro, a lawyer for the New York Public Interest Research Group. "I think that very large number of legislators are completely clueless when it comes to virtually any issue that is not directly related to some pet project in their district."

HEADLINE: World Trade Pact Now Seen At Least Several Months Off

BYLINE: By ROGER COHEN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: DAVOS, Switzerland, Jan. 29

 BODY:
Arthur Dunkel, the man overseeing crucial world trade talks, today ruled out the possibility of a global trade pact before March 2, the deadline for a procedure allowing rapid approval of an accord in Congress.
It was the first time that Mr. Dunkel, the director of the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade, had conceded that an agreement was impossible before this date. As a result, it appears inevitable that final approval of the so-called Uruguay Round of trade negotiations will be put off for several months at least. 
 "I am not here to tell you things that are unrealistic, and I have to say now that it will not be possible to reach a package acceptable to all parties by March 2," Mr. Dunkel told reporters at the World Economic Forum, an annual gathering of political and business leaders in this Swiss mountain resort.
 
New Snags Arise
 Encouraged by an accord on farm trade reached last November by the European Community and the United States, Mr. Dunkel had hoped to wrap up the GATT talks in the first two months of this year. But since then, French objections to the agricultural agreement, uncertainty over the intentions of the new Clinton Administration and the United States imposition this week of tariffs on foreign steel imports have stalled progress and undermined relations between Europe and Washington.
Under the so-called fast-track legislation, which covers agreements completed by March 2, a President is entitled to negotiate a trade agreement and submit it to Congress for expedited consideration with no amendment allowed. The authority has been considered essential by a succession of Presidents and foreign trade negotiators to prevent a second round of trade talks over amendments when an agreement reaches Congress.
President Clinton and his trade representative, Mickey Kantor, have not yet clarified whether, or for what period, they intend to seek a renewal or extension of the fast-track authority. Mr. Kantor is to meet Europe's top trade negotiator, Sir Leon Brittan, in Washington on Feb. 11.
A trade agreement is widely regarded as essential to revive the sagging world economy and head off looming protectionism in both the United States and Europe.
 
'The Great Stagnation'
Indeed, Mr. Dunkel's announcement came as Lester Thurow, a leading economist, declared at the Davos meeting that "what we have is not the Great Depression. It is the great stagnation." He added that he believed President Clinton would have to stem imports to achieve the domestic economic growth of 4 percent he seeks.
"You would call it protectionism; I would say it's isolation, but he would say it's common sense to get re-elected," said Mr. Thurow, the dean of the Sloan School of Management at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology.

HEADLINE: Canada Imposes Tariffs on Steel From U.S.

BYLINE: By CLYDE H. FARNSWORTH, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TORONTO, Jan. 29

 BODY:
The Canadian Government imposed provisional tariffs on steel from the United States today, two days after the United States placed steep import duties on Canadian steel.
Canadian officials insisted that the proximity of the decisions was coincidental, but they also left little doubt that their investigation of unfair trade by American steelmakers was a tit-for-tat reaction to similar moves by Washington in relation to Canadian steel imports. 
 Canada and the United States both took action against imported steel they say is sold in their markets at prices below what it fetches in the country of origin. The practice is known as dumping.
Canada's action, which placed tariffs ranging from 4.5 percent to 124.2 percent on American steel, was also an effort to bolster Canada's case to recognize the integrated nature of the North American steel market and help reduce the threat of further conflicts.
Canada's Minister of Trade, Michael Wilson, spoke out against "the counterproductive nature of both countries taking anti-dumping action against imports from each other." He said a steel pact between the nations might be able to "short-circuit the whole business."
The two countries are each other's biggest steel customers. American exports to Canada in 1991 at 1.7 million tons were a quarter of total exports of 6.3 million tons. Imports from Canada of 2.9 million tons the same year were nearly a fifth of the United States' total imports of 15.7 million tons in 1991.
In their enforcement actions, the two countries also acted against other suppliers engaged in what they say is unfair pricing. Canada placed tariffs on steel from Germany, France, Italy, New Zealand and Britain today in addition to the United States. The Commerce Department named Canada Wednesday along with 18 other countries.
Eleven American steelmakers were singled out for the punitive duties. Those found to be most seriously at fault were the Advance Steel Company, the Kasle Steel Corporation, the McLouth Steel Corporation and the Sharon Steel Corporation, which were all assessed duty increases of 124.2 percent. The Commerce Department's determination had set duties as high as 68 percent on steel imports from Canada.
Today's duties, on hot-rolled steel sheet used mostly in the auto industry, resulted from complaints filed in September by Stelco of Hamilton, Ontario, and Algoma Steel Inc., of Sault Ste. Marie, Ontario. American companies initiated their complaints against the Canadians June 30.
For the time being, the Clinton Administration appears to be rebuffing the Canadian efforts to obtain a two-way accord on steel. Marshall Casse, the American minister-counselor for economic affairs at the United States Embassy in Ottawa, said the United States preferred an agreement involving more nations.
"We think that's a better approach to improve the climate for trade in steel than the proposed bilateral, sectoral arrangement between the United States and Canada," he asserted.
Canada's main interest, trade officials said, is in restructuring the antidumping laws. "We've been in discussions with our counterparts in the U.S. on this, and we expect these discussions to continue," one Ottawa trade official commented.
Fred Telmer, chairman and chief executive of Stelco, Canada's biggest steel company, said, "Something has to be wrong with the rules when Canadian steelmakers and American steelmakers are both found to have similar dumping margins when selling in this integrated market."
As in the United States, a decision on permanent duties will be made in several months, but steelmakers must put up money immediately to cover potential liability from the additional duties. Another Government agency, the Canadian International Trade Tribunal, has to find that the domestic industry has been materially injured. The tribunal has 120 days to make its finding.

HEADLINE: Consumer-Confidence Level

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News

 BODY:
The University of Michigan's consumer sentiment index for January fell to a revised level of 89.3, from the originally reported 90, according to those with access to the survey, which is distributed to subscribers. In December, the consumer sentiment index was 91.
The university also reported that the final January index of consumer expectations dropped to 83.4 from 89.5 in December. The preliminary January index was 81.9. 
 The drop in the expectations index contrasts with a gain in the January index of current economic conditions, which rose to 98.6 from 93.4 in December. The preliminary index for January was 102.6.
The consumer sentiment index is 21.8 points above the January 1992 level of 67.5. The record high for the sentiment index is 97.9, which was established in January 1989. The low is 63.9, set in October 1990.

HEADLINE: From Port to 17 Fathoms, Views on Dredging Differ;
On the Docks Shipping Is the Issue, But at Sea They Worry About the Fishing Industry

BYLINE: By CHARLES STRUM, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: NEWARK, Jan. 29

 BODY:
Fish are partial to a varied seascape; a flat ocean floor holds little allure. But give them a mound, a slope, a canyon, and they will materialize in communities large enough to attract food they can eat, predators that can eat them and fishermen who glory in it all.
This is what has happened over the last 79 years at a federally managed disposal site called the Mud Dump, six miles east of Sandy Hook, N.J., and 10 miles south of the Rockaway peninsula. A mere spot on nautical charts, it has been the repository of uncounted tons of New York Harbor's dredged muck since World War I and is now in the middle of some of the richest fishing grounds on the East Coast.
 
Port Versus Fishermen
 It is also in the middle of a big fight about what to do with the removed sediment, or dredge spoils, from Newark Bay that are contaminated with the industrial poison dioxin and whether they will cause harm if placed in the habitat of scallops, crabs and other bottom feeders and the fish that graze nearby. 
 In many ways, it is a classic confrontation between economics and ecology, with all of the familiar questions about risk and how to manage it, with implications that may resonate in ports around the country.
The Port of New York and New Jersey maintains some of the most lucrative container-ship berths on the Atlantic, in a vast bayside warren called the Port Newark/ Elizabeth Marine Terminal. To insure the survival of almost 200,000 jobs and a $20 billion waterfront industry, it must dredge the berths annually. The environmental risks of the dredging, if any, are within acceptable limits set by the Government, say port officials, who have spent almost three years trying to address concerns about dioxin, a relatively new addition to the gallery of environmental villains. A permit issued Jan. 6 by the Army Corps of Engineers was blocked a week later by the Federal Environmental Protection Agency because of concern over the amount of spoils to be dredged.
The fishermen, and the environmentalists who support them, say a commercial and recreational fishing industry in the region, along with a large bluefin tuna business based in New England, are vulnerable as well. They see the risk of dioxin contamination as frightening and irreversible.
"The commercial-fishing industry isn't opposed to dredging and dumping dredge spoils in the ocean," said Jim Lovgren, whose trawler leaves Point Pleasant, N.J., dragging huge nets at depths of 200 feet to harvest whiting and ling. "We're an industry so we understand the needs of industry. But what's being overlooked and ignored is the money involved in the commercial and recreational fishing industry. They're dumping on another industry."
"Commercial and recreational fishing amounts to about $100 million a year in the region," said Stephen Sloan of the Association of Atlantic Charter Boats and Captains. "This is the best bluefish spot in the New York bight, if not the East Coast. Hundreds and hundreds of boats hit this spot on the weekends, from Belmar, Manasquan, Sandy Hook, Sheepshead Bay and Freeport. If they can find a way to do this, it will wreak havoc up and down the coast."
Mr. Sloan's concerns, and those of many other fishermen and environmentalists center on their fear of dioxin, both because of what science has said dioxin can do and because of what science acknowledges it still does not understand.
"This could be 'Silent Spring' incarnate," said Mr. Sloan, who also advises the National Marine Fisheries Association. "The substance they want to dump is highly toxic."
 
Toxic, but How Toxic?
 Scientists know that dioxin, a byproduct of herbicide manufacture, is readily absorbed in the food chain. But a Federal panel this fall said its review of studies of dioxin had indicated that it was most dangerous only to those who had prolonged exposure to it. The abandonment of an entire town -- Times Beach, Mo. -- a decade ago because of dioxin accumulation in the soil has since been called an overreaction.
And it is no longer clear that dioxin is, as scientists have proclaimed, the most toxic compound known to man. The latest data about its effects on people, fish and wildlife are still being evaluated by the E.P.A.
Thursday afternoon, as two days of meetings to revisit the issue came to a close at the E.P.A. regional headquarters in Manhattan, officials of labor, industry and the port met in a kind of pep rally at Berth 23 in Port Newark. Their mission, accomplished as a chilling breeze blew in off the bay, was to show solidarity with the longshoremen and other port workers whose jobs they feel are threatened by the E.P.A.'s delay.
"This isn't an issue of us against them," said Raymond L. Bramucci, the New Jersey Labor Commissioner. "It's an issue of public policy that has to come to a sensible resolution.
"It sounds terrible," he said of dioxin. "It may be terrible. But while we find this out, we have to keep this port open." He was joined by Stanley Brezenoff, the Port Authority's executive director; Barbara W. McConnell, the New Jersey Commerce Commissioner, and John Bowers, president of the Internatinal Longshoremen's Association, among others.
A spokeswoman for the E.P.A., Mary Breitenbach, said that there was no timetable for a decision on the dredging permit. She said the agency had a large amount of data to study and would probably have nothing to say on ocean dumping at least until the end of next week.
 
Cap's a Question, Too
 Besides concerns about dioxin's absorption in fish tissue, ecologists are also troubled by how the Mud Dump would be capped, and when. The Mud Dump is about a mile from a position known as 17 Fathoms, an area in which sports fishermen who come for bluefish, bonita, false albacore, blackfish and fluke are almost assured a good catch. Continual dumping of spoils and the sand caps that cover it have created a mound perhaps 45 feet high and two miles wide.
Despite assurances by the Corps of Engineers, fishermen believe that the sediment does not stay put. And they say the big December storm that caused widespread coastal flooding and severe damage churned up large amounts of the muck, spreading it around the ocean bottom.
The contaminated spoils would be capped with fresh sand, but ecologists say this might not happen until the dredging project ends. Port officials have said that 29 of the 62 berths would be dredged in a continuous operation over 40 days. The port will need to seek an additional permit each year.
"They can't cap till they finish dumping," said Bill Greaux of the Hi-Mar Striper Club, a fishing club. "So as they dump and send it through the water column, it'll hit the beaches when the tide turns."
Referring to New Jersey State Health Department guidelines on eating fish that may already be tainted by other commonly soluble chemicals, Mr. Greaux said, "What irks me is that we have one government agency telling us not to eat this stuff and another government agency dumping this stuff."

HEADLINE: For Some, Loan Proposal Calls Up Ugly Memories

BYLINE: By ALAN FINDER

 BODY:
For two decades, ever since New York City's questionable financing practices of the late 1960's and early 1970's nearly forced the city into bankruptcy, the idea of borrowing money to pay for operating expenses has been anathema at City Hall.
So dire were the consequences of avoiding the city's underlying problems by refinancing operating debts that any current budget proposal that smacks even remotely of the practice sets off alarm bells that can be heard as far away as Albany. They were ringing loudly yesterday. 
 A small but significant element of the financial plan that Mayor David N. Dinkins proposed yesterday calls for the city to borrow $215 million from an investment bank, using unpaid city property taxes as collateral. The proposal, a tiny element in a spending plan that totals $31.9 billion, immediately evoked concern among some fiscal experts and politicians.
 
Smoothing the Flow
 Senior mayoral aides called the loan from Chemical Securities, the public finance arm of Chemical Bank, a creative way of helping the city smooth its flow of tax revenue through a difficult time. They said the device is used often, in different forms, by other local governments and by private companies. An investment analyst and several fiscal experts agreed.
The bank, in a complex arrangement, would in effect lend the city the $215 million at about 5 percent interest, taking as collateral $400 million in unpaid property tax obligations. The city would then repay the money in the following fiscal year, the one that begins in July 1994, after the delinquent taxes have been collected.
Philip R. Michael, the city's budget director, described the transaction as an accounting maneuver. "It's not borrowing on your future," he said. "It's not borrowing on your credit card."
Mr. Michael said that, because property-tax delinquencies have increased during the recession, the loan was a way for the city to "smooth out our cash flow," to obtain earlier, via the loan, what the city will eventually receive from collecting the delinquent taxes.
"We are just getting an advance against our accounts receivable," he said. "It's an absolute lock that in that pool of $400 million in delinquencies, you'll get the $200 million. In fact, it's a lock that we'll get the $400 million. There's no risk."
"This is not going out there to borrow money," Mr. Michael added. "We already have the money owed to us. It's money that's already ours. We're just having it accelerated."
 
Seems Gimmicky to Some
 But some elected officials and fiscal experts did not share Mr. Michael's confidence in the arrangement. They said the proposal -- intended to help the city close a potential budget gap of $2.1 billion in the next fiscal year, which begins on July 1 -- seemed reminiscent of some of the discredited financial gimmickry of the 1970's.
Raymond D. Horton, the president of the Citizens Budget Commission, a nonprofit group that monitors the city's finances, said the loan was different in scale and in many particulars from the deficit financing that helped bring the city down 20 years ago. But Mr. Horton contended that the underlying principle was strikingly similar.
"It's just borrowing against the future," Mr. Horton said.
"This is small, it really is," he said. "Sometimes we're accused of being real purists on this issue. City people may say, 'Look, it's only $200 million and the state's not helping us and so we have to do things that we don't really want to do.' But it's really a repeat performance of what went on in the late 60's and early 70's. The complaint then was that nobody sounded the alarm."
 
A 'Disturbing Sign'
 Other fiscal experts expressed less criticism than did Mr. Horton, but many said they were still concerned about the strategy. The State Comptroller, Edward V. Regan, said in a prepared statement that the loan was a "disturbing sign" that the Dinkins administration was failing to get the city's long-term expenses in line with its long-term revenues.
Allen J. Proctor, the executive director of the State Financial Control Board, said that, while the loan might not technically be construed as deficit financing, he objected to it because it would let the city put off more difficult choices.
"The city is saying that it wants 1995 income in 1994, and it's paying 5 percent to do so," Mr. Proctor said.
"These kinds of things put off confronting the very difficult reality: some tax is going to have to be raised or some service is going to have to be eliminated," he said.
 
Criticism Was Expected
 Mr. Michael and his aides -- who said the idea of the loan had come from the investment bank and that there had been intense negotiations over the proposal in recent weeks -- clearly expected some criticism of the loan. Mr. Michael said, for example, that he had discussed it on Thursday with bond analysts at Moody's Investor Services.
The director of the public finance department at Moody's, Daniel Heimowitz, said yesterday that the practice of selling uncollected receipts was common in private industry and that, in various ways, other local governments do similar things, like selling off tax liens. Mr. Heimowitz said the city's proposal did not alarm him.
"Given the budget pressures, it is not inconsistent with some of the risks and pressures that the city is dealing with," he said.
"To us, it doesn't seem to be a tip of an iceberg or an opening up to something broader," Mr. Heimowitz said.
"The loan is very clearly identified against a very specific tax," he said. "It's a very specific receivable with a history of collection. It's a much more defined sort of advance of revenue than the kinds of practices that the city maintained in the early 70's. This is different."
Some fiscal experts predicted that as the city's financial picture alters during the next several months, the Mayor and his aides would probably drop the loan from their proposed budget.
In the complex politics of the budget, the city usually offers a grim snapshot of its finances in January. Then, after it has wrung from the State Legislature all that it can in March, the city revises its budget in April and May, generally showing an improved outlook. Last year the city acknowledged by late spring that it had accumulated a surplus of more than $500 million.
The Mayor and Mr. Michael say that such a large surplus is unlikely this year. But Mr. Michael, when pressed yesterday by a reporter who said that some people expect the surplus to grow this spring, smiled and said, "We hope they're right."
