HEADLINE: TELEVISION VIEW;
Stop the Spaceship, I Want to Get Off

BYLINE: By Gerald Jonas; Gerald Jonas writes the Science Fiction column for The New York Times Book Review.

 BODY:
THE LEAST WE CAN EXPECT OF science fiction, which focuses so assiduously on the future, is that it be up to date. According to this criterion, the science-fiction series that have proliferated on television this season -- "Star Trek: Deep Space Nine," "Space Rangers" and "Time Trax" -- fall short. Instead of representing an evolutionary step forward from the "Star Trek" era, which began 26 years ago with the first voyage of the starship Enterprise and is still going strong with "Star Trek: The Next Generation," the new shows prompt a sense of deja vu. Inside their futuristic packaging they offer familiar premises and plots mostly borrowed from past television shows and movies.
For all its public resolve to be grittier and sexier than its forebears, "Deep Space Nine," launched this month with a two-hour special (its regular time slot is 7 P.M. Sundays on Channel 11 in New York), comes across as no more than a "Star Trek" spinoff, set in a seedy 24th-century space station instead of a spotless starship. "Space Rangers," which began on CBS this month and is seen at 8 P.M. Wednesdays, offers 22d-century space cops in a higher-tech, lower-brow "Mission: Impossible" laced with secondhand "Aliens." "Babylon 5," a syndicated movie to be broadcast in late February, features yet another space station filled with touchy humans and nonhumans with funny faces and hairdos. "Time Trax, " a syndicated hourlong series (8 P.M. Wednesdays on Channel 9), apes a different model: by setting 22d-century villains loose in 1993 to be chased by a 22d-century policeman, it promises to be an interminable "Terminator." 
 You might think television would bring out the best in science fiction, whose essence is neither dialogue nor psychological insight but the pictures it awakens in the imagination. Of course, all good fiction asks the reader's cooperation in imagining a credible reality, a detailed, coherent world -- Proust's Paris, Joyce's Dublin, Faulkner's Mississippi -- in which characters can plausibly act and interact. But science fiction asks even more because the elaborate worlds it seeks to construct in the mind -- ecosystems, technologies, social structures -- are themselves imaginary.
In good science fiction, as in all good writing, it's important to get the details right. Science-fiction writers consult texts and experts in astrophysics, meteorology, plate tectonics, evolution, ecology and more to find scientifically defensible justifications for the worlds they want to write about: worlds with gravity a million times stronger than the earth's, worlds where space or time travel are commonplace. Some writers become so involved in these details that they have no energy left to worry about characters or plot, so they fill their ingenious intellectual constructs with stick figures. The best science fiction, however, creates new worlds that cast light on the people who live there -- people whose problems arouse sympathy despite their distance from us in time and space and biology.
In the hands of writers like Aldous Huxley, George Orwell, Arthur C. Clarke and Ursula Le Guin, and movie makers like Stanley Kubrick, science fiction can be a powerful stimulus to fresh thinking. It can restore to jaded sensibilities a sense of wonder about the unlimited potential of life in an infinitely large and various universe. Meanwhile, television continues to stumble over the essential first step: creating a background world. The problem is partly lack of money, partly lack of imagination.
To set a story in a plausible 23d-century New York or Paris or Dublin would require the construction of futuristic dwellings, streets, transportation, offices, theaters. With current technology and budgets, this is impossible for a weekly series. "Time Trax," which needs no special sets for present-day scenes, bypasses the problem but runs the risk of looking like every other cops-and-robbers shoot-'em-up. The solution most producers have chosen is to stage the bulk of the action on a permanent indoor set, with telescreens and transporter beams and holodecks to allow limited access to the outside world. Fortuitously, science fiction provides the ideal set: the controlled environment inside a spaceship or space station.
"Star Trek" was hardly the first series to discover this economical solution. In the early 50's, when television was live and special effects were minimal, "Captain Video" and "Tom Corbett, Space Cadet" sailed the space lanes, ridding the galaxy of assorted evildoers. Watching these shows placed a special strain on the audience, whose job was not so much to imagine a detailed world of the future as to ignore the obvious gaps in the world on screen. I remember one space captain trying to dodge a collision with the sun by giving a couple of hard turns to a steering wheel that might have been lifted from my father's Oldsmobile 88.
As the original "Star Trek" demonstrated, viewers will forgive almost any inadequacies of style if the characters, however wooden, are amusing enough and if the stories are stimulating. The pilot episode of "Deep Space Nine" had a thought-provoking scene in which Comdr. Benjamin Sisko (played by Avery Brooks, formerly the street-smart Hawk on "Spenser: For Hire") used baseball to explain to a species that lives in an eternal moment what it is like to experience time in a linear manner. At the least, with so many species rubbing noses in cramped quarters, the spaceship/space-station setting can be used to preach against ethnic prejudice, a staple of the original "Star Trek."
But the producers of the new shows have limited their horizons by repeating the "Star Trek" formula so slavishly. By adopting a military model, in which the main characters are bound to one another hierarchically, these shows lose the chance to explore other kinds of social organization. Even on the gentler programs, like "Star Trek: The Next Generation," the temptation to resolve conflict through force is always at hand.
The "Star Trek" universe is more than 300 years in the future, more than enough time for provocative social experiments to have emerged. What about a series set in a society that rotates leadership by gender? Or by lottery? Economies where transactions are measured in units of pleasure and pain instead of money? Such speculations, which animate much of the best science fiction, would not be out of place on television. What might the special-effects wizards be able to create in the way of universities, houses of worship, prisons, parliaments of tomorrow?
Rumor has it that breakthroughs in computer graphics will soon make it possible to match any kind of background illusion with live actors, so characters will be able to stroll through a city of the future as easily as they now dissolve into subatomic particles to be beamed through space. When those illusions become television reality, the only barrier to the creation of good science fiction on the small screen will be the imagination of the creators.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Rene Auberjonois, left, and Armin Shimerman in "Star Trek: Deep Space Nine," one of several new science-fiction series -- Cop shows in 22d-century disguise. (Kim Gottlieb-Walker/Paramount)

HEADLINE: Prague's Jewish Heritage

BYLINE: By RUTH ELLEN GRUBER; RUTH ELLEN GRUBER'S book, "Jewish Heritage Travel: A Guide to Central and Eastern Europe," was published last February by John Wiley & Sons.

 BODY:
THE rooftop of Prague's Jewish Town Hall is dominated by a large clock; on its face are Hebrew letters instead of numbers, and hands that run backward. The building itself, which has a bright pink, stylized Baroque facade and a cupola-topped tower, dates to the 16th century, but is still the center of community life for the 2,000 or so Jews who live in Prague today -- with a kosher restaurant on its ground floor, offices and function rooms upstairs and bulletin boards fluttering with notices of events.
Other historic buildings nearby, including several ancient synagogues, were incorporated into the Jewish Museum, originally founded in 1906, after the Holocaust. The buildings are exhibitions in themselves, but also house the museum's extraordinary collection of precious textiles, gold and silver ritual objects, manuscripts and artwork that bear breathtaking yet painful witness to the splendor of the Jewish past. The museum owes its unparalleled collection -- parts of which toured the United States in the 1980's as the "Precious Legacy" exhibition -- to a cruel twist of history. The bulk of the exhibits are treasures and artifacts looted by the Nazis from 153 destroyed Czech Jewish communities and brought to Prague for what they planned as a "museum of an extinct race." The city was home to more than 35,000 Jews at the outbreak of the war; most of them perished. 
 Under the Communist regime, the monuments in the old Jewish quarter were maintained by the state and became popular tourist attractions. Today, they attract more than a million visitors a year.
For centuries one of the most important Jewish centers of all Europe, Prague contains relics documenting almost the entire tumultuous sweep of Jewish history in central Europe -- from the first Jewish settlement in Prague, about 1,000 years ago, and the rise of the city as a renowned center of Jewish scholarship and culture at the end of the 16th century and beginning of the 17th.
ALTHOUGH Empress Maria Theresa briefly expelled Jews from Prague in the 1740's, conditions improved so much during the reign of her successor, Joseph II, that Jewish Town was renamed Josefov. In 1867 Jews won full equality and soon such Jewish writers as Franz Kafka, Max Brod and Franz Werfel became prominent in Prague's intellectual life.
Prague's major sights of Jewish interest are concentrated in the old Jewish Town, on the low-lying right bank of the Vltava River, near the Old Town Square off Parizska Street. The quaint neighborhood is high on historic atmosphere but looks very little as it did to the generations of Jews who lived here over the last 800 years. Sweeping urban renewal at the turn of the 20th century demolished the one-time dank warren of narrow alleys, passages and courtyards and replaced them with the planned streets and opulent if now somewhat dilapidated buildings, lavishly ornamented with sculptural reliefs and ironwork. But a handful of landmarks, like the Old Town Hall,, still evoke the historical character of the old ghetto.
The museum has no one main hall, but all the buildings housing individual exhibitions, as well as the Old Cemetery and the museum offices, are near the Old Town Hall, within a few steps of each other. Tickets may be purchased at the entrances of the various exhibitions.
I recommend starting with the Old Jewish Cemetery. I doubt if any Jewish relic anywhere outside Israel has captured the imagination the way this cemetery, founded at the beginning of the 15th century and in use until 1787, has. Here, some 12,000 gravestones are crowded together, tipped over, tilted, eroded and sunken into the earth in an evocative testament of the past. Over the centuries lack of space forced graves to be placed on top of each other, layer upon layer -- as many as 12 hummocky layers in all. Many of the jumbled tombstones feature exquisitely lettered Hebrew epitaphs or carved decorative motifs referring to names and professions or replete with Jewish symbolism: a tailor's scissors, for example, or hands raised in priestly blessing.
MANY famous personalities are buried here, including the mathematician, historian and astronomer David Gans (1541 to 1613), the Jewish Town Mayor Mordechai Maisel (1528 to 1601) and Rabbi Jehudah Ben Bezalel Loew (circa 1525 to 1609), celebrated in legends as the creator of the golem -- a soulless man of clay brought to life with a magic incantation. Visitors by the hundreds place pebbles on their tombs in remembrance or leave messages written on slips of paper asking intercession to bring good luck or blessing. Every possible surface of Rabbi Loew's massive tomb, which is built like a miniature house whose oversize endpieces have carved pillars, ledges and other decoration, is covered by pebbles and paper. The oldest identified gravestone, dating from 1439, is that of Chief Rabbi Avigdor Kara, who survived a bloody pogrom in 1398 and whose dramatic elegy on the tragedy is still recited in Prague on Yom Kippur: "Heavy fell our Lord's scorn on Golden Prague, the city of beauty we mourn."
Just next to the Old Cemetery is the pseudo-Romanesque mortuary, built in the early 20th century, housing the Jewish Museum's Holocaust exhibitions. Particularly poignant is the collection of crayon drawings -- of butterflies and flowers -- by children who were held in the wartime ghetto at Terezin, most of whom perished.
Also near the cemetery entrance is the 17th-century Klausel Synagogue, which until recently contained the museum's precious collection of Hebrew prints and manuscripts. It was reopened this summer as the site of special temporary exhibits after extensive renovation.
At the edge of the cemetery a block away, on Siroka Street, is the peak-roofed, bright orange-yellow Pinkas Synagogue, originally constructed in the early 15th century as the private prayer house of a wealthy family and even today set in a small courtyard and complex of buildings including dank subterranean rooms housing an ancient well and mikvah, or ritual bath. The synagogue's soaring Gothic arches and vaulted ceiling are highlighted with decorative frescoes and frame tall windows through which you can glimpse the eerie shapes of tombstones. After World War II, the synagogue was turned into a memorial to the 77,297 Czechoslovak Jews who were slain in the Holocaust: Their names were inscribed on the sanctuary walls. Unfortunately, they were removed during a lengthy restoration project, but they are being re-inscribed.
A block away, next to the Jewish Town Hall, is the Old-New Synagogue, which is said to be the oldest synagogue in use in Europe. (Two synagogues are currently in use in Prague. The other is the highly ornate, Moorish style 19th-century Jubilee synagogue on Jeruzalemska Street near the main train station.) Built around 1270, the Old-New Synagogue got its strange name after a still "newer" synagogue was erected. A tiny, Gothic structure, its floor is below street level and its steeply peaked roof is decorated with distinctive brick gables added in the 15th century. Inside the whispery, dimly lighted interior, twin naves are surmounted by Gothic vaulting. Seats run around the outer edge of the small sanctuary, all facing the bimah, where the cantor and those officiating are generally to be found during a service. Defining the bimah are central support pillars and encased by a decorative ironwork grille. This synagogue was already old (and apparently mysterious) in Rabbi Loew's day. Legend has it that the golem still sleeps, hidden by the rabbi, in the synagogue's attic.
Across Cervena Alley, the buttressed, 16th-century High Synagogue contains the exhibit of Jewish textiles -- everything from gold and silver stitched Torah mantles and colorful, richly embroidered curtains to prayer shawls and items of clothing, including a marvelous cape worn by the 16th-century visionary Schlomo Molcho. Around the corner on Maisel Street, the museum's collection of silver ritual objects is found in the newly refurbished sanctuary of Maisel Synagogue, originally built by Mordechai Maisel as a private prayer house in the 1590's. Here are displayed finely wrought Torah shields and decorations, pitchers and ritual wash basins, spice boxes, candlesticks, seven-branched menorahs and the like. The ornate, Moorish-style Spanish Synagogue, slightly farther afield across Parizska Street, is also part of the museum, but has long been closed.
A new museum opened in the Jewish Town district last year, an exhibit commemorating Franz Kafka, who lived most of his brief life in Prague. The museum is set up at 5 U Randice Street, where Kafka was born on in 1883. Under the Communists, Kafka's dark, psychological tales were virtually banned, but since the political changes two years ago he has been embraced as a local hero. The little museum illustrates Kafka's life, providing interesting insights into the sophisticated Jewish life in Prague in the early 20th century. On a nearby corner is a bust of the author.
THERE are numerous Jewish sights outside the old Jewish Town district, including former synagogues and historic cemeteries. Among the most interesting is the cemetery on Fibichova Street in eastern Prague's Zizkov district, which was opened in 1680 and was the main Jewish burial ground in Prague for a century following the closure of the Old Cemetery in 1787. It has many fine tombstones.
The vast New Jewish Cemetery on Nad Vodovodem Street in eastern Prague was laid out in 1890 to accommodate 100,000 graves and is still used by the local Jewish community. A seemingly endless silent expanse of elaborate tombstones shrouded in ivy and obscured by shrubs, saplings and undergrowth, it is a poignant sight: a powerful contrast to the Old Cemetery and a memorial to the Holocaust.
Signs at the gate point the way to the simple white stone marking the tomb of Kafka and his parents; nearby is a plaque commemorating the writer Max Brod. All around are tombstones bearing witness to the wealth and importance of the pre-war Jewish community: Many are sculpturally elaborate family tombs in styles ranging from neo-Gothic to Art Nouveau. Already, though, most are overgrown; many are tipped over or cracked. No descendants have been left to tend the graves. This vast, silent forest of lonely stones is a far cry from the well-maintained monuments of Jewish Town. But the story it tells is just as dramatic, just as important to witness.
 
 
 
VISITOR'S GUIDE TO THE CAPITAL
Getting There
 International airlines fly to Prague, and there is also regular train service from Vienna, Berlin and elsewhere. Americans do not need visas for the Czech Republic.
 
Accommodations
 It can be very difficult to find a hotel room in Prague, even in the off season, so book well in advance. Be prepared to pay first-class rates in a first-class hotel. The country dialing code is 42, the city code for Prague is 2.
The modern, American-style Inter-Continental Hotel (43-45 Curieovych, Prague 1; telephone 231-1812; room for two $275) is a few minutes' walk from the Old Jewish Cemetery and other sights in old Jewish Town.
Elsewhere, the Forum (4 Nusle Kongresova; 410-364; room for two $214) is modern and has good restaurants; the Palace (12 Panska Prague 1; 235-9394; room for two $305) is a famous old world hotel near Wenceslas Square. Budget-conscious visitors should look into the numerous new agencies renting out rooms and flats. One such agency is the Accommodation Service (Hastalske, Prague 1; 231-0202).
 
 
Dining
 Beside the kosher restaurant in the Jewish Town Hall, 18 Maiselova Street, which serves set price meals for $10 and up, there are several inexpensive places to eat in the immediate neighborhood. U Golema (8 Maiselova; 261-898) is an atmospheric tavern. U Stare Sinagogy, on Parizska Street next door to the Old-New Synagogue, is a simple but lively place.
It can be very difficult to get reservations at Prague's top restaurants. New, private restaurants, geared to foreigners and averaging $20 to $25 a person, are springing up as a free market economy takes hold. First-time visitors to Prague should try Obecni Dum (1090 Republiky; 231-1268) for the splendid Art Nouveau decoration, if not the cheap but unimaginative food.
 
Sites of Interest
 Jewish Museum, open daily except Saturday 9 to 5, as are most of the institutions associated with it. Tickets cost about $1.
Old-New Synagogue, Cervena Alley.
High Synagogue, Cervena Alley.
Old Cemetery, entrance from U Stareho Hrbitova Street.
Mortuary, U Stareho Hrbitova Street.
Klausel Synagogue, U Stareho Hrbitova Street.
Pinkas Synagogue, Siroka.
Maisel Synagogue, Maiselova.
Spanish Synagogue, corner of Dusni and Vezenska.
 
Information
 Jewish Museum office, 3 Jachymova; telephone 231-0681.
Jewish community office, Jewish Town Hall, 18 Maiselova; 231-8559.

HEADLINE: DOWN AND OUT

 BODY:
Kevin Phillips's article "Down and Out" (Jan. 10) is timely. The euphoria evident during this past Christmas shopping season bears out the hope the middle class has for the success of the campaign promises. But, of course, politics is one thing; government is another. Many things have happened since the campaign (Somalia, Bosnia, Iraq, etc.) to derail, or curtail, the amount of time and money President Clinton will be able to spend on his domestic programs.
Although I'm in the lowest of income-tax groups, I pay well over 35 percent of my income in Federal, State, City and FICA taxes (I'm self-employed), and I don't really expect this to go down, no matter what has been promised. Nor do I expect my income to return to what it was in the mid-1970's, when I could afford vacations and the theater. I've been doing without these for a long time. But if my standard of living were to go down further, I'd have to resort to food stamps. The jobs lost in my field (publishing) are not coming back, or, at least, not in the quantity or at the salaries they were 15 years ago. 
 The promises of politicians are evanescent. Too many unexpected problems crop up. We're in for the very long haul.
 
YVONNE R. FREUND
New York, N.Y.

SECTION: Section 10; Page 1; Column 2; Real Estate Desk

LENGTH: 2601 words

HEADLINE: COMMERCIAL PROPERTY: The Gulf ands Western Building;
Twisting in the Wind On Columbus Circle

BYLINE: By MERVYN ROTHSTEIN

 BODY:
FOR almost a quarter century, a 44-story black-and-silver-striped skyscraper known almost from the moment of its conception as the Gulf and Western Building has stood ungainly guard over Columbus Circle. Sneered at by architecture critics, the building from early in its life has been called structurally problematic and inappropriate for its site.
Now it sits mired in 15-month-long bankruptcy proceedings amid allegations of "nightmarish conditions" created by structural defects that cause the building to twist slowly in the wind and hazardously misaligned facade panels that an engineer hired by the owner says have the potential to blow off in a windstorm and endanger pedestrians below. 
 The bankruptcy itself is almost certain to bring about a change of ownership that real-estate specialists say could eventually lead to the rectangular-slab building's being stripped to its steel framework, or even demolished, perhaps to make way for a luxury hotel, a residential building or a combination of both directly across from Central Park.
The story of the building, completed in 1970 as the culmination of a decade-long renewal of the Columbus Circle area and as part of an attempt by New York to keep major businesses from leaving the city, is a saga of what can go wrong in the world of commercial real estate. The questions surrounding the building will also have an effect on Columbus Circle, which has been the focus of controversy over plans to replace the New York Coliseum building with an office tower called Columbus Center.
"The problem," said Albert Cleary, a director of Cushman and Wakefield, a major international real-estate concern, "is the building, not the site. If it were a different building, with that very attractive location and those views, any vacancy would be filled in a second -- or Paramount would be renewing."
Paramount Communications, the entertainment and publishing giant that used to be called Gulf and Western and whose companies include Paramount Pictures and Simon and Schuster, is the building's prime tenant. Its lease expires in April 1995. According to court papers, Paramount has indicated it does not plan to renew and has already begun to look elsewhere.
Jerry Sherman, a Paramount spokesman, said the company had retained Goldman, Sachs and the Georgetown Group and was "currently exploring the midtown area and studying our options as to space requirements."
Mr. Cleary said Paramount was also looking outside New York City.
The departure of Paramount Communications would leave about 85 percent of the building empty in a city where there is already a glut of unused commercial space -- a situation that should enable the company to strike a good deal elsewhere. The new owner of the Columbus Circle building, most likely its principal creditor, General Electric Pension Trust, would have difficulty attracting other concerns to a troubled building when there is so much other office space from which to choose.
The expected presence of asbestos, a problem in many 1960's buildings, and the high cost of repairing the building's major defects also add to the likelihood that the building might have to be rehabilitated or replaced, and increase the difficulty of finding someone willing to buy it.
General Electric Pension Trust is part of GE Investment, the investment arm of General Electric. A spokesman for the parent company, Jack Batty, would say only that "we are talking to the tenants about possible future courses of action and we intend to work with them; I can't say anything more about the future of the building."
The current owner, 15 Columbus Circle Associates, part of Winthrop Financial Associates, a large Boston-based real-estate company, said through a spokesman, Philip J. Brannigan Jr., that it would not comment on any issues involving the building while the bankruptcy proceedings were continuing.
The building's cloudy future adds even more uncertainty to the prospects for the Columbus Circle area in the difficult real estate market. Directly across Broadway sits the old New York Coliseum, the proposed site for a 2.1 million square-foot, multitowered office, apartment and retail complex called Columbus Center, to be built by Mortimer B. Zuckerman through the Boston Properties development concern.
BUT even though the last legal obstacle to the deal was removed last June and a closing on the $338.6-million purchase of the site could have taken place in December, no date has been set, with Boston Properties saying only that it is committed to the deal "as soon as it is economically feasible."
The arrival of economic feasibility, however, is not on the short-term horizon. Cushman & Wakefield reports that the vacancy rate for Class A office buildings in midtown Manhattan in the fourth quarter of 1992 was 16.3 percent, compared with 9.7 percent in 1986. "We're still operating in a negative demand area," Mr. Cleary said.
As for the old Gulf and Western Building, he added: "Everybody's been trying to sell it, but as far as I know there have been no takers."
"The speculation is that considering the site, a hotel would be the best use, with a residential building next best," he said. "But the biggest problem with the building has always been structural issues, and I don't know whether you can keep the building and upgrade it. The way the floors are designed you could probably turn it into a hotel, but structurally I don't know if the building makes sense. There have always been a lot of problems with the wind. Some people think you'd have to tear the building down."
James D. Kuhn, president and chief operating officer of Newmark and Company, a large New York real-estate concern, agreed that a hotel would be a natural for the site, as would a mixed-use building, part hotel and part residential, with condominiums on the upper floors. But he said that despite the structural problems, which had existed "from day one," and although "anything's possible," he did not think it likely that the building would be torn down.
"I'm not sure with today's zoning rules that you could build as much footage as you could in the past," he said.
What seems more achievable, he said, is "a gut rehabilitation, stripping the building down to the steel, reinforcing it and putting up a new curtain wall," with GE "most probably selling the building to somebody else and walking away, or co-redeveloping with somebody."
David W. Levinson, executive managing director of Edward S. Gordon, acknowledged that there had been talk in the industry about demolishing the building because of the huge structural problems, but he said doing so would be too costly. "It does need a substantial amount of work," he said, "but I think there's a way to salvage some part of the structure."
Mr. Levinson added that whether the building became a hotel or a residential building "was more a function of the timing," of how long it takes to do a feasibility study and make the necessary changes.
"Right now, we don't need an additional hotel in midtown," he said. "If when the time comes the office market is very soft, it could be used for residential. But the office market as a whole is tightening, and my expectation is it could come back as an office building again."
THE building is not the only Manhattan office tower to have trouble with the wind, but it is perhaps the one that has received the most notice. Its problem is an unhappy legacy of a change in architectural design that began in the 1950's, when architects started to replace the rigid steel frames of skyscrapers with lighter, more flexible frames of stronger steel. As a result, some of those buildings began swaying in the wind. The swaying occurs, engineers say, when walls, floors, stairwells and glass exteriors designed for more rigid buildings are put in ones with the flexible frames. The sway at Gulf and Western created cracks in the interior stairwells and core walls, and workers began complaining that the building's movement made them queasy.
Concern about the sway was a significant part of court papers filed in connection with the bankruptcy proceedings by the building's second largest tenant, BDO Seidman, an accounting and consulting firm. Lawyers for the firm said that conditions in the building were "nightmarish" and that the owner had "failed to take steps to remedy major structural and other problems."
"The entire skin of the building is defective and must be redone," the lawyers said. "This and the sway of the building causes the window seals to crack, resulting in leaks when it rains. The debtor also has failed to repair large cracks in the concrete of the building and there are problems with the building's exterior columns. The entire building is now surrounded by scaffolding which has remained long beyond any reasonable period of time."
In its response to the court, the owner called the assertions "nothing more than a blatant, last-minute attempt" by BDO Seidman "to avoid its legitimate obligations under leases with above-market rents, in order that it might capitalize on the current weak commercial real estate market in New York City by relocating to newer quarters at a more favorable rental rate."
Jerry Walsh, a spokesman for BDO Seidman, said the company would have no further comment. But 15 Columbus Circle Associates itself, in its original papers seeking Chapter 11 bankruptcy protection, acknowledged as significant liabilities "the alleged presence of asbestos and certain structural defects," such as "the twisting of the building in the wind, penetration of rain and gaps in the elevator shafts."
A report on the condition of the building's facade prepared after an inspection on Sept. 26, 1990, said it was in an unsafe condition. The report, prepared by an engineering concern for the building's owners, said that "there are many severely misaligned facade panels, particularly at the column bays and at the corners of the building," and urgently called for "immediate remedial measures" to "establish and maintain safe conditions."
The report noted that a protective sidewalk bridge, or sidewalk shed, was in place around the perimeter of the building but added that "if any facade panels were to detach from the building during a windstorm, they could be blown far beyond the sidewalk bridges."
More than two years after the filing of the report, no such remedial measures have apparently been taken, said Vahe Tiryakian, a spokesman for the city's Buildings Department. Violation notices were issued on Dec. 5, 1990, and on Aug. 5 and Oct. 20, 1992. Because no report had been submitted by the owner on any actions taken to repair the facade, a hearing was held Dec. 17 at the city's Administrative Control Board, Mr. Tiryakian said, and a $400 fine was imposed.
Mr. Tiryakian said that the severe windstorm in December had not caused any pieces of the facade to blow away, "so it doesn't look as if this material is about to fly off the building." But he added that the condition could be deteriorating, "and we will focus on getting remedial action under way at that facade."
The owner of the building and the land on which it sits, 15 Columbus Circle Associates, filed for bankruptcy in November 1991 after the General Electric Pension Trust, which holds the third and fourth mortgages on the building, began foreclosure action on the property. The owner of the building had obligations of $577,000 a month to the pension trust.
IN the proposed distribution of assets under the owner's Chapter 11 plan of reorganization, General Electric Pension Trust would receive the property in partial compensation for its total claim of $56 million. The case is being heard by Judge Tina L. Brozman in Federal Bankruptcy Court in Manhattan; the next hearing is due in mid-February.
Winthrop Financial Associates of Boston, of which 15 Columbus Circle Associates is a part, controls through its various entities -- which include the First Winthrop Corporation and 19 N.Y. Properties -- more than $6 billion worth of real estate in 42 states and Canada and employs about 1,900 people. First Winthrop bought the Gulf and Western Building in 1984 from the John D. and Catherine T. MacArthur Foundation as part of a $400 million deal involving 19 prime commercial properties in New York. Paramount Communications, the former Gulf and Western, has been the building's main tenant since construction was announced in 1967.
The building contains 649,000 rentable square feet, of which Paramount Communications occupies 542,039, or about 85 percent, including the second through fifth and 13th through 44th floors, as well as part of the concourse and subconcourse levels. Paramount's companies in the building include Paramount Pictures and parts of Simon and Schuster and Prentice-Hall. BDO Seidman leases about 12 percent of the rentable square footage, including the 6th through 8th and 10th through 12th floors. Its leases expire between 1995 and 2002.
The location that is now known as 15 Columbus Circle has never been particularly lucky. The site at the turn of the century of Durland's Riding Academy, one of the largest riding schools in the world, it was bought by the newspaper magnate William Randolph Hearst, who hoped to turn Columbus Circle into Hearst Plaza. He planned a 30-story American Circle Building, but because of steel shortages during World War I the building was limited to two stories when it went up in 1915. It remained until 1966, much of it occupied in its later years by radio station WINS. But it was best known as the home of the 55-foot-tall Coca-Cola thermometer sign, which provided the temperature and the next day's weather to a generation of New Yorkers and was taken down on Aug. 20, 1965.
In March 1965 plans were announced for a 45-story, $20 million office tower on the site. But in June 1966 the proposal was changed to a 35-story circular apartment tower perched atop a 10-story office-and-restaurant base shaped like the prow of a ship. It would be known as 1 Central Park West and would carry the highest rents of any Manhattan luxury residences: well over $100 a room a month.
The builders of the office-apartment tower, however, could not get the needed mortgage financing and sold the land. And on Oct. 26, 1967, Mayor John V. Lindsay announced plans for a 44-story, $20 million building that would be the home of a large industrial corporation that was consolidating its headquarters in New York City: Gulf and Western.
THOMAS E. STANLEY, head of the Dallas architectural firm that designed the marble, glass and aluminum-sheathed structure, said it would "provide a nice transition from Central Park." But when it opened in 1970, architecture critics generally disagreed, calling it too tall, the wrong shape for its site and completely out of context.
Adjacent to the building is a moviehouse, the Loews Columbus Circle (formerly the Loews Paramount). Robert Smerling, president of Loews Theaters, said he expected that it would remain despite the problems. The lease extends to April 1995, "with some options beyond that," Mr. Smerling said, "and though I don't think anybody has focused on what we're going to do then, it's been a successful theater for us and depending on the rent and the marketplace we expect to continue there."
As for the building itself, "It's all subject to what's going to happen to those tenants," said Mr. Cleary of Cushman and Wakefield. "If Paramount commits to someplace else, then you have something. With the tenants in possession, there's not much you can do."

HEADLINE: Perspectives: Mixed-Income Housing;
Finding a Way To Keep Building

BYLINE: By ALAN S. OSER

 BODY:
"PEOPLE have a perception that New York City is suffering worse than it is," says Stephen M. Ross, president of The Related Companies, a development, management and financial services firm based in Manhattan. "Actually, the rental market is strong."
When a developer says "people" he usually has lenders in mind. Mr. Ross has been busy of late trying to get lenders' perceptions to reflect his own experience lately in renting out apartments. He has succeeded sufficiently, it seems, to win a financial commitment for his next Manhattan project from a pension fund he declined to identify, a 180-unit residential building on the southeast corner of First Avenue and 84th Street. Groundbreaking is expected in late spring. 
 Eighty percent of the apartments will be market-rate, mostly bigger family-sized apartments. The rest will be for low-income people. An 80-20 income mix is characteristic of the buildings that use tax-exempt financing. The Related Companies, historically a user of low-income housing programs in its construction projects, comes to it naturally.
What is new in Manhattan is that conventional mortgage financing is so unavailable that builders who have not previously used governmental programs are beginning to turn to them. Much of what little new multifamily housing is forthcoming in New York City in the early 90's is likely to make use of government-backed bonds.
Related has other new projects close to a construction start in the region. The company has received a financial commitment for a large-scale mixed-income development known as The Ninth Square in downtown New Haven, a $100 million joint venture with a St. Louis real-estate company, McCormack Baron & Associates. Construction is to start in early March after six years of planning.
The site consists of four city blocks near the southeast side of the New Haven Green, diagonally across from the Yale campus. Yale is an investor in the project, as are the Connecticut Housing Finance Authority, the state's Departments of Housing and Economic Development, the Federal Department of Housing and Urban Development and the city of New Haven itself.
There will be 335 rental apartments, dispersed in one new nine-story building, a smaller five-story building and two sets of rehabilitated historic buildings. Beyond that there will be two parking decks for 644 cars and 49,000 square feet of retail space. The architects are Herbert Newman & Associates of New Haven and Smith Edwards Architects of Hartford.
And in the Strawberry Hill section of Stamford, Related expects to start construction in early spring on 263 rentals in a string of four-story elevator buildings on a site near St. Joseph's Hospital. The architect is BGA Associates of Dallas.
Mr. Ross, who is 52, is a tax lawyer who worked in investment banking on Wall Street after he came to New York from Michigan in the late 60's. He founded Related in 1972, specializing in syndicating the tax benefits available to investors under various Government low-income housing programs. Profits were used to support the company's own development work, primarily subsidized housing at the time. Related developed about 5,000 units of subsidized housing. Later it turned to commercial projects and conventionally financed housing.
"To be successful as a real-estate developer you must be in another business and have another source of income," Mr. Ross says. "You should not be forced to do deals. You will do a lot of bad deals that way."
IN retrospect he perhaps wishes he had not made certain deals in the mid-80's, when Related, like other real estate companies, expanded broadly across the country, usually in joint ventures with local development partners. In New England the regional company eventually closed down with a loss of nearly $40 million. The lenders were paid off on two market-rate condominiums and the undeveloped land where another was planned. Similarly, an operating company that had embarked on several commercial ventures in California was closed down, with substantial losses.
There were successes as well, including a million-square-foot office park in Westchester called The Centre at Purchase. And in Manhattan, Related bought a major interest in the 550,000-square-foot office building at 625 Madison Avenue, on the south blockfront from 58th to 59th Street, and rehabilitated it for new tenants, itself included.
One project that Related long tried to develop but never could was Riverwalk, a mixed-use extravaganza of 1,800 apartments on 30 acres along the East River from East 16th to 24th Street. Related was designated as developer in the early 80's by the administration of Mayor Edward I. Koch. Ultimately, with extraordinary environmental problems causing long delays and the market timing lost, the city's designation was withdrawn.
Last November, Related completed a restructuring as a limited partnership, with new investment capital. The new partners are Jorge M. Perez, who was a joint-venture partner in The Related Group of Florida, in Miami; Max Fisher, a Detroit financier, and the Moses Ginsberg family, long active in shipping and real estate in New York.
The Florida company has several active development ventures, both market-rate and subsidized, in the southeastern part of the state.
In Manhattan, Related's most recent project, the 522-unit Monterey on the northeast corner of 96th Street and Third Avenue, is a demonstration of the potential for mixing low-income units in a building essentially marketed to middle-income people.
IT has been successful even though total rents on the market-rate units averaged only $28 a square foot a year, Mr. Ross said. The rents were about $3 a square foot below projections. But the financing was tied to short-term interest rates, which fell at the same time rents were falling. At $28 a square foot a year, a typical one-bedroom apartment rents for about $1,500 a month.
A profile of the market-rate tenants indicates that they are representative of the young professional and executive class that is a key element of Manhattan's workforce. Providing decent housing for this class of tenants helps bolster the city's jobs base.
Close to 60 percent of these tenants are new arrivals to New York City, Related's rental office said. About 35 percent of the tenants work in banking or financial services, 20 percent in the medical profession and 20 percent in law. The rest are in assorted fields. And 70 percent of all the tenants are between the ages of 26 and 38.
The average income of single tenants is $68,000. When there is a second income-earner in the household, that person's income has averaged $43,000.
There is a fully equipped health club at the Monterey. The management reports that 396 of the 710 residents in the building have joined it. The fee for residents is $450 a year.
At the 84th Street site, the apartment mix will be more heavily weighted to larger apartments, which should bring in an older, upper-income tenancy in a location that has recently seen the development of another high-end rental, the Lucerne, at 350 East 79th Street, at First Avenue. STATE or city authorization to use tax-exempt bonds on a particular project is not alone sufficient to trigger new construction. The builder must be able to find a guarantor for the bonds so they will be salable in the marketplace. Although in the past Related has used pension funds or financial- services firms that specialize in credit enhancement, the expectation is that builders will increasingly be obliged to look to the Federal Housing Administration, an operating arm of the Department of Housing and Urban Development, to accept this role.
The extension of New York's Section 421a tax abatement program to 20 years from 10 years improved the prospects for financing of new housing starts in Manhattan. The Brodsky Organization, a builder of condominiums and market-rate rentals in the 80's, is expected to turn to bond financing for the first time at its Manhattan West project -- 1,000 units on West End Avenue between 61st and 64th Streets. It is expected to get under way this year.
Even Glenwood Management Corporation, the builder of the Lucerne and other fully market-rate rentals on the East Side for many years, is considering this form of financing for its next major project, a 270-unit rental at 92d Street and York Avenue, said Gary Jacob, executive vice president of the company.
"The standards for loans have gone from one extreme to the other," said Mr. Jacob. "They were liberal in the 80's. Now they're overly restrictive."
According to Alan H. Wiener, chairman of American Property Financing Inc., which assists builders in obtaining public financing, five or six development sites in Manhattan are candidates for it.

HEADLINE: Child Care Makes a Mark in Office Parks

BYLINE: By MARY McALEER VIZARD

 BODY:
IN 1989, when Sandra Beetner was looking to relocate her computer graphics company, she had a 5-month old girl. As a result, she viewed child care as an important amenity, not only for herself but for her employees, many of whom were also young parents.
"We were moving from Manhattan, and were used to lots of conveniences and services, like pick-up and delivery and printing services," she said. "I can't say that child care was the top priority, but its availablily unquestionably influenced my decision." 
 Genigraphics, the company of which Ms. Beetner is president, eventually leased 16,000 square feet at Enterprise Corporate Towers, in Shelton, Conn., one of the first corporate parks in the metropolitan area to have a child-care center. The 104-child center was built in 1988.
Since then, several other developers have decided that, to be competitive, they, too, will have to provide child care.
"About four years ago, we started questioning our tenants and our own employees, and we found out they had a real problem finding good child care," said Michael Grossman, vice president of leasing for the Robert Martin Company, the first developer in Westchester County to build on-site child-care centers at its corporate parks. The company now has four centers.
The first, opened in February 1990, is in the Cross Westchester Executive Park, in Elmsford. It now has others in the Mid Westchester Executive Park in Hawthorne and in the South Westchester Executive Park in Yonkers. Recently, it opened a second one in the Elmsford park at the request of medical companies in the area, since the park was already zoned for child care.
"It seems whether we like it or not, we're in the child-care business," Mr. Grossman said. "We plan to eventually have centers at every one of our properties." In addition to its three executive parks, the company owns two office buildings in Tarrytown and just under a million square feet of office space in downtown White Plains.
When Ms. Beetner was planning to relocate, she looked at some of Robert Martin's properties. "I remember she mentioned she had a baby," Mr. Grossman said. "That's about the time we decided that day care was a competitive necessity for us. I mean, when the president of the company has a baby, it's pretty clear that child care is going to be important to that firm."
The best evidence of the success of Robert Martin's strategy, according to Mr. Grossman, is that two of its centers now have waiting lists and the first one the company built, at the Elmhsford site, is now considering adding a kindergarten.
John Chapman, a vice president for customer services at Basys Automation Systems, has two children at the South Westchester center. "It really simplfies my life," he said. "The kids love it. It's a creative environment for them. And instead of traveling around, dropping them off and picking them up, they're in the building next door to me. It easily saves me a half hour each day."
ONE of the latest developers to take the plunge into child care is the Rockefeller Center Development Corporation, which is building a 7,000-square-foot, free-standing building at its International Trade Center, a 6.7 million square foot corporate park in Mount Olive, N.J. The 105-child center is scheduled to open in August.
More than 2,000 employees are expected to move into the park by next year. BASF, an international chemical company, is now completing construction of a 675,000-square-foot building, and eventually plans to build a total of 1.7 million square feet of office space.
The construction is part of a major consolidation of the company's northeast operations. "At total capacity, we could have 5,000 people at the site," said George Neumann, BASF's site director for the project.
Eugene A. Preston, director of marketing for the International Trade Center, said that having a child-care facility "is almost a necessity in marketing a big park nowadays, in order to attract the kinds of businesses that we want."
The developer is also considering adding a child care facility at its flagship property, Rockefeller Center in Manhattan. "To date, centers have been harder to do in an urban setting with space limitations and other restrictions," Mr. Preston said. He also explained the logistical difficulty of having parents commuting into the city by mass transit with a child in tow.
Few developers own or operate the centers they build. They usually hire outside companies specializing in child care, like Bright Horizons, which operates 52 corporate child-care centers on the East Coast.
Michael Day, development manager for Bright Horizons, said he's now negotiating with several other developers who are also planning child-care centers in corporate parks.
He attributed the flurry of activity in the metropolitan area to the current economic climate. "On the one hand, it's difficult to justify the expense, but on the other, developers are more competitive with each other," Mr. Daly said. "A lot of times, it's simply a matter of the tenants going to the existing landlord and making a request."
The child-care operator is often very involved in the design of the facility. For instance, for its free-standing structure, Rockefeller Development hired its architectectural firm, D.W. Arthur Associates Architects of Boston.
BUILDING a child-care facility is fraught with complications and expense. The building codes vary by state, but typically there are strict requirements, such as having at least 35 square feet of activity space per child, an outdoor play area and "lots of plumbing with miniature fixtures," Mr. Grossman said. "They're cute, but expensive." They must also have kitchens with commercial equipment, special air-conditioning and heating systems and indoor areas that are exposed to natural light.
After building a center, the developer then essentially subsidizes it by renting at a drastically reduced rate or, in some cases, without charge.
"I was more interested in getting the best quality people to run the center than getting the highest rent," said Robert D. Scinto, developer of Enterprise Corporate Towers.
For the Robert Martin Company, the effort has been worthwhile. "It's now a part of our package," said Mr. Grossman. "It's what differentiates us from the competition, and helps us maintain our high occupancy levels."
According to Mr. Grossman, all of Robert Martin's three parks with day-care facilities are now more than 95 percent occupied.
The developers who have entered the child-care business seem prepared to stay. "With two-income families the norm now, the need will continue to grow," said Mr. Preston. "I don't see that changing any time soon."
When his company first moved to Enterprise Towers a year and a half ago, Richard Schwartz wasn't sure how many employees would make use of the on-site center. "We have 150 people and almost 80 percent are female," said Mr. Schwartz, president of the northeast region of Prudential Relocation Management, which relocates corporate employees. "About eight people use it now," he said.
"But that number is increasing as people get used to the facility. And several of our newer employees have had babies since we moved."
For some developers, the pay-off in having child care is often more a matter of image than substance. "No one came here because of a day-care center," said Mr. Scinto. "But it's an important part of our image. It says we're family oriented and helps us be more than just a group of office buildings."
One of Mr. Scinto's tenants, Ms. Beetner, believes that having children around adds a human touch. Her office is adjacent to the center, connected by a walkway. "The kids come by trick-or-treating," she said. "They're not intrusive. It's just nice to see them."
She also believes it's helped her business as well. When her administrative assistant recently had a baby and "she was having trouble weaning herself from her baby," Ms. Beetner said, "the process was made so much easier by having the center nearby. I think it made the difference in her being able to come back at all."

HEADLINE: Lottery Winners Years Later

BYLINE: By ELISABETH GINSBURG

 BODY:
ON St. Patrick's Day in 1971, Edward Henry of Morristown became the first member of an elite club -- New Jersey lottery millionaires. Since then, the lottery has bestowed individual jackpots of $1 million or more on dozens of people.
Most winners spend their first hours riding high on a wave of euphoria and publicity. After that, anything goes. The windfall can become an asset or a liability or something in between, depending on the personality of the winner. 
 Dr. H. Roy Kaplan, an Asbury Park native who is now adjunct professor of sociology at the Florida Institute of Technology, studied early lottery winners (including many in New Jersey) in the 1970's and did follow-up studies in the 80's. He concluded that "open, extroverted" people generally adjusted better to becoming instant millionaires than more reticent types.
Extroverted or not, the first thing many winners acquire is an unlisted telephone number to prevent the inevitable calls from long-lost relatives, would-be investment advisers and the host of strangers who are -- or claim to be -- down on their luck. Some winners, like Mr. Henry, refuse to discuss their experiences publicly, even years afterward.
Donald Bakely, Evelyn Marie Adams and Pat Grace are New Jerseyans who became lottery millionaires in the 80's. Though other winners have left the Garden State in search of warmer climates or have requested unlisted phone numbers, they have stayed put and stayed accessible. All three were willing to step briefly back into the spotlight to describe what "normal life" is for an instant millionaire.

 
 
Co-Workers' Attitude Led A Winner to Quit His Job
 Mr. Bakely of Thorofare is an electrical engineer who won $4.2 million in 1982. He decided to retain his listing in the local telephone directory. "I like to talk to people," said Mr. Bakely, who confirmed that over the last 10 years he has received all kinds of solicitations.
Unwilling to turn away the genuinely needy, Mr. Bakely keeps a list of government and charitable organizations and makes referrals whenever he is approached. A few years ago, he received one especially memorable letter containing a plea for $2,000 and ending with a request that the money be sent via certified check or money order. "He wouldn't accept my personal check," Mr. Bakely said.
Strangers were not the only ones looking for handouts. An old friend who borrowed $2,000 was resentful when Mr. Bakely asked about repayment. "He didn't think a millionaire should be worried about such a small amount," Mr. Bakely said. "I feel badly about losing the money, but I feel worse about losing a friend."
The windfall also changed his relationship with his co-workers. "For the first six months after I won, they treated me like a celebrity, but after that it turned to jealousy," he said. Eventually, he quit his job.
Though his millions have caused Mr. Bakely more than a few headaches, there have been compensations. He bought an expensive new house and a luxury sports car. Living out a longtime fantasy, he put the car through its paces at a high-performance driving school. His 15-year-old daughter, Kathleen, attends a private school and will be able to afford any college she chooses when the time comes.
 
 
 
Time for Community Service
 Since his "retirement," Mr. Bakely has had another luxury -- free time. He donates many hours to community service projects and is active in his local Kiwanis Club.
Now more than halfway through his 20-year lottery stipend, Mr. Bakely is looking ahead to the day when the checks stop coming. His house will be paid for, and his investments should provide a steady income, though he admits that it will not be as much as he has received from the lottery.
He does not foresee a return to the working world. As a designer of nuclear power plants, he has watched the demand for his specialty diminish as his years away from it have increased. The lottery may well have saved him from needing that other kind of state stipend -- unemployment insurance.
Dr. Kaplan noted that Mr. Bakely was not alone in leaving his job. About three-quarters of the earliest lottery winners quit their jobs for one reason or another, he said. By the 1980's, more of the winners were younger and opted to continue working, though some used jackpot money to retrain themselves for more gratifying jobs. Dr. Kaplan also noted the tendency of many winners to buy themselves new homes.

 
 
A Two-Time Winner Loses Her Privacy
 Like most lottery winners, Ms. Adams of Point Pleasant Beach endured the publicity barrage that goes with winning a big jackpot. Unlike her fellow Pick-6 winners, Ms. Adams was thrown into the spotlight twice, as the first two-time winner of the New Jersey Lottery.
After winning $3.9 million in October 1985 and $1.4 million four months later, Ms. Adams found that she no longer had the privilege of privacy. "I was known," she said, "and I couldn't go anywhere without being recognized."
Ms. Adams, who was a convenience store clerk when she won for the first time, also received many requests for financial aid. "I gave a lot of money away at first," she said. "Now I am more selective."
Like Mr. Bakely, Ms. Adams found that some people to whom she lent money did not think it necessary to repay her. Within her own family, reactions to her extraordinary good fortune were mixed. "Most of my family was great," she said, "but a few of my relatives were angry because I had so much."
From the lottery's 1971 inception until she won, Ms. Adams spent $25 a week on tickets. Afterward, she increased her ticket expenditures to $100 a week.
 
Many Plans, Including Marriage
 After winning her second jackpot, the newly wealthy woman was full of plans for the future. She intended to marry her fiance, who owned the convenience store where she worked. She also hoped to use her lottery winnings to return to school and study music, with the goal of one day opening a music store.
Seven years have passed since the double windfall, and Ms. Adams no longer works in the convenience store. She married her fiance, and his store, which had become popular with ticket buyers, was sold.
For a short time after the sale of the store, neither Ms. Adams nor her husband, Edward, worked. He eventually became bored, and returned to work as an "independent inventory control specialist," she said.
She has deferred her dream of leaving Point Pleasant Beach and returning to school until her daughter, Tracy, graduates from high school next year. In the meantime, the two-time winner is in the process of supervising an extensive home remodeling project. She still buys an occasional lottery ticket.
Ms. Adams has attended reunions of former lottery winners, and she found that they all have the same stories about the joys and sorrows of becoming wealthy overnight. She is philosophical about her own experience. "It didn't change me," she said, "but it did change the people around me."

 
 
A Master's Degree And a New Career
 Mrs. Grace, who lives in Hunterdon County and won $2.8 million in 1986, also talked about the changes her good luck brought -- and did not bring -- to her family. "We stayed in the same town and shopped in the same stores," she said. Her son, Sean, who was in high school at the time, suddenly found himself more popular with some of his classmates
Mrs. Grace stressed the good things that her prize money brought. After winning, she was able to afford to get a master's degree in speech pathology. Now the lottery income allows her to practice her profession at a day-care center in East Orange where the pay is low but the work is emotionally satisfying.
Her husband, John, who was a semiretired golf professional in 1986, has been able to play "even more golf," she said. Sean Grace was able to attend Notre Dame instead of a less expensive college, and his parents live in a big new house they built for both enjoyment and investment purposes.
Mrs. Grace said the master's degree was not the only education she has received since winning the jackpot. After losing some money because of bad investments, she was forced to learn, she said, "the basics of sound investing."
The Graces' goal is to manage their money in such a way that will provide a comfortable income when their stipend runs out 13 years from now. Mrs. Grace said her winnings "don't seem like $2.8 million," mostly because of the sizable yearly tax bill. But, she said, "you can't complain when you have a six-figure income."
Like Ms. Adams, Mrs. Grace still buys lottery tickets. "I intend to be a double winner," she said.

HEADLINE: 10 Years of Making Ailing Children's Wishes Come True

BYLINE: >By JAY ROMANO

 BODY:
LAST September, Theresa Beauchamp of Union made a call to the Make-A-Wish Foundation in Elizabeth. Mrs. Beauchamp, who had just been told that her 3-year-old son had leukemia, called to find out how he could make a wish.
The person who answered said it was simple: put the boy on the phone.
"I want a tree house," said Christopher (Kiki) Beauchamp. 
 Two weeks later, Kiki had his tree house.
"Basically, no wish goes ungranted," said Norma C. Godin, executive director of the New Jersey chapter of the Make-A-Wish Foundation in Elizabeth. Over the years, the state chapter, which is part of a nationwide organization with 78 independent chapters, has granted hundreds of wishes for children, like Kiki, who have life-threatening illnesses.
Two weeks ago, as the chapter celebrated its 10th anniversary, officials assessed the challenges ahead as terminal illnesses, particularly AIDS, claim increasing numbers of young lives.
"Being part of Make-A-Wish puts everything else in perspective," said Richard C. Wischusen, president of the board of the New Jersey chapter. "You begin to realize that all the problems in your life don't add up to a fraction of what some people go through."
 
Requests Expected to Rise
 Mr. Wischusen, a lawyer in New Providence, said that over the last three years the chapter had granted more wishes than in all the preceding years combined. And, he said, the number of new wishes is expected to increase in 1993.
"More people are becoming aware of us," he said, "and it's my perception that the H.I.V. virus is adding to our numbers."
The wishes granted by the foundation range from modest to magnificent. The chapter's first wish was a simple request for a birthday card to be sent to a 7-year-old Union County boy with a terminal illness. In contrast, the chapter fulfilled a more elaborate request last year when it sent a seriously ill boy and 11 members of his family on a 14-day trip to Israel.
"It's not the kind of wish we could do every day," said Ms. Godin, who became director of the chapter about three years ago.
Over the last 10 years, she said, the chapter granted 810 wishes. About a quarter -- 207 -- were granted in 1992 alone. Next year, Ms. Godin said, she expects to grant about 250 wishes. The value of the 1992 wishes was estimated at more than $928,000, though the actual cash spent is somewhat less.
"We try to get as many 'in-kind' donations as we can," Ms. Godin said, adding that all the chapter's working capital comes from donations. The bulk of the donations come from individuals and from fund-raising events held throughout the year. The events range from golf outings for business people to cake sales run by employees of businesses in the state.
"About 90 percent of our donations go directly to wishes," Ms. Godin said, adding that 10 percent is used for administrative expenses, which include three full-time employees.
The majority of wishes, she said, are for trips to Disney World in Florida. Typically, the foundation will cover the entire cost of the trip for the child and family members, including brothers and sisters. Even spending money is included.
But not every child wants a trip to Florida.
"I asked him if would rather go to Disney World, but he just wanted the tree house," Mrs. Beauchamp said, explaining that her son's wish was inspired by a visit to a cousin's tree house last summer.
In keeping with the chapter's practice of fulfilling wishes as economically as possible, officials looked for someone who could build a tree house. They didn't have to look far.
Two of the chapter's board members, Jack and Robert Silverstein, owners of Rojac Construction in Ocean, volunteered labor and materials for the job.
The problem was that the only tree on the property was a pine tree -- not exactly the ideal setting for a treehouse. So instead of building inside the tree, the builders instead put the tree house next to it, a matter of little significance to Kiki.
"I like it a lot," he said while pedaling around his driveway on a miniature bulldozer. "I can play G.I. Joe."
A different kind of wish was granted to Tony Johnson, 15, a Hudson County resident whose AIDS was diagnosed in September 1991. Last year, Tony, who was writing a book of essays about his life before and after AIDS, was becoming frustrated writing the book in longhand.
The Make-A-Wish Foundation found out about his plight through a social worker and offered him a computer. "Now I wonder how I ever did without it," he said.
Tony finished his book, "A Rock and a Hard Place," last spring, and it will be published in April by Crown Publishers of New York. "I'm working on my second book now," he said.
Most wishes originate when doctors, nurses or social workers tell parents of a sick child about the foundation. Typically, the child or the parents will describe the wish in a letter. The foundation contacts the child's doctor to determine whether the illness is life threatening.
If it is, the foundation's board meets to determine whether the child's wish can be fulfilled without impairing the chapter's ability to grant other wishes. Once the wish is approved, it is sent to a local office where volunteers handle the details.
The foundation's New Jersey headquarters are in a modest two-room office in Elizabeth. A sign proclaims "Don't Postpone Joy," and walls lined with photographs document that philosophy. Children who wished for meetings with their heroes are seen being hugged by celebrities ranging from Mr. Rogers to Mr. T. Photographs of children with stars like Hulk Hogan, Michael Jordan and Bruce Springsteen are displayed along with pictures of children with former President Ronald Reagan and United States Senator Bill Bradley.
The common thread that unites the images is an enchanted child in the midst of building a memory.
"That's what really fired me up," said Greg Jones, president of McCormick Corporate Communications in Long Valley, who has just become a member of the board. "There's the obvious benefit to the child, but beyond that, there are memories being made for the family as well."
Bob Bellacosa of Livingston reflected on the value of such memories. "In May of 1991, our son was diagnosed with a brain tumor," Mr. Bellacosa said, adding that the boy, Robbie, was 4 at the time. "In the late stages of his illness, our pediatrician came across the Make-A-Wish Foundation."
Mr. Bellacosa said the doctor contacted Ms. Godin and told her of Robbie's avid interest in professional wrestling and of his worsening medical condition.
"He was bedridden," Mr. Bellacosa said. "Ms. Godin came to visit him at 10 o'clock in the morning, and by 5 o'clock that afternoon we had this behemoth Russian wrestler in our house, spending time with our son."
Nine days later Robbie died.
"But that visit was such a bright spot," Mr. Bellacosa said. "It was really good medicine, not only for Robbie, but for the family as well because of the memory it leaves. It was a very good day. We still talk about it. And when it's all over, that's all you have to hold on to."
 
'He's Doing O.K. Now'
 When Ryan Ian Poyatt was 4 and living in Haddon Township, it was found that he had a brain tumor. Though most of the tumor was surgically removed, Ryan was left blind, and a portion of the tumor remained.
"He's doing O.K. now," said his mother, Mary Riggin Poyatt. "He hasn't stopped progressing. I've got a lot of my Ryan back."
Ms. Poyatt said the Make-A-Wish Foundation called her several times, asking her if there was anything her son might need. "I didn't want to ask for something just for the sake of getting it," she said.
But then, after discovering that Ryan enjoyed being pushed along for rides on his tricycle, Ms. Poyatt told him about a bicycle she had seen in a magazine. The bicycle had two seats and three wheels and could be painted any color of the rainbow.
A few weeks later, after sending his letter to the Make-A-Wish Foundation, Ryan sat on the back of a red two-seater, his mother providing the pedal power, and the spring breeze on his face providing proof that some wishes indeed come true.

HEADLINE: MARKET WATCH;
Harking Back To the Days of Malaise

BYLINE: By Floyd Norris

 BODY:
REMEMBER the last time that the Democrats controlled both the White House and Capitol Hill? Then Washington was convulsed by a long and ultimately inconclusive battle over a national energy policy, a fight that pitted region against region.
By opening up the possibility of new energy taxes, the Clinton Administration has inadvertently walked into a possible re-run of that battle, a fight that many on Wall Street seem to have forgotten. Last week, when J. Bennett Johnston, the Louisiana senator who chairs the Energy Committee, proposed a tax on imported oil aimed at assuring a price of $25 per barrel for domestic oil, the oil stocks went crazy. 
 The idea is a bit batty for a lot of reasons, including the fact it would raise little revenue for the Government while providing oil companies with windfall profits. And as Salomon Brothers' Bill Randol noted, chemical companies that use petroleum as a raw material would be devastated. To be sure, it does play brilliantly in Louisiana. But in Maine, the home of George Mitchell, the Senate Democratic leader, and a place where a lot of homes are heated by imported oil, it is hard to imagine a less popular idea.
By Friday, the implausibility of the Johnston proposal had begun to sink in on Wall Street, and oil stocks slipped back. But the Standard & Poor's index of domestic integrated oil stocks was still up 6.4 percent for the week, perhaps ending a long period during which the oils have been dead in the water.
A big part of that move, as is noted on page 15, stemmed from reports that the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries was actually going to get its act together and cut production enough to boost world prices. Stranger things have happened, but the probability of that happening when oil demand is depressed by weak growth in this country and recessions in Europe and Japan would seem to be low. It would no doubt be rational for the oil producers to cooperate, but even now it appears likely that enough of them would cheat on a production accord to render it ineffective.
Still, it is understandable why value-oriented money managers would find oil companies enticing. Exxon's market value rose $3.7 billion last week, as the stock gained $3 to $61.125, but it still trades at just 15 times estimated 1993 earnings, not an impressive number in the current frothy environment. Oil companies are seen as plays that benefit from inflation and strong economic activity, and the conventional wisdom is that neither of those will come again. For those who doubt that, the long-term contrarian argument for the oils has great appeal, whatever happens at the next OPEC meeting.
The Wall Street reaction to the uproar over possible energy taxes is not that important, although lots of money may be made. The more significant question is whether a regional battle will again paralyze the Government. It is high time for President Clinton to stop the trial balloons and come up with a tax policy that can pass Congress.
