HEADLINE: Pondering an Energy Tax That Can't Please All the People

BYLINE: By MATTHEW L. WALD

 BODY:
THE energy tax being considered by the Clinton Administration could reduce the budget deficit, cut oil imports and meet environmental goals. But before the Administration asks Congress to approve one, it will have to decide which problems it wants cured the most, and whom it is willing to offend in the process.
Last weekend the Treasury Secretary, Lloyd Bentsen, said the Administration was considering a "broad-based energy tax." Experts say that there are three practical ways to levy such a tax. First is the "B.T.U." tax, in which each unit of energy, no matter what the source, would be taxed at the same rate; second is a sales tax on the fuel used by utilities, industries and individuals ; third is a "carbon tax" based on how much carbon dioxide the fuel's use creates. Other possibilities -- not so broad-based -- are taxes on oil imports and on gasoline. 
 Each produces a different set of winners and losers, allies and enemies. The carbon tax, for example, would hit residents of the Midwest very hard, and the oil import tax would hurt the Northeast; both would be felt much more mildly in the Pacific Northwest.
If any energy tax is enacted, consumers would pay more not just for the energy they buy directly, like gasoline or electricity. Everything has an energy component, from maple syrup, which is produced with prodigious amounts of oil, to a dentist's filling a cavity, which requires electricity for the drill. Indeed, most people pay more for energy through the goods and services they buy than they pay directly in utility or fuel bills. In that sense, a "broad-based energy tax" could resemble a national sales tax or a value-added tax.
Picking which tax will emerge, if any, is difficult. A spokesman for the Treasury Department, Scott Dykema, said on Friday that "nothing has been ruled in and nothing has been ruled out."
But Roger Altman, deputy Treasury Secretary, said in an interview with John McLaughlin, host of the television program "The McLaughlin Group", on Friday that "there are good arguments for a B.T.U. tax, and there are good arguments for an ad valorem tax." An ad valorem tax is a sales tax on energy.
"I think those two are the primary options in this area," he said. "I don't think that a gasoline tax is as attractive, I don't think that a carbon tax is as attractive, but whether the President will ultimately decide on an energy tax at all, and how he'll come down as between these two or some of the other options, I don't know."
On Capitol Hill, key players have yet to take a position. Dan Rostenkowski, the chairman of the House Ways and Means Committee, reiterated last week that he favors a gasoline tax, 25 cents a gallon or more, and has also said he would support other tax increases if the President wants them. But he has not been more detailed on the subject of energy taxes.
Likewise, Daniel Patrick Moynihan, the New York Democrat who is chairman of the Senate Finance Committee, has supported an energy tax but not expressed any preference thus far.
Whatever tax is chosen, implementation would probably be complicated. And the tax would create a drag on the economy -- perhaps even a sudden burst of inflation. But such taxes are also extremely attractive as a way to raise money. The national energy bill is about $475 billion a year, nearly 10 percent of the gross domestic product. "There's real money here," said Phillip K. Verleger Jr., an energy and commodity economist. If the Administration holds to its goal of cutting the deficit by $145 billion in four years, that implies major revenue growth even if there are also spending cuts.
Some people in the energy industry say that energy should not be singled out for taxation. "Good economics tells you you should raise revenue from the broadest source possible," said Bruce G. Humphrey, the chief economist at the Edison Electric Institute, the trade association of investor-owned utilities. "An energy tax literally excludes 90 percent of the rest of the economy." The result, he said, is distortions in the economy.
But that is the idea, say proponents who want to change the way energy is used. However, such considerations may be secondary, especially in Mr. Bentsen's mind. The trial balloon for this tax was floated by the Treasury Secretary, not the Energy Secretary, Hazel Rollins O'Leary, who told the Senate Energy Committee in her confirmation hearings a few days earlier that she did not favor energy taxes.
Following is a description of the options:
 
The 'B.T.U.' Tax
 Fuel comes in many forms and when engineers compare them -- to decide, say, whether to run a factory on oil or natural gas -- they refer to the heat content as measured by B.T.U.'s, or British thermal units. Trivia buffs will recall that a B.T.U. is the quantity of heat needed to raise the temperature of one pound of water by one degree Fahrenheit at or near 39.2 degrees.
Some people, including Mr. Altman, say that rather than taxing coal by the ton or oil by the barrel, which effectively means different taxes for different kinds of fuels, they should be taxed according to a common standard. Each B.T.U., no matter what fuel it came from, would be taxed at the same rate.
Under this tax, the hardest hit would be coal, because its price per million B.T.U.'s is the lowest, around $1.50, so on a percentage basis, any fixed tax would add to coal's cost the most. The price of a million B.T.U.'s of natural gas varies by season but is generally around $2. The price of heavy oil, the kind most often in competition with gas and coal, varies sharply but recently has been around $2.50 per million B.T.U.'s.
By contrast, a B.T.U. tax would fall only lightly on the uranium used in nuclear power plants. Uranium, which is not priced by the B.T.U., in any case costs far less than fossil fuels. (Most of nuclear energy's costs come from capital and labor.)
A B.T.U. tax could also be applied to energy from windmills and hydroelectric plants. Currently the Energy Department measures the electric output of those plants and calculates how many B.T.U.'s would have been required to make that electricity from the average plant running on fossil fuels.
"A B.T.U. tax might be viewed as being regionally fairer" than a carbon tax, which would hit some fuels more than others, said Roger Dower, an energy expert at the World Resources Institute, a nonprofit group in Washington that advocates the use of taxes to achieve environmental goals. But Mr. Dower added that such a tax, while more politically palatable, is not aimed at the fuels that cause the most damage.
Some analysts say the tax would drive some users to oil from coal, therefore increasing reliance on oil imports. Also, a B.T.U. tax would discourage the use of not just fossil fuels but all energy, including environmentally benign "renewable" sources like wind, geothermal energy or wood from tree farms.
As measured in B.T.U.'s, about 40 percent of the energy consumed in this country comes from oil, 24 percent from natural gas, 23 percent from coal, 8 percent from nuclear power and 4 percent from hydroelectricity. If the tax were levied by energy content, those fuels would bear it in those proportions.
A variant of a B.T.U. tax would be a fixed tax on the price of all fuels -- say 5 percent. It would be just like a sales tax. This would exempt windmills, hydroelectric dams and geothermal energy, whose operators do not pay for fuel. Nuclear power would benefit, too, because uranium is very cheap, even if reactors are expensive.
 
The Carbon Tax
 Most environmentalists favor something more targeted: a tax on fuels in proportion to the amount of carbon dioxide their use creates. Carbon dioxide is implicated by scientists as the single most important gas in global warming. Among fossil fuels, natural gas produces the least carbon dioxide, oil is in the middle and coal produces the most. Hydroelectricity and nuclear power produce none; windmills, which are on the threshold of commercial success, and solar electric systems, which are a step behind wind, are also free of carbon dioxide. Vice President Gore, when he was a Senator, expressed support for a carbon tax.
"I think a carbon tax makes sense," said Dr. Harold M. Hubbard, now a fellow at the Hawaii Natural Energy Institute, who wrote an article in Scientific American two years ago that environmentalists hail as a landmark in explaining why energy should be taxed. "A carbon tax is probably the most politically acceptable and even-handed way," he said, pointing to the goals of reducing dependence on fossil fuels, moving to "sustainable" energy sources, and "spreading the misery over a broader base."
But some regions would suffer much more than others from a carbon tax. Almost 90 percent of the electricity in Ohio comes from coal. By contrast, about 90 percent of the electricity in Washington state is from hydroelectricity or nuclear power. That means the energy-intensive aluminum mills of the Pacific Northwest could continue almost unaffected by a carbon tax, while the farms and factories of the Midwest would face huge new costs.
Mr. Humphrey of the Edison Electric Institute said hypothetically that a tax of $100 per ton of carbon emitted by burning fuel would raise the price of a ton of coal by 230 percent, of oil by 65 percent, and of natural gas by 90 percent.
At most utility power plants a million B.T.U.'s turns into about 105 kilowatt-hours of electricity. This implies an increase of about 2.4 cents per kilowatt-hour from coal-burning plants on a national average price of electricity of about 7.5 cents -- a big blow to customers.
The tax would favor renewable energy sources and nuclear power over other sources. But John H. Lichtblau, chairman of the Petroleum Industry Research Foundation, said the main effect would be to make coal less competitive against gas. "Most inter-fuels competition is in the electric sector, with coal and gas," he said.
Proposing a carbon tax would also create an instant nexus of opposition, from the coal industry and coal-state members of Congress. Environmentalists and tax experts refer to Robert C. Byrd, the West Virginia Democrat who is the President Pro Tem of the Senate, as if he were a force of nature when they describe how difficult it would be to enact a tax over his opposition.
 
The Oil-Import Fee
 Both a carbon tax and a B.T.U. tax would dampen demand for oil, but neither would do anything for domestic oil producers, which have lost nearly half a million jobs in 10 years and are now producing less oil than at any time since the early 1960's. The independent producers -- that is, those whose companies only produce oil, as opposed to refining or marketing it -- recently called for a fee of some kind on oil imports. Backed by some oil-patch members of Congress, this would amount to a more narrowly based energy tax.
On Thursday, J. Bennett Johnston, the Louisiana Democrat who is chairman of the Senate Energy Committee, said he would propose a floor price of $25 a barrel on oil imports. If the world price were lower, the Government would impose a tariff to effectively raise the price to $25.
But the United States has ratified a free trade agreement with Canada, one of this country's largest sources of oil, and has signed, if not ratified, a similar agreement with Mexico, another major producer. Washington would also like to avoid destabilizing Venezuela, a big source of imports, or offending an ally, Saudi Arabia.
An oil-import tax also has problems. For example, of the roughly 94 million housing units in this country, more than half, about 48 million, use natural gas, and only about 13 million use heating oil. Nearly all of those 13 million are in the Northeast.
Alternatively, Washington could propose a tax only on gasoline, said Jeffrey C. Genzer, the lawyer for the National Association of State Energy Officials. That would raise the ire of people in the West, who typically drive longer distances, but it would also upset state officials, who would see that increase as pre-empting future increases in state gasoline taxes.
In budget maneuvering last year, Congress shied away from a gasoline-tax increase of even 5 cents. Such a tax produces severe differences in impact by region and income. But in its favor, the price of gasoline is low now, which might make it more politically palatable.

HEADLINE: Making a Difference;
Up Against Icahn's Muscle

BYLINE: By VERONICA BYRD

 BODY:
THE chairman and chief executive of E-II Holdings Inc., Steven J. Green, says there is no challenge too daunting, but he's certainly being put to the test now.
First, E-II, the parent company of Samsonite luggage, Culligan water treatment equipment and McGregor men's wear, has been operating under Chapter 11 bankruptcy protection since July 1992. Those subsidiaries operate independently and are not directly affected by the bankruptcy. 
 More recently, the company came under attack by the financier Carl C. Icahn, who sought bankruptcy court permission to file a competing reorganization plan.
Mr. Icahn, who holds $220 million of E-II's junior bonds, said his plan, which the court rejected on Friday, would be better for bondholders. He also said he wanted to see Mr. Green removed as chairman.
Mr. Green, 47, said there were some encouraging signs of growth at the company.
When Mr. Green took the helm of E-II Holdings in December 1990, its senior bonds were trading at 22 cents on the dollar and its junior bonds were trading at 9 cents. As of Jan. 25, senior bonds were trading at $1.18 and juniors at 71 cents.
"What we decided to do was totally insulate the operating companies from the reorganization process," Mr. Green said. "Our goal was to globalize all the companies."
An aggressive advertising campaign abroad "stressed our product's quality for the money," Mr. Green said, and now 60 percent of Denver-based Samsonite's earnings come from Europe. The luggage maker also opened a travel shop in the GUM store in Moscow's Red Square. Botany 500 also opened a Moscow shop.
Darryl Schall, an analyst with Dabney/Resnick and Wagner Inc. in Los Angeles, which specializes in securities issued by troubled companies, said the company's expansion into Europe was a good move and described the restructuring roller coaster as "a saga" with several financial titans battling to control a solid company with solid potential.
He said the prospects for E-II "are very good, especially in light of an improving economy."

HEADLINE: Stanching the Loss of Good Jobs

BYLINE: By LOUIS UCHITELLE

 BODY:
THE layoff announcements come with painful regularity. Facing stiff competition and disappearing profits, corporate America has been throwing workers overboard, by the millions. Last week alone, four corporate giants announced that nearly 100,000 jobs -- most of them "good" ones, meaning full-time work with health and pension benefits -- would soon disappear. The Clinton Administration is debating measures that might slow the process. But what is really needed, nearly everyone agrees, is a wholesale reorganization of the way Americans work.
The reorganization, emphasizing worker training, flexibility and initiative, is hardly new. Some American companies have been trying for years to make the transformation. But now, with the job cutbacks showing no sign of easing off, the need to change is becoming more urgent. The hope among many public policy experts and executives is that jobs will somehow be saved if companies constantly train and retrain their workers, so that even the lowliest stock clerks learn new tasks while carrying out their old ones in an entrepreneurial and hustling spirit. 
 Recognizing the urgency, the National Association of Manufacturers called a news conference in Washington this month to campaign for change from the traditional system of designing jobs that demand little from most workers. "The best thing you, Mr. Employer, can say to your workers is, 'You are valuable, you are my most important asset and I am going to train you to within an inch of your life,' " said Phyllis Eisen, a senior policy director at the N.A.M.
Despite such talk, the "new" work ethic is not a panacea. Even if Boeing, McDonnell Douglas, United Technologies and Sears, Roebuck were fully committed to the new approach, they still would have announced job cutbacks last week, most experts say. But the total would probably have been much less than 100,000 -- perhaps only half that number.
"There is not a single magic bullet to save jobs," said Harley Shaiken, a labor economist at the University of California at San Diego. "But reorganizing work is one way to slow the cutbacks."
Making workers more efficient and entrepreneurial will not cure weak economic growth or overcapacity. Boeing, for example, is structured to make more planes than the airlines now want to buy. What's more, union leaders say the new system is an excuse, in some cases, for getting rid of seniority, work rules and even collective bargaining.
 
Making It Pay Off
 But when the new work ethic does succeed, the results can be eye-catching -- and job-saving. The money made from a worker's rising output can more than offset training expenses, high health insurance premiums and other costs -- and sometimes even defeat a competitor's low-wage operation in the third world.
That, at least, is the message from Christoph Schmid, president of Luk Inc., which makes clutch plates in Wooster, Ohio, for manual-shift cars. Luk managed to overtake the Borg-Warner Corporation, the market leader, which had shifted its production of plates to Brazil.
"They were counting on labor-cost savings, but we drove them out of the business in the United States," Mr. Schmid said. (Borg-Warner sold the Brazilian operation in 1990.)
Reaching for such successes and prodded by the Government to maintain jobs, a growing minority of American companies is trying to reorganize its offices and factories. Employees are encouraged to work in teams, learning to do many tasks, so that a machine operator can also repair machinery, a bank teller can fill in as a loan officer and an airline reservation clerk can push ticket sales by recommending a hotel and museum to visit on arrival. In the process, seniority and work rules are often discarded, along with many managers, since workers ideally govern themselves.
"We have rules and boundaries, but the boundaries can be quite broad for people who have enough information and background to operate on their own," said Richard Chandler, general manager of Motorola Inc.'s cellular products division in Arlington, Ill. He mentioned a production worker who became annoyed with defective parts for cellular telephones from an Atlanta supplier. She asked to fly to Atlanta and meet with the supplier. "I was nervous about it, but I let her go," Mr. Chandler said. The defects were corrected.
That sort of initiative and knowledge requires a difficult switch from the decades-old American practice of dividing work into simple, repetitive tasks carried out by workers who are never greatly respected by their bosses, or trusted to perform without being prodded and policed. More than 90 million people in a labor force of 124 million still fall into this category. So far, only 5 percent of the nation's 5 million companies have made the transition to the new way of working or are trying to do so, according to various surveys.
 
The Clinton Proposals
 Endorsing the transformation, the Clinton Administration is considering proposals that would encourage companies to hire and train -- proposals that the new Labor Secretary, Robert B. Reich, describes as carrots and sticks. One proposal is a tax credit that would reward companies for each employee hired beyond a certain base number. Another, according to Administration officials, would require companies to spend part of each dollar they receive in Federal research grants for employee training.
In a third approach, companies would be required to spend on training an amount equal to 1.5 percent of their payrolls, with the training spread among all workers. Currently, two-thirds of the $30 billion that companies now spend each year on training goes for college graduates -- although they make up only 25 percent of the labor force, according to the Labor Department.
Finally, Administration officials are reported to be considering an idea that Mr. Reich raised publicly before he became Labor Secretary this month. Corporations might be allowed to hold down base pay, but would be required to pay to all workers annual bonuses linked to profits -- a change intended to keep pay linked to earnings.
"Some companies are upgrading their workers and work practices on their own," Mr. Reich said in an interview, referring to such well-known examples as Motorola, Xerox and Corning. "But what we see across the landscape is that most American companies are not doing much."
The biggest obstacle is job security. Even a Motorola is reluctant to promise no layoffs, although so far Mr. Chandler, with more than 3,000 workers in the cellular division, has been able to cover periods of weak demand and falling production with such devices as mandatory vacations. "Only once did we send people home for a month without pay," Mr. Chandler said.
 
Worker's Perspective
 The problem is that workers are unwilling to go all out to increase their productivity only to find themselves jobless.
"To get that sort of commitment, the line worker has to be sure that he or she would not make a suggestion for some new efficiency that would put that person out of work the following day," said Dennis Cuneo, a vice president at New United Motor Manufacturing Inc., the General Motors-Toyota joint venture in Fremont, Calif., that is rated as perhaps the most efficient auto plant in the nation.
New United, famous for embracing the new approach to work and turning a G.M. factory that had failed into a huge success, agreed in 1984 to no layoffs -- except in the most dire straits, and then only after managers' pay is cut along with other expenses. "When you say management will take some of the pain, that makes a difference," Mr. Cuneo said.
The no-layoff agreement has been written into United Auto Workers contracts with New United. And in the late 1980's, when production did fall sharply, New United found itself with 300 idle workers, 10 percent of its employees. To keep them busy, they were rotated into four-week training programs, at full pay, until production picked up eight months later.
The state government in California subsidized some of the cost, justifying the outlay with the argument that unemployment pay would have been more expensive. But if the slowdown had lasted a few more months, even New United might have resorted to layoffs and attrition, Mr. Cuneo said.
Republic Engineered Steel, having been purchased by its employees and managers in 1989 from the failing LTV Corporation, takes a different tack. Pressed lately to come up with $80 million a year in savings to remain solvent, the employee-controlled board eschewed layoffs but asked the company's 5,000 workers for cost-cutting proposals. One thousand ideas came in, each one reviewed by a committee of managers and representatives of the United Steel Workers union.
So far, $63 million in annual savings have been implemented, none of which involve cutting staff, said Harold Kelley, a company vice president. One such step, for example, recycles water used as a coolant in the casting of steel bars from melted scrap.
 
Necessary Bleeding
 But such tactics are not always sufficient without some layoffs. Overwhelmed by Japanese competitors, Xerox first shrank its staff by 20,000 in the early 1980's to get rid of excess capacity, and then proceeded to reorganize jobs and retrain the employees who remained. So did Corning. And now I.B.M., Sears and General Motors are similarly shrinking their operations, having become stuck with too much capacity and too many people for their declining sales -- no matter how well trained or motivated these workers might be.
"The ethos of job security dies quickly in the onslaught from I.B.M. and General Motors," said David Wyss, chief economist at DRI/McGraw-Hill.
More subtly, the new work practices assume that workers can be trained -- even if the company has to start by teaching them basic math and reading -- and then will bring energy and initiative to their jobs. That is not always the reality.
At German-owned Luk, the manufacturer of clutch plates, Mr. Schmid, the president, transplanted some German work practices, trying to get his 340 employees to constantly search for ways to improve output and quality.
Several workers, for example, figured out that robots that were moving clutch plates from one station to another for different steps in the manufacturing process could not do the job as quickly and efficiently as a special conveyor belt that these workers then helped to design.
For these entrepreneurs, jobs are guaranteed, Mr. Schmid said. But that is not the case for the "person who only runs a machine and comes up with no ideas and no suggestions for improvement. That person does not have the same job security, and a good number of them are pushed out and replaced each year."
There are losers, then, in the new world of work, people who find themselves unable to adapt to the faster pace. That has drawn criticism from union leaders. "You have this meritocracy, and what people overlook is that a meritocratic system tends to favor younger, energetic workers," said Leslie Nulty of the United Food and Commercial Workers. "You can't run a society where older people cannot make a living."
But the alternative is to remain locked into a work tradition rooted in America's prowess at developing mass production technologies earlier in this century. For 75 percent of the labor force, work is still largely divided into simple, repetitive tasks.
The problem is that, increasingly, automated machinery, computers and robots can do these simple tasks. And so can workers in third world countries, at much lower wages, whether the task is assembling a motor or taking airline reservations by phone. Sticking with old work habits makes the labor force endlessly vulnerable to layoffs, lower wages, part-time work and fewer benefits as work is automated or shifted overseas.
Reflecting these forces, full-time employment among the nation's jobholders, after rising through most of the 1980's, has remained unchanged since 1989 at 60 percent. Wages have stagnated or fallen for 80 percent of American households.
To generate jobs, the nation has traditionally relied on two developments, neither of which is providing much assistance now. One lifesaver would be a dramatic new product, based on new American technology, that would be manufactured initially in American factories. The computer industry grew from such a breakthrough, but another is not in sight.
The other fallback is strong economic growth, so strong that consumer demand for goods and services keeps companies operating at full capacity despite the competition from abroad and however inefficiently work is organized. But the American economy remains weak.
 
The 3M Experience

Indeed, even a company like 3M, which is among the most deeply involved in the new work practices, is feeling the pinch these days.
For more than a decade, 3M has maintained domestic employment at 50,000. Layoffs are unknown and when some of 3M's 60,000 products, most of them different types of tapes, experience a sales downturn, people are shifted to the manufacture and distribution of those that are selling well. In exchange for this job security, workers have played a big role in finding ways to increase their average output by 8 percent a year, says Richard Lidstad, vice president for human resources.
The problem is that since 1989, sales have failed to keep up with the growth in the company's production capacity. Instead of sales gains of 8 percent or more a year -- the norm in the 1980's -- 3M is experiencing gains of 5 percent or less.
"The goodwill of a work force is very important to productivity," Mr. Lidstad said. "But if the 5 percent sales growth persists, we could have a problem. I suppose we would have to shrink the work force through attrition. We would go to great lengths to avoid layoffs."
 
 
 
WHEN JOB GROWTH IS A ZERO-SUM GAME

WEAK as the economy is, BancOne, headquartered in Columbus, Ohio, has been going against the grain of corporate America, adding workers instead of shedding them. But like many other thriving companies, BancOne has not added much to total employment in the United States.
Since 1987, BancOne's payroll has risen to 43,000, from 20,000. Much of this growth, however, has come from acquiring community banks in 11 states, along with their employees, or serving the customers of failed savings and loan associations, and sometimes hiring an S.& L.'s employees.
What new jobs there are come mostly from extending banking hours -- some branches are now open on Sundays, for example -- or adding services that a local bank had not offered in the past.
"If you buy a small bank, they often do not have a car leasing or mortgage operation, so we put the people in there to do these tasks," said the chairman of BancOne, John B. McCoy.
Job training plays only a small role in motivating or improving the skills of this growing work force. Ironically, Mr. McCoy counts on turnover -- 10 percent of BancOne's employees on average leave each year -- to motivate those who remain.
"Some turnover is healthy," Mr. McCoy said. "What it really does is allow the people to move up in the company. People can say to themselves, 'I can get a better job just by staying at BancOne.' "
There isn't much turnover in upper management, but Mr. McCoy has solved that problem, so far, through expansion: newly acquired banks need chief executives.
BancOne's chief financial officer, for example, is about to move up to the presidency of a major new operation in Arizona, and the president of a Wisconsin bank is transferring to Columbus to be chief financial officer.
"These are powerful promotions," Mr. McCoy said.
But how does BancOne generate the turnover?
Well, 20 percent of BancOne's employees work part-time, and their ties to the company are tenuous. College students are hired, mostly to work late shifts, and they leave after graduation. And BancOne's tellers earn only $17,000 to $18,000 a year.
"I am not sure many people retire as tellers," Mr. McCoy said.

HEADLINE: It's Not Just How Well You Play the Game . . .

BYLINE: By CALVIN SIMS

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES

 BODY:
The DALLAS COWBOYS, playing in the Super Bowl today, have always liked to promote themselves as America's Team. Winning Super Bowl XII in 1979 took them one step further, to America's sports merchandiser. For the next six years the Cowboys sold more sweat shirts, T-shirts, hats, coffee mugs and other paraphernalia than any other team in professional sports.
At one point, sales of goods decorated with the Cowboys' distinctive star accounted for 30 percent of the National Football League's merchandise sales. 
 But the world of sports merchandising is changing dramatically. In recent years, as it has grown into a business with more than $12 billion in sales, teams that win a championship are no longer guaranteed first place at the cash register in a clothing store.
Consider the Colorado Rockies, a baseball team that will not even play its first game until next season. Yet in 1992 the Rockies sold more merchandise than the Mets, Dodgers, the world champion Toronto Blue Jays and many other teams. Indeed, only the Chicago White Sox, Atlanta Braves and New York Yankees outsold the Rockies.
Or consider the San Jose Sharks. Last year, their first season in the National Hockey League, they performed dismally -- and sold more merchandise, $150 million worth, than any team in professional sports except the Chicago Bulls, which sold $300 million in goods as they won their second consecutive basketball championship.
How could this be?
The reason, merchandising experts say, is that the color, design and quality of goods have become more important to sales than fan loyalty or game success. Sports merchandise, if not quite high fashion, is fashion nonetheless.
"Although sports has become more a part of our popular culture, people are buying these sports clothes mainly because they like the way they look," said Karen Raugust, editor of the Licensing Letter, a trade publication based in Brooklyn. "It's become a fad. A lot of these folks are wearing T-shirts, and they don't even know what sport the team plays."
The Sharks -- were they lucky or shrewd? -- chose teal, a very hot color last year, to go with black and silver as the team colors. Their logo is a racy shark biting a hockey stick in two (some hockey fans have noted that a broken stick is a perfect metaphor for the Sharks' performance on ice). The Rockies' colors are black, silver and purple, their logo a baseball soaring to a Rocky Mountain high.
And the Chicago White Sox jumped from back in the pack to lead all baseball teams in merchandise sales after it changed the team colors to silver and black. It's a hot combination: the best-selling football team, the Los Angeles Raiders, and the second-best-selling hockey team, the Los Angeles Kings, also wear silver and black.
"Teams are switching their colors and uniforms in order to become more appealing to the fans and to sell more merchandise, and that's something that has never happened before in the history of professional sports," said Richard White, president of Major League Baseball Properties.
"A few years ago, merchandising had little impact economically on the leagues," he added. "But now there is no owner who does not recognize that these products have a very substantial impact on their bottom line."
And how. In some cases, merchandising accounts for as much as 10 percent of a team's profits, Mr. White said.
Sales of all sports merchandise -- not just team-name goods from the major pro sports but also products from the Olympics, professional wrestling, automobile racing and other sports -- increased 9 percent last year to $12.1 billion, from $11.1 billion in 1991, and 66 percent in the last five years, from $7.3 billion in 1987.
The boom was made possible, in part, by the public's greater appetite for casual wear; a generation of children now go to school in sweat shirts and sweat pants. And sales were vigorously pushed along by a determined marketing effort by the four major professional sports.
In each league, the proceeds of all team merchandise sales are pooled and divided equally among the teams -- an approach that the teams in smaller markets would love to extend to broadcasting rights, and that the wealthier teams steadfastly oppose.
Ms. Raugust said the National Football League, Major League Baseball, the National Basketball Association and the National Hockey League have all increased both the number of products they offer and the number of distribution channels.
In all four professional leagues, merchandising is coordinated by a central properties division that licenses hundreds of manufacturers to produce thousands of products, including hats, shirts, jackets, balls, uniforms, shoes, bags, watches, telephones and videos. The leagues now offer clothing lines designed specifically for women, men and children, right down to sleepers for newborns, embossed with a team logo.
"We try to make items that people will wear every day," said John Bello, president of National Football League Properties. "We spend a lot of time in Europe looking at the latest fashions and then we try to incorporate them into our sports gear."
As a result of this hard work, Mr. Bello estimates, about 67 percent of American households have at least one item bearing an N.F.L. logo.
The N.F.L. sold about $2.1 billion in licensed merchandise last year, Major League Baseball about $2.4 billion. Sales of basketball merchandise have been growing the most quickly of any of the major sports and reached $1.4 billion last year, while the National Hockey League lags (as it does in fans and broadcast revenue), with merchandise sales of about $600 million.
The major leagues aside, many minor league teams also sell merchandise vigorously, albeit on a much smaller scale. And the market for college sports merchandising is also huge, an estimated $1.5 billion last year.
Mr. Bello said all the professional leagues have expanded not just what they sell, but how it is sold.
Once largely available only at concession stands at ballparks and coliseums, and at sports equipment shops, team merchandise is now sold in many clothing stores and most department stores. Teams develop mailing lists of season ticketholders and anyone who orders tickets by mail, and sends merchandise catalogues to fans' homes. And some leagues are making plans to market goods through home television shopping.
Even if the teams that do particularly well in selling licensed merchandise must share their profits with laggards, intangible benefits accrue, like national name recognition and even influence within the league. The National Hockey League has agreed, for example, to let San Jose host the All-Star Game in 1995.
And with the wide disparity in sales among teams in the same league -- precise totals are not available, because the leagues will provide only rankings of sales, and not the actual numbers -- some of the most successful merchandising teams are now proposing to end the sharing of their profits.
If ticket sales are confined, as a matter of course, to fans within easy reach of a team, the demand for merchandise of new teams and lesser-known teams extends nationwide, and indeed, overseas, executives of professional teams and leagues say. That, too, is a sharp contrast to the past.
The next major market for sports merchandising may well be overseas. All the major leagues have been cultivating foreign audiences, playing exhibition games in Europe and Japan.
Broadcast of games and highlight shows abound, and many of the biggest North American sports stars are already well known overseas. This provides a ready market for merchandising.
And league executives are already exploring Australia, South America and other parts of Asia, where sport is immensely popular and other American exports, like music and videos, have done very well.
 
COLLEGES BENEFIT FROM FASHION
BENEFITING from growing public demand for sports merchandise, colleges and universities have experienced a boom in sales of products bearing their logos.
The Collegiate Licensing Company, which coordinates licensing agreements for 126 colleges and universities, said that college merchandising has been growing at about 25 percent to 30 percent a year and reached $1.5 billion in sales last year.
That's good news, say college and university officials who are facing severe budget cuts and seeking new sources of revenue. The officials declined to disclose specific sales figures, but the top-selling schools acknowledge that they have surpassed $1 million in annual merchandise revenues.
Unlike professional sports merchandisers, colleges and universities have done little to capture new sales. In most cases, retailers have approached the schools seeking to put their names and logos on products.
"Most college logos and colors date back many, many decades, and the schools are reluctant to change them," said Barbara Bailey, director of marketing for Collegiate Licensing. "Success in college merchandising is tied mainly to how well the teams play and also to the public's perception of the institution."
Brian McGuire, assistant athletic director for promotions for Georgetown University, Collegiate Licensing's top seller, attributes the school's success to its popularity as "a strong academic institution that is also good at sports." However, Mr. McGuire said that the school's recent surge in sales might have something to do with the fact that President Clinton is an alumnus and frequently wears Georgetown sweat shirts while jogging.

HEADLINE: Mutual Funds;
Those Wacky Little Stocks Abroad 

BYLINE: By Carole Gould

 BODY:
COLLECTING oddball little stocks from around the world has catapulted the Acorn International Fund into first place among overseas stock funds, at least for the few short months since its startup. Acorn soared 8.5 percent from Sept. 23 through Jan. 22 compared with a 0.7 percent gain for its peers, according to Morningstar Inc., the mutual fund researchers in Chicago.
Its offbeat strategy may make Acorn a good bet for the long haul, said John Rekenthaler, editor of the investment newsletter Morningstar Mutual Funds. But the double risks of currency shifts and small-stock investing suggest the fund is not for everyone. 
 With just $39 million in assets so far, Acorn International is an offshoot of the $1.45 billion Acorn Fund, both managed by Chicago-based Wanger Asset Management. The original Acorn, a small-stock fund begun in 1970 by Ralph Wanger and still run by him, returned 17.3 percent a year compared with 12.4 percent for the average small-stock fund in the decade ended Dec. 31, 1992.
Acorn International's co-managers are Leah Zell and Gary Greenberg.
Ms. Zell arrived at the Acorn Fund in 1984 via Harvard University, where she earned a doctorate in economic history in 1979, and Lehman Brothers, where she had a position in corporate finance. She and Mr. Wanger were married in 1985.
Mr. Greenberg also specialized in corporate finance, after earning a graduate degree in international management from the American Graduate School of International Management (known as the Thunderbird School) in Arizona in 1985. He worked first at Continental Bank, then at Vickers da Costa, international brokers based in New York. He went to the Acorn Fund in early 1989.
Since the mid-80's, Acorn senior has invested about one-quarter of its assets overseas. Acorn junior was set up partly because the original Acorn was closed to new investors in 1990 and partly for people seeking more overseas exposure, said Mr. Wanger.
One of the three analysts working on Acorn International (Marcel Houtzager assists the two co-managers) hits the road every six weeks to scour the world for what Ms. Zell calls "wacky little stocks" -- under-researched or misunderstood growth companies, preferably with a solid market niche.
They look for companies with market capitalizations of $200 million to $750 million, using "growth at a reasonable price" as a yardstick, giving them more of a growth focus than Acorn senior. Research takes a three-pronged approach: selecting a country, then an industry, then the companies.
Right now, 30 percent of the Acorn International portfolio is in Southeast Asia (excluding Japan), and 28 percent in Europe. Seven percent each is invested in Latin America and Japan, with the balance mostly in Canada, Australia, Israel and Korea.
Europe is good now, Ms. Zell said, because while the economies are weak, "interest rates are clearly on the downtrend and that usually means healthy markets." The two-year recession there has narrowed the spread in prices between high-quality and lower-quality companies, letting the fund pick up solid companies at much lower prices than similar firms would command in the United States. Two favorites: Guilbert, France's premier supplier of office products, and Telegraaf, a Dutch newspaper company with dominant market share, excess cash and a state-of-the-art printing plant.
The managers hedge European holdings by owning exporters in dollar-sensitive markets like Scandinavia, which are expected to do well as the American dollar strengthens over the next year or so. One favorite is Huhtamaki, the Finnish company that produces Milk Duds, Heath bars and Norplant, the controversial new implant contraceptive. Also, they hedge European currencies to reduce risks from a rising dollar.
Growth in Southeast Asia is being helped along by growth in China, the managers said. Acorn owns shares in Tigaraska, an Indonesian distributor of Johnson & Johnson products like Band-Aids, and in Cafe de Coral, a Hong Kong company with a chain of fast-food restaurants (fried chicken feet, corn on the cob and a Coca-Cola costs $2.50) that is moving into the South China market.
Acorn likes Mexico because of its market liberalization, falling interest rates and restrained inflation. The fund owns shares in Grupo Herdez, which produces enough mole sauce and mayonnaise to capture most of the Mexican bottled condiments business; the company has had a joint venture with American spice producer McCormick since 1947.
Other holdings include Teva, an Israeli generic drug company, and Affymax, a Dutch biotechnology firm.
The new Acorn fund is not for the faint-hearted, venturing as it does into far-flung companies that most investors probably never heard of. One way its managers minimize risk is by diversifying broadly. Six industries account for roughly 9 percent each of the fund -- drugs, publishing, financial services, leisure, food and retail -- while another four hold 20 percent more.
Leisure is an important theme based on the rising standard of living worldwide. The fund owns shares in several gaming companies in Malaysia, and in Sega, a Japanese video game maker whose next big product is a line of virtual reality helmets for children. Another favorite: Costa, an Italian cruise ship company.

HEADLINE: All About/Commercial Real Estate;
It's the Business Tenant Who's Calling the Shots

BYLINE: By CLAUDIA H. DEUTSCH

 BODY:
LOTS of people are talking about an uptick in business these days. Those people are not in commercial real estate.
The industry's motto of the early 90's -- "stay alive 'til '95" -- has deteriorated to "find something to do 'til 2002." 
 The lingering effects of the recession, combined with the unrelenting pace of corporate cutbacks, have continued to pummel the commercial real estate industry. Office vacancy rates are at all-time highs, while rents are stuck in the gutter.
"We won't see thriving markets any time soon," said James S. Vanderslice, managing director of Cushman & Wakefield, an international real estate services firm.
 
New Negotiating Style
Any Concession May Be on the Table
 The effect of the slump has been a turnabout in how business is done. While it is a terrible time to own an office building, it is a terrific time to be a corporate tenant.
"Tenants have evened the playing field," said Julian J. Studley, chairman of the New York real estate firm that bears his name. Alvin I. Apfelberg, a Manhattan real estate lawyer, is even more bullish: "There is a nationwide renaissance of tenant strength."
Stores and small companies that once looked longingly at Park Avenue or Rodeo Drive now find them within reach. Brokers, traditionally paid commissions as a percentage of rent, are accepting fees that give them more incentive to strike hard bargains. And auditors are making sure that landlords no longer play fast and loose with negotiated yearly rent increases.
Tenants are using their new sense of power in many ways: negotiating liberal sublease clauses; obtaining more generous allowances for customizing their spaces, securing many months of free rent and gaining other concessions that, in better times, landlords would have laughed at. In fact, tenants are changing the nature of real estate negotiations in ways that, even when the economy recovers, may never change back.
"Corporate real estate departments are looking at every aspect of every lease, seeking ways to save money," said Peter Santora, director of Arthur Andersen's corporate real estate group in southern California. "And, they are finding them."
Take the International Business Machines Corporation, which as recently as 1988 either owned or rented 118 million square feet in the country. That has shrunk to less than 107 million square feet, and is still going down.
Beyond adding to the already bloated inventory of office space, I.B.M. is eking out savings in its remaining space. It has been auditing the annual escalation charges in its leases and dunning landlords for rebates. It has insisted its brokers settle for flat fees based on the amount of space rented. And it has consolidated as many divisions as possible into I.B.M.-owned space.
Hughes Aircraft is cutting 750 people from its corporate staff in Los Angeles and moving the remaining 500 from its headquarters overlooking the Pacific Ocean. The military contractor is seeking to lease the building's 550,000 square feet of office space.
Companies that are expanding, meanwhile, are wringing huge bonuses from financially strapped landlords. Landlords historically provided a lot of money to new tenants so they could customize office space before they moved in. Now a growing number of tenants are taking the space as is, in return for "low rent and extra months of free rent," said Edward Goldman, a managing director with Edward S. Gordon.
Cash is not always the issue. Wachtell, Lipton, Rosen & Katz, the big New York law firm, recently rented seven floors of as-is space in Black Rock, the CBS corporate headquarters on West 52d Street.
Not only was the law firm able to negotiate rent down to the low $30's a square foot from the high $30's, it persuaded CBS to release the partners from any personal liability should the firm stop paying its rent. Wachtell, Lipton won't talk, but many real estate brokers say that was the make-or-break point on the deal.
Time was, corporate leases were just a dozen pages, mainly boilerplate. Today, with sublease clauses and contingencies for foreclosures, they look like the unedited version of "War and Peace."
"Landlords, tenants, sublessees, banks, just about everyone wants to make sure they're covered on all possible problems," said Christopher F. Graham, a partner with the Manhattan law firm of Thacher, Proffitt & Wood.
Modern leases are likely to include clauses on who is responsible for removing asbestos or for making the premises accessible to people with disabilities.
Landlords, clinging to some semblance of control, are demanding codicils restricting subletting. "Sophisticated landlords are now insisting that tenants not sublease to anyone with a lower credit rating than their own," said Paul Rennert of Rennert, Diana Real Estate in Union, N.J.
 
Nonprofit Competitors Even Churches Eye
The Added Revenue
Last year, Chase Manhattan's tellers went to church together. Hundreds of high school students taking S.A.T. preparation classes went to temple. Primerica's shareholders, meanwhile, got to play Carnegie Hall, the site of their company's annual meeting.
"We're not just a concert hall anymore," said Bret Silver, Carnegie Hall's manager of event-sales. "We want to be thought of as a venue, a facility."
Many nonprofit organizations are renting out space -- often competing with for-profit operations in ways that have them skirting dangerously close to violating tax rules on unrelated business income.
It's a nondenominational problem. Ralph M. Birnberg, administrator of Temple Beth El in Hollywood, Fla., noted that more than half the people he spoke to at a recent convention of synagogue administrators complained of huge deficits. "Those that have decent space are viewing it as a wonderful source of income," he said.
Some churches are positively unabashed in their approach. The Universalist Church on Central Park West has a "space available" sign right between its front doors, with a number to call. And, it has a full time marketing director, Fred Seidler, whose sole job is to find rental business.
Apparently, he's been doing the job quite well. Receptions and events, like Chase Manhattan Bank's Teller Appreciation Night last fall, have grown more common.
All told, rentals -- which average $2,500 for weekend events, $1,000 for weekdays -- accounted for more than 85 percent of the church's $460,000 operating budget last year.
 
 
 
A MARKET TOO TIGHT FOR SNOBBERY
 THROUGHOUT the 1970's mid-price stores like Toy City or discounters like Kmart stayed out of New York. The market was too chancy, the rents too rich, the landlords too snooty to welcome a discounter.
Now they're humming "I Love New York."
"This is the best retail real estate market tenants have faced in 25 years," said Charles Aug, president of Garrick-Aug Worldwide, a huge Manhattan-based retail real estate broker.
While the movement of new stores is clearly visible in Long Island and other nearby suburbs, and in Queens, a walk down some of Manhattan's premier shopping streets really tells the tale. Eddie Bauer, known mainly as a mail-order clothing and sporting goods company, has opened shops on 58th Street and Madison Avenue and 45th Street and Third Avenue. The Innovation Luggage discount chain has a store at 43d Street and Fifth Avenue, in space where Nynex once sold computers.
Cosmetics Plus, a discount shop, is on 57th Street, opposite Carnegie Hall. Barnes & Noble used to be there -- also a discount store, true, but one that sold culture so, in the salad days, slipped in as snottily acceptable.
Even co-ops are lowering their standards. Shareholders who once balked at restaurants or any other business that might generate smells now woo them.
"Co-ops used to want only high-image stores," said Neil Kreisel, president of the Kreisel Company, which manages co-ops and condominiums in New York. "Now they'll live with a cheese store and bags of garbage outside, as long as it generates revenues."

HEADLINE: Sound Bytes;
Meeting Challenges With Inward-Looking Focus

BYLINE: By Peter H. Lewis

 BODY:
AST Research Inc. originally made memory and multifunction expansion boards for early I.B.M. personal computers. Now, it clearly has momentum in a fiercely competitive market. As president and chief executive, Safi Qureshey (pronounced KRESH-ee) has presided over such innovations as upgradable PC's, 24-hour computer bulletin board and facsimile customer support, and a segmented product line, ideas that have since been adopted by I.B.M., Apple, Compaq, Dell and others.
Question: Your company is in the top 10 in PC makers and is gaining market share. Why are you successful when so many others have slipped? 
 Answer: It's a matter of focus. No company can do well unless it has a full-time focus. You cannot participate in this business part time. We, along with a few other companies, try not to distract ourselves by going into areas that may look very tempting but that could dilute our resources.
Q: But your new Manhattan multiprocessor system, a powerful network server, broadens your focus, doesn't it?
A: The core technology is derived from our efforts in desktop and notebook computers. I call it complementary expansion. We took the time to do it right. Compaq came out with its multiprocessing machine almost three years ago, and people were asking when we were going to do one. Now the time is right.
Q: What's your biggest challenge?
A: It's internal. As I look outside I see tremendous competition, technology changes, companies going out of business. But the confidence I have is that there's a very large market for personal computers and it is growing, despite the recession and other issues. As we go from just less than $1 billion in sales last fiscal year to more than $1 billion, with the price compression we are producing 60 to 70 percent more computers today than we did last year. The challenge is, how do we create a management infrastructure that can continue to be flexible and responsive as we continue to grow?
Q: What's your growth rate?
A: The industry grew 8, 9, 10, 11 percent last year; I don't know the exact number. We grew close to 40 percent.
Q: How do you keep up with customer support?
A: We have been adding service capabilities. One example: We realized that if we upgraded all the computers that our service technicians use, from 386 to 486, we could cut about 15 to 20 seconds off the time it took to get the right information to the customer. Yeah, it was an added expense, but just the savings on the telephone line was worth it.

HEADLINE: Mutual Funds;
A Fizzle in the Overseas Sizzle

BYLINE: By Carole Gould

 BODY:
THE good, albeit short, track record of Acorn International flies in the face of the recent experience of most overseas investors. The Morgan Stanley Capital EAFE Index, a proxy for the stock markets in Europe, Asia and the Far East, has gained just 1.3 percent a year, on average, in the last five years compared with 16 percent jumps in the Standard & Poor's 500 index.
The EAFE index is heavily skewed toward Europe and Japan, and the disappointing results mainly reflect the recessions in those regions. But many analysts assert that economic cycles in those regions lag the United States and so should soon be ready to turn. 
 Investing overseas has produced profits over the long term, so the recent poor performance of international funds should be seen as an aberration, even as a signal to move money into those markets while they are still near their lows, said John Rekenthaler, editor of Morningstar Mutual Funds in Chicago.

HEADLINE: The Executive Computer;
Paradox for Windows: Worth the Wait?

BYLINE: By Peter H. Lewis

 BODY:
BORLAND International Inc. is finally ready to begin selling Paradox for Windows, its long-awaited data-base management program. As an introductory offer for at least the next 90 days, Borland is charging $139.95 for Paradox for Windows, more than 80 percent off its list price of $795.
Paradox for Windows was expected to be delivered last year, but businesses that have been waiting for a powerful and elegant Windows data base may well find the wait worth it. Initial testers say the program appears to be superior to other Windows data bases for ease of use, the ability to work with other data-base formats on a network and for application development. 
 But if that is good news for Borland, it comes late in the day. The Paradox problem and an even more frustrating delay with Borland's Quattro Pro for Windows spreadsheet have cost the company dearly. Once a darling of Wall Street, Borland stock has been hammered in recent months and the company recently laid off 15 percent of its work force.
The delay for Paradox was especially damaging because in the interim the Microsoft Corporation was able to rush its Access for Windows data base to market, at a special introductory price of $99. Microsoft's $99 promotion is scheduled to expire on Monday, with Access reverting to its list price of $495. But Borland's entry may extend the discount -- and Microsoft has far deeper pockets.
"It's break point for Borland," said Stuart D. Woodring, director of software strategy research for Forrester Research, a consulting firm in Cambridge, Mass. "The delays made it difficult for Borland to compete in the face of the huge user migration to Windows, and Borland was sorely hurt when Microsoft came out with Access."
In this fierce battle, Borland has the two most popular DOS programs, Paradox and dBASE, while Microsoft has Access and Fox Base. (No other company is a significant player in this market.)
Microsoft's bold pricing guaranteed that Access would get in the door, and forced Borland to similar bargain-basement levels. Microsoft has reportedly sold several hundred thousand copies of Access since November, a phenomenal rate, but software analysts say they cannot tell whether businesses are buying it for evaluation (in which case it becomes "shelfware") or whether they are incorporating it into day-to-day activities.
With the broad shift in corporate desktop computing to Microsoft Windows as a de facto operating system, many companies will be more willing to make a major change in data base programs. As long as they have to change applications anyway, to take advantage of Windows, they might as well examine new data base engines.
A company that has relied on the DOS version of Paradox will probably move to its Windows version, but Borland cannot take customer loyalty for granted. The Lotus Development Corporation's Lotus 1-2-3 for Windows, for example, has not been nearly as successful as its DOS versions were; the Wordperfect Corporation dominated the DOS word processing category but has not repeated its success under Windows.
A company's decision to change data-base managers is certainly not taken lightly. Data-base managers are perhaps the most important business application, since they provide the framework and tools for organizing a company's most important information. Accordingly, the reliability of a data base is of paramount importance.
Companies have little tolerance, and rightly so, for software bugs that can cause the corruption or loss of data, and Microsoft's Access 1.0 has been criticized by many early users for problems that can cause system crashes and data loss. Borland officials say they delayed releasing Paradox for Windows until they were sure it was bug-free. But savvy computer users know they should always be cautious with any software designated version 1.0.
A powerful data base like Paradox serves several functions. At the simplest level, it stores records of customers, orders, sales, personnel, inventory and other vital information, and provides a way for users to sift, sort, enter, view and print the data. Most simple Windows data bases can do that.
What sets Paradox and its competitors apart from the low-end programs is built-in programming language, which can be used to craft custom business applications, including on-screen forms and special report generators. Most businesses need the flexibility that programmable data bases offer, since they would rather tailor the software to the way they do business than change the way they do business to match the software.
It is also critical for a data base to be able to work with all the data in a company's computer system, whether the information resides in the user's computer, in a departmental server or in a mainframe somewhere else in the company.
Paradox for Windows has a data-access technology called an Interbase Engine, which can grab data from Paradox, dBASE and other files within Paradox for Windows, and allows the user to view information in a variety of formats. However Borland has not released the software "drivers" that many customers need to gain access to any data in their systems. By midyear, the company says, it will have the ability to connect to Interbase, Oracle, Microsoft Sybase, SQL and DB2 formats.
Paradox for Windows also rises above its competitors by virtue of its use of object-oriented technology. Simply put, each facet of the data-base screen, including graphs, buttons, boxes, forms, fields and so on, is a separate object with its own distinct abilities. And those objects can be used again and again in other projects.
Paradox for Windows "has one of the better front ends I've seen, as far as creating forms," said Cliff Emrich, a programmer at Southern California Edison's San Onofre nuclear power plant in San Clemente, who has been working with preliminary versions of the program for what he said "seems like years."
Using Borland's ObjectPAL programming application language, programmers can concoct Windows data-base applications with relative ease, including complex routines that draw data from a variety of sources.
For people who write software, "it is extremely powerful and offers a lot of features that others don't have," said a programmer for a large health-care company in New York. He added, "The object-orientation makes developing your second application much easier because of the re-useability."
Paradox for Windows certainly isn't perfect, the programmer added. "The big problem is that it's slow. It does seem to be very demanding of memory."

PUB-DATE: January 31, 1993

SECTION: Section 9; Page 1; Column 4; Styles of The Times

LENGTH: 2293 words

HEADLINE: The Word Police

BYLINE: By MICHIKO KAKUTANI

 BODY:
THIS month's inaugural festivities, with their celebration, in Maya Angelou's words, of "humankind" -- "the Asian, the Hispanic, the Jew/ The African, the Native American, the Sioux,/ The Catholic, the Muslim, the French, the Greek/ The Irish, the Rabbi, the Priest, the Sheik,/ The Gay, the Straight, the Preacher,/ The privileged, the homeless, the Teacher" -- constituted a kind of official embrace of multiculturalism and a new politics of inclusion.
The mood of political correctness, however, has already made firm inroads into popular culture. Washington boasts a store called Politically Correct that sells pro-whale, anti-meat, ban-the-bomb T-shirts, bumper stickers and buttons, as well as a local cable television show called "Politically Correct Cooking" that features interviews in the kitchen with representatives from groups like People for the Ethical Treatment of Animals. 
 The Coppertone suntan lotion people are planning to give their longtime cover girl, Little Miss (Ms?) Coppertone, a male equivalent, Little Mr. Coppertone. And even Superman (Superperson?) is rumored to be returning this spring, reincarnated as four ethnically diverse clones: an African-American, an Asian, a Caucasian and a Latino.
Nowhere is this P.C. mood more striking than in the increasingly noisy debate over language that has moved from university campuses to the country at large -- a development that both underscores Americans' puritannical zeal for reform and their unwavering faith in the talismanic power of words.
Certainly no decent person can quarrel with the underlying impulse behind political correctness: a vision of a more just, inclusive society in which racism, sexism and prejudice of all sorts have been erased. But the methods and fervor of the self-appointed language police can lead to a rigid orthodoxy -- and unintentional self-parody -- opening the movement to the scorn of conservative opponents and the mockery of cartoonists and late-night television hosts.
It's hard to imagine women earning points for political correctness by saying "ovarimony" instead of "testimony" -- as one participant at the recent Modern Language Association convention was overheard to suggest. It's equally hard to imagine people wanting to flaunt their lack of prejudice by giving up such words and phrases as "bull market," "kaiser roll," "Lazy Susan," and "charley horse."
Several books on bias-free language have already appeared, and the 1991 edition of the Random House Webster's College Dictionary boasts an appendix titled "Avoiding Sexist Language." The dictionary also includes such linguistic mutations as "womyn" (women, "used as an alternative spelling to avoid the suggestion of sexism perceived in the sequence m-e-n") and "waitron" (a gender-blind term for waiter or waitress).
Many of these dictionaries and guides not only warn the reader against offensive racial and sexual slurs, but also try to establish and enforce a whole new set of usage rules. Take, for instance, "The Bias-Free Word Finder, a Dictionary of Nondiscriminatory Language" by Rosalie Maggio (Beacon Press) -- a volume often indistinguishable, in its meticulous solemnity, from the tongue-in-cheek "Official Politically Correct Dictionary and Handbook" put out last year by Henry Beard and Christopher Cerf (Villard Books). Ms. Maggio's book supplies the reader intent on using kinder, gentler language with writing guidelines as well as a detailed listing of more than 5,000 "biased words and phrases."
Whom are these guidelines for? Somehow one has a tough time picturing them replacing "Fowler's Modern English Usage" in the classroom, or being adopted by the average man (sorry, individual) in the street.
The "pseudogeneric 'he,' " we learn from Ms. Maggio, is to be avoided like the plague, as is the use of the word "man" to refer to humanity. "Fellow," "king," "lord" and "master" are bad because they're "male-oriented words," and "king," "lord" and "master" are especially bad because they're also "hierarchical, dominator society terms." The politically correct lion becomes the "monarch of the jungle," new-age children play "someone on the top of the heap," and the "Mona Lisa" goes down in history as Leonardo's "acme of perfection."
As for the word "black," Ms. Maggio says it should be excised from terms with a negative spin: she recommends substituting words like "mouse" for "black eye," "ostracize" for "blackball," "payola" for "blackmail" and "outcast" for "black sheep." Clearly, some of these substitutions work better than others: somehow the "sinister humor" of Kurt Vonnegut or "Saturday Night Live" doesn't quite make it; nor does the "denouncing" of the Hollywood 10.
For the dedicated user of politically correct language, all these rules can make for some messy moral dilemmas. Whereas "battered wife" is a gender-biased term, the gender-free term "battered spouse," Ms. Maggio notes, incorrectly implies "that men and women are equally battered."
On one hand, say Francine Wattman Frank and Paula A. Treichler in their book "Language, Gender, and Professional Writing" (Modern Language Association), "he or she" is an appropriate construction for talking about an individual (like a jockey, say) who belongs to a profession that's predominantly male -- it's a way of emphasizing "that such occupations are not barred to women or that women's concerns need to be kept in mind." On the other hand, they add, using masculine pronouns rhetorically can underscore ongoing male dominance in those fields, implying the need for change.
And what about the speech codes adopted by some universities in recent years? Although they were designed to prohibit students from uttering sexist and racist slurs, they would extend, by logic, to blacks who want to use the word "nigger" to strip the term of its racist connotations, or homosexuals who want to use the word "queer" to reclaim it from bigots.
In her book, Ms. Maggio recommends applying bias-free usage retroactively: she suggests paraphrasing politically incorrect quotations, or replacing "the sexist words or phrases with ellipsis dots and/or bracketed substitutes," or using "sic" "to show that the sexist words come from the original quotation and to call attention to the fact that they are incorrect."
Which leads the skeptical reader of "The Bias-Free Word Finder" to wonder whether "All the King's Men" should be retitled "All the Ruler's People"; "Pet Semetary," "Animal Companion Graves"; "Birdman of Alcatraz," "Birdperson of Alcatraz," and "The Iceman Cometh," "The Ice Route Driver Cometh"?
Will making such changes remove the prejudice in people's minds? Should we really spend time trying to come up with nonmale-based alternatives to "Midas touch," "Achilles' heel," and "Montezuma's revenge"? Will tossing out Santa Claus -- whom Ms. Maggio accuses of reinforcing "the cultural male-as-norm system" -- in favor of Belfana, his Italian female alter ego, truly help banish sexism? Can the avoidance of "violent expressions and metaphors" like "kill two birds with one stone," "sock it to 'em" or "kick an idea around" actually promote a more harmonious world?
The point isn't that the excesses of the word police are comical. The point is that their intolerance (in the name of tolerance) has disturbing implications. In the first place, getting upset by phrases like "bullish on America" or "the City of Brotherly Love" tends to distract attention from the real problems of prejudice and injustice that exist in society at large, turning them into mere questions of semantics. Indeed, the emphasis currently put on politically correct usage has uncanny parallels with the academic movement of deconstruction -- a method of textual analysis that focuses on language and linguistic pyrotechnics -- which has become firmly established on university campuses.
In both cases, attention is focused on surfaces, on words and metaphors; in both cases, signs and symbols are accorded more importance than content. Hence, the attempt by some radical advocates to remove "The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn" from curriculums on the grounds that Twain's use of the word "nigger" makes the book a racist text -- never mind the fact that this American classic (written in 1884) depicts the spiritual kinship achieved between a white boy and a runaway slave, never mind the fact that the "nigger" Jim emerges as the novel's most honorable, decent character.
Ironically enough, the P.C. movement's obsession with language is accompanied by a strange Orwellian willingness to warp the meaning of words by placing them under a high-powered ideological lens. For instance, the "Dictionary of Cautionary Words and Phrases" -- a pamphlet issued by the University of Missouri's Multicultural Management Program to help turn "today's journalists into tomorrow's multicultural newsroom managers" -- warns that using the word "articulate" to describe members of a minority group can suggest the opposite, "that 'those people' are not considered well educated, articulate and the like."
The pamphlet patronizes minority groups, by cautioning the reader against using the words "lazy" and "burly" to describe any member of such groups; and it issues a similar warning against using words like "gorgeous" and "petite" to describe women.
A S euphemism proliferates with the rise of political correctness, there is a spread of the sort of sloppy, abstract language that Orwell said is "designed to make lies sound truthful and murder respectable, and to give an appearance of solidity to pure wind." "Fat" becomes "big boned" or "differently sized"; "stupid" becomes "exceptional"; "stoned" becomes "chemically inconvenienced."
Wait a minute here! Aren't such phrases eerily reminiscient of the euphemisms coined by the Government during Vietnam and Watergate? Remember how the military used to speak of "pacification," or how President Richard M. Nixon's press secretary, Ronald L. Ziegler, tried to get away with calling a lie an "inoperative statement"?
Calling the homeless "the underhoused" doesn't give them a place to live; calling the poor "the economically marginalized" doesn't help them pay the bills. Rather, by playing down their plight, such language might even make it easier to shrug off the seriousness of their situation.
Instead of allowing free discussion and debate to occur, many gung-ho advocates of politically correct language seem to think that simple suppression of a word or concept will magically make the problem disappear. In the "Bias-Free Word Finder," Ms. Maggio entreats the reader not to perpetuate the negative stereotype of Eve. "Be extremely cautious in referring to the biblical Eve," she writes; "this story has profoundly contributed to negative attitudes toward women throughout history, largely because of misogynistic and patriarchal interpretations that labeled her evil, inferior, and seductive."
The story of Bluebeard, the rake (whoops! -- the libertine) who killed his seven wives, she says, is also to be avoided, as is the biblical story of Jezebel. Of Jesus Christ, Ms. Maggio writes: "There have been few individuals in history as completely androgynous as Christ, and it does his message a disservice to overinsist on his maleness." She doesn't give the reader any hints on how this might be accomplished; presumably, one is supposed to avoid describing him as the Son of God.
Of course the P.C. police aren't the only ones who want to proscribe what people should say or give them guidelines for how they may use an idea; Jesse Helms and his supporters are up to exactly the same thing when they propose to patrol the boundaries of the permissible in art. In each case, the would-be censor aspires to suppress what he or she finds distasteful -- all, of course, in the name of the public good.
In the case of the politically correct, the prohibition of certain words, phrases and ideas is advanced in the cause of building a brave new world free of racism and hate, but this vision of harmony clashes with the very ideals of diversity and inclusion that the multi-cultural movement holds dear, and it's purchased at the cost of freedom of expression and freedom of speech.
In fact, the utopian world envisioned by the language police would be bought at the expense of the ideals of individualism and democracy articulated in the "The Gettysburg Address": "Fourscore and seven years ago our fathers brought forth on this continent a new nation, conceived in liberty and dedicated to the proposition that all men are created equal."
Of course, the P.C. police have already found Lincoln's words hopelessly "phallocentric." No doubt they would rewrite the passage: "Fourscore and seven years ago our foremothers and forefathers brought forth on this continent a new nation, formulated with liberty, and dedicated to the proposition that all humankind is created equal."

HEADLINE: Give Vent to the Public And Watch the Weal

 BODY:
I found John Hurley's letter of Dec. 26 an informative and interesting statement of the problems arising from a lack of citizen involvement in overseeing the public purse strings. Though his focus was on energy and utility matters, there are several areas in the public sphere, especially here in the suburbs, where Mr. Hurley's approach would raise interesting questions for discussions.
Commendations for giving him the space for an important and interesting viewpoint. Citizen involvement may be an old and familiar idea, but it appears to be more honored in its breach than observance. Informed involvement in monitoring the purse strings seems like a tedious and time-consuming undertaking. However, in relieving "we the people" of responsibility, all levels of government seem to be raising our taxes in proportion as the "burden" of informed consent is lightened. 
 More than ever there is a need to hear from the thoughtful and well-educated public that the institutions of government and media do not generally offer access to. Professional columnists sound to me as tired and repetitive as the professional politicians they speak with and about. In the spirit of the recent national political campaign, let's hear from, and about, the rest of us on the editorial page.
 
JOHN BEASLEY
Elmont

HEADLINE: There Comes a Time To Control the Borders

 BODY:
In response to the letter by Patrick Young [Jan. 3] in which he claims that my comments concerning illegal aliens are xenophobic and wrong. If the comment "Why do American citizens, who joyfully help those who come here legally, have obligations to those who criminally entered this country?" is xenophobic, so be it. While he states El Salvadorans who come to this country are not criminals due to their status as war refugees, he does not comment on the illegal aliens from other countries, too numerous to mention. He does not mention that having a fake Social Security card to illegally get a job is a crime. We are not told of the costs through increased taxes to take care of these people's need for special education, bilingual teachers and health care.
He states, "The Statue of Liberty is more than a monument it is an ideal." I agree. As I stated, "The United States admits more legal immigrants than any other country." Unfortunately the time when the United States was an unexplored wilderness with the space for any and all is over. In the past the immigrants who came here were mostly on their own to sink or swim. Now however a vast "safety net" has been mandated in which we pay for those who have entered this country illegally. While I am proud of our country's status as a nation of immigrants, there comes a time which our resources become limited and we cannot expect to receive all of those driven by economics and population pressures out of their countries. Any country must retain some control of its borders.
 
ROBERT F. LA PORTA
Dix Hills
