LENGTH: 1518 words

HEADLINE: Ready or Not, Clinton Is Rattling the Country

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
IN his inaugural address, President Clinton recalled Thomas Jefferson's belief "that to preserve the very foundations of our nation we would need dramatic change from time to time. Well, my fellow Americans," Mr. Clinton said, "this is our time. Let us embrace it." Not everyone was in such an embracing mood last week, but few doubted any longer that the season of change was here. From Capitol Hill to the Pentagon to the radio call-in shows, the message heard across the land was: My fellow Americans, fasten your seat belts.
Mr. Clinton, who was known for a rather messy but nevertheless effective style of governing in Arkansas, insisted that he was not distracted by the frenzy surrounding his decision to lift the ban on homosexuals in the military. The truth be told, Clinton aides say, it was distracting everybody. It was a cacophony, though, that people probably had better get used to, for it could be the theme music for the early Clinton Administration -- a symphony inherent in its very diverse agenda and the personality traits of the boss himself. 
 What happened to Mr. Clinton last week on the issue of homosexuals in the military was that one of his agendas outran the others due to poor planning. Mr. Clinton arrived in Washington with two main programs in his briefcase. One was a social values agenda, which aimed not only at reversing 12 years of Republican policies on issues from abortion counseling at Federally funded clinics to homosexuals in the military, but also at introducing new legislation like the family-leave bill. A lot of this could be implemented through executive orders, with the stroke of a pen -- and that is what he did.
But there was a lot more in the briefcase: plans for economic reform, affordable health care, welfare reform, national service, job creation. These were the big-ticket items of Mr. Clinton's broader agenda for revitalizing the country -- the program for which he was elected. Mr. Clinton's ability to move ahead with these would be the great test of his contention that activist government, in the mold of Franklin D. Roosevelt and Lyndon B. Johnson, can still work to fix mega-problems requiring not just the stroke of a pen but the reshaping of huge institutions and the building of huge coalitions.
What happened was that the first agenda got put in place immediately while the second one was still in the briefcase -- because Mr. Clinton's White House staff was not up and running in time, because many of his top appointees were not in place in the government agencies and because his economic team had not been able to agree on the plan he hoped would be ready by day one. Mr. Clinton was a victim of his own sloppy transition.
Had his economic plans been ready last week, as he had promised, the gays-in-the-military story might have been cast in a somewhat different light, and not left what was probably a false impression, that this is a radical-left Administration more interested in sexual politics than middle-of-the-road economics. Indeed, all early signs are that Mr. Clinton favors economic proposals that tilt toward the conservative deficit-cutters, a foreign policy as cautious as George Bush's, and middle-of-the-road welfare reform. The danger, though, is that first impressions will become lasting impressions.
No wonder, then, that when Mr. Clinton's spokesman, George Stephanopoulos, was asked if the President planned to speak out regularly on behalf of lifting the ban on homosexuals, he could not answer fast enough: "The President is going to speak out now on health care, his economic plan and national service."
If the plot of last week's mini-series was about a new President riding into town with an activist agenda on a political machine without all the wheels screwed on tightly yet, some of the subplots were equally intriguing.
Subplot I: Machiavelli meets Sam Nunn.
Machiavelli in his advice to new princes allowed that it is "much safer to be feared than loved" because "men have less hesitation to offend one who makes himself loved than one who makes himself feared." Translation: Avoid losing your first test of wills with Congress or the Pentagon. Presidents build their authority by asserting their will and sustaining it. That is why Roosevelt would sometimes ask his aides to bring him some bills to veto, knowing that the occasional slam dunk into the circular file is what keeps opponents in line.
Mr. Clinton did not slam dunk anyone on the gay rights issue. He clearly had to make concessions both to the military brass and Senator Nunn, the Georgia Democrat who chairs the Senate Armed Services Committee and who opposed lifting the homosexual ban. It was a reminder to Mr. Clinton that gridlock in Washington comes in many varieties, and that he can have as much trouble with Democratic power barons used to having their own way as with their Republican counterparts across the aisle.
Still, much nonsense is being written about how terrible a blow last week was to Mr. Clinton. It will be a terrible blow if he does not get his health-care package adopted. It will be a terrible blow if he does not get his economic program enacted. But compromising on a highly contentious issue like homosexuals in the armed forces -- a compromise that allows him to still move ahead with lifting the ban while defusing the issue for a while -- is hardly likely to be his Presidency's undoing.
"One week after Clinton takes office people are saying, 'The Presidency is dead -- he did not call Sam Nunn early enough on the gays issue,' or, 'Homosexuals in the army is overwhelming his economic agenda,' " said Thomas Mann of the Brookings Institution. "I heard the networks comparing him already to the failed Carter Presidency. Would everyone please just sit back and take a deep breath for a minute? I mean, really. There will be plenty of time to handicap the Clinton Presidency -- after it is more than a week old."
Subplot II: Zeal at home, caution abroad.
What was striking about Mr. Clinton's first week in office was the way he swung for the fences on the domestic front and laid down bunts in the foreign affairs field. In domestic affairs Mr. Clinton was quick to assert a break with the Bush Administration, but in foreign affairs he was equally quick to assert continuity.
Yes, there has been much talk about his Administration "reviewing" policy options for possible intervention in Bosnia. But Mr. Clinton has just forced the Pentagon to accept the admission of homosexuals; he will soon ask it to accept major spending cuts. His ability to force the Pentagon, on top of all that, to alter its intense opposition to involvement in the Balkan quagmire would now seem to be severely constricted. This is not the time for a President who avoided the draft to call up the cavalry for a dubious mission.
 
Press Resentment
 Subplot III: Mr. Clinton in the press room with a knife.
In perhaps the shortest honeymoon since Zsa Zsa Gabor's last marriage, there is already bad blood between Mr. Clinton and the White House press. It is fed in part by the fact that the established media for the first time in recent memory are covering a President and senior staff their own age if not younger. During the campaign, that may have produced some fawning identification with the Clinton team, but in the period of governing it seems to have created an undercurrent of resentment.
There is also a loose sense in the press corps that Mr. Clinton has not always told the truth, and has gotten away with it, leaving journalists doubly skeptical of his every utterance.
"I think the Clinton people's contempt for the press is left over from the Gennifer Flowers and draft controversies during the campaign," said Larry J. Sabato, professor of government at the University of Virginia. "The Clinton people deeply resented the coverage they got on those issues. What feeds their contempt is a feeling that they survived it all and learned how to operate without the mainstream media, and they plan to go on doing just that."
This tension has helped to create a bizarre disconnect between the attitudes toward the Clinton Presidency inside the Washington Beltway and outside of it. "It's odd," said Representative Jim Leach, an Iowa Republican. "The inside-the-beltway crowd is really setting Clinton up for a fall in the very near future, but the rest of the country desperately wants his Presidency to succeed, and they are still ready to give him time to deliver on the economy and health care."
Subplot IV: The politics of jogging.
When Mr. Clinton went out to jog for the first time after moving into the White House, his escort motorcade found itself driving along sidewalks on the National Mall and heading the wrong way on one-way streets in an effort to keep up with him.
That scene was an apt metaphor for Mr. Clinton's first week in office, when Congress, the military and the public also scrambled to keep up with the new President, who seemed to be following his own unique traffic patterns. The question for the coming months: Who will have the right of way?

HEADLINE: Conversations/Walt Whitman Rostow;
A Vietnam Hawk's Battle Plan For a City's Mean Streets

BYLINE: By ROBERTO SURO

DATELINE: AUSTIN, Tex.

 BODY:
WALT WHITMAN ROSTOW recalls coming home at 3 A.M. from the White House during the 1962 Cuban missile crisis to find his wife, Elspeth, a history professor, sitting cross-legged in bed, grading papers. He remembers exactly what she said: "You haven't been so cheerful since the Second World War."
Now, some 30 years later, Mr. Rostow, who was a foreign policy adviser to Presidents Kennedy and Johnson, is experiencing a not dissimilar good cheer, born of clear purpose and a sense of optimism about overcoming an ominous threat. 
 The economist-diplomat-warrior heads a civic organization called the Austin Project, which immodestly intends to solve the problems of urban America, starting with the capital of Texas. Mr. Rostow, a professor of economics at the University of Texas, has not been a notable figure in local politics, but in little more than a year the project has attracted public officials, foundations, private social service agencies and business leaders.
The City Council voted unanimously last September to begin the first phase of the project's strategy, which calls for greatly expanding and coordinating programs, both private and public, aimed at prenatal care and at aiding disadvantaged children up to the age of 8. This month Mayor Bruce Todd promised to try to double the local spending on those programs in 1993, with help from foundations and the state and Federal governments.
Mr. Rostow, 76 years old, says his approach to the cities is colored by the same optimism that he and others of his generation brought to government when John F. Kennedy became President. As another generation takes over Washington in an era bedeviled by deficits and mistrust of government, Mr. Rostow insists that a robust sense of possibilities is the key to success.
"We were a generation that had seen very bad times in the Depression and during the war, in 1942," he said, "when the nation faced great military danger, and we had become used to saying to ourselves, 'O.K., suck up your gut and get at it, we'll win because we have to.' That wasn't cheap optimism in the Kennedy days, and that spirit has not been lost in this country. You see it today in people young and old when they are committed to an idea."
 
Fighting Pessimism
 He readily acknowledges that years of "cumulative failure and chronic stagnation have left people both in the inner city and outside profoundly pessimistic about these problems, but it is not in the American character to sit by passively and watch the ship go down."
Mr. Rostow proposes a disarmingly simple solution. That may help explain why it has quickly won wide acceptance in Austin.
"You rapidly and drastically increase your prevention efforts with the young until they are equal to the scale of the problem," he said, "and in a short time the investment will pay for itself because you'll pay less for damage control."
An effective prenatal care program that reaches every teen-age mother, for example, will save much more than its cost by reducing the number of low birth-weight babies who need expensive neonatal care. Similarly, programs that succeed in reducing dropout rates produce huge savings in lowered welfare and law enforcement costs.
"It's just a problem of transition," he said. "How are you going to get the investment in prevention high enough to where the level of pathology begins to fall and still keep up the damage control? If you can get through that interval, money won't be a problem."
Things seem simpler in Austin (pop. 465,000) because of its size. Doubling local spending on the programs for children sounds ambitious, but in Austin that only means raising $2.6 million, which the Austin Project reasonably plans to accomplish in a year. And problems still seem to be manageable. Sure, there are street gangs in the black and Hispanic neighborhoods on the east side of town, but the police counted only 37 murders throughout the city last year.
Austin's small scale also makes it easier for a coalition to form. "This country's inherent strength is that it is a highly diffuse society with many points of initiative," he said. "If you can get everyone to agree on a common strategy, then you have a chance of orchestrating all these marvelously variegated elements so each does its part. Whether you are in Austin or New York, there are plenty of dedicated people with good ideas for saving the cities, but usually they do not function together as a unit."
By means of illustration, Mr. Rostow said that earlier that day during a board meeting of the Austin Project, a police officer, a social worker, a university professor and a computer company executive each addressed aspects of the project's strategy.
As in the Atlanta Project, organized by former President Jimmy Carter, and similar efforts in other cities, Mr. Rostow says, the fundamental goal in Austin is "to see how you create an operational unit out of a community." By getting many public and private agencies to agree on a program, he hopes the project will reduce duplication and expenses. But more important is his conviction that a common purpose greatly multiplies ingenuity and commitment.
 
The Good War
 Asked if he had any models, he answered without hesitation, "The extreme model is this country waging a war that commands overwhelming support." No doubts about the strategy and everyone doing their part. The images he drew came from one war in particular: "women working in factories, not buying new automobiles, rationing."
Certain ironies were unavoidable during an afternoon spent with Mr. Rostow. His office at the Lyndon B. Johnson Library is just down the hall from a replica of the Oval Office, where he spent many hours helping direct the Vietnam War.
For someone of a younger generation it was impossible not to ask how that war fit into his model of strategic consensus. After stumbling and digressing a bit he said: "The way we fought the war in Vietnam reminds me of the way we are trying to deal with the cities, running after all the symptoms and putting Band-Aids on them instead of going for the cause." Instead of getting sucked into a guerrilla war in South Vietnam, he says, we should have massed enough ground troops and air power along the Ho Chi Minh trail "because they would have had to stand and fight there or be dead."
As things turned out, he admits, "It was a harrowing way to fight a war."
Mr. Rostow shifts much more readily to that earlier conflict, vividly recalling events like the liberation of Paris. Of a friend from that time he says, "he really had a gallant war."
His visitor, denied such memories and instead recalling frustration and dissent, asks how someone whose war was Vietnam could summon that same bold faith in common purpose typical of the World War II generation.
Mr. Rostow, that rare combination of intellectual and optimist, pauses and a cloud passes over his face. "I just don't know," he says. "It's a mystery to me."

HEADLINE: JAN. 24-30: Blue Chip Blahs;
America's Corporate Giants Cope by Jettisoning Thousands of Jobs

BYLINE: By STEVE LOHR

 BODY:
The economic enigma facing the Clinton Administration -- strong growth but few new jobs -- can be explained by looking at the battered blue chips of corporate America.
Last week, I.B.M. announced that its chief executive, John F. Akers, would step down within three months. A week earlier, the computer giant had reported a $4.97 billion loss for 1992, the largest in corporate history. Since 1986, the International Business Machines Corporation has sliced its work force by 100,000 people and more cuts are planned. 
 In the past two weeks big work force cuts were also announced at Sears, Boeing, United Technologies, McDonnell Douglas and Eastman Kodak.
The job rolls of big companies have been shrinking for years, with the employment of the Fortune 500 companies down more than 3.6 million in the decade ended 1991. But the process seems to have accelerated recently, even though most economic indicators now point upward.
In certain industries, special circumstances are responsible for the turmoil. Defense contractors are struggling to adjust to the post-cold war world. Yet there are forces that affect the entire corporate economy: fast-changing technology, international competition and the deregulation of finance and telecommunications.
"Large, bureaucratic styles of management cannot adjust to these changes," said Daniel Mitchell, a professor at the University of California at Los Angeles graduate school of management, "and that's why the Fortune 500 companies have been shrinking."

HEADLINE: IDEAS & TRENDS;
Usually, the Illegality in Domestic Work Is Benefits Denied

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
FAMILIES who do not pay Social Security and unemployment taxes for their maids and nannies are not just breaking the law. They are denying their household help the pensions and protection against disability and joblessness to which the workers are entitled.
The practice is even more pervasive in affluent communities than the hiring of illegal aliens, which was the focus of most of the attention this month surrounding Zoe Baird and the withdrawal of her nomination to be Attorney General. 
 Take the case of a maid who has worked steadily all her life at the minimum wage. If she retires or becomes disabled today and the proper Social Security taxes have been paid all along, she will receive nearly $500 a month, an amount that will rise along with inflation. If no Social Security taxes have been paid, she will receive nothing. And if the taxes have been paid on only one-quarter of her earnings, she will get only $180 a month.
Or take the case of a nanny who, as often happens, is dismissed when the children are old enough to be home alone. If her unemployment insurance taxes were paid, she can get Government unemployment checks for 26 weeks or more, covering up to half of the weekly wages she had been receiving. No taxes paid, no checks.
These taxes are not optional. If the household worker, even a once-a-week cleaning woman, is paid $50 in any calendar quarter, the employer is required by law to pay Social Security taxes. If the worker is paid $1,000 in a quarter, unemployment tax is due. As Ms. Baird learned, the taxes are owed even if the worker is an illegal alien and has no Social Security card.
But for one reason or another, the vast majority of families with maids or nannies, even ones who are American citizens, do not pay these taxes.
The law is hardly ever enforced, and nothing usually happens to scofflaws. But the workers -- many of whom are members of minority groups, most of whom are poor and nearly all of whom are women -- are cheated.
"This is not a victimless crime," said James C. Corman, a former Democratic congressman from California who now practices law here. "The victims are the poorest among us, the working poor, and they are victimized by people who are among our most affluent."
The Census Bureau reports about 560,000 household workers in the country. The Bureau of Labor Statistics counts about 875,000. Many, of course, work in more than one home.
According to the Internal Revenue Service, about 500,000 taxpayers paid Social Security taxes for household help last year. The I.R.S. has made a rough calculation that 2 million should have paid. There is evidence that that is an underestimate; a survey by the Bureau of Labor Statistics found that about 7 million households spent money in 1991 for "housekeeping services."
Some of those households may not have owed the taxes. The law that determines who is an employee and who is a contract worker, for whom Social Security taxes are paid by someone else, is, like so much else in the tax code, mind-numbingly complicated. The I.R.S. says 20 separate factors must be considered.
But usually it boils down to this: If the maid or nanny is hired and paid by a baby-sitting agency or cleaning service, then she is a contractor. Otherwise, she is an employee. Nancy Duff Campbell, co-president of the National Women's Law Center, said virtually all full-time maids and nannies and most who work part-time are employees, not contractors.
The Social Security and Medicare tax is 15.3 percent, of which half is supposed to be paid by the employer and half withheld from the worker's pay. The unemployment compensation tax varies but is generally about 2 percent of the first $7,000 of wages, all paid by the employer.
 
A Paper Flurry

Representative Andrew Jacobs Jr. of Indiana, who heads the Social Security subcommittee of the Ways and Means Committee, said he did not think most people who failed to pay proper taxes were "willful violators." Many, he said, do not know they must pay taxes. Others are put off by the paperwork -- the quarterly statements to be filed with the I.R.S., the various state forms and the W-2 form totaling the year's wages and taxes, six copies of which must be sent out every January.
More important, many maids and nannies say they would rather work off the books and not have the money withheld. "Poor people in our country are in a terrible double bind," said Nancy Amidei, who teaches at the University of Washington School of Social Work. "Right or wrong, they see the Social Security tax, which would provide for their retirement in the future, as taking food off their table today."
So why doesn't Congress do something? Why doesn't the I.R.S. crack down on violators? Why doesn't the Government advertise the importance of paying Social Security taxes and having benefits accrue?
One reason is that maids and nannies have no organized advocates -- no unions, no lobbies, no one who contributes money to political campaigns. Mr. Corman, the former congressman, offered another reason. "Crimes committed by well-off people against poor people," he said, "do not get as much attention as crimes committed by poor people against well-off people."

HEADLINE: THE WORLD;
A New President Finds the Trade Combatants Acting Up

BYLINE: By JAMES STERNGOLD

DATELINE: TOKYO

 BODY:
AFTER several years watching China dump raw materials for steel-making on the market at unfairly low prices, the Government decided to get tough last week. It imposed import duties on the product, ferrosilicon manganese, and made it clear that such predatory practices would not be tolerated.
The story may sound familiar, but the venue was not Washington. It was here in Tokyo. 
 Japan, the country with the world's largest trade surplus, had for the first time retaliated against a country it accused of dumping. It was showing the world its stern new face on trade policies -- and that included a threat that any American attempt to curb imports from Japan might draw retaliation.
But as much as Japan has worked to recast its image as a trade liberal fighting for free markets, last week it ran into uncomfortable reminders of the old accusations that it uses unfair practices to gain economic advantage at home and abroad.
The United States Commerce Department accused several countries, including Japan, of selling steel at unfairly low prices and said it would impose stiff tariffs. Detroit's Big Three automakers said they are considering filing a suit seeking punitive duties on all imported autos, arguing that European and Japanese car-makers are selling cars for less than they do at home.
And the American Chamber of Commerce in Japan issued a report detailing many barriers it said foreign businesses still face here, despite more than a decade of negotiations. It listed 34 industries where it said discrimination still exists and 14 practices, many by the Japanese Government, that hobble foreign businesses.
The report was careful to note that there have been improvements. But what was most striking about the list -- which included things as odd as an effective restriction on dog food makers offering buy-one-get-one-free promotions and as important as barriers to Japanese car dealers selling imported autos -- was that it made clear that the Clinton Administration is facing the same kind of trade problems with Japan that past administrations were supposed to have cleared up.
"Despite Japanese Government actions to liberalize and internationalize, years of protection and export-driven strategies have shaped an environment inherently unfavorable to foreign firms," said Richard I. Johannessen Jr., president of the chamber and head of the Asian operations of Rockwell International.
For instance, the United States Government made a major effort to open Japan's byzantine transportation and wholesaling system over the last two years. There have been several agreements, but, the chamber's report said, "the overall results have been disappointing."
 
Primed to Compete
 The persistence of these problems has lent weight to those who have argued that Japan's economic playing field is not level because it is not playing the same game as the United States. America's trade policies have been premised on the assumption that if you pinpoint and then remove impediments, the free markets will flourish. The evidence is now supporting the view, many argue, that the Japanese system itself is an impediment and so America should change, in part by adopting even tougher policies.
Chalmers Johnson, an expert on Japanese political economy and industrial policy at the University of California at San Diego, has described the notion at the heart of the Japanese economy as "developmental capitalism." The state is organized, he said, almost solely for the purpose of making Japanese industry and exporters more competitive, and the apparatus for achieving this goal is one that no trade negotiations can dismantle.
On one level, it is hard to dispute such arguments. In 1980, Japan had a $10 billion trade surplus with the United States. Since then, its imports from the United States more than doubled, from $20.8 billion to $48.15 billion in 1991, but its exports to the United States nearly tripled, from $30.9 billion to $91.6 billion in 1991.
The Japanese have admitted their markets have needed to open up, but they have also rejected some of the blame for the trade imbalance, saying it results from the United States' huge budget deficits and a lack of effort on the part of American manufacturers. Some conservative American economists agree.
It took until just a few months ago, for example, for the Chrysler Corporation to import a version of its popular Jeep Cherokee with the steering wheel on the right-hand side for Japanese drivers.
The Compaq Corporation has recently initiated a price war with Japanese computer makers that has shaken up the market, but it took years before Compaq bothered to break into Japan, the world's second-largest computer market.
Now, some influential Japanese are acknowledging that discrimination is an integral part of the Japanese system and are calling for deep changes in the way businessmen and bureaucrats here think.
"We should lend an ear to the criticism that Japan in itself has become a fortress," Akio Morita, the chairman of Sony, said in a recent essay. "Japan has been late in opening its markets to the world and assuring the transparency of those markets. What changes it has made have been piecemeal, caused by direct foreign pressure."
This debate over whether the Japanese economy really is fundamentally different, and how the United States should respond if it is, has been remarkably bitter. There is surprisingly little agreement on the most basic questions since they ultimately reflect on how the United States economy ought to be managed in an increasingly competitive world.
Some Clinton Administration officials have already signaled that they intend to be tougher with Japan and friendlier toward tariffs as levers to force open Japanese markets.
What they cannot yet say is whether the approach will continue to be piecemeal, whether it will be matched by new strategies at home to make American companies more competitive, and whether four or eight years from now the Chamber of Commerce will be issuing the same complaints all over again.


GRAPHIC: The American Chamber of Commerce in Japan released a harsh report last week detailing laws and business practices it said hobble American companies trying to do business there. Among the examples it cited:
 
Foreign law firms still cannot hire Japanese lawyers or form partnerships with Japanese firms. Foreign firms cannot even use their real names: they must be known by the names of the lawyers working in Japan.
 
The Ministry of Transportation dictates what air carriers can charge, what discounts they can offer and services they can provide. It prohibited a foreign airline from offering business-class passengers a limousine pick-up service, which Japan Airlines does not offer.
 
Laws sharply restrict sales promotions, making it hard for newcomers to break into markets. One company cannot discount another's product so, for example, a hamburger chain cannot offer a free soft drink with each purchase. Expensive promotions are restricted, so a hotel cannot offer an extra night's stay, and airlines cannot offer upgrades.
 
Discriminatory import guidelines for wheat and barley make it hard for American grain companies to compete, and enable Japanese trading houses to market American wheat to other Asian countries at prices the American companies cannot match.
 
Cellular telephones cannot be purchased in Japan; they must be leased from the telephone companies.
 
American food companies have been excluded from industry groups discussing new freshness-dating rules.
 
American producers of soda ash, used in making glass, must funnel all imports through a terminal controlled by the four major Japanese producers. The fees are high and frustrating delays are common.

HEADLINE: With Casino Profits, Indian Tribes Thrive

BYLINE: By FRANCIS X. CLINES, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LEDYARD, Conn.

 BODY:
With such adaptations as "wampum" credit cards and waitresses in skimpy maiden tunics, casino gambling on Indian reservations has become a multibillion-dollar industry that is fueling a renaissance of American Indian tribal fortunes and culture.
A movement that began tentatively in 1980 with a court fight over a Florida bingo hall has spread to at least 17 states and 52 tribes operating more than 100 casino and large-scale bingo sites. Many more states, including New York, are negotiating casino agreements. 
 And amid such progress Indians are turning back from diaspora and going home to depressed reservations after three centuries of decline. They are eager to document their blood lines and get in on the upturn in fortunes.
 
A Piece of the Pequots' Pie
 "They see the succcess we're having and would like a piece of the pie," said Joseph J. Carter, who turned away from urban American life a decade ago to join his brother, Gary, and scores of others in reviving the Mashantucket Pequot tribe and creating the 'state of the art' Foxwoods reservation casino here. Both brothers now have important roles in the casino.
The tribe, once down to fewer than a dozen residents on the reservation, now has over 225 returned or rediscovered members, plus a growing list of applicants who insist they are entitled.
"I think it's wonderful that people who suffered adversity for centuries around here finally have a chance to get theirs," said Irving Wizenfeld, a visiting gambler sensing a higher cause in his losses, as the adaptative tribe uses its gambling profits to tutor the young about massacre and disease at the hands of colonialist usurpers. "Isn't that what America is supposed to be about?"
 
Free Tuition and Travel
 Led by its chief and chairman, Richard Hayward, one of the national leaders of the Indian casino movement, the tribe now guarantees college tuition for all its children and world travel for its elders, and it has hired its own archaelogist to disinter lost tribal history.
All this and more, including hotels for visitors and home construction for tribe members, is under way because of the booming profits from its modern, woodsy gambling palace that had to go to 24-hour operations to handle the action soon after it opened last February on the 1,800-acre reservation here, a three-hour drive east from New York City.
No less impressive, the tribe has sought another profit line by branching into casino management consulting. They deal with tribal chiefs from across America who are steadily visiting so they can shape their own grand plans for tribal resurrection.
Within the pastel-toned gambling factory, the sounds of the outside forest are lost to the lucrative whirrings of slot machines and roulette wheels and the clipped intensity of high-stakes poker tables where players are free to set their own limits at hundreds of dollars a bet on the Indians' sovereign grounds.
 
Boon Beyond Reservation
 A second casino building is already under construction, and officials plan to triple the current 3,500-member payroll. This boon extends well beyond the reservation, since most workers are not tribe members and are surviving in a troubled economy depressed by the post-Cold War cutbacks in local armaments industries. Casino officials declined to show a balance sheet, but total bets can range from $500,000 on a slow day to $3 million on a busy weekend day, with the tribe netting at least a third, in the estimate of one Foxwoods official.
Spurred by successes like Foxwoods, Indian casinos have become a $6-billion-a-year industry across the country, with steady growth predicted by Indian gambling officials to total at least $1 billion annually, based on the rush of new casino compacts now in negotiation and under study in Washington. Although Indians control less than 1 percent of the overall American gambling industry, court-tested Federal law gives them far greater power than other casino operators to set up in states where gambling is restricted.
As the Indians pressed for casinos and opposition grew, Congress passed the Indian Gaming Regulatory Act of 1988, outlining compacts between states and tribes. The law allows Federally recognized tribes to negotiate casino agreements in any state where casino-style gambling is allowed, even for limited charity purposes. While most states allow such limited forms of gambling, only Nevada and New Jersey permit full-scale casinos outside reservations.
 
Mohawks and Oneida in Line
 Representatives of tribes in New York are among the Indian leaders visiting the casino here; they are trying to negotiate gambling agreements with state officials. Some New York officials estimate that compacts are likely to be reached with the St. Regis Mohawks and Oneida Nation, although this would be only the opening step in a laborious entrepreneurial exercise that, for all its intricacies, is being attempted by dozens of tribes across the country.
"They're getting very inventive out there," said Bob Walker, a spokesman for the Federal Department of the Interior, which oversees the compacts between the states and the tribes under the 1988 law.
"Tribes are marrying up with private gambling interests lately," he said, noting proposals for tribal-sponsored casinos in downtown Detroit and Wichita, Kan., in suburban Chicago and a dozen other places far removed from reservations.
This could be done by ceding city lands in trust to tribal sponsors as reservation sanctuaries, an approach that leaves Federal officials wary. Abetted more and more by commercial gambling specialists from established casino spas like Atlantic City and Las Vegas, the projects even include a proposed casino in Iowa co-sponsored by an out-of-state tribe, the Santee Sioux of Nebraska, and a Nevada resort casino.
 
America's Grimmest Past
 But the Indians' more certain path is by individual tribal compacts, which most states are mandated by Federal law to negotiate. The Indians' new casinos and high-stakes bingo halls from these compacts have already created tens of thousands of jobs. The most inviting ones are going to their own people, whose families have suffered some of the grimmest experiences of poverty, prejudice and denied opportunities in America.
In Minnesota, for example, 5,000 jobs have been created at a dozen reservation gambling sites. The Mille Lacs band of Chippewas, which in recent years had an unemployment rate of 45 percent, with major health and alcoholism problems, now reports nearly full employment after a new casino began producing $15 million in annual revenues and opened a world of community services.
"Gambling has proved vital to us," said Reid A. Walker, a member of the Mandan tribe of North Dakota and an executive of the National Indian Gaming Association, an association spearheading the casino movement for 70 tribes.
"It has brought us great hope. It's a renaissance for Indian country, giving us power to start our own programs in health care, infrastructure, education and more."
But lately, says the association, various states have been resisting harder in state courts and attempting greater regulation as the Indians' potential becomes clear. In Idaho, for example, voters in a referendum last November favored banning casino-type gambling. A similar referendum is being held on restricting existing Indian casinos in Wisconsin.
 
Reservations Are Sovereign
 But the only way a state can head off Indian casinos is to bar casino-type games totally throughout the state, often a complex constitutional move that risks the opposition of church and other charity groups. The Indians' gambling sovereignty is rooted in the Supreme Court's decision in 1976 that while states may have some civil and criminal jurisdiction over Indian tribes, they do not have regulatory powers.
Here in Connecticut, the Mashantucket Pequots, after having to sue the state earlier to force it into negotiations, dealt cleverly with the threat of competition in recent weeks by offering $100 million of its annual revenues to the state as community aid. In return, Gov. Lowell P. Weicker Jr., who has resisted casino gambling beyond the reservations, gave the Indians permission to use slot machines and thus apparently defused growing pressures to extend casino gambling to needy cities.
In theory, the Indians might police their gambling alone, but the Foxwoods chieftains are advising other tribes to cooperate closely with outside authorities, including state police, the Federal Bureau of Investigation and even the Central Intelligence Agency, in guarding against inroads from organized crime.
 
'All About the Bad Guys'
 "We want to know all about the bad guys," said Mr. Carter of Foxwoods. The security here, with workers in green blazers watching quietly and with videotapes recording through mirrored windows, has already led to the arrest of criminal figures attempting a credit-card scam at the casino, Mr. Carter said. He says the Indians' own sense of tribalism is itself a barrier to the underworld.
The tribes stress the far more tangible effects of casino gambling. There is the new power plant, 950-unit housing development, fire, police and court systems, universal employment, health care and education for the once impoverished Cabazon band of Mission Indians in California who established the first reservation casino a decade ago. There is the $86-million annual operating budget of Wisconsin's Oneida tribe. There is the 600-year-old Sandia tribe in New Mexico, renewing its roots and culture with $16 million in annual gambling revenues and helping the Albuquerque economy.
Mr. Carter's personal history on the reservation began with a $5-an-hour job clearing trees for the casino site. Now he does tribal public relations at "somewhere between $55 and $80 an hour," he said with a smile. His duties require travel to Atlantic City, Las Vegas and other casino spas where he can finally place a bet, something tribal officials do not permit their members here at the Foxwoods tables.
"You need money to practice sovereignty," was the lesson drawn by Foxwoods' Mr. Carter, wandering his tribe's crowded casino here. He savors the flood of would-be Indians, lately petitioning the chieftains over hoped-for tribal ties, as much as he values the gamblers wielding their wampum cards with that endless hunch that they can be winners. "Hey, everybody wants to be a Mashantucket," Mr. Carter declared, surveying the latest action.

HEADLINE: SOME SEE RICHES IN TROUBLED LOANS

BYLINE: By SAUL HANSELL

 BODY:
For the first time since real estate prices collapsed three years ago, the nation's banks are finding eager buyers for the troubled loans and repossessed property that have pushed many of them to the brink of collapse.
Since 1990, banks had been able to do little to rid themselves of their real estate problems other than watch their $80 billion in bad real estate debts lose value, and wait for prices to rebound. But now, real estate executives at the largest banks are being deluged with telephone calls from people and companies offering to buy their troubled assets -- property that banks have repossessed, or on which the owners are no longer keeping up their loan payments.
 
A Chance for a Killing
 This developing market means a reprieve not only for the banks but for the Federal Government. Troubled real estate loans were the main reason several large banks failed in recent years, costing the Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation billions of dollars and raising the prospect of a costly Federal bailout. 
 Potential buyers of the troubled properties include large companies like the General Electric Capital Corporation, Wall Street houses like First Boston and wealthy individuals like the Texas investor Robert Bass. Most of them became interested in buying distressed properties cheaply as the Government sold off big pools of real estate it had taken over from failed savings and loan associations; but by now, most of that property has been sold, and these investors are looking for more bargains elsewhere.
They are prepared to buy as much as $20 billion to $30 billion worth of real estate from the banks, says Stephen A. Roth, president of the Secured Capital Corporation, who helped auction properties for the Government's Resolution Trust Corporation. They are looking for annual returns of 20 percent or more and are offering to pay about half the original value of a property.
This is forcing the bankers to choose whether to take cash today or to sit on the assets themselves, holding out for the possibility of a higher price later.
"The banks have to make a choice," said Mr. Roth, who has also turned his attention to the bank portfolios. "There is now a relatively efficient market. The issue now is price."
Which option they choose could well be the most important issue bank executives face this year. If banks are able to persuade regulators and investors that the last of their loan problems are behind them, their stock prices will rise. That would allow them to merge with other banks, bringing the consolidation that experts say is necessary for the industry to return to health.
But there is a risk to the strategy, too. If the banks sell their assets too quickly and too cheaply, they will deprive themselves of much needed capital and further weaken the financial system.
 
'Better Off Selling'
 As real estate prices have edged upward, more and more banks have opted to take that risk. Late last year, both Fleet Financial Group, of Providence, R.I., and Nationsbank, of Charlotte, N.C., sold large packages of real estate assets, originally valued at more than $500 million each, to investment groups.
"We felt we were better off selling these problem assets in the marketplace and taking the proceeds and investing them in earning assets," said James H. Hance Jr., chief financial officer of Nationsbank, the country's fourth largest bank.
And just last Tuesday, the BankAmerica Corporation said it had agreed to sell assets originally valued at about $2 billion at half that price to a special corporation set up by the Morgan Stanley Real Estate Fund, an investment pool with funds from institutional investors.
These transactions are encouraging other bankers to consider these bulk sales.
"At first, the buyers could drive a pretty hard bargain, but the prices have been getting more reasonable on both sides," said Robert S. Strong, a senior vice president in charge of real estate for the Chase Manhattan Bank. "We are increasingly optimistic that we may see deals that make sense, " he said. Chase has about $3 billion in troubled real estate loans that it will sell if the price is right, Mr. Strong said.
 
Some Banks Are Cautious
 But other banks, including Citicorp, the nation's largest real estate lender, and Wells Fargo & Company, argue that the prices offered by these speculators are still far too low, and say they intend to sell their properties one at a time over the next few years.
The Chemical Banking Corporation recently offered pieces of its $12 billion real estate portfolio to leading buyers of real estate in bulk. It found that the bids were 15 to 30 percent below what it estimated it could get for the properties individually.
"The discounts in the bulk sale market are absurd," said Joseph A. DeLuca, Chemical's executive vice president in charge of real estate finance. "We have time and again been able to achieve better prices selling properties individually." With Chemical's capital strength and earnings, he went on, "we don't have to pursue an uneconomic path for a short-term gain."
The bank sold $150 million in real estate last quarter and Mr. DeLuca expects to continue selling properties at that pace. "We want to get it done quickly, but we want to get it done well."
To many in the real estate industry, the banks are being unrealistic in the prices they are asking. The banks, used to setting prices in calmer times, assume that a price that gives an investor a 13 to 15 percent return on a property is fair. But the real estate crisis has left many investors needing higher returns to compensate them for the risk in the market, and has made them willing to pay less.
"Look at the risk involved in owning strip shopping centers today," said Thomas J. Barrack Jr., chief executive of Colony Capital Inc., a fund that is affiliated with General Electric Capital, the Robert Bass Group and the Cargill Corporation. "There is no liquidity in the market."
It is just these risks that makes some banks eager to sell, even at 50 cents on the dollar.
"When you're in a falling market, our feeling is the smartest thing to do is take the first reasonable offer," said Allen D. Brown, executive vice president of First Interstate Bancorp in Los Angeles. "I don't want to sit here a year from now having to take another write down on a property I could have gotten out the door this year."
While First Interstate has been quick to get out of its troubled real estate, it has generally sold property to local investors individually or in smaller pools of less than $20 million, rather than through bulk sales.
Similarly, even though Citicorp has $13 billion in real estate assets in North America, $6 billion of it troubled in some way, it still wants to sell properties individually.
"At the end of the day all real estate is sold retail, one building at a time, to the end users," said Robert A. McCormack, Citicorp's executive vice president in charge of North American real estate. "A financial intermediary will take a group of properties off your hands wholesale, but will charge you a hell of a price," he said.
Citicorp, which has 500 people to manage its $13 billion North American real estate portfolio, can operate and sell the properties more efficiently than most investment funds, he said.
While Citicorp may be an exception, the real estate companies argue that most banks do not have the resources to manage the properties properly so they can be sold for the highest value. "Are banks able to do what is necessary to get the most value out of these assets?" asked James M. Allwin, president of Morgan Stanley Realty. "In many cases the marketplace feels others may do better."
Indeed, Nationsbank calculates that each year it costs 10 to 20 percent of the value of a loan to manage the properties and administer the problem loans, Mr. Hance said. Moreover, by getting rid of the problems, the bank can reassign the bankers who have been watching them to winning new business, he said.
Even if the hard numbers argue for keeping real estate rather than selling at current prices, some banks argue that there is considerable benefit to creating the appearance of taking tough, decisive action.
"We wanted a clean slate to re-energize our earnings and re-energize the morale of our people," said John W. Ballantine, chief credit officer of the First Chicago Corporation, which has put $2.1 billion in real estate up for sale. "Also, quite frankly, we wanted to get the stock price up."
Investment bankers, looking to cut short the debates over what the future prices of real estate, have concocted ways for banks to sell their assets now and still retain some benefit if the market rises more than expected.
The BankAmerica transaction is an example. The troubled assets were sold to a shell corporation, which will raise money by selling bonds and stock. BankAmerica will invest somewhat less than $100 million in that stock, distributing it to its shareholders. If the real estate is eventually sold for more than a certain amount, the shareholders will receive part of the proceeds.
Over the next year, any bank with more than $100 million in real estate will want to consider this sort of structure, said Edward S. Annunziato, an investment banker at Merrill Lynch who specializes in commercial banks. "The banks know it is a bad time to sell, but cosmetically they are forced to," he said. "So they can put together a package where the shareholders can get something back later."

HEADLINE: CLINTON WEIGHING FREEZE OR NEW TAX ON SOCIAL SECURITY

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 30

 BODY:
President Clinton's economic advisers and Democratic Congressional leaders have concluded that retirees receiving Social Security benefits and Government pensions should pay a significant part of the price of deficit reduction, officials in the White House and Congress said this week.
But the advisers and the top lawmakers disagree over how they should pay: whether through limiting cost-of-living increases in benefits or by increasing taxes on benefits. President Clinton has not come down on either side, the officials said in interviews, but they added that Senate leaders had warned him that any attempt to freeze benefits would be rejected by the Senate.
 
Political Dangers Ahead
 Over the years, cuts in Social Security have been thought to represent political suicide. But Administration officials and Democratic lawmakers seem to agree that they cannot exempt the elderly when asking all Americans to make equal sacrifices to reduce the budget deficit. 
 The mere fact that the Democrats are talking about either limiting Social Security payments or taxing them more heavily is an indication of how difficult it will be to lower the deficit and how serious the new Administration is about tackling the problem. The 30 million retirees who receive Social Security checks each month are a powerful political force.
 
Many Tough Choices
 But one official said Social Security cuts were being considered because "we have picked the bones clean of the easy political choices, and none are left."
The White House said Mr. Clinton and his advisers were working on the economic plan at the Presidential retreat at Camp David, Md., over the weekend. Another meeting with Congressional leaders is scheduled for Monday evening.
Social Security is only one of scores of difficult budget decisions in the offing. Other elements of the deficit-reduction package will almost certainly involve higher taxes on the wealthy, a new energy tax, stiffer enforcement of tax laws affecting foreign corporations with operations in the United States and reduced spending for the military and various domestic programs.
Some of those measures could also be politically unpopular. But Mr. Clinton's goal of cutting the current $300 billion deficit nearly in half by the time he leaves office cannot be accomplished without political cost.
 
1-Year Freeze Proposed
 Providing an account of a meeting at the White House last Wednesday, the officials said that Leon E. Panetta, the budget director, strongly advocated a one-year freeze in cost-of-living increases for Social Security recipients and retired Federal workers who receive civilian or military pensions. Social Security checks, which now average about $650 a month, rise each year to reflect the previous year's rate of inflation.
Mr. Panetta's plan was supported by Representative Richard A. Gephardt of Missouri, the House majority leader, but Senators at the meeting vigorously opposed it, saying the plan stood no chance of passage in the Senate.
The Senators -- including George J. Mitchell of Maine, the majority leader; Daniel Patrick Moynihan of New York, chairman of the Finance Committee, and Jim Sasser of Tennessee, chairman of the Budget Committee -- said it would be fairer to make a larger portion of Social Security benefits subject to income taxes, a method that they said would raise at least as much revenue as a freeze.
Under current law, retired couples with annual incomes above $32,000 and individual retirees with incomes above $25,000 must pay income tax on half of their Social Security benefits.
Treasury Secretary Lloyd Bentsen, a former Senator from Texas, tended to side with the Senators, according to reports from the meeting; President Clinton is reported to have said little and did not indicate where he stood.
Mr. Clinton's spokesman, George Stephanopoulos, said Thursday that Social Security changes were under consideration but that no decisions had been made.
 
Bitter Fiscal Medicine
 Mr. Clinton has said publicly that he hopes to propose measures next month to cut the deficit by $145 billion in the 1997 fiscal year, the last year of his term. Unless some action is taken, the deficit that year will be $319 billion, the Congressional Budget Office projected this week.
During his Presidential campaign, Mr. Clinton said he would oppose any reduction in Social Security benefits, but he said he might be willing to support a higher tax on those benefits in some circumstances.
Some lawmakers believe the Administration is putting out word that the inflation adjustments might be frozen so that when less painful medicine is prescribed it can be swallowed more easily.
 
10 Percent of Burden
 John Rother, executive director of the American Association of Retired Persons, one of Washington's most influential lobbies, suggested in an interview that the Administration would have a big fight on its hands if it proposed any changes in Social Security taxes or benefits. "If the standard is shared sacrifice," he said, "then people can sacrifice in any one of several ways. It's not fair to ask the same people to be hit on Medicare and Social Security, too."
The Administration is considering an increase in Medicare insurance premiums for upper-income retirees.
A one-year freeze in cost-of-living increases next year would save as much as $15 billion in 1997, or about 10 percent of the total deficit reduction being sought. Taxing a larger share of Social Security benefits or lowering the income at which taxes are imposed could raise at least that much revenue.
Pensions for Government workers who retire from the military or civilian jobs are already fully taxed.
Gov. Roy Romer of Colorado, the chairman of the National Governors Association, which is meeting here, today endorsed the idea of putting Social Security proposals on the table as the Administration considers ways to cut the deficit. He said the governors want to have a role in drafting deficit-reduction policies and would meet with Mr. Clinton this week.
 
Contrasting Precedents
 Retirement benefits account for about a fourth of the entire Federal budget, and Administration officials and the Congressional leaders agreed that deficit reduction would be impossible if those benefits were left untouched.
Changes in the law that affect pensioners adversely are never easy politically, but they are not impossible either. In 1983, with the support of the Reagan Administration and Democrats in Congress, cost-of-living increases were delayed for six months to put the Social Security system on a sounder financial footing.
On the other hand, a political firestorm erupted in 1988 when Congress imposed a surtax on the most affluent 40 percent of retirees to pay for Medicare coverage of catastrophic illness. The law was repealed the next year.
 
House vs. Senate
 The officials who reported on the meeting Wednesday night gave this account of the arguments:
Mr. Panetta emphasized the importance of a public perception that sacrifice would be borne evenly. He said the Administration's goal was a deficit-reduction package that contained twice as much savings from spending reductions as new revenues from tax increases. That ratio would be difficult to meet, he said, if the tax on Social Security pensions was increased and the benefits were not reduced.
Supporting Mr. Panetta, Mr. Gephardt maintained that as a political matter, retirees would feel the cost-of-living once, early in Mr. Clinton's term, and would not hold it against the Democrats four years from now if the overall economy had improved. On the other hand, he said, a permanent tax increase would be felt year after year when retirees fill out their tax forms.
The Senators countered that freezing the inflation adjustments would mean little to wealthy retirees but would push hundreds of thousands of pensioners into poverty. Taxing more of the benefits would have its greatest effect on upper-income retirees; poorer retirees do not generally owe income taxes.

GRAPHIC: Chart: "The President's Economic Plan Takes Shape"
 
President Clinton and his economic advisers insist that no final decisions have been made about the economic program the President plans to present to Congress Feb. 17.
 
But the shape of some aspects of the program and the general direction of some others are clear. In public statements, Mr. Clinton and others in his Administration have mentioned these elements.
 
OVERALL PRINCIPLE
With immediate tax cuts and new spending, Mr. Clinton would allow the budget deficit to rise slightly this year to give the economy a quick stimulus. But for the longer term, he would like to lock into place this year tax increases and spending reductions that would reduce the deficit, now about $300 billion, by $145 billion in the last year of his term.
 
 TAX INCREASES
The top tax rate, now 31 percent, would be raised to at least 36 percent for families with incomes above $200,000.
A surtax, probably 10 percent, would be imposed on income above $1 million.
The minimum tax, owed by taxpayers with big deductions or a large amount of tax-exempt income, would be increased.
A new tax on all forms of energy is under consideration. A separate gasoline tax and a tax on carbon emissions have been ruled out.
New measures would be taken to collect more taxes from foreign companies doing business in the United States.
 
STIMULUS
The deficit this year would be allowed to rise by slightly more than $20 billion. One-third of that money would be spent on public works projects like roads and bridges; one-third would be spent for job-training and education, and one-third would involve tax breaks for business to encourage new investment.
 
TAX CUTS
Businesses would be allowed to lower their tax payments by a percentage, probably 10 percent, of the money they spent on new machinery and equipment. Rules would limit this tax credit to investments that otherwise would not have been made. The capital gains tax would be reduced on investments made in small businesses and held for at least five years.
 
RETIREMENT BENEFITS
Either cost-of-living increases in Social Security benefits would be limited or more income taxes would be imposed on these benefits.
Upper-income people would be required to pay more for Medicare insurance covering doctors' fees.
The pensions of retired Government employees, military and civilian, would be reduced in some way.
 
MILITARY SPENDING
Pentagon spending over the next four years would be cut by at least $40 billion below what the Defense Department had been anticipating.
 
WORKERS
The minimum wage, now $4.25 an hour, would be increased annually to keep pace with inflation.
Workers now unemployed would be allowed more weeks of unemployment compensation. (pg. 24)

HEADLINE: CLINTON PLANNING A SHIFT OF FOCUS

BYLINE: By GWEN IFILL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 30

 BODY:
Anxious to move beyond a fight over homosexuals in the military that President Clinton felt was forced on him prematurely, the Administration plans an aggressive campaign in February to focus on the middle-class issues, like health care, that helped Mr. Clinton win election.
The President has acknowledged that he fell short of his goal when he accepted a compromise on Friday allowing the ban on homosexuals to remain largely in place for six months. But in the wake of the agreement, White House officials made it clear that they considered the gay ban a peripheral issue and now plan to turn the discussion to health care, the economy and restructuring the welfare system. 
 Even though the issue looms as another potential problem six months from now, Administration officials say they are confident that voters demand no more from the President than that he stand by his principles.
"To even suggest you're going to win on an issue like this is crazy," said George Stephanopoulos, the White House communications director. "All you can hope to do is limit your losses."
 
Middle-Class Agenda
 Beginning with a meeting next week on health care with the nation's governors and leading up to State of the Union speech on the economy Feb. 17, Mr. Clinton hopes to persuade voters to forget his stumbling start and think instead about issues that surveys show continue to resonate with voters.
"You'll see action on ideas like health care, welfare reform, reciprocal service and the responsibility of people to give something to their country," said one close Clinton adviser. "All of that is solid middle-class, value-based agenda."
If this approach works, his advisers hope that most Americans will have trouble remembering the name of Zoe Baird, his failed nominee for attorney general, let alone the battles lost with Senator Sam Nunn and Gen. Colin L. Powell over the ban on homosexuals.
By moving quickly over the trouble spots and on to the subjects he wants to build his Presidency around, one Clinton adviser said the President plans to "shut down one kind of conversation" and shift to another.
 
Focus on Leave Bill
 The first order of business next week will be to win Congressional passage of the family and medical leave measure that Mr. Bush vetoed and that Mr. Clinton has pledged to sign. That measure is expected to be voted on in the House and Senate this week.
White House officials said that Mr. Clinton was also planning to name a welfare task force that would begin to put meat on the bones of the President's campaign pledge to "end welfare as we know it" and satisfy moderate and conservative Democrats who had feared the White House was backing away from the issue.
And in appointing his wife, Hillary Rodham Clinton, to run a task force on health care that is charged with presenting a comprehensive plan to Congress within his first 100 days in office, Mr. Clinton has signaled the importance of yet another social issue.
"People are fascinated with Hillary Clinton," said Mandy Grunwald, a political consultant who advises the Clinton White House. "And that fascination is now attached to an issue that they have a deep concern about. Anything about the economy and anything about health care is listened to with much more acute ears."
 
Meeting With Governors
 The President plans to use the annual winter meeting of the nation's governors, a old favorite platform, for his announcements this week. He has invited the Governors to a two-and-one-half-hour private meeting on Monday and will speak at the meeting's plenary session on Tuesday.
"His agenda's their agenda," one state official said. "It's all domestic policy, welfare reform, Medicaid, infrastructure. There's a close alignment on the issues."
But the Republicans do not plan to cede the stage to Mr. Clinton so easily, and Senator Bob Dole of Kansas, the minority leader, made clear that he intended to do his part to keep the spotlight trained on the Administration's mistakes.
The Clinton forces are already preparing their defense should Senate Republicans, as expected, attempt to make the gay ban law by attaching it to the family leave bill with the hope that the President will be forced to sign a provision he opposes or veto a bill he dearly wants.
It is important to Mr. Clinton that he snatch the spotlight back. In his news conference on Friday, Mr. Clinton made it clear that he longed to return to a different set of priorities.
"My Administration came to this city with a mission to bring critical issues of reform and renewal and economic revitalization to the public debate, issues that are central to the lives of all Americans," Mr. Clinton said in a statement that was little noticed amid the uproar over homosexuals in the military.
Geoffrey Garin, a veteran Democratic consultant who does not work for the White House, said Mr. Clinton had a good chance to turn the discussion around and determine what news headlines will say about him in coming weeks.
"The next two weeks are an incredibly important two weeks," Mr. Garin said. "It is important to set the stage for the announcement of his economic program. He has to give people a way to think about it, define the terms of the debate and ultimately that will occur.
"There is a lot riding on the next two weeks," he added. "In some respects, it is to Clinton's advantage to play to that drama."

HEADLINE: $105 Million Poorer Now, Chain Mends Race Policies

BYLINE: By RONALD SMOTHERS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: NASHVILLE, Jan. 29

 BODY:
For more than 20 years, some of its employees charged, the Shoney's restaurant chain deliberately shunted blacks into low-paying and low-visibility jobs when it hired them at all and clearly showed a preference for white servers, cashiers and managers.
This week the Nashville-based company was spared a trial on the accusations when a Federal judge in Florida gave final approval to a consent decree ending a four-year lawsuit over racial discrimination in hiring. 
 Without admitting wrongdoing, Shoney's Inc. agreed to pay $105 million over the next five years mostly to an estimated 10,000 blacks who either worked for or were denied employment by the company in the last seven years, according to the company and terms of the settlement. Some white employees who were dismissed for not following orders to dismiss blacks will also receive money.
The settlement is described by lawyers and industry analysts as one of the nation's largest in a racial discrimination suit.
 
'A Changed Company'
 Taylor J. Henry Jr., Shoney's chairman and chief executive, said that even before the settlement the company was changing. "The suit focused our priorities on doing what is right," he said in an interview. "We are a changed company, and we regret any mistakes we made in the past."
The settlement, tentatively reached last November, was negotiated by the company, private lawyers and the NAACP Legal Defense and Educational Fund, which represented the employees as a class. Judge Roger Vinson of Federal District Court in Pensacola, Fla., approved it on Monday.
The suit, filed in 1989 by several Shoney's employees in Pensacola, charged that the chain's founder and former chairman, Raymond L. Danner, ordered managers to dismiss blacks when he believed that there were too many employed at a particular restaurant and that he once offered to match any employee's contribution to the Ku Klux Klan. They said Mr. Danner, 68 years old, frequently used racial slurs when referring to black workers.
 
Items in Settlement
 The settlement, which covers the period from Feb. 4, 1985, to Nov. 3, 1992, could mean as much as $100,000 each to some original plaintiffs. The amounts for others will depend on how long they worked for Shoney's or how many times they applied for employment or promotions and were denied.
In addition, the settlement calls for the company to embark upon an aggressive affirmative-action program in hiring and promotions over the next 10 years. The plan sets goals that the company must reach in staffing its 759 company-owned restaurants. It envisions blacks accounting for 20 percent to 23 percent of managers and assistant managers and 3 of the 25 regional directors by 1998.
The company owns or franchises 1,803 restaurants in 36 states under the names Shoney's, Captain D's, Lee's Famous Recipe Chicken, The Fifth Quarter and Pargo's. The consent decree covers only the company-owned restaurants.
Kerry A. Scanlan, a lawyer with the NAACP Legal Defense and Education Fund who worked on the case, said one of the most significant aspects of the settlement was the program to seek out black applicants for entry-level positions, because Shoney's has historically promoted its managers from within the ranks.
"It is a field where you can, with little education and a lot of hard work, work your way up to a management job and make an extremely good salary," Mr. Scanlon said. "This is a part of our economy that is going to grow and where jobs will be secure."
Dewitt Nelson, one of the original plaintiffs, was dismissed from Shoney's in 1989 after four years' work in the kitchen. He said that although the settlement came too late for him to advance at Shoney's it "will let some other black people be promoted."
Terry Toney, who had managed one of the company-owned restaurants here and who is one of the whites likely to participate in the settlement, said he learned from Mr. Danner personally the company's attitude toward black workers.
Mr. Danner stated his "philosophy" of keeping the number of black employees in line with the proportion of black customers, Mr. Toney said in a recent interview, and "shortly thereafter he fired me because I didn't do what he told me to and I didn't fire some black employees."
 
An Active Boss
 Mr. Danner, a former professional musician and tool and dye maker, grew up in humble circumstances in Louisville, Ky., and built the company from a single Shoney's Big Boy restaurant in Nashville in 1959 into a $1-billion-a-year business. Court papers depict him as a man with an intense management style who was known to visit his restaurants unexpectedly and pitch in if they were very busy or close them temporarily if he found them unsatisfactory.
Shoney's said Mr. Danner would not comment on the settlement, but according to his own deposition in the suit, he was not shy about sharing his theories about hiring blacks.
"I have on occasion given my opinion that a possible problem area was that the specific store in question had too many black employees working in it as compared to the racial mix of the geographical area served by the store," Mr. Danner said in the deposition.
According to a deposition by Mike Vinson, a manager of Shoney's restaurants in the Prattville, Ala., area, managers with what were considered too many black employees in serving positions were often told with a wink that it was to "too cloudy" in the restaurant and were directed to "lighten it up some." At other times, another one white manager, Daniel Gibson, said in his deposition, Mr. Danner was blunter, saying, "I don't like niggers, and I don't want to see them in my stores."
As a result, lawyers for the plaintiffs said, blacks accounted for 38.6 percent of Shoney's kitchen workers in 1989, 8.4 percent of its servers, 3.7 percent of its mid-level managers and trainees and 1.8 percent of the managers.
Mr. Henry, the chairman, would not comment on the accusations against Mr. Danner. He said that in recent years Mr. Danner had removed himself from the company's daily operations though he remained on its board. He also said that since the suit was filed Mr. Danner had done much to aid civil rights groups and historically black colleges.
Thomas Warren, a Pensacola lawyer who filed the original suit, said the company "had made an enormous change in a positive way" even before the settlement. For example, he said, it now has about 10,000 black employees scattered throughout the company, whereas three years ago there were only 5,000, concentrated in lower-paying jobs.
"The tone of the negotiations and their willingness to agree to some rather overbearing provisions tell me that this company has learned that they don't want to go through this again," Mr. Warren said. "I'm optimistic and excited about what we can do in the way of affirmative action over the next 10 years."

HEADLINE: Democrats Live It Up (a Bit) Before Biting Bullet (Maybe)

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD KRAUSS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BALTIMORE, Jan. 29

 BODY:
Aside from the coq au vin dinner, the twist contest and the spectacle of Dan Rostenkowski carousing in a conga line with a tablecloth draped over his head, this year's House Democratic retreat was hardly any fun at all.
The Representatives and their spouses were invited by their leaders to cut loose a bit and to celebrate the envisioned end of gridlock on Capitol Hill. But the two-day conference at Johns Hopkins University that ended on Friday was a party that only a policy maven untethered to electoral politics could love. 
 The food and the disc jockey were good, but the news was bad: The country is saddled with high debt, low savings and workers who do not produce enough. And in a reversal of the logic most Representatives find easiest to follow, lecturers opined that taxes are too low and spending is too high.
The 150 Representatives who attended the conference here heard one senior member of the Administration after another plead with them to swallow the bitter medicine of austerity. "There will be tough votes ahead," Treasury Secretary Lloyd Bentsen told the gathering Thursday night. "If we wait it may be too late."
 
Liberal Grumbling
 More than a few liberals grumbled that their leaders were forgetting why they had become Democrats.
Representative Anthony C. Beilenson of California, a member of the Budget Committee, was downright caustic after hearing Mr. Bentsen. "It is poetic justice," he said, "that he's in charge of economic problems his votes helped create. He, like half the Democrats in Congress, voted to cut taxes and raise defense spending."
Still, the Democrats demonstrated that they were becoming attuned to the new mood of austerity and the public outcry against perks. The retreat was less stylish than in the past: meals were served in a gymnasium.
Lobbyists were prohibited from attending. They used to pay $6,000 to join the merrymaking, and their money was used to finance it.
While entertainment was not lavish, the sock hop Thursday night was definitely outside-the-Beltway boisterous. As a disc jockey ushered the Representatives through various chorus line dances from the hokeypokey to the electric slide, he urged them to "grab a peer; it will help."
The lawmakers dutifully swung into action. Representative Jack Brooks of Texas took Mr. Rostenkowski of Illinois, a fellow committee chairman, into his arms for a minute before Representative Dick Swett of New Hampshire broke in to dance what looked like a tango with Mr. Rostenkowski.
 
Lots of Wiggle Room
 Representative Charles E. Schumer of Brooklyn tossed M & M's in the air while dancing and caught them in his mouth without missing a beat.
"Is this on C-Span?" Representative Richard A. Gephardt, the majority leader from Missouri, asked in a tone of amusement mixed with concern. "If we can get them to do this, then we can get them to vote for the budget!"
Maybe yes, maybe no. The twist contest might have been a metaphor for the turf battles in the House. Mr. Brooks, Mr. Rostenkowski, Mr. Gephardt and Representative John D. Dingell of Michigan, the chairman of the Energy and Commerce Committee, appointed themselves judges.
Mr. Dingell unilaterally disqualified Mr. Rostenkowski from the contest for conflict of interest. Mr. Rostenkowski twisted anyway, and the other chairmen overturned Mr. Dingell's ruling. Putting seniority above dancing talent, they awarded Mr. Rostenkowski an honorary prize.
With their memories of the sock hop still fresh, the lawmakers gave a standing ovation this morning to Leon E. Panetta, who left the House and the chairmanship of its Budget Committee to become President Clinton's Director of the Office of Management and Budget.
Acknowledging the cheers, Mr. Panetta said, "That's not going to change the news I have to give you." He went on to urge the slashing of Government bureaucracies and a retooling of welfare and entitlement programs benefiting retirees that Democrats have worked to expand since the New Deal.
"There can be no smoke and mirrors," he said. He reminisced how as a Congressman he had hated to cast unpopular votes, but added: "There's no choice here. We must make choices we have avoided the last 12 years."
House leaders joined Administration officials in voicing the view that Mr. Clinton could be a one-term President if Democrats do not unite quickly to put the budget in order. But the liberals remained unconvinced.
"The deficit is an excuse for not taking action," said Representative Ronald V. Dellums of California, the new chairman of the Armed Services Committee. "You put people to work and you'll start bringing down the deficit."
