HEADLINE: Officials Say Redrawn Census Area Will Affect Federal Spending

BYLINE: By JONATHAN RABINOVITZ

 BODY:
The Federal Government is redefining the boundaries of the New York metropolitan area to include far-flung suburbs, a little-noticed move that Government and health officials say could cost hospitals in New York City $121 million a year and could make thousands of New Jersey and Long Island residents ineligible for Federal housing subsidies.
The change, which lumps Long Island, northern New Jersey and one county in Pennsylvania with New York City in the same primary metropolitan statistical area, has raised a storm of protest among public officials and civic leaders. They say the new district could affect Federal aid because it will alter statistics, such as the cost of living and labor costs, used in allocation of Federal funds. 
 Medicare payments, for example, are linked to a wage index for the metropolitan area. The city and New York counties have higher hospital labor costs than the northern New Jersey and Pennsylvania counties, hospital experts said. If the new metropolitan area is imposed, the wage index would go down for the New York areas and up for the New Jersey and Pennsylvania counties.
Many officials have questioned why the Federal Office of Management and Budget has expanded the New York metropolitan area to include rural and suburban counties that do not wish to have their identities overwhelmed by the city.
 
A Rejected Recommendation
 Although revisions in the metropolitan statistical areas are done every 10 years, after new Census figures become available, the reasons for this change remain controversial. Many statisticians and planners say they are particularly puzzled because the 1990 census figures did not suggest a need to re-draw the boundaries.
"When I saw the definitions, I was taken aback by some of the gerrymandering," said Ken Shipp, a Federal Bureau of Labor Statistics employee who served on the advisory committee overseeing the process.
He and other committee members said they were surprised to discover recently that the final proposal rejected the committee's recommendation to keep Long Island and northern New Jersey as separate metropolitan areas.
A Government official involved in the process, who spoke on condition of anonymity, conceded that the new boundaries were flawed, partly, she said, the result of a job that was six months behind schedule. She said the Government was soliciting local opinion on the matter and would reconsider the situation by the end of June.
The process of redrawing metropolitan areas after each census is traditionally accompanied by controversy. This time around, after about eight months of meetings, the Office of Management and Budget announced last month that it had consolidated a number of areas across the country in what appears to be the most significant revisions ever.
 
Country's Largest Area
 Experts agree that the most striking revision was in the New York metropolitan area. While Nassau and Suffolk Counties, 13 New Jersey counties and Pike County in Pennsylvania had previously been grouped as part of eight separate metropolitan areas, the new arrangement joins them with the previous New York City metropolitan area, which included Westchester, Rockland and Putnam Counties.
Connecticut is not affected by the change. The other region most dramatically affected, officials say, is Boston.
The new metropolitan area is the largest in the country, with 17 million people, spanning 24 counties from Montauk, L.I., on the east to just beyond the Delaware River, across from northwest New Jersey, on the west. The New Jersey counties are: Bergen, Passaic, Hudson, Essex, Union, Morris, Sussex, Middlesex, Somerset, Hunterdon, Monmouth, Ocean and Warren.
Metropolitan areas, as used by the census, were first developed in 1950 as a framework for collecting, tabulating and publishing data by geographic area.
 
Impact on Hospitals Cited
 No comprehensive studies have assessed yet whether the new metropolitan definition would substantially affect the distribution of Federal money. At least one economist, Regina Armstrong of the nonprofit Regional Plan Association, in Manhattan, expressed skepticism that it would lead to much of a difference.
But several New York State and City officials said the most serious impact would be a severe drop in Medicare reimbursements to New York hospitals as a result of the changed metropolitan area.
According to calculations by the Hospital Association of New York State, the hospitals in New York City would lose $121 million in Medicare reimbursements, with municipal hospitals losing perhaps as much as $25 million of that. The association estimated that Long Island's hospitals would lose $11 million. Westchester, Rockland and Putnam Counties' hospitals would lose $18 million, the association said.
In turn, the Hospital Association said, the New Jersey and Pennsylvania counties stand to gain about $150 million.
 
To Take Effect in October
 Judy L. Chesser, the director of the city's Federal Affairs Office, called the situation "totally unacceptable" and said the city would be working with the New York Congressional delegation to "reverse this catastrophe." The new funding formulas would take effect in October.
Long Island and New Jersey officials also speculated that a change in the borders of the metropolitan area could affect how they administer projects financed with Federal Housing and Urban Development money.
"It would be a mess," said Don Campbell, the Acting Housing Commissioner in Nassau County. "We would still receive the same amount but could spend it in fewer places."
For instance, Mr. Campbell and others said, community block grant programs that offer people assistance in buying homes often require that the buyer's income be no more than 80 percent of the median, or halfway point, for the metropolitan area.
If Long Island and northern New Jersey were joined with New York in one metropolitan area, the median income would fall significantly in Nassau and Suffolk Counties and in parts of New Jersey. Many suburbanites who were considered moderate or low income in the past would no longer qualify for such aid.
 
A Slap in Long Island's Face
 Aside from the impact on government funds, officials said the decision would have a devastating effect on their status as economic centers independent of New York. The lack of statistics specific to their areas would make it more difficult to attract business, they said, as well as make it harder for local planners to gauge trends in the subregional economies.
"It really does take away any identity from northern New Jersey," said Connie Hughes, director of the New Jersey Labor Department's data center. "People here are very upset." Local officials in New Jersey last week were studying the measure and trying to decide whether to fight it.
For Long Island, which rejoiced 20 years ago when the Federal Government allowed it to step out of the shadow of New York and designated it a separate metropolitan area, the announcement has been interpreted as a slap in the face. The Island's representatives in the House as well as both New York Senators have written to the Office of Management and Budget, objecting to the decision and calling for its reversal. Officials say they are optimistic that Long Island will be returned to its separate status.
Lee E. Koppelman, executive director of the Long Island Regional Planning Board, said: "Long Island -- with almost 3 million people -- has more population than 20 states, and to treat it like a tail to New York City is absurd. We are ready to go to war over this."
Some officials say the newly defined metropolitan area rightfully highlights an integrated economic region that has grown even more cohesive in the last decade. But they quickly added that the new arrangement was too large for statistical purposes.
 
Reasons for Change Confusing
 Dr. Koppelman contended that the redefinition was the last step in a 12-year trend, started under President Ronald Reagan, for the Federal Government to try to save money by limiting the amount of data it collects.
An Office of Management and Budget spokesman declined last week to discuss the motives for a decision that had been made under a previous administration, but several Government officials knowledgeable about the decision denied that cost-cutting was a factor.
It remains confusing why the change was made.
Several officials, who insisted on anonymity, explained the decision as follows: If Long Island and northern New Jersey were to retain their independent metropolitan area status, Federal rules required them to show local opinion in support of it.
These officials said that letters were sent on Oct. 1 to the Congressional delegations of these areas, warning that they would be merged with New York if no comment was received in 45 days. While members of Congress from other parts of the country answered similar letters, no one responded from Long Island, New York City or northern New Jersey, they said.
Other officials, however, questioned this interpretation of the regulations. They said that the Federal Government's timing did not take into account the fact that many of the Representatives from Long Island and New Jersey were retiring and almost all of the others were embroiled in tough re-election campaigns.
Dr. Koppelman called the explanation for the change "very suspicious."
"It's ridiculous to write to Congressmen in the final weeks of a campaign," he said. "What kind of response did they think they were going to get?"
"Why didn't they write to the regional planning board that's the most familiar with this issue?" he asked.

HEADLINE: Scoffing at Its Skeptics, Buffalo Prepares for University Games

BYLINE: By LINDSEY GRUSON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BUFFALO

 BODY:
The last time this city held a major international event, the 1901 Pan-American Exposition, it ended in a catastrophe: President William McKinley was assassinated.
Now Buffalo is trying again. It is scrambling to prepare for the July 8 opening of the World University Games, the largest athletic event in the world this year. And there are predictions of a disaster, once again. 
 "It's about time people started realizing that it's a flop," Mayor James D. Griffin said two months ago. "I don't want no part of it. There's no way this isn't going to end up costing us money and a lot of pain."
The prediction echoes the skepticism that has become normal before international sports events. They are so expensive and complex that it seems impossible to prepare on time or recoup the investment. Some do; others don't. Sheffield, England, which was host of the last games in 1991, lost $18 million.
 
Hint of Personal Vendetta
 Organizers of the Buffalo competition acknowledge early management failures. But they say the criticism by the Mayor, an early supporter of the games, is little more than a personal vendetta reflecting the social and demographic tensions shaking the area's long-fixed political landscape.
The organizers say they are gaining momentum after a slow start, and predict a whopping success. They say the games, held every two years, which are expected to attract a worldwide television audience and 7,000 college-age athletes from perhaps 180 countries, will make Buffalo a sporting megalopolis and provide an economic and psychological boost.
"For the first time since the turn of the century, we will be showing the magnificent beauty of Western New York to the world," said the Erie County Executive, Dennis T. Gorski, one of the games' godfathers. "It's a big thing for us. We're not Washington or New York, the glamour cities. This is a vivid demonstration that Buffalo can compete toe to toe with any city."
A study prepared by Nichols Applied Management of Edmonton, Alberta, predicts the games and a concurrent cultural celebration will pump $150 million into the region by 1993, at a cost of $72.3 million to city, state and Federal governments. The company also concluded that the 10-day event would raise the national profile of colleges and universities in the area and provide a long-term stimulus to the area's economy.
 
Doesn't Like to Fly
 "We're going to be left with world-class facilities," said Kurt P. Alverson, president of the Greater Buffalo Convention and Visitors' Bureau. "That should bring us events and dollars for years to come."
Armed with the study, the Mayor at first supported efforts to win the games. Since he doesn't like to fly, he sent a deputy to help lobby the United States Olympic Committee and the International University Sports Federation, the Brussels-based governors of the games. "It is my pleasure to renew my support for efforts to bring the 1993 World University Games to Buffalo and Erie County," he wrote in a 1989 letter to the president of the federation.
But he had changed his mind by the time his 1991 race for county executive was in full wing. It was a vitriolic, personal campaign with the games as a focus. But the rancor over the event reflected long-repressed tensions from suburban flight, industrial decline and efforts to retool the recession-racked region into a service center, the gateway to metropolitan Canada and the Great White North.
Those conflicts simmered for decades as Buffalo decayed into a small-scale paradigm of urban crisis, a shell surrounded by whiter, richer suburbs.
 
A Generational Brawl
 But the campaign also was a generational brawl between Mr. Griffin's traditional neighborhood-based politics and the corporate government style symbolized by Mr. Gorski, an efficiency maven. Mr. Griffin, who at 63 is 15 years older and much gruffer than Mr. Gorski, has become a combative political fixture since his election 16 years ago. The County Executive, by contrast, is a newcomer, a polished, rising political star who was seeking re-election to a second term.
In their only face-to-face debate before the Democratic primary, Mr. Griffin accused his rival of lying; Mr. Gorski claimed that the Mayor allowed corruption and cronyism to run amok at City Hall. Mr. Griffin criticized Mr. Gorski for hiring high-priced executives to run the games and predicted they would be a "shambles"; Mr. Gorski responded that they would become the greatest event in the area's history.
"If Mr. Gorski thought it was good for Western New York, Jimmy Griffin probably thought it wasn't," Richard F. Gorko, vice president of business development for the Greater Buffalo Chamber of Commerce, said.
 
Self-Fulfilling Prophecy
 Primary night was an embarrassment for the Mayor. He was beaten 32 percent to 68 percent; Mr. Gorski even beat the Mayor by a 2-to-1 margin in Buffalo. But Mr. Griffin soldiered on, running in the general election on the Republican and Right-to-Life lines. He was trounced again, losing by 22 percentage points.
The Mayor, who this year seems likely to face the toughest re-election campaign of his career, only stepped up criticism of the games and predicted a bust. It seemed likely to be a self-fulfilling prophecy. Although 12,000 Buffalonians had volunteered to work at the event, business leaders hesitated. So did sponsors. Local news organizations began focusing on the games' management problems. What began as a campaign to revitalize the depressed area seemed on the verge of collapsing.
But organizers now say they have turned the corner. They have reduced their budget to $34 million, one-third less than first planned. And they have begun to line up sponsors.
 
Financial Avalanche
 The best news for organizers was provided by New York Telephone and its parent, Nynex. In November they announced a $1 million challenge grant. While organizers must raise $7.5 million more in three stages to receive the entire grant, they say the grant has started a financial avalanche and that they will soon release the names of myriad more sponsors.
"Once the focus shifts away from the financial side and onto the athletes, I think people will become really excited," said Scott Brown, a spokesman for Mr. Gorski.
The Mayor may never be a vocal supporter of the event. But in recent weeks he has stopped his public criticism. "I promised I won't say another negative word about the games and I won't do it," he said in an interview. It was less than a ringing endorsement. But that is major progress for organizers.
"He doesn't have to love us; we would just like him to treat us with respect," said Burt P. Flickinger Jr., a local philanthropist and chairman of the games.

HEADLINE: From Well-Known Givers, Help for Neediest Fund

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD J. LEVY

 BODY:
New York Mets fans may remember it as a nasty episode in a particularly demoralizing fifth-place season. During a game last September, the outfielder Vince Coleman became so incensed after striking out that he began arguing with an umpire and then got into a shoving match with his manager, Jeff Torborg.
When the Mets punished Mr. Coleman by suspending him for two days without pay, he filed a grievance with the players' union and the two sides went to arbitration. But after the season, the dispute was settled. The winner was The New York Times Neediest Cases Fund. 
 Mr. Coleman agreed to drop his protest and the Mets returned to him the salary he lost during the suspension. He then donated that money -- $21,405.83 -- to the Neediest Cases.
"My wife, Lynette, and I are fortunate enough to have two healthy children," Mr. Coleman said from his home in Scottsdale, Ariz. "I'm just glad that the kids of New York will be able to benefit from the money."
 
Dozens of Notables
 Jay Horowitz, a spokesman for the Mets, said the ball club was eager to put the dispute behind it. "We're going into a new season," he said. "Both sides wanted to start anew. This was the best solution."
Dozens of other notables have donated to the fund during the current campaign, often as a gesture of thanks for their good fortune.
Peter Falk, the actor best known for his portrayal of Lieutenant Columbo, the bumbling-yet-brilliant sleuth, contributed $5,000. Chris Columbus, the director of "Home Alone 2," gave $20,000.
The tennis star John McEnroe, a veteran contributor, donated $10,000 through his foundation. Roger C. Altman, the new Deputy Treasury Secretary in the Clinton Administration, and his wife, Jurate Kazickas, sent $1,000. And the actor Tony Randall gave $1,000 in memory of his wife, Florence Randall, who died last April.
 
'Making Life a Little Better'
 Muriel Siebert, the first woman to buy a seat on the New York Stock Exchange, donated $10,000 to the Neediest Cases on behalf of her firm, Muriel Siebert & Company, a discount brokerage in New York City.
"Over the years," she wrote in a letter to the fund, "I have followed the annual drive and have had admiration for the perseverance that The New York Times has exhibited in its continuing and very difficult task of making life a little better for needy people."
Paul Newman, the actor and purveyor of salad dressing, spaghetti sauce and other foodstuffs, gave $50,000 to the fund through the company that he established with his longtime friend, the author A. E. Hotchner. The company, Newman's Own, donates all profits to charity.
Edgar M. Bronfman, chairman and chief executive officer of The Seagram Company Ltd., donated $5,000 after reading an editorial in The Times about Safespace, a residence for homeless teen-agers run by the Community Service Society of New York, one of the charities supported by the Neediest Cases. The Joseph E. Seagram & Sons Inc. Fund separately gave another $10,000.
 
Supports Seven Charities
 The New York Times Neediest Cases Fund supports seven charities that help tens of thousands of people. The Times pays the expenses for the Neediest Cases appeal, so all contributions are given directly to the charities. This year's campaign, the 81st, ends on Feb. 28.
Three men active in the civic life of New York City -- Felix G. Rohatyn, Andrew J. Stein, and Howard J. Rubenstein -- made contributions. Along with his wife, Elizabeth, Mr. Rohatyn, a partner at Lazard Freres & Company who is chairman of the New York State Municipal Assistance Corporation, donated $2,000.
Mr. Stein, the City Council President who has indicated he intends to challenge Mayor David N. Dinkins in the 1993 Democratic mayoral primary, sent $2,000. Mr. Rubenstein, the public relations executive who has advised Mayor Dinkins and worked to calm tempers in Crown Heights and other neighborhoods in New York City, contributed $1,000. He wrote that he hoped the money would "help ease some of the tense race relations fueled by economic distress and social need."
David McCullough, author of the best-seller "Truman," gave $1,000. "The Neediest Cases Fund is one of the most worthy and important of all campaigns in a city I love and depend on," he wrote.
And Howard Stringer, president of the CBS Broadcast Group, contributed $2,000 with his wife, Dr. Jennifer Patterson. "In a year of charity overload, sorting out targets for donations has been especially difficult," Mr. Stringer wrote. "Still, your remarkable fund continues to stand out, shorn as it is of middlemen. So here is our annual donation -- a drop in an increasingly overflowing bucket."

HEADLINE: Political Memo;
G.O.P. Seeks Identity and a Message

BYLINE: By RICHARD L. BERKE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ST. LOUIS, Jan. 30

 BODY:
Intent on erasing the bad memories from the Houston convention and the November election, the Republican Party met here in search of its identity.
But instead of departing today with new resolve, party leaders found themselves as balkanized by ideological disputes as the Democrats were after Jimmy Carter lost the White House in 1980. 
 The new watchwords of Republicanism were uttered repeatedly: "inclusion" and "diversity." Yet they sounded like empty sloganeering as an unusually timid Republican National Committee stayed clear of taking stands on issues that could further underscore its fissures.
In a contrast to the harsh tones at the Republicans' last big gathering in August, today members refused even to consider resolutions critical of President Clinton's pledge to lift the ban on homosexuals in the military or on abortion.
 
'Have to Stand for Something'
 About the only thing the membership could agree on this weekend was that Haley Barbour, a lawyer-lobbyist and veteran Republican insider, should be the new chairman, and even that took three ballots. It was a battle not over ideas, but who would make the best television spokesman and technician. (Mr. Barbour was nominated as a candidate who could "sit across from Bryant Gumbel and not let Bryant Gumbel get the best of him.")
"We must learn the lesson of 1992," Mr. Barbour declared in his opening address to the party today. "The lesson of 1992 is very, very clear, and that is: We Republicans have to stand for something. For us to succeed, for us to win elections, people have to know what we believe in, and why, and to feel like we will adhere to those principles."
But first, as Senator Bob Dole of Kansas, the minority leader, put it, the new chairman must "sit down and find some common ground."
"I don't have any remedy," Mr. Dole said.
The party is still struggling for a unifying message. The religious right and moderate Republicans are locked in a tense state-by-state, precinct-by-precinct fight over the social agenda. And even mainstream Republicans are at war over fundamental issues like the economy, with Mr. Dole warning Republicans here about the "staggering deficit," and Jack F. Kemp, the former Housing Secretary, pitching a more populist, growth-oriented economic message to conservatives last week.
 
Warning on 'Zealotry'
 While the new party leadership sought to strike a harmonious public pose, the fact that Republicans are still mired in finger-pointing and recriminations was underscored in the valedictory address of Richard N. Bond, the departing chairman.
In an about face on Friday that reverberated through Republican ranks more than any speech he delivered when he ran the party, Mr. Bond called on Republicans to abandon their anti-abortion stand in the party's platform and to "not cling to zealotry masquerading as principle."
Mr. Bond said in an interview later that he had made the speech because he believed the party could rebuild itself only "if we have an honest, sincere and open discussion with those who disagree -- the sooner the better."
But at least for the short term, Mr. Bond's words seemed to encourage the factional disputes. "Rich Bond is advocating political suicide for the Republican Party," said David O'- Steen, executive director of the National Right to Life Committee.
 
'Big Tent' and 'Big Magnet'
 Like Mr. Bond, Senator Phil Gramm of Texas seemed intent on distancing himself from the Republican National Convention last August. Though Mr. Gramm was the keynote speaker at the convention, he sidestepped a question today about whether the August gathering made the party appear intolerant, insisting that the only convention speeches he recalled were President George Bush's and his own. "I didn't hear the whole convention," he said.
Even as he promoted his idea that the party update its "big tent" theme of inclusiveness by calling itself the "big magnet" party, Mr. Gramm refused to say whether that meant abolishing the Republican plank against abortion. "It is clear we are not going to have unanimity on that issue," he said, adding that the decision to alter the language was "something that's got to be decided by a vote of the delegates" at the next national convention.
Some Republicans took heart in suggesting that even if they do not make peace among themselves, they can resurrect the party around the notion, as Morton C. Blackwell, a committee member from Virginia put it, that "Mr. Clinton will be so bad."
The new Administration made good fodder for this gathering, as party leaders were not bashful in declaring that the new President's honeymoon was over. Though the rank and file was reluctant to take up the resolution on homosexuals in the military, both Mr. Barbour and Mr. Gramm, who is chairman of the National Republican Senatorial Committee, attacked Mr. Clinton for playing politics.
 
'Pay Off a Political Debt'
 "The very first thing Bill Clinton did when he came into office is to pay off a political debt to a powerful special interest group, the Democratic Party, by saying our first national security priority is to lift the ban on homosexuals in the military," Mr. Barbour said.
In a hint of what the Democrats can expect from Mr. Barbour, who is a native of Yazoo City, Miss., he attacked Mr. Clinton with his trademark folksy anecdotes. This is how he described the new President's turnabout on his campaign promise of a middle-class tax cut:
"I tell you, it reminds me that they're very much like an armed robber who served a 12-year sentence in a penitentiary," Mr. Barbour said. "And now he gets out and the first thing he does, he knocks over the first liquor store he sees. I mean, that is exactly their mentality about this. They haven't been in office for a week and they've got five good new taxes they want to impose on the American people."
Perhaps because they are not accustomed to losing the White House, Republicans are borrowing from the rhetoric of the Carter years as they try to reposition themselves as the party of the people outside the capital. In 1976, Mr. Dole told Republicans, "We are a top-down party instead of a grass-roots-up party."
Today, 16 years later, Mr. Barbour spoke these words: "We have to understand that we are a bottom-up party; the Democrats are a top-down party."

HEADLINE: Status Issues Occupy Candidates

BYLINE: By WAYNE KING, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TRENTON, Jan. 28

 BODY:
In many ways the two Republican candidates for New Jersey Governor have a lot in common. Both are moderates in the mold of former Gov. Thomas H. Kean. He is a well-paid partner in one of the state's most prestigious law firms, while she is a wealthy horse-country patrician. And both hired illegal aliens as domestic help.
But in the first days of the campaign to pick a challenger to Gov. Jim Florio, the two candidates, Cary Edwards and Christine Todd Whitman, find themselves fighting over just how wealthy and privileged they are. 
 And while other issues are certain to be heard as the campaign progresses, the debate over rank and class has produced a wealth of press coverage that neither would otherwise have attracted so early in the campaign.
 
The First Shot
 Mr. Edwards, who was State Attorney General in the Kean administration, fired the first shot with his campaign biography, which is headed by the quotation, "I know what it's like to struggle to make ends meet."
"I was one of the original latchkey kids," Mr. Edwards said in an interview, noting that his mother was divorced when he was 11. "I was a 19-year-old kid in Bergen County, and my working mother couldn't get a mortgage because she was a woman, and I signed the mortgage, 19 years old, because I was a man, and they would take my signature."
"Christie Whitman," he said, biting off the words sharply, "Christie Whitman never had to decide between paying the mortgage and paying for food. That is the difference in our backgrounds."
The words sound a bit incongruous in Mr. Edwards's Parsippany law office, surrounded by garnet leather and mahogany. Until he asked to be put on advisory status during the governor's race, he was the managing partner for New Jersey in the Manhattan firm of Mudge Rose Guthrie Alexander & Ferdon, once the firm of Richard M. Nixon and John Mitchell.
 
A Silver Spoon
 Mrs. Whitman, who grew up riding to hounds and shooting skeet on the family estate at Oldwick, goes head to head with the class issue, which grew hotter last week with the admission by both that they had hired Portuguese nationals as illegal domestic help. "I have been fortunate, privileged," she said as she formally announced her candidacy at one of three stops in the state on Jan. 26. "I don't apologize for it."
Asked if she would live in the newly refurbished governor's mansion near Princeton, she again said that she and her husband, John, a financier, were "privileged to live" on the family estate at Oldwick, and would probably have no need for the governor's mansion.
Nor, she said, would she accept the full $130,000-a-year governor's salary, although she said she had not settled on a figure. Governor Florio refused a raise and takes only $85,000 of the $130,000.
But while Mrs. Whitman, who ran strongly in a Senate campaign against Bill Bradley in 1990, vowed economy and an effort to roll back part of Mr. Florio's 1990 tax increase, demonstrators carried picket signs protesting the hiring of illegal aliens. "Jobs for New Jersey, Not Portugal," said one.
 
The Alien Issue
 Although a similar admission by President Clinton's nominee for Attorney General, Zoe Baird, forced her to withdraw, it is unclear how the issue will affect the campaign here. In the media-driven world of sound-bite politics, it could fade away, as both candidates acknowledge they hope will happen now that they have agreed to pay taxes on the wages paid to the undocumented workers. Or it could dog them through the June 8 primary.
Although another Republican might enter the race, experts expect one of the two to be Mr. Florio's opponent on Nov. 2.
"I've never said that Jim Florio will be a pushover," said Mrs. Whitman, echoing the new conventional wisdom about the Governor, who, even before both his potential opponents ran into trouble on the alien issue, was making a modest comeback in the polls after two and a half years of approval ratings below 30 percent.
A poll of likely voters in The Asbury Park Press three weeks ago showed the Governor trailing Mrs. Whitman by 6 percentage points, 36 to 30, and almost even with Mr. Edwards, who was preferred by 32 percent of likely voters compared with Mr. Florio's 31, with about a third of respondents undecided.
Given the margin of error of about 5 percentage points, that put Mr. Florio in a virtual dead heat with either opponent.
Governor Florio has been relatively quiet on the issue of illegal workers, saying only that he had never hired an illegal alien himself and expressing concern over people of privilege hiring undocumented workers.
Republicans, on the other hand, have tried to put the best possible face on it. Virginia Littell, the party chairwoman, said the issue was moot and predicted it would have little effect on the election.
 
The Economy, Kean Says
 Former Governor Kean, now the president of Drew University and the Republican Party's most respected elder statesman, said he thought the issue would fade and the campaign turn to broader issues of government.
"In the end, it will be a debate over taxes, the economy, education," Mr. Kean said.
For their part, both candidates said they wanted to run on the issues, Governor Florio's stewardship of the state being the major one, not least the $2.8 billion in new taxes he pushed through the Legislature.
But both are also putting first things first, and the first hurdle is the June 8 primary. "I think it will a very tough primary," Mrs. Whitman said. "I respect Cary as a campaigner."
Mr. Edwards said, "I like Christie Whitman. I just don't think she should be governor."
He has accused her of threatening cuts in he state's Homestead Rebate program, which was greatly expanded by Governor Florio, then promptly slashed by more than half by the Republican Legislature in a tax backlash a year ago. He has also accused her of insensitivity in money matters.
Mrs. Whitman denies urging further cuts in the rebates and lumps Mr. Edwards into the category of "career politicians who will say or do anything to get elected."
Both candidates say that the women's vote will be critical in the primary, and Mrs. Whitman maintains that her candidacy has already forced Governor Florio to appoint 16 women as judges. "I call them the Whitman judges," she said.
 
The Early Years
 Mr. Edwards's campaign biography dwells on his early years and that his mother, Virginia, "a gifted professional with a B.A. in psychology, was paid half what a man would have made as a social worker."
In an interview, he talks of his parent's separation and divorce when he was 11. "She paid $25 installments for five years to pay the lawyer's fee," he said. When she was 68 years old, he said, and he was a member of the State Assembly, she ran for the city council in Fairlawn, winning and going on to serve two one-year terms as Mayor in the mid-80's.
"I will not surrender one inch to Christie Todd Whitman because of her gender," he said. "I fought for the equal rights amendment and have been on the forefront on women's and family issues."
Mr. Edwards's first foray into politics came in 1975, when he was elected to the Oakland Borough Council. The next year he won a seat in the State Assembly. He was Governor Kean's legal counsel from 1982 to 1986, and his Attorney General from 1986 until he resigned in 1988 to run for Governor, running second in a crowded primary to Representative James A. Courter, who was defeated by Jim Florio. While Attorney General, Mr. Edwards pursued environmental issues aggressively.
Mrs. Whitman, 46, was a member of the Somerset County Board of Chosen Freeholders from 1983 through 1987, and served as the president of the New Jersey Board of Public Utilities in the Kean administration from 1988 to 1990.
Her father, Webster Todd, who died in 1989, was a chairman and fund raiser for the Republican Party in New Jersey, and her mother, Eleanor Todd, who died in 1990, was a Republican national committeewoman.
 
A Sacrificial Lamb With Teeth
 She was a relative unknown when she took on the formidable task of running against Senator Bill Bradley in 1990, but stunned everyone by nearly unseating him with 49 percent of the vote to Mr. Bradley's 51 percent. She sensed his aloofness and the voters' resentment of that and campaigned aggressively. Nominated virtually as a sacrificial lamb, she ran more strongly than anyone had expected.
She has been running for governor ever since, under the aegis of a political action committee she called Citizens for an Affordable New Jersey.
In her 1990 race, she put her net worth at between $700,000 and $1.3 million. Her husband, John Whitman, then chairman and chief executive officer of Prudential Bache Interfunding, a group that financed leveraged buyouts, reported a net worth of between $1.5 million and $2.7 million.
Mr. Edwards, who did not release a financial statement in his 1989 primary run for governor, would say only that his net worth is "less than seven figures," or a million dollars.

HEADLINE: THE GAY TROOP ISSUE;
Compromise Dampens Republican Hopes on Ban

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD KRAUSS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 30

 BODY:
Efforts by Republicans in the Senate to retain a ban on homosexuals in the military have apparently lost momentum since President Clinton announced a compromise Friday that delays a final resolution of the issue for six months.
Saying they have a winning political issue, Senate Republicans said they still hope to add an amendment codifying the current ban to legislation providing family and medical leave. But they acknowledged that they lost crucial votes for such an effort when Mr. Clinton and Senator Sam Nunn, Democrat of Georgia, reached an agreement in which the armed forces will stop asking recruits about sexual orientation while continuing discharge proceedings for homosexuals until the Pentagon drafts a new policy in six months. 
Mr. Nunn, the chairman of the Armed Services Committee, was preparing to offer a compromise amendment of his own that would put the agreement into law, senior Democratic aides said. Should the Republican effort stumble completely, Mr. Nunn may not offer the amendment at all.
 
'Smoke-screen Compromise'
 "The Democrats may have the votes because of this smoke-screen compromise," said Walt Riker, a spokesman for Senator Bob Dole of Kansas, the Republican minority leader. "The Democrats have figured out a way to string everyone along."
Senate Republicans will meet on Tuesday to discuss an amendment, which would be offered in the full Senate and voted on separately from the family leave bill. The family leave bill is expected to be the first measure to come up for a vote next week.
However, Republicans seem divided over the wisdom of emphasizing a social issue like homosexuality after an election in which voters demonstrated far more concern about the economy.
Senator Alan K. Simpson of Wyoming, the Republican Senate whip, appeared to distance himself from the effort to codify the ban. "That will not necessarily be a Republican strategy, and it's not one that will be totally agreed upon by Republicans," he said today. "I have heard of no strategy for all Republicans."
Nonetheless, on Friday, some Republicans who favor an amendment proclaimed that Mr. Clinton had put his own legislative program in jeopardy by fulfilling a campaign pledge to a sector of the American public that is out of step with mainstream thinking.
"President Clinton's statement that his mind is made up makes a mockery of real consultation," said Senator Daniel R. Coats of Indiana, a Republican member of the Armed Services Committee. "He is acting on the assumption that this policy will eventually be abandoned, but that conclusion is premature and high-handed."
Senator Phil Gramm, Republican of Texas, said: "I can assure you that the Joint Chiefs of Staff could have gone for an eternity without ever considering this problem. It was the President, in the commitment he made to a special interest group, that brought this problem to a head."
 
Seeing an Advantage
 White House officials said they doubted Senate Republicans could attach the ban to family leave legislation, and they were prepared to seize whatever political advantage they can from criticizing the Republicans as trying to block legislation that is overwhelmingly popular with families with two working parents.
George Stephanopoulos, the White House communications director, said that the Administration plans to do little lobbying on the military issue over the next six months other than to stand by Mr. Clinton's general commitment to the elimination of the ban on homosexuals.
"As the process goes on and people lose some of their worst fears, it's possible to reach some agreement," he said. "And if not, we still stand by our principles."
Senate Democrats appeared as divided as the Republicans on the issue. Conservatives, especially those who serve on the Armed Services Committee, are reluctant to saddle the military with new rules that they fear could will hurt morale and combat effectiveness. But they are equally apprehensive about confronting Mr. Clinton, especially so early in his term.
A senior aide to a Democratic member of the Armed Services panel said conservative Democrats feel they have been let off the hook by the Clinton-Nunn compromise, at least for six months. Over that time, the committee will hold hearings in which witnesses favoring restrictions on homosexuals in the military will be given the opportunity to make their case.

HEADLINE: SUPER BOWL XXVII;
Where the Roman Numerals Dance With the Dollar Signs

BYLINE: By RICHARD SANDOMIR

 BODY:
When Super Bowl XXVII, between the Dallas Cowboys and the Buffalo Bills, kicks off this evening it will renew an American rite of sports and marketing that was born in relative modesty but has grown to be the undisputed champion of sports extravaganzas and a testament to cash and cachet.
"It's almost anti-American not to be a viewer of the Super Bowl," said Dick Enberg, who will announce the game for NBC-TV.
 
One Big Living Room
 At least 100 million viewers across the country (worldwide estimate: 750 million) will tune in the Super Bowl, with an expected kickoff at 6:18 P.M., Eastern time. Diehard fans will start the ritual with what amounts to a two-and-one-half-hour television tailgate party. 
 Between commercials, NBC's pre-game host Bob Costas (estimated annual salary: $2 million) will entertain viewers with features, interviews and endless reasons why this Super Bowl cannot be missed. After the 150-minute appetizer, Mr. Costas will hand off to Mr. Enberg (estimate: $1.5 million) and Bob Trumpy (estimate: $300,000), at the Rose Bowl in Pasadena, Calif., and after two weeks of hyped-up anticipation, the game, which has rarely lived up to its billing, will begin.
"I've never been more nervous than at the Super Bowl, what with the size of the audience," said Mr. Enberg. "The great bulk of the audience doesn't watch football all season."
It is that audience that induces television networks to bring out their heavy technical artillery, compels advertisers to spend millions of dollars to court consumers with splashy commercials, often to unveil new products, and motivates cities to grant sweetheart deals to pro football in order to bring tens of thousands of visitors to their environs.
The Super Bowl is expected to be a small, much-needed profit center for the network, which is paying $752 million to the National Football League for its share of a four-year contract.
Making it worthwhile for NBC, which has lost about $80 million on its pro football investment in the first three years of the pact, will be such advertisers as Pepsi-Cola, Subaru of America, Frito-Lay, Nike, Anheuser-Busch and Gillette willingly spending $850,000 for 30-second commercials, up $50,000 from 1992.
A rare chance to tap into the nexus of 100 million beer-drinking, soda-sipping, chip-munching, car-buying consumers -- a World Series game draws about half the viewership -- means paying a price that dwarfs the second-highest amount paid for prime-time advertising, $310,000 for 30 seconds on CBS's "Murphy Brown."
Put another way, it will cost $28,333.33 per second -- and hundreds of thousands of dollars in production costs -- to introduce Crystal Pepsi, Subaru's Imprenza line and Nike's Michael Jordan-Bugs Bunny commercial.
 
A Capacity Audience
 Advertisers cannot reach a critical mass of 100 million in any other programming, and to divide their expenditures into several shows over a month's time would distill their message. How sure an investment is it? Of the 25 top-rated programs in television history, 12 have been Super Bowls.
Bringing a Super Bowl to a city (this is the fifth visit to Pasadena) is big-time politics. And money. The Super Bowl XXVII Host Committee in Pasadena spent an estimated $250,000 in beating out other cities to be the host.
Why? Cash pouring into the Southern California economy is expected to top $83 million -- including $58.4 million from visitors -- 66 percent greater than the spending at last year's site, the Minneapolis-St. Paul area, where winter limited Super Bowl tourism to the great indoors.
"The message of the Super Bowl being in the Los Angeles area?" said Rich Perelman, the host committee's director of communcations. "With the Michael Jackson halftime show and the associated Heal L.A. project, and all the attention on this area, it says we're still here. It's still sunny. It's still fun."
 
No Rent at the Rose Bowl
 So prestigious is the Super Bowl's presence that the Rose Bowl, owned by the city of Pasadena, will get no rent, a largess that has become de rigueur for Super Bowl hosts. With 103,000 seats sold at $175 apiece (one price fits all), the N.F.L. will realize $17-$18 million in gate receipts.
In and around the Rose Bowl, about 100,000 20-ounce cups of beers will sell for $6 apiece, 55,000 hot dogs will go for $2 and 6,000 slices of pizza will be consumed for $2.50 apiece. Commemorative T-shirts will sell for up to $28, and sweatshirts for $65..
The N.F.L. is spending between $12 million and $15 million on its big Pasadena adventure. It pays the winning team expenses of $1.2 million and the losing team $960,000. Each of the 90 rings for players and staff of the champion team cost $4,000; the losers' rings cost $2,000. A replica of the Vince Lombardi Trophy for the winning team to tote home to Buffalo or Dallas costs $20,000 to $23,000, depending on the price of silver.
Members of the champion team will get 90 winner's shares worth $36,000, while losers will get 90 shares of $18,000. The shares have been unchanged since 1982, frozen because of the absence of a labor agreement between the owners and players. Although shares should increase significantly now that labor peace has been achieved, the football figures for this year compare badly with those of the World Series champion Toronto Blue Jays, who received $114,962 for a full share, and the runner-up Atlanta Braves, who got $73,323.
 
A Theme Park, Too
 The league is spending $2 million to stage the N.F.L. Experience, a pigskin theme park adjacent to the Rose Bowl that is offering free attractions -- once you pay an admission fee of $10 at the door ($5 for children). The amusements include games to test fans' football skills, chances to pose in authentic uniforms and player autographs.
If the Super Bowl is a money magnet for the city it is played in, it is also a lure for gambling. The Super Bowl is the single biggest sports betting event in Nevada, with virtually every angle, from who wins the coin toss to who will win, available for wagering.
Last year, when the Washington Redskins trounced the Bills, 37-24, $50.3 million was bet at the 104 licensed sports books in Nevada, but the legal bookies kept only $301,280, according to the Nevada Gaming Commission.
The year before, a close 20-19 New York Giants victory over the Bills, saw $40 million wagered, with $3.5 million netted by the legal books. Of course, this figure is a tiny percentage of the total amount wagered illegally (estimate: $3 billion, with every dollar untold).

HEADLINE: The Nuclear Risk Shifts to Asia

 BODY:
After America and Russia agreed to radical arms reductions, the risk of nuclear war seemed to recede, to the relief of most ordinary mortals. But the risk may just have been relocated -- to South Asia, where India and Pakistan are careering along on a nuclear collision course. Turbulent politics in both countries may be accelerating the pace.
A standstill agreement between India and Pakistan, policed by mutual inspections, could arrest this dangerous development. But there may be precious little time left for the Clinton Administration to help arrange it. 
 India's ruling Congress Party has been quietly building the bomb, as well as a mighty military establishment. It does so at the expense of improving conditions for its people. That misjudgment only speeds India's disintegration.
Not to be outdone, Congress's Hindu fundamentalist rival, the Bharatiya Janata Party, is currying favor with nationalists by flaunting India's status as a nuclear power. "We don't want to be blackmailed and treated as oriental blackies," says a B.J.P. spokesman. "Nuclear weapons will give us prestige, power, standing. An Indian will talk straight and walk straight when we have the bomb."
In fact, the bomb could have the opposite result if neighboring Pakistan follows suit and builds its own; nothing could add more to India's insecurity. A Pakistani bomb seems more likely following the death of the Army Chief of Staff, Gen. Asif Nawaz, who was a prominent nuclear dove.
Pakistan still says it's willing to stop bomb-making if India does. India is balking. It claims it needs to deter China, whose untimely nuclear test last May did little to allay Indian fears.
The U.S. and others can induce India to cooperate. India wants improved political and economic ties with the U.S. and allies like Japan and Britain. They can be held up pending a favorable response on nuclear talks. Arms sales to India can also be slowed, although President Boris Yeltsin's announcement last week that Russia plans to proceed with sales of arms and rocket engines seriously weakens this leverage.
At the same time, India's security concerns can be eased if Pakistan puts its bomb-making on hold and China offers reassurances on India's border concerns. Neither neighbor will be safer with a nuclear-armed India.
Nuclear arms will do little to ease India's more pressing domestic needs. Nor will a display of nuclear pride do much for its prosperity and unity.

HEADLINE: Teen-Age Gangs Are Inflicting Lethal Violence on Small Cities

BYLINE: By ERIK ECKHOLM, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LITTLE ROCK, Ark.

 BODY:
Around the intersection of 15th and Oak in the center of the town that just gave the country a President, the streets are lined with trees and small wooden houses with yards, the picture of an older Southern neighborhood. On a cold winter's day the sidewalks are empty except for a cluster of teen-agers who melt away as two strangers cruise by slowly.
The placid-looking corner is the city's most violent spot. With three killings there in four months last year, all in broad daylight, and six there or nearby in two years, it may be as dangerous as any place in America. Those disappearing teen-agers, it turns out, are gang members selling crack, and this is territory where rival gangs cross paths. 
 Little Rock, with a modest population of 177,000, is experiencing a big-city epidemic of gangs, guns, crack and murder. It had a record 61 murders in 1992, up from 52 in 1991 and an average of 37 over the five years before that. For the last two years, Little Rock's murder rate in relation to population has equaled that of New York or Los Angeles.
 
Teen-age Murders
 Since the mid-1980's, many smaller cities and towns have seen devastating increases in assaults and killings, led by a doubling in the murder rate among juveniles. Most of the killers and most of the victims are young black men.
Perhaps nowhere has the change been faster than here, for reasons no one can explain beyond the area's chronic poverty. "This hit Little Rock with the power of a sledgehammer," said Steve Nawojczyk, the county coroner, whose job includes picking up the bodies from the streets and who has made the study of teen-age gangs his avocation.
Mr. Nawojczyk (pronounced na-VOY-check) describes an evolution here that is typical. What were bands of delinquent teen-agers only four or five years ago, he said, have quickly grown into crack-selling gangs with caches of semiautomatic weapons.
In the last two years, drive-by shootings and what are known as walk-by shootings (for those too young to drive) have become common.
Most of Little Rock, about two-thirds white, one-third black, remains peacefully suburban. But a 20-block area surrounding Central High School, where Federal troops were sent to enforce desegregation in 1957, is now known to the police as the war zone. Mainly black and poor and infested with crack and weapons, this neighborhood and other areas to the east are riddled with gang graffiti, the coded boasts and taunts of Bloods and Crips, Vice Lords and Folks, names borrowed from Los Angeles and Chicago gangs with which some local groups are loosely affiliated.
To the initiated, the scrawlings tell menacing tales. On one wall is inscribed "187" -- the California penal code number for homicide -- someone's threat or declaration of achievement. On another is a pitchfork signifying the Folk Nation, and on another is "SLOB," the Crips' insulting name for the Bloods. More and more, too, does "R.I.P." appear, monuments to slain comrades.
With one crack gang lurking eight short blocks from the Governor's Mansion, Bill Clinton was well aware of the increasing violence and helped promote countermeasures like counseling and summer activities for teen-agers. But officials here have learned how hard it is to combat trends rooted in decades of poverty, rising teen-age pregnancies, broken families and broken communities.
 
Thousands Involved In Gang Culture
 Here, as much as in New York or Los Angeles, more and more killings seem senseless. Some involve fights over drug turf or drug deals, like the drive-by shooting of 18-year-old Cedric Fowler in August as he rode his bicycle at 15th and Oak. Some, like the nearby killing of 17-year-old Kevin Gaddy last February for his Sporter athletic jacket, seem more a reflection of greed and coldbloodedness.
Many shootings result from nothing more than gang bravado. Nineteen-year-old Kelvin Cohen had traded opposing gang hand signals with the occupants of a passing car when they opened fire, leaving him dead on the street in September. While residents of gang-plagued neighborhoods say they cower nightly to the sickening rhythm of gunshots, most people here, especially in the better-off area to the west, feel scarcely touched.
"Some people think it's a black issue, but it's everyone's problem," Mr. Nawojczyk said. "How many of these kids do you think have health insurance?" he asked, noting the burdens the violence was placing on city emergency rooms and the enduring expenses of those disabled by gunfire. He also noted that one of the most violent gangs is composed of white middle-class children in the southwest part of town.
Sharon Priest, who just stepped down as Mayor, said, "When you have kids shot in a drive-by shooting, that tends to get your attention." In 1991, as the violence rose, Governor Clinton called a meeting of civic leaders, and the county and city started several new youth programs as well as community policing. But the gangs were already well entrenched.
Mark Stodola, the chief prosecutor of Pulaski County, said his office had identified 41 named gangs in the county, most involving young black men but including at least two female gangs and a few white gangs. His new gang unit has identified 500 members by name, but thousands of teen-agers and young adults are believed to be involved to some degree.
With organized gangs so new here, most members are still young, with an average age of 16 or 17, as against 26 in Los Angeles, Mr. Nawojczyk said. Many say they were first drawn in at the age of 12 or 13. Some drop out of school or are expelled; others join their "home boys" after school.
The groups have neighborhood names, like Oak Street Posse, but most also identify with famous big-city gangs, taking on their colors, slang, secret rituals and hatreds. About half the local gangs identify with the Crips or their rivals the Bloods of Los Angeles, and half with the Folk Nation or rival Vice Lords of Chicago, Mr. Stodola said. In turn, the Crips and Folks, and the Bloods and Vice Lords, have formed alliances here.
For these youngsters, abstract rivalries take on mortal meaning. "The Slobs and Vice Lords are our natural enemies," said one 16-year-old, a member of a Folk Nation group, who therefore is forbidden from calling Bloods by their proper name.
Facing an attempted-murder charge, the youth attends Changing Directions, a special school for delinquent teen-agers run by the county juvenile agency. He admits to shooting two people, once in a car theft and once because "I was just being bad." He said he had always carried a gun on the street. "Somebody could end up doing something behind your back," he explained.
While the Little Rock gangs identify earnestly with big-city gangs, organizational ties between cities are often loose if they exist at all, Mr. Stodola said. A Los Angeles Blood may move to Little Rock or come for the summer, sent to stay with relatives by parents who thought they were removing him from trouble, only to form a new group here.
But in some cases, Mr. Stodola said, the big-city gangs use local affiliates as distributors for crack, which sells here for twice what it does in Los Angeles. The chance to make hundreds of dollars a day. enough to pay for clothes and jewelry and cars, is one of the prime appeals.
Gang members have disdain, though, for the "fiends," as they call their addicted customers, who include both whites and blacks of all income levels. Many gangs prohibit their own members from using crack.
Guns are widely owned in Arkansas, and gang members say they have little trouble getting them. Some steal or borrow a parent's weapon; others trade crack to addicts for guns obtained through burglary or bought legally. Gangs have obtained whole caches at once by crashing cars into pawn shops.
 
The Tilt of a Hat Can Prove Fatal
 Talking with gang members and those trying to turn them around makes it clear that the gangs offer what many teen-agers have missed in their own childhoods: family, rules to live by, power, respect. Boys who will kill without remorse use the word love to describe their bonds with their street friends.
Yet as exciting and expansive as it may seem to the members, the gang world is one of narrow horizons, bound by rigid adolescent notions of honor and retribution. An 18-year-old chief of a Vice Lord group described how easily a conflict can begin.
A few members might be sitting in a restaurant when a boy walks in with the brim of his cap tilted to the right -- a possible sign of Crip or Folk loyalty. "I'd say to him, 'Why do you wear it like that?' and ask him to straighten it or tilt it to the left like mine," said the 18-year-old, who would only give his street name, Kee Kee. If the boy gives the wrong answer, "there would be a battle."
What if the other boy was first to pull a gun? "If you're there for your cause, you don't think about that," Kee Kee said. "One day you're going to die anyway."
Kee Kee used to deal crack and served probation for robbing a boy of his jacket at gunpoint, then did community service for threatening a rival with a gun. But he decided to straighten out, he said, when he saw how his mother wept after his second arrest. Now he is back in high school, a senior, and works 30 hours a week at a Kmart for $4.55 an hour. "It ain't nothing compared to what I used to make," he said, "but I don't have to look over my shoulder."
He says he avoids criminal activity, but he remains in the gang and says he cannot make the others stop selling crack. As he spoke on a recent day, his Washington Redskins stocking cap -- two feathers in the team symbol form a Vice Lord "V" -- was carefully placed with the symbol to the left.
 
Last Stop Before Prison
 Carol Carlan, a former police officer who teaches "life skills" in the Changing Directions school to teen-agers who have been referred by the courts or expelled from school, describes her program as "the last-ditch effort before these kids hit the penitentiary."
No officials here have any doubts that for the most hardened youths there is no alternative to incarceration. But no one believes, either, that prison does more than keep them off the streets for a time; inmates often return to the community with deeper gang ties.
Social workers argue that most gang members are victims of circumstance themselves and that with enough close attention at least some can be helped to change.
Robert S. McCathern, who heads a youth project with New Futures, a nonprofit community group, took a group of gang members on a camping trip two years ago after a friend was killed. "They were seen as incorrigible," he recalled. But in unfamiliar terrain, with a sympathetic leader, their shells began to soften. "Beneath those hard exteriors were vulnerable children," he said.
Now, several young men from that original group work with Mr. McCathern trying to coax youngsters into legal, safer lives. Through group activities and therapy, Mr. McCathern said, they try to provide the emotional support the youths get from gangs.
"What scares me is that we no longer see the 13-year-old as a kid," Mr. McCathern said. "We just see the shootings.
The search for solutions is crippled by dismal economic conditions. Even without the allure of drug sales, minimum-wage jobs, which are all that many poorer youths think they can hope for, hold little attraction, and poor neighborhoods offer few good role models.
A visitor asked seven gang members from Ms. Carlan's class whether any had a brother, father or other male relative with a regular job. The question brought out nothing but embarrassed giggles until one gang member said he had an uncle who was planning to build a restaurant.
Those who go through Ms. Carlan's class have a standing offer of a job, at a local car wash. "They look at the wages and ask why they should work at a car wash or a McDonald's," Ms. Carlan said. "We say, Yes, but look at the outcome if you don't."

HEADLINE: Failings but No Solutions for Hartford Schools

BYLINE: By GEORGE JUDSON

 BODY:
IN six weeks of testimony in State Superior Court in West Hartford, civil rights lawyers have presented a compelling case that something is deeply wrong in Hartford's public schools. In a state that prides itself on the quality of its public education, the capital's schools are failing to educate large numbers of children.
The lawyers do not blame Hartford. In a suit known as Sheff v. O'Neill, they name the state as defendant because it, not individual towns or cities, is responsible for public education under Connecticut's Constitution. And the state, they say, has not protected children in Hartford from the consequences of racial and economic segregation so extreme that 90 percent of the city's enrollment is black or Hispanic, and 63 percent is poor. 
 "You have the poorest of the economic poor, with the worst of the educational worst, in the highest of high racially impacted districts," a lawyer for the Sheff plaintiffs, John Brittain, said on the first day of the trial, Dec. 16.
But in a case that raises the prospect of a school-desegregation order covering the entire Hartford metropolitan area, the lawyers have yet to answer the question of what should be done about Hartford's segregated schools -- or, more to the point for many city and suburban parents, of what a plan linking Hartford schools to suburban ones would look like.
Instead, as the Sheff lawyers end their testimony and the state begins its defense, issues raised in court have been left to reverberate in an anxious debate led by radio talk-show hosts and contributors to letters-to-the-editor columns.
If one goal of the Sheff suit was to use a trial to educate the public, the lesson is in danger of being lost on many people who readily deplore the conditions in Hartford's schools, but who cannot comprehend solving in their own children's classrooms the social and economic problems that divide American society.
 
Problems Defined
 "They are spending an enormous amount of time and energy on defining the problem, but they have carefully avoided addressing the solution," said the chairman of the West Hartford Board of Education, John W. Lemega.
"To state there's a problem -- of course there's a problem," he continued. "It's the failure of the plaintiffs in Sheff to offer a solution that has significantly undercut the impact of their case."
Much of the testimony offered by the Sheff lawyers focused on the shortcomings in Hartford's schools: overcrowded buildings and classrooms with broken equipment, outdated textbooks and few supplies. Looming over all were stark measurements of failure: scores on state mastery tests and Scholastic Aptitude Tests that put Hartford at the bottom of the state's districts. The suit is named for the lead plaintiff, Milo Sheff, a 13-year-old Hartford eighth-grader who was the first student to testify at the trial.
Yet Hartford, unlike troubled cities in many other states, spends more per pupil than most of its suburban neighbors: $7,746 in the 1990-91 school year, $904 more than the state average. All that money produces so little, testified sociologists, teachers and principals, because so much must be spent not on education, but on addressing the poverty of Hartford's children.
 
Compensating for Poverty
 An undue share of the budget goes to psychologists, social workers, special education and bilingual teachers, health clinics and other special needs, many of which are required by the state, they testified. Even then teachers confront classrooms full of children who come to kindergarten already behind in their development, change schools frequently, and are often hungry or depressed.
"Children who are suffering from poverty bring other kinds of problems to the school system that we must compensate for," testified Hartford's deputy superintendent of schools, Charles Senteio.
"What we can do depends greatly on the children that you give me in my classroom," he said. "Hartford provides the best that it can with the resources it has. But it is impossible for us to provide what I'm going to term education at its fullest."
This dismaying catalogue of deficiencies had to be presented, to support the lawyers' contention that Hartford children are denied the equal educational opportunity promised by the State Constitution. And the issue of poverty was central to their argument that providing more money for the city's schools is not enough; the burden must be shared by more districts.
The effects of concentrated poverty are so disruptive to a classroom, witnesses said, that teachers cannot teach effectively. "Even when I had just 19 children, I had 19 problems," testified a retired bilingual teacher, Gladys Hernandez.
The plaintiffs and their lawyers acknowledge that by focusing on the city's problems they risked creating a portrait of schools so troubled that no parents would want their child to attend them.
 
Beyond the Courtroom
 The state itself has seized on the issue of poverty. If the poor outcome of Hartford's schools is the result of social and economic factors, its lawyers argue, a realistic solution cannot rely solely on schools; the proper forum is not a courtroom, but the General Assembly, which can also address issues like housing and transportation.
And while Gov. Lowell P. Weicker Jr. has proposed that towns and cities across the state draft their own regional integration plans, lawyers for the state will have experts testify that mandatory desegregation plans drive middle-class white children out of public schools. The result is greater segregation not only by race but also by class, as those with the means to leave do so, while the poor remain.
The plaintiffs' lawyers have presented testimony from education experts on why Judge Harry Hammer should find in their favor, and testimony from desegregation experts on different kinds of plans that have been tried elsewhere.
The plaintiffs' lawyers have avoided outlining a plan themselves, in part perhaps because the numerous civil rights groups involved in the group are not in agreement on their ultimate goal.
 
Better Schools, Not Racial Balance
 The suit, after all, seeks two results: a better education for Hartford children by providing more resources, and a better education for all children in the metropolitan area by linking city and suburban schools. Many people in Hartford believe that winning better schools for their children is more important, if it comes to that, than winning integrated schools.
The plaintiffs also fear that offering a specific proposal would narrow, rather than open, public discussion on the issue. They do not want to be sidetracked by a hue and cry against forced busing, when that is their last choice, too.
In the end, outside the courtroom, they will have to make a sophisticated argument to self-interest: that Connecticut cannot afford to continue failing poor children in cities like Hartford, and that committing enough money to regional plans will improve schools for everyone. The goal is not racial balance, they will say, but better schools.
"We're sophisticated enough to say, if you just throw poor kids and rich kids together, that's not enough to make it work," said Jeffrey Forman, the assistant to Hartford's superintendent of schools, Josiha Haig. "The state will try to make it seem like that's all a judge can do."
 
Basics of the School Case
 At the heart of the school desegration suit known as Sheff v. O'Neill  are provisions of the State Constitution that guarantee equal rights, that allow people to sue if their rights are violated, and that make public schools the responsibility of the state. Here are the those provisions:
ARTICLE FIRST: Declaration of Rights.
Sec. 1. All men when they form a social compact, are equal in rights; and no man or set of men are entitled to exclusive public emoluments or privileges from the community.
Sec. 10. All courts shall be open, and every person, for an injury done to him in his person, property or reputation, shall have remedy by due course of law, and right and justice administered without sale, denial or delay.
Sec. 20. No person shall be denied the equal protection of the law nor be subjected to segregation or discrimination in the exercise or enjoyment of his civil or political rights because of religion, race, color, ancestry or national origin.
ARTICLE EIGHTH: Of Education.
Sec. 1. There shall always be free public elementary and secondary schools in the state. The general assembly shall implement this principle by appropriate legislation.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Milo Sheff, a Hartford student, is one of 19 black, white and Hispanic children who are plaintiffs in the Sheff v. O'Neill desegregation suit. (Steve Miller for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: THE GAY TROOP ISSUE;
After Just 11 Days, TV Issues Verdict on Clinton

BYLINE: By ELIZABETH KOLBERT

 BODY:
If all honeymoons were like this, heart-shaped pools might never have been invented.
Just 10 days ago, Bill Clinton took the oath of office in a warm, exuberant burst of television coverage complete with blue skies, fluttering flags and gauzy shots of the Capitol dome. But no sooner had the President hung up his formal wear than the glow of the inauguration gave way to cold assessments of his first 100 hours. 
 After what may be remembered as one of the shortest grace periods in Presidential history, many in the news media were already pronouncing Mr. Clinton's Administration in serious trouble before he had even had a chance to unpack his position papers.
"From up close, the Clinton White House has looked like the 'Not-Ready- for-Prime-Time Players,' " Lisa Myers of NBC said two days after the new President was sworn in.
"He hit the ground back-pedaling," Fred Barnes said on CNN's "McLaughlin Group" after three days.
"It is just the first week of the Clinton Administration," Dan Rather said on CBS after seven days. "But oh, what a week."
 
A Rush to Judgment
 Gauging by the tone of the coverage, it took just a bit more than a day for Mr. Clinton to be converted from the inspiring leader of a new generation to an old-fashioned, embattled incumbent. This rush to judgment was apparent both in the print and the electronic media. But it was especially pronounced among the television pundits who, it seemed, could barely conceal their impatience for an opportunity to evaluate Mr. Clinton's performance.
Fortuitously, such an opportunity arose within 48 hours of Mr. Clinton's swearing in, when Zoe Baird, his choice for Attorney General, was forced to withdraw her nomination. The President had suffered his first official embarrassment. Among the pundits, Ms. Baird's sudden departure prompted much talk about how Mr. Clinton, once so attuned to his middle-class constituency, seemed to have lost touch with popular opinion.
Ms. Baird had not yet been forgotten when, appearing Sunday on the NBC News program "Meet the Press," the President's Treasury Secretary, Lloyd Bentsen, said that the Administration was considering a broad-based energy tax to reduce the deficit. This provoked much head-shaking over what appeared to be the President's backing away from his campaign pledge not to raise taxes on the middle class.
By Tuesday the debate about energy taxes had already been eclipsed by the growing public outcry against the President's efforts to allow homosexuals in the military.
On Wednesday, it was time to look back on the President's first full week in office. A casual viewer tuning into these sober retrospectives might have been forgiven for wondering if, like Mel Gibson in "Forever Young," he had somehow been frozen and missed the entire Clinton Administration.
In a feature titled "Reality Check," Eric Engberg of CBS, reviewed the President's first seven days, one blunder at a time. Mr. Engberg's conclusion: Mr. Clinton's "promise to focus on the economy like a laser seemed to come unstuck in the Washington centrifuge."
The political panel on NBC's "Today" show had a similarly gloomy assessment of Mr. Clinton's tenure.
"Well, if he's trying to get the Republicans overconfident, he's succeeding," said Al Hunt of The Wall Street Journal. "I think it's been a rough six or seven days."
To be sure, even at the end of the President's first seven days, there were those who said they did not yet have enough information to reach a conclusion about the new Administration. "This is not, it seems to me, a signature for the next few years," Tom Brokaw of NBC said. "We ought to judge him week by week."
But it was a sign, perhaps, of just how harshly Mr. Clinton was treated last week by the news media that it was left to Ross Perot, his once and potentially future rival, to offer the most vigorous defense of the new Administration.
Appearing on the CBS program "This Morning," Mr. Perot was asked by Paula Zahn whether he thought Mr. Clinton was faltering.
"I think it's too soon to tell," he replied. "You see you're up there all alone. You're like being inside of a barrel with everybody beating on it with sticks and hammers, and I'm sure he is just sort of stunned now with everything hitting him at once."
