LENGTH: 1478 words

HEADLINE: 4 Upstart Carriers Find Niche at Newark Airport

BYLINE: By BARBARA STURKEN

DATELINE: NEWARK

 BODY:
INSPIRED in part by the economic woes of the major airlines, four new carriers have suddenly made an appearance at Newark International Airport in the last four months.
 Most of the newcomers -- three of which are already operating daily jet flights, with the fourth set to begin in March -- offer cheap fares but few frills; some even charge a fee for checking bags and encourage customers to take their own food aboard. And many of the new entries' employees once worked for airlines that no longer exist. 
All of which is reviving memories of People Express, the low-fare maverick that in the early 1980's transformed a shabby, underused terminal at Newark Airport into a mecca for affordable air travel.
 The airline was wildly successful at first, spawning a host of imitators, most of which later followed People into aviation history. The conventional wisdom since then has been that small airlines cannot survive in an industry dominated by a handful of huge companies like American and United.
 That assumption was called into question last fall when two new companies, Kiwi International and Skybus, began discount flights from Newark. Houston-based Ultrair, on the other hand, is offering what is billed as first-class service at coach prices. Family Airlines is scheduled to start cut-rate flights to the West Coast on March 1.
 Newark is the airport of choice for this new breed of airline for several reasons, including the size of the potential market. The New York-New Jersey metropolitan region "has the largest concentration of people who use air transportation in the world," with an estimated 68 million airline passengers a year at Newark, Kennedy International and La Guardia, said Robert Iverson, chief executive officer of Kiwi International.
 An upstart airline would have virtually no hope of obtaining landing rights at saturated La Guardia in Queens. Kennedy Airport is not considered a feasible alternative, Mr. Iverson said, since it is used mainly for long-distance and international flights.
 The new airlines face uncertain prospects.
 "History has shown that it is extremely difficult to make it in the long run," said Vincent Bonaventura, general manager of Newark Airport.
 For one thing, travelers are often leery of trying an unknown carrier. Glori Barchenko, a travel agent with VTS Travel in Secaucus, said that so far most of her clients had avoided the new lines. "They don't want to take a chance," she said.
 But the upstarts have some advantages. The large airlines have been losing record amounts of money, and of necessity are expected to increase their fares, while small airlines have lower costs and can afford to charge less. Most of the new airlines are staffed by airline veterans who contend that they have learned how to avoid the mistakes of their predecessors.
 The airline industry's current woes have, paradoxically, produced the most favorable climate for starting a new airline since the early 80's. The recession caused many aircraft to be idled, causing a 50 percent drop in the cost of leasing a used jet. New airlines can also draw on a vast pool of experienced employees who lost their jobs in the recent wave of airline failures.
 The price of jet fuel, another major expense, has dropped steadily in the last year or so.
 Regular users of Newark may benefit from the selective price cuts that typically follow a newcomer's arrival as established airlines rush to protect their share of the market. Soon after Kiwi started service to Atlanta, Chicago and Orlando, Continental and Delta lowered prices on flights to those destinations.
 
Kiwi International
 Kiwi International is the only one of the new airline companies that is based in New Jersey. On Sept. 29 it began operating three daily weekday round trips from Newark to Chicago and two from Newark to Atlanta and Orlando. It offers a reduced schedule on weekends.
 Kiwi advertises a "simplified" fare structure. For example, it charges $119 one way to Chicago, withno restrictions. One the same route, it also sells booklet of six one-way trips for $590, or $98.33 a trip.
 The airline's name, a reference to the flightless kiwi bird, is an in-joke of sorts. The airline was founded by a group of former airline workers grounded by airline failures. In 1989, Mr. Iverson, an Eastern Air Lines pilot for 18 years, joined other Eastern pilots in honoring a strike by machinists against the airline. Eastern went bankrupt and shut down altogether in January 1991.
 Kiwi's initial capitalization was about $7 million, all privately raised from employees. An additional $2 million has been raised from outside investors. Kiwi also received a $1.7 million loan guarantee from the State of New Jersey. The airline now employs 300 in the Newark area. Its headquarters are across from the airport.
 The airline has four Beoing 727 planes, formerly in use at Lufthansa German Airlines, which are configured to hold 150 seats, instead of the normal 177 for that type of jet. It plans to take delivery of more jets this year and to start service to Dulles Airport in Washington and to Buffalo.
 Mr. Iverson's strategy for avoiding the airline graveyeard is to limit service to a level that the larger airlines will tolerate. He says that because his airline's costs are abou 40 percent lower than those of the large airlines, it does not have to fill as many seats to break even. Recently, it had to fill only 75 to its 150 seats to start making money, he said.
 
Family Airlines
 Most of the pilots and flight attendants at Family Airlines, which is based in Las Vegas, Nev., hail from Pan American, which stopped flying a little more than a year ago. Barry Michaels, a former chiropracter who specializes in backing new companies, is the chairman. The airline is scheduled to make its debut tomorrow with nonstop flights from Newark to Los Angeles for $249 round trip and to Miami for $149 round trip.
 The airline will be using the jumbo Boeing 747's, redesigned to hold 500 all-coach passengers rather than the usual 400 or so in the three-class format.
 People Express tried the same strategy when it flew from Newark to London and Los Angeles for rock-bottom prices.
 Family can keep fares down by charging extra for meals and other amenities "that drive up operating costs," said Alan Loflin, a company spokesman, who is a former Pan Am official. Passengers who resist the extra charge will be allowed to take their own food aboard.
 
Skybus
 Skybus, which is run by Richard Dent, a former Pan Am pilot, began flying daily between Newark and Fort Lauderdale, Orlando and Tampa, Fla., on Sept. 30, with one-way fares of $99 or $119, depending on the time of the flight. It charges $1 for coffee or soft drinks and $5 for a sandwich. It is actually a charter operator, contracting with an airline called Private Jet Expeditions for use of a fleet of MD-80's that hold 165 passengers.
 Skybus, which is based in Fort Lauderdale, has applied to the Transportation Department for authority to become a scheduled airline. That will allow it to selel tickets through travel agents.
 
Ultrair
 Founded by Barney Kogen, formerly had of the Houston-based Lifeco Travel Agency, Ultrair is taking on Continental Air Lines by providing a more luxurious level of service at regular coach fares on two round-trip flights daily between Newark and Houston.
 The airline, which started out on Jan. 17, is putting only 89 seats on its 727's. There are two classes of service, coach and "ultra class." In coach, the rows are separated by 37 inches, versus 30 inches in standard coach seating. Meals are served on china, and passengers can choose from several entrees like a 16-ounce steak or grilled swordfish. Doggy bags are provided
 There are no discount fares availabel on Ultrair. The coach fare is $590 coach way, which is comparable to the full fare charged by the amjor carriers serving the route. If those4 fares are too steep for vacationers, that is the intention: the airline has advertised that it wants to offer a haven for business travelers from coach cabins crammed with families and crying babies.
 
Other Airlines>
 Yet another airline may soon arrive at Newark. Air Margarita, a Venezuelan line that currently exists only on paper, has asked the Federal Transportation Department for permission to start scheduled service between Newark and Margarita Island, off the Venezuelan coast.
 And then there is Carnival Air, which began service several years ago an a affiliate of Carnival Cruise Lines, carrying guests from Miami to the cruise line's resort, the Crystal Palace, in Nassau, the Bahamas. Carnival established a five-day-a-week service from Newark to San Juan, P.R., in 1991 and last month started scheduled daily flights between Fort Lauderdale and Newark for $99 one way.

PUB-DATE: January 31, 1993

SECTION: Section 13WC; Page 1; Column 1; Westchester Weekly Desk

LENGTH: 1500 words

HEADLINE: Helping the Homeless Believe in Tomorrow

BYLINE: By ELSA BRENNER

DATELINE: HARRISON

 BODY:
THE homeless men in the shelter joked and bragged at the kitchen counter while dinner was being prepared. Talk was about the future. It had been a long time since this group had some real successes to boast about, much less futures to look forward to.
The men were former addicts, derelicts who once roamed the streets at night looking for crack and alcohol -- who no longer have "to duck the light, or duck anything or anybody," as one of them said. 
 Steven Rowe was behind the counter, a cook's apron around his waist. "A lot of people don't realize they're only a paycheck away from being here," he said. "Things can change very fast in life."
Mr. Rowe is 37 and has lived in the shelter for more than a year. He remembers his two children, a life that once held promise and a downhill spiral that finally put him on the street. Now he has gained control over his problems and has begun to plan for a job and living independently.
The good cheer and optimism in the 60-bed Volunteers of America shelter at the County Airport that afternoon was a feather in the cap of Westchester's social services system, which in recent years has stepped up efforts to return some of the county's homeless population to productive lives.
The Volunteers of America is one of more than 20 nonprofit organizations under contract with the County Department of Social Services to provide assessment, shelter and counseling services for the homeless in Westchester.
 
'Better Than Our Sister to the South'
 As New York City and the state come under fire for failing to deal adequately with the problems of homelessness, Westchester, too, has been struggling with the issue.
"I would not say we've solved the problem, but I think we're doing better than our sister to the south," said County Executive Andrew P. O'Rourke.
The county's rate of homelessness last month -- 1 in 250 people -- was higher than New York City's, which was 1 in 306. But Westchester officials are developing a plan that they say holds promise for reducing its numbers -- in part because it offers the legions of homeless who seek refuge in the county's shelter system more than what social workers often refer to as "three hots and a cot."
Or as Mr. O'Rourke said, "You can't just stack up people like hardwood and give them a place to sleep, because they will still be there in the morning."
Although the county does not have figures for all of the homeless in the so-called assessment centers who have later been placed in permanent housing, statistics from the airport shelter suggest that the county's plan for rehabilitating the homeless may be working. Nearly a third of the 363 single men assigned to transitional housing there last year were able to move into permanent housing and resume their lives, said Catherine J. Marsh, director of the local office of the Volunteers of America.
"What we're trying to do," Ms. Marsh said, "is to get people to change the behavior that brought them to a shelter in the first place. Otherwise, they will continue to be homeless -- if not on a constant basis, then at least cyclically."
Despite the high rate of homelessness, the county cites a downward trend in recent years: 3,850 homeless people in shelters last year compared with a high of 4,553 the year before.
"We are beginning to get a handle on the situation," said Mary E. Glass, Commissioner of the County Department of Social Services. "But is it enough? Of course not."
Despite Westchester's $55.2 million budget last year for the homeless and the county's programs to address the underlying causes of homelessness, the county has been stymied in efforts to find enough permanent housing for many who are ready to leave shelters.
Part of the problem, Commissioner Glass said, was created in the 1980's, when the value of real estate in the county soared, and many rents hit levels that were beyond the reach of welfare recipients. The current housing allowance for a family of four is $393 a month, while the average two-bedroom apartment in Yonkers, for example, is $819 a month. In White Plains, that figure is $1,050, according to the Westchester County Department of Social Services. In addition, Commissioner Glass said, the State Department of Social Services has not increased shelter allowances for public assistance, and the county is now suing the state for higher allotments.
The latest figures for the homeless are still more than three times higher than in 1985, when there were 1,173 counted in the county.
 
'A Frail Group'
 Officials say that domestic violence, alcohol and drug abuse, lack of job skills or education and mental illness -- along with social and economic factors, including the recent recession and the county's shortage of affordable housing -- are the major causes of homelessness.
Officials also say that among single homeless men, like the group that gathered in the shelter's kitchen here, the problems are especially acute.
"They are a frail group," Miss Glass said, "and generally speaking, 65 percent of them are not going to walk off readily into the sunset, although some will be able to change their lives."
Of the residents in transitional housing at the airport shelter last year, 56 percent had mental disorders like schizophrenia and paranoia, according to the results of interviews and tests.
In drawing the profile of typical residents at the airport shelter, the Volunteers of America reported that 63 percent were 18 to 35 years old. Among those 18 through 21, 61 percent had been in institutions or had had foster care as children and 83 percent had spent time in jail.
 
Testing Is Required
 Although many of the men need drug and alcohol counseling and some must take medication for psychiatric problems, most are not dangerous to themselves or others -- and therefore they do not meet the state's criteria for institutionalization in mental hospitals, Ms. Marsh said.
Because of cuts in state-financed mental-health programs and the reduction of available psychiatric hospital beds, Ms. Marsh said, shelter systems statewide have "de facto become the mental health system" for the state.
Although the county runs drop-in shelters that can accommodate people on a short-term basis, Westchester requires all homeless who seek long-term shelter to enter an assessment program, which can include physical and psychiatric examinations, intelligence testing and job-skills evaluations. Placement is based on the results of those tests.
For example, after his evaluation by the Department of Social Services, Mr. Rowe was assigned to the airport shelter. And like the other men there, he is driven each day to one or more places like a mental health center, a work program, or meetings of Narcotics Anonymous or Alcoholics Anonymous.
 
Different Profile for Families
 As social workers attempt to address the needs of single homeless men, they say that a different profile emerges when they work with homeless families.
Sojourner McCauley, director of family services for the shelter operations division of the nonprofit group Westhab, said that caseworkers are seeing both one- and two-parent families, many of whom are victims of the recession and have lost their jobs and fallen behind in their rent.
They have also noted an increase in the number of single fathers, who have taken over the care of children after their wives leave home.
"But basically, the face of homelessness among families has not changed over the years, although some things are more pronounced lately," Ms. McCauley said.
 
Basics Are the Same
 Whether dealing with homeless singles or families, the county's philosophy is the same. "They need to learn life skills and money-management skills," Ms. McCauley said. "And sometimes they need treatment for substance abuse. In other words, they need to tackle the problems that made them homeless in the first place, so those problems don't resurface."
Like the Volunteers of America, Westhab is also under contract with the County Department of Social Services to provide for the homeless. It runs three shelters for homeless people -- the Vernon Plaza in Mount Vernon, the Westhab Family Shelter in Elmsford and the Westhab Employment Residence in Greenburgh.
From June 1991 to July last year, Westhab's assessment center in Elmsford had a caseload of 174 families -- among them 38 families with 2 adults present, 126 single-adult families and 10 single-male households.
In addition to its airport shelter, the Volunteers of America operates a 30-bed emergency shelter in Valhalla for homeless people who do not want to enter an assessment program and often look for housing on a night-by-night basis and the Huston Cortez Residence in Valhalla for 13 people infected with H.I.V, the virus that causes AIDS.
Other providers of services for the homeless in the county include Cluster Inc. in Yonkers, the Grace Church Community Center in White Plains and Westhelp in Mount Vernon and Greenburgh.

GRAPHIC: Photo: People at the Volunteers of America shelter at the County Airport. "They're only a paycheck away from being here," said Steven Rowe, inset at right. (Photographs by Susan Harris for The New York Times)

The New York Times

PUB-DATE: January 31, 1993

SECTION: Section 2; Page 5; Column 1; Arts & Leisure Desk

LENGTH: 2463 words

HEADLINE: STAGE VIEW;
Shaw, the Eternal Schoolmaster, Can Still Be Wise

BYLINE: By A. R. Gurney

 BODY:
This season, two of George Bernard Shaw's plays will open on Broadway within weeks of each other -- not a common phenomenon. A revival of "St. Joan" by the National Actors Theater opens this afternoon at the Lyceum Theater, directed by Michael Langham and starring Maryann Plunkett. In March, the Roundabout Theater Company will mount Shaw's romance "Candida," directed by Gloria Muzio, with Mary Steenburgen in the title role. In this article, the playwright A. R. Gurney, who has two new plays opening this spring, "The Fourth Wall" in Chicago and "Later Life" at Playwrights Horizons in New York, reminisces about the Shaw he has known for a lifetime. 
 Several months ago, I received a letter from a professor in Virginia who pointed out that "1992 marks the 100th anniversary of Bernard Shaw's first play, 'Widowers' Houses.' " This means, he said, that we are celebrating "the 100th anniversary of modern drama in the English language." The professor was organizing a conference and planned to present a paper on Shaw's effect on American drama. He wondered if I had any thoughts on the subject. "As an educated American," he wrote almost pleadingly, "you must be familiar with Shaw." When I was a teacher, did I ever teach him? As a playwright, was I at all influenced by him? Did I have anything special I'd like to say about him now?
The letter reminded me how much Shaw and his plays once permeated my consciousness, and how little I have thought about him in the last 10 or 15 years. He was, of course, a dominant literary figure during the first half of the century, receiving the Nobel Prize at the peak of his career in 1925. Still alive and writing when I was a child, he was invoked and referred to simply by his initials: G. B. S., like F. D. R. or M-G-M. I recall hearing him discussed at the dinner table with some irritation by my father, and with considerable appreciation by my mother. I also remember seeing him in a Movietone newsreel, clowning for the camera in front of his cottage at Ayot St. Lawrence in England on one of his birthdays -- a spry old man with a scruffy beard, an Irish accent and a twinkle in his eye. He died in 1950, when I was in college.
By then I had already read several of his works and was fast becoming a convert. To a 20-year-old, he seemed startlingly fresh and exciting, as well as something of a wise guy, which was right down my alley. I had collaborated in writing two student musical revues, and had become so entranced with Shaw that I proposed to the head of the drama department that for my senior thesis I write and run the whole show, which would be a musical version of "Pygmalion." The idea was summarily rejected as unworkable, so I ended up doing a study of "The Winter's Tale." But Shaw stayed in my blood.
His plays were being produced all over the place in those days. His bright, quirky wit and passionate intelligence seemed to cut through the smug, self-congratulatory attitudes of the postwar 50's, challenging and engaging audiences in just the way he would have liked. I myself was bowled over by two particular productions: the first, "St. Joan" in 1951 with Uta Hagen, at City Center in New York, was simply the most stupendous thing I had ever seen. All I wanted in the world was to write a play like that -- strong, funny and moving, all at the same time, with a gutsy heroine dying defiantly for a brave cause, while everyone else told us what it meant for European History.
The other production that hit me where I lived was a touring company of "Don Juan in Hell" that same year. This was the third act of "Man and Superman," performed by Charles Boyer, Agnes Moorehead, Charles Laughton and Cedric Hardwicke, who all stood at music stands in elegant evening clothes, half reading, half reciting their sumptuous parts. I was entranced by the language and the ideas, and by the thought that you didn't have to have scenery or even much of a plot to hold an audience spellbound.
Shaw was well served then. It seemed that a year didn't go by without some all-star production of one of his plays ("Heartbreak House," "Major Barbara") sailing majestically into New York, with actors like Laughton, Maurice Evans, John Gielgud and Pamela Brown tossing his dialogue around the infield. "My Fair Lady" musicalized and popularized him, and the LP chattered continually in the background of parties. A Shaw festival sprang up at Niagara-on-the-Lake, near my hometown, Buffalo, and, after I saw my parents, I could see Jessica Tandy or Zoe Caldwell or Douglas Rain doing the great parts to a turn.
Even the movies did Shaw justice. Not only were the classic English films of "Pygmalion" and "Major Barbara" regularly revived in art houses and libraries, but now there was "Caesar and Cleopatra," with Vivien Leigh and Claude Rains, a refreshing antidote to the sodden historical extravaganzas it seemed to parody. Shaw was a little too subversive for Hollywood, but still one studio managed to release an independent version of "The Devil's Disciple," with Burt Lancaster and Kirk Douglas, and Laurence Olivier cleaning up as Burgoyne. There was even a production of "Androcles and the Lion," with Jean Simmons -- or someone who should have been Jean Simmons -- playing a gorgeously doomed Christian.
Movie stars returned to the stage in Shaw (Ingrid Bergman and Greer Garson both took a crack at "Captain Brassbound's Conversion"), and stage stars fell back on him (Katharine Cornell revived and toured both "Candida" and "The Doctor's Dilemma") because he always seemed to work. Amateur and college theaters did Shaw constantly (particularly "Arms and the Man"). Plus, we saw a lot of Shaw on "Hallmark" and "Philco" and "Playhouse 90," in the days when television had cultural aspirations.
By then I was teaching in the department of humanities at M.I.T., and I'd squeeze Shaw into a course whenever I could. Several times I ran a full semester seminar confined strictly to his plays and prefaces. Most of my students, committed as they were to future careers in science or engineering, embraced and enjoyed him. His sharp intelligence and utopian aspirations were exactly what they wanted. Besides, there was just enough absurdity in his plots to make him seem fresh and contemporary, not far removed from writers like Beckett and Ionesco, who were coming into vogue.
Then gradually he slipped away from us. My students didn't buy into him as readily and I found myself apologizing for him in class. The prefaces seemed increasingly strident and tiresome, the tone of the plays themselves felt smug and self-congratulatory. And he was performed less and less on the stage. Now -- except for such star-studded triumphs as the recent London production of "Heartbreak House," the occasional revival tends to evoke bemused pats on the head from the critics, and no major stampedes. Why not? What happened?
Part of the problem is that most of Shaw is expensive to produce. Economic considerations have a lot to do with whether a nonmusical play is done these days, and late-20th-century playwrights are as restricted by the balance sheet as Aeschylus once was by religious traditions. Even as popular a playwright as Neil Simon pretty much confines himself to a single set.
But Shaw is different. Most of his plays call for sizable casts and a number of detailed, realistic settings. You can cut him a little, you can even try to eliminate a character or two (as the Circle in the Square did in a production of "Heartbreak House"), but to mess around with the scenery can be tricky because his sets so strongly reinforce his theme. The set progression in "Major Barbara," for example, as we descend from an upper-class drawing room through a Salvation Army relief center to a housing project for a munitions factory, reinforces his argument about the underpinnings of industrial society.
In Chekhov, the characters seem to dissociate themselves from their milieu, and so, when his plays are revived today, the sets may be abstracted and simplified. Shaw's people, on the other hand, are very much committed to the environments they have made for themselves, and much of the action in his plays has to do with their difficulty in changing and living in new places in a new way. The steam goes out of his work when designers, in the interest of economy, make the setting insubstantial or abstract -- there's nothing for his characters to push against. Occasionally, producers find a way of doing him simply -- as they did with "Don Juan in Hell" -- but most of his plays work best when they're staged in the solid surroundings he calls for.
Yet production difficulties might be surmounted if Shaw hadn't come to seem like such a cold fish. His treatment of human relations, particularly between the sexes, strikes audiences today as arch and intellectualized. Freud, his contemporary, opened a number of closets, but Shaw doesn't show much interest in peering into any of them. Audiences nowadays, when they pay homage to the fathers of modern drama, seem to prefer the warm baths of Chekhov or the steam rooms of Ibsen and Strindberg to the bracing cold showers of Shaw.
Besides, he's long and "talky," people say. Shaw would be the first to agree with that, but he'd argue that his talk is exceptionally good. He also might add that much more happens in his plays than in either Chekhov or Ibsen or even Neil Simon, all of whom are talky, too. After all, even in as languid and garrulous a play as "Heartbreak House," there are sudden burglaries, accidental explosions and far-fetched recognition scenes. Most drama is talk, Shaw would say. The issue is what's talked about.
And here may be the real reason why Shaw is no longer so popular. Unlike any other major writer in the 20th century, Shaw, at least until the last years of his life, thought he had an answer. By the time he was 40, he had managed to fabricate for himself a philosophy that seemed to synthesize a majority of the major ideas of the 19th century and tie them together so that everything came out right in the end. Most of all, he fell in love with the concept of evolution, but to him it was conscious, creative and purposeful. To Shaw, for example, giraffes had long necks because they wanted to have them to reach higher leaves, and those with this will to reach higher would pass on this characteristic to their progeny. (Darwin, of course, argued that long necks came by genetic accident, and subsequent long-necks survived simply because they happened to be more fit to survive.)
Shaw grafted his notion of creative evolution onto Nietzsche's ideas of the Superman and the Will to Power and Hegel's sense of the dialectic. To Shaw, Caesar and Joan and even Napoleon were examples of the superman struggling to improve human events, and his fictitious heroes like Jack Tanner or Henry Higgins or Dick Dudgeon were more comic versions of the same. He persuaded himself that the world was being nudged forward by a Bergsonian elan vital, or Life Force, toward a higher consciousness and a more just society. Our job as responsive Shavians was to plug into this force and translate it into action. And his job was to write plays that dramatized these forces and made them accessible to us. Shaw was a good enough playwright to recognize that audiences needed story, suspense, conflict and humor when they went to the theater, but you always have the sense that he's got the answer in the back of his book.
There is nothing remotely tragic in Shaw's vision, and he was very uncomfortable with the sense of anxiety or despair that pervades so much modern drama. Even in his "St. Joan," which has the most serious ending of any of his plays, he has to tack on an epilogue where his heroine returns bouncily for a congenial discussion with all the people who let her down. What angers Shaw, and depresses him later on, is not that people are cruel or evil or arbitrary, but rather that they simply don't learn fast enough. He is the schoolmaster, his plays are the lesson plan and audiences are a recalcitrant group of students who have to be lectured, cajoled, entertained and reprimanded into doing homework. Yet underneath there seems to be the cheerful assumption that we'll all ultimately graduate with high honors.
Possibly, then, it is this fundamentally jaunty belief in ultimate human progress that has lately caused students and audiences to shrug him off. For all his passionate indignation at the evils of modern society, and for all his deep concern for such contemporary issues as feminism and internationalism and disarmament, there remains in Shaw the feeling that underneath all our 20th-century turbulence is an evolutionary net that will catch us when we fall. He may amuse us now and then, but since we no longer can subscribe to such a buoyant philosophy, we can no longer embrace him with the enthusiasm we did in our youth, when the century was still young.
In these pessimistic days, I am almost tempted to argue that Shaw is really not the father of modern drama in English at all, but rather a quaint and quirky throwback to an almost Dickensian sensibility where all the knots had to be tied up in a happy ending.
Yet maybe, like Dickens, Shaw is to be considered one of those writers who transcends their own limitations. Certainly we can find elements in many of his plays that seem to go against the grain and give him a surprising thickness and ambiguity. Several of his villains and naysayers, for example, have such seriousness and persuasiveness that they tend to steal the show. In his later plays, when even his heroes begin to lose their faith in the improvability of the world, this countercurrent of pessimism becomes increasingly poignant, couched as it is in the rich Shavian style that he forged from the rolling cadences of the King James Bible and his oratorical experiences at Hyde Park Corner.
Ultimately, Shaw should speak for himself, as he always did. In "Too True to Be Good," a defrocked minister frantically tries to hold onto an ideology that is slipping away:
"I stand midway between youth and age like a man who has missed his train: too late for the last, too early for the next. What am I to do? What am I? . . . I am by nature a preacher. I am the new Ecclesiastes. But I have no Bible, no creed: the war has shot both out of my hands. . . . I must have affirmations to preach. Without them, the young will not listen to me; for even the young grow tired of denials . . . the preacher must preach the way of life. Oh, if I could only find it. . . . And meanwhile my gift has possession of me: I must preach and preach and preach no matter how late the hour and how short the day, no matter whether I have nothing to say . . . ."
